<TITLE><PERSON>Abraham Lincoln</PERSON></TITLE>

<PERSON>Abraham Lincoln</PERSON> (February 12, 1809 - April 15, 1865), the sixteenth President of the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION>, successfully led his country through its greatest internal crisis, the American Civil War, only to be assassinated as the war was coming to an end. Before becoming the first Republican elected to the Presidency, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> was a lawyer, an <LOCATION>Illinois</LOCATION> state legislator, a member of the <ORGANIZATION>United States House of Representatives</ORGANIZATION>, and an unsuccessful candidate for election to the <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION>.

As an outspoken opponent of the expansion of slavery in the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> won the <ORGANIZATION>Republican Party</ORGANIZATION> nomination in 1860 and was elected president later that year. During his time in office, he contributed to the effort to preserve the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> by leading the defeat of the secessionist <ORGANIZATION>Confederate States of America</ORGANIZATION> in the American Civil War. He introduced measures that resulted in the abolition of slavery, issuing his Emancipation Proclamation in 1863 and promoting the passage of the Thirteenth Amendment to the Constitution, which passed <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> before <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s death and was ratified by the states later in 1865.

<PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> closely supervised the victorious war effort, especially the selection of top generals, including <PERSON>Ulysses S. Grant</PERSON>. Historians have concluded that he handled the factions of the <ORGANIZATION>Republican Party</ORGANIZATION> well, bringing leaders of each faction into his cabinet and forcing them to cooperate. <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> successfully defused a war scare with the <LOCATION>United Kingdom</LOCATION> in 1861. Under his leadership, the Union took control of the border slave states at the start of the war. Additionally, he managed his own reelection in the 1864 presidential election.

Opponents of the war (also known as "Copperheads") criticized <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> for refusing to compromise on the slavery issue. Conversely, the Radical Republicans, an abolitionist faction of the <ORGANIZATION>Republican Party</ORGANIZATION>, criticized him for moving too slowly in abolishing slavery. Even with these road blocks, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> successfully rallied public opinion through his rhetoric and speeches; his <ORGANIZATION>Gettysburg Address</ORGANIZATION> is but one example of this. At the close of the war, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> held a moderate view of Reconstruction, seeking to speedily reunite the nation through a policy of generous reconciliation. His assassination in 1865 was the first presidential assassination in <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> history and made him a martyr for the ideal of national unity.



<PERSON>Abraham Lincoln</PERSON> was born on February 12, 1809, to <PERSON>Thomas Lincoln</PERSON> and <PERSON>Nancy Hanks</PERSON>, two uneducated farmers, in a one-room log cabin on the  Sinking Spring Farm, in southeast <LOCATION>Hardin County</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Kentucky</LOCATION> (now part of <LOCATION>LaRue County</LOCATION>), making him the first president born outside the original Thirteen Colonies. <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s ancestor <PERSON>Samuel Lincoln</PERSON> had arrived in <LOCATION>Hingham</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Massachusetts</LOCATION> in the 17th century, but his descendants had gradually moved west, from <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION> to <LOCATION>Virginia</LOCATION> and then westward to the frontier.

For some time, <PERSON>Thomas Lincoln</PERSON>, <PERSON>Abraham</PERSON>'s father, was a respected and relatively affluent citizen of the <LOCATION>Kentucky</LOCATION> backcountry. He had purchased the Sinking Spring Farm in December 1808 for $200 cash and assumption of a debt. The family belonged to a Hardshell Baptist church, although <PERSON>Abraham</PERSON> himself never joined their church, or any other church for that matter.

In 1816, the <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> family was forced to make a new start in <LOCATION>Perry County</LOCATION> (now in <LOCATION>Spencer County</LOCATION>), <LOCATION>Indiana</LOCATION>. He later noted that this move was "partly on account of slavery," and partly because of difficulties with land deeds in <LOCATION>Kentucky</LOCATION>: Unlike land in the <LOCATION>Northwest Territory</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Kentucky</LOCATION> never had a proper <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> survey, and farmers often had difficulties proving title to their property.

When <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> was nine, his mother, then 34 years old, died of milk sickness. Soon afterwards, his father remarried to <PERSON>Sarah Bush Johnston</PERSON>. <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> was affectionate toward his stepmother, whom he would call "Mother" for the rest of his life, but he was distant from his father.

In 1830, after more economic and land-title difficulties in <LOCATION>Indiana</LOCATION>, the family settled on public land in <LOCATION>Macon County</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Illinois</LOCATION>. The following winter was desolate and especially brutal, and the family considered moving back to <LOCATION>Indiana</LOCATION>. The following year, when his father relocated the family to a new homestead in <LOCATION>Coles County</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Illinois</LOCATION>, 22-year-old <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> struck out on his own, canoeing down the <LOCATION>Sangamon River</LOCATION> to the village of <LOCATION>New Salem</LOCATION> in <LOCATION>Sangamon County</LOCATION>. Later that year, hired by <LOCATION>New Salem</LOCATION> businessman <PERSON>Denton Offutt</PERSON> and accompanied by friends, he took goods from <LOCATION>New Salem</LOCATION> to <LOCATION>New Orleans</LOCATION> via flatboat on the <LOCATION>Sangamon</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Illinois</LOCATION> and <LOCATION>Mississippi</LOCATION> rivers.

<PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s formal education consisted of about 18 months of schooling, but he was largely self-educated and an avid reader. He was also a talented local wrestler and skilled with an axe. <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> avoided hunting and fishing because he did not like killing animals, even for food. At 6 foot 4 inches (1.93 m), he was unusually tall, as well as strong.

<PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> began his political career in 1832, at age 23, with an unsuccessful campaign for the <ORGANIZATION>Illinois General Assembly</ORGANIZATION>, as a member of the <ORGANIZATION>Whig Party</ORGANIZATION>. The centerpiece of his platform was the undertaking of navigational improvements on the <LOCATION>Sangamon River</LOCATION>. He believed that this would attract steamboat traffic, which would allow the sparsely populated, poorer areas along the river to flourish.

He was elected captain of an <LOCATION>Illinois</LOCATION> militia company drawn from <LOCATION>New Salem</LOCATION> during the Black Hawk War, and later wrote that he had not had "any such success in life which gave him so much satisfaction."

For several months, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> ran a small store in <LOCATION>New Salem</LOCATION>. In 1834, he won election to the state legislature, and, after coming across the Commentaries on the Laws of <LOCATION>England</LOCATION>, began to teach himself law. Admitted to the bar in 1837, he moved to <LOCATION>Springfield</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Illinois</LOCATION>, that same year and began to practice law with <PERSON>John T. Stuart</PERSON>. With a reputation as a formidable adversary during cross-examinations and in his closing arguments, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> became an able and successful lawyer.

He served four successive terms in the <ORGANIZATION>Illinois House of Representatives</ORGANIZATION> as a representative from <LOCATION>Sangamon County</LOCATION>, and became a leader of the Illinois Whig party. In 1837, he made his first protest against slavery in the <ORGANIZATION>Illinois House</ORGANIZATION>, stating that the institution was "founded on both injustice and bad policy." It was also in this same year that <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> met <PERSON>Joshua Fry Speed</PERSON>, who would become a close friend.

<PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> wrote a series of anonymous letters, published in 1842 in the <ORGANIZATION>Sangamon Journal</ORGANIZATION>, mocking State Auditor and prominent Democrat <PERSON>James Shields</PERSON>. Two years later, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> entered law practice with <PERSON>William Herndon</PERSON>, a fellow Whig. In 1854, both men joined the fledgling <ORGANIZATION>Republican Party</ORGANIZATION>. Following <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s death, <PERSON>Herndon</PERSON> began collecting stories about <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> and published them in <PERSON>Herndon</PERSON>'s <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>.



On November 4, 1842 <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> married <PERSON>Mary Todd</PERSON>, daughter of a prominent slave-owning family from <LOCATION>Kentucky</LOCATION>. The couple had four sons. <PERSON>Robert Todd Lincoln</PERSON> was born in <LOCATION>Springfield</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Illinois</LOCATION> on August 1, 1843. Their only child to survive into adulthood, young <PERSON>Robert</PERSON> attended <ORGANIZATION>Phillips Exeter Academy</ORGANIZATION> and <ORGANIZATION>Harvard College</ORGANIZATION>.

The other <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> children were born in <LOCATION>Springfield</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Illinois</LOCATION>, and died either during childhood or their teen years. <PERSON>Edward Baker Lincoln</PERSON> was born on March 10, 1846, and died on February 1, 1850, also in <LOCATION>Springfield</LOCATION>. <PERSON>William</PERSON> "Willie" <PERSON>Wallace Lincoln</PERSON> was born on December 21, 1850, and died on February 20, 1862 in <LOCATION>Washington</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>D.C.</LOCATION>, during President <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s first term. <PERSON>Thomas</PERSON> "Tad" <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> was born on April 4, 1853, and died on July 16, 1871 in <LOCATION>Chicago</LOCATION>.

A Whig and an admirer of party leader <PERSON>Henry Clay</PERSON>, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> was elected to a term in the <ORGANIZATION>U.S. House of Representatives</ORGANIZATION> in 1846. As a freshman House member, he was not a particularly powerful or influential figure. However, he spoke out against the Mexican-American War, which he attributed to President <PERSON>Polk</PERSON>'s desire for "military glory" and challenged the President's claims regarding the <LOCATION>Texas</LOCATION> boundary and offered Spot Resolutions, demanding to know on what "spot" on <LOCATION>US</LOCATION> soil that blood was first spilt.

<PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> later damaged his political reputation with a speech in which he declared, "God of Heaven has forgotten to defend the weak and innocent, and permitted the strong band of murderers and demons from hell to kill men, women, and children, and lay waste and pillage the land of the just." Two weeks later, President <PERSON>Polk</PERSON> sent a peace treaty to <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION>. While no one in <LOCATION>Washington</LOCATION> paid any attention to <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>, the Democrats orchestrated angry outbursts from across his district, where the war was popular and many had volunteered.

Warned by his law partner, <PERSON>William Herndon</PERSON>, that the damage was mounting and irreparable, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> decided not to run for reelection. His statements were not easily forgotten, and would haunt him during the Civil War. These statements were also held against him when he applied for a position in the new <PERSON>Taylor</PERSON> administration. Instead, <PERSON>Taylor</PERSON>'s people offered <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> various positions in the remote <LOCATION>Oregon Territory</LOCATION>, primarily the governorship. Acceptance of this offer would have ended his career in the rapidly growing state of <LOCATION>Illinois</LOCATION>, so <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> declined the position. Returning to <LOCATION>Springfield</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> gave up politics for several years and turned his energies to his law practice.

By the mid-1850s, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s caseload focused largely on the competing transportation interests of river barges and railroads. In one prominent 1851 case, he represented the <ORGANIZATION>Alton & Sangamon Railroad</ORGANIZATION> in a dispute with a shareholder, <PERSON>James A. Barret</PERSON>. <PERSON>Barret</PERSON> had refused to pay the balance on his pledge to the railroad on the grounds that it had changed its originally planned route. <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> argued that as a matter of law a corporation is not bound by its original charter when that charter can be amended in the public interest, that the newer route proposed by <ORGANIZATION>Alton & Sangamon</ORGANIZATION> was superior and less expensive, and that accordingly, the corporation had a right to sue <PERSON>Barret</PERSON> for his delinquent payment. He won this case, and the decision by the <ORGANIZATION>Illinois Supreme Court</ORGANIZATION> was eventually cited by several other courts throughout the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION>.

The civil case which won <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> fame as a lawyer was the landmark <ORGANIZATION>Hurd v. Rock Island Bridge Company</ORGANIZATION>. <LOCATION>America</LOCATION>'s expansion west, which <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> strongly supported, was seen as an economic threat to the river trade, which ran north-to-south, primarily on the <LOCATION>Mississippi</LOCATION> river. In 1856 a steamboat collided with a bridge, built by the Rock Island Railroad, between <LOCATION>Rock Island</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Illinois</LOCATION>, and <PERSON>Davenport</PERSON>, <LOCATION>Iowa</LOCATION>, the first railroad bridge to span the <LOCATION>Mississippi</LOCATION>. The steamboat owner sued for damages, claiming the bridge was a hazard to navigation. <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> argued in court for the railroad and won, removing a costly impediment to western expansion by establishing the right of land routes to bridge waterways.

Possibly the most notable criminal trial of <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s career as a lawyer came in 1858, when he defended <PERSON>William "Duff" Armstrong</PERSON>, who had been charged with murder. The case became famous for <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s use of judicial notice a rare tactic at that time to show that an eyewitness had lied on the stand. After the witness testified to having seen the crime by moonlight, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> produced a Farmers' Almanac to show that the moon on that date was at such a low angle that it could not have provided enough illumination to see anything clearly. Based almost entirely on this evidence, <PERSON>Armstrong</PERSON> was acquitted.

<PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> was involved in more than 5,100 cases in <LOCATION>Illinois</LOCATION> alone during his 23-year legal career. Though many of these cases involved little more than filing a writ, others were more substantial and quite involved. <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> and his partners appeared before the <ORGANIZATION>Illinois State Supreme Court</ORGANIZATION> more than 400 times.

<PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> returned to politics in response to the Kansas-Nebraska Act (1854), which expressly repealed the limits on slavery's extent as determined by the Missouri Compromise (1820). <LOCATION>Illinois</LOCATION> Democrat <PERSON>Stephen A. Douglas</PERSON>, the most powerful man in the <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION>, proposed popular sovereignty as the solution to the slavery impasse, and incorporated it into the <LOCATION>Kansas</LOCATION>-<LOCATION>Nebraska</LOCATION> Act. <PERSON>Douglas</PERSON> argued that in a democracy the people should have the right to decide whether or not to allow slavery in their territory, rather than have such a decision imposed on them by <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION>.

In the October 16, 1854, "<LOCATION>Peoria</LOCATION> Speech", <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> first stood out among the other free soil orators of the day:



Drawing on remnants of the old Whig, <ORGANIZATION>Free Soil</ORGANIZATION>, <ORGANIZATION>Liberty</ORGANIZATION> and Democratic parties, he was instrumental in forming the new <ORGANIZATION>Republican Party</ORGANIZATION>. In a stirring campaign, the Republicans carried <LOCATION>Illinois</LOCATION> in 1854 and elected a senator. <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> was the obvious choice, but to keep the new party balanced he allowed the election to go to an ex-Democrat <PERSON>Lyman Trumbull</PERSON>. At the Republican convention in 1856, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> placed second in the contest to become the party's candidate for Vice-President.

In 1857-58, <PERSON>Douglas</PERSON> broke with President <PERSON>Buchanan</PERSON>, leading to a fight for control of the <ORGANIZATION>Democratic Party</ORGANIZATION>. Some eastern Republicans even favored the reelection of <PERSON>Douglas</PERSON> in 1858, since he had led the opposition to the <ORGANIZATION>Lecompton Constitution</ORGANIZATION>, which would have admitted <LOCATION>Kansas</LOCATION> as a slave state. Accepting the Republican nomination for <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION> in 1858, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> delivered his famous speech: "'A house divided against itself cannot stand.'(Mark 3:25) I believe this government cannot endure permanently half slave and half free. I do not expect the Union to be dissolved I do not expect the house to fall but I do expect it will cease to be divided. It will become all one thing, or all the other." The speech created an evocative image of the danger of disunion caused by the slavery debate, and rallied Republicans across the north.

The 1858 campaign featured the Lincoln-Douglas debates, a famous contest on slavery. <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> warned that "The Slave Power" was threatening the values of republicanism, while <PERSON>Douglas</PERSON> emphasized the supremacy of democracy, as set forth in his <ORGANIZATION>Freeport Doctrine</ORGANIZATION>, which said that local settlers should be free to choose whether to allow slavery or not. Though the Republican legislative candidates won more popular votes, the Democrats won more seats, and the legislature reelected <PERSON>Douglas</PERSON> to the <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION>. Nevertheless, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s speeches on the issue transformed him into a national political star.

<PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> was chosen as the <ORGANIZATION>Republican</ORGANIZATION> candidate for the 1860 election for several reasons. His expressed views on slavery were seen as more moderate than those of rivals <PERSON>William H. Seward</PERSON> and <PERSON>Salmon P. Chase</PERSON>. His "Western" origins also appealed to the newer states: other contenders, especially those with more governmental experience, had acquired enemies within the party and were weak in the critical western states, while <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> was perceived as a moderate who could win the West. Most Republicans agreed with <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> that the <PERSON>North</PERSON> was the aggrieved party as the Slave Power tightened its grasp on the national government. Throughout the 1850s he denied that there would ever be a civil war, and his supporters repeatedly rejected claims that his election would incite secession. On May 9-10, 1860, the <LOCATION>Illinois</LOCATION> Republican State Convention was held in <LOCATION>Decatur</LOCATION>. At this convention, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> received his first endorsement to run for the presidency.

Throughout the general election, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> did not campaign or give speeches. This was handled by the state and county Republican organizations, who used the latest techniques to sustain party enthusiasm and thus obtain high turnout. There was little effort to convert non-Republicans, and there was virtually no campaigning in the South except for a few border cities such as <LOCATION>St. Louis</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Missouri</LOCATION>, and Wheeling, <LOCATION>Virginia</LOCATION>; indeed, the party did not even run a slate in most of the South. In the North, there were thousands of Republican speakers, tons of campaign posters and leaflets, and thousands of newspaper editorials. These focused first on the party platform, and second on <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s life story, making the most of his boyhood poverty, his pioneer background, his native genius, and his rise from obscurity. His nicknames, "Honest Abe" and "the Rail-Splitter," were exploited to the full. The goal was to emphasize the superior power of "free labor," whereby a common farm boy could work his way to the top by his own efforts.

On November 6, 1860, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> was elected as the 16th President of the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION>, beating Democrat <PERSON>Stephen A. Douglas</PERSON>, <PERSON>John C. Breckinridge</PERSON> of the <ORGANIZATION>Southern Democrats</ORGANIZATION>, and <PERSON>John Bell</PERSON> of the new <ORGANIZATION>Constitutional Union Party</ORGANIZATION>. He was the first Republican president, winning entirely on the strength of his support in the North: he was not even on the ballot in nine states in the South, and won only 2 of 996 counties in the other Southern states. <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> gained 1,865,908 votes (39.9% of the total), for 180 electoral votes; <PERSON>Douglas</PERSON>, 1,380,202 (29.5%) for 12 electoral votes; <LOCATION>Breckenridge</LOCATION>, 848,019 (18.1%) for 72 electoral votes; and <ORGANIZATION>Bell</ORGANIZATION>, 590,901 (12.5%) for 39 electoral votes. There were fusion tickets in some states, but even if his opponents had combined in every state, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> had a majority vote in all but two of the states in which he won the electoral votes and would still have won the electoral college and the election.

With the emergence of the Republicans as the nation's first major sectional party by the mid-1850s, politics became the stage on which sectional tensions were played out. Although much of the West the focal point of sectional tensions was unfit for cotton cultivation, Southern secessionists read the political fallout as a sign that their power in national politics was rapidly weakening. Before, the slave system had been buttressed to an extent by the <ORGANIZATION>Democratic Party</ORGANIZATION>, which was increasingly seen as representing a more pro-Southern position that unfairly permitted Southerners to prevail in the nation's territories and to dominate national policy before the Civil War. But they suffered a significant reverse in the electoral realignment of the mid-1850s. 1860 was a critical election that marked a stark change in existing patterns of party loyalties among groups of voters; <PERSON>Abraham Lincoln</PERSON>'s election was a watershed in the balance of power of competing national and parochial interests and affiliations.

As <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s election became more likely, secessionists made it clear that their states would leave the Union. On December 20, 1860, <LOCATION>South Carolina</LOCATION> took the lead. By February 1, 1861, <LOCATION>South Carolina</LOCATION> was followed by six other cotton-growing states in the deep <LOCATION>South</LOCATION>. The seven states soon declared themselves to be a new nation, the Confederate States of <LOCATION>America</LOCATION>. The upper South (<LOCATION>Delaware</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Maryland</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Virginia</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>North Carolina</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Tennessee</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Kentucky</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Missouri</LOCATION>, and <LOCATION>Arkansas</LOCATION>) listened to, but initially rejected, the secessionist appeal. President <PERSON>Buchanan</PERSON> and President-elect <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> refused to recognize the Confederacy.

Attempts at compromise, such as the <ORGANIZATION>Crittenden Compromise</ORGANIZATION> which would have extended the Missouri line of 1820, were discussed. Despite support for the Crittenden Compromise among some Republicans, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> denounced it in private letters, saying "either the Missouri line extended, or... Pop. <PERSON>Sov</PERSON>. would lose us everything we gained in the election; that filibustering for all South of us, and making slave states of it, would follow in spite of us, under either plan",while other Republicans publicly stated it "would amount to a perpetual covenant of war against every people, tribe, and state owning a foot of land between here and <LOCATION>Tierra del Fuego</LOCATION> [at the far end of <LOCATION>South America</LOCATION>]."

President-elect <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> evaded possible assassins in <LOCATION>Baltimore</LOCATION>, and on February 23, 1861, arrived in disguise in <LOCATION>Washington</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>D.C</LOCATION>.

At his inauguration on March 4, 1861, the German American Turners formed <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s bodyguard; and a sizable garrison of federal troops was also present, ready to protect the capital from Confederate invasion and local insurrection. In his <ORGANIZATION>First Inaugural Address</ORGANIZATION>, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> declared, "I hold that in contemplation of universal law and of the Constitution the Union of these States is perpetual. Perpetuity is implied, if not expressed, in the fundamental law of all national governments," arguing further that the purpose of the <ORGANIZATION>United States Constitution</ORGANIZATION> was "to form a more perfect union" than the Articles of Confederation which were explicitly perpetual, thus the Constitution too was perpetual. He asked rhetorically that even were the Constitution a simple contract, would it not require the agreement of all parties to rescind it?

Also in his inaugural address, in a final attempt to reunite the states and prevent the looming war, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> supported the pending Corwin Amendment to the Constitution, which had already passed <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION>. This amendment, which explicitly protected slavery in those states in which it already existed, had more appeal to the critical border states than to the states that had already declared their separation.

By the time <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> took office, the Confederacy was an established fact, and no leaders of the insurrection proposed rejoining the Union on any terms. No compromise was found because a compromise was deemed virtually impossible. <PERSON>Buchanan</PERSON> might have allowed the southern states to secede, and some Republicans recommended that. However, conservative Democratic nationalists, such as <PERSON>Jeremiah S. Black</PERSON>, <PERSON>Joseph Holt</PERSON>, and <PERSON>Edwin M. Stanton</PERSON> had taken control of <PERSON>Buchanan</PERSON>'s cabinet around January 1, 1861, and refused to accept secession. <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> and nearly every Republican leader adopted this position by March 1861: the Union could not be dismantled. Believing that a peaceful solution was still possible, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> decided to not take any action against the South unless the Unionists themselves were attacked first. This finally happened in April 1861.

Historian <PERSON>Allan Nevins</PERSON> argues that <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> made three miscalculations in believing that he could preserve the Union, hold government property, and still avoid war. He "temporarily underrated the gravity of the crisis", overestimated the strength of Unionist sentiment in the <LOCATION>South</LOCATION> and border states, and misunderstood the conditional support of Unionists in the border states. In connection with <PERSON>Nevins</PERSON>'s conclusions, it is interesting to note an incident from this period reported in the memoirs of <PERSON>William Tecumseh Sherman</PERSON>. Then a civilian, <PERSON>Sherman</PERSON> visited <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> in the <ORGANIZATION>White House</ORGANIZATION> during inauguration week, with his brother, <LOCATION>Ohio</LOCATION> Republican <PERSON>John Sherman</PERSON>. This meeting left the future General <PERSON>Sherman</PERSON> "sadly disappointed" at <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s seeming failure to realize that "the country was sleeping on a volcano" and the South was "'preparing for war.'"

In April 1861, after Union troops at <LOCATION>Fort Sumter</LOCATION> were fired upon and forced to surrender, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> called on the governors of every state to send detachments totaling 75,000 troops to recapture forts, protect the capital, and "preserve the Union," which in his view still existed intact despite the actions of the seceding states. <LOCATION>Virginia</LOCATION>, which had repeatedly warned <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> that it would not allow an invasion of its territory or join an attack on another state, responded by seceding, along with <LOCATION>North Carolina</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Tennessee</LOCATION>, and <LOCATION>Arkansas</LOCATION>.

The slave states of <LOCATION>Missouri</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Kentucky</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Maryland</LOCATION>, and <LOCATION>Delaware</LOCATION> did not secede, and <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> urgently negotiated with state leaders there, promising not to interfere with slavery. After the fighting started, he had rebel leaders arrested in all the border areas (especially in <LOCATION>Maryland</LOCATION>) and held in military prisons without trial. Over 18,000 were arrested, though none were executed. One, <PERSON>Clement Vallandigham</PERSON>, was exiled; but all of the remainder were released, usually after two or three months (see: Ex parte <PERSON>Merryman</PERSON>).

In July 1862, <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> moved to free the slaves by passing the Second Confiscation Act. The goal was to weaken the rebellion, which was led and controlled by slave owners. While it did not abolish the legal institution of slavery (the Thirteenth Amendment did that), the Act showed that <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> had the support of <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> in liberating slaves owned by rebels. This new law was implemented with <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s "Emancipation Proclamation."

Ending slavery was always a primary goal of the <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> administration. However, the American public was slow to embrace the idea. In a shrewdly penned letter to <PERSON>Horace Greeley</PERSON>, editor of <ORGANIZATION>The New York Tribune</ORGANIZATION>, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> masked his goal of ending slavery by making it subservient to the cause of preserving the union:



The Emancipation Proclamation, announced on September 22 and put into effect on January 1, 1863, freed slaves in territories not under Union control. As Union armies advanced south, more slaves were liberated until all of them in Confederate hands (over three million) were freed. <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> later said: "I never, in my life, felt more certain that I was doing right, than I do in signing this paper." The proclamation made the abolition of slavery in the rebel states an official war goal. <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> then threw his energies into passage of the Thirteenth Amendment to permanently abolish slavery throughout the nation.

In September 1862, thirteen northern governors met in <LOCATION>Altoona</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION>, at the Loyal War Governors' Conference to discuss the Proclamation and Union war effort. In the end, the state executives fully supported the president's Proclamation and also suggested the removal of General <PERSON>George B. McClellan</PERSON> as commander of the Union's Army of the <LOCATION>Potomac</LOCATION>.

For some time, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> continued earlier plans to set up colonies for the newly freed slaves. He commented favorably on colonization in the Emancipation Proclamation, but all attempts at such a massive undertaking failed. As <PERSON>Frederick Douglass</PERSON> observed, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> was, "The first great man that I talked with in the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> freely who in no single instance reminded me of the difference between himself and myself, of the difference of color."

Although the Battle of <LOCATION>Gettysburg</LOCATION> was a <ORGANIZATION>Union</ORGANIZATION> victory, it was also the bloodiest battle of the war and dealt a blow to <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s war effort. As the <ORGANIZATION>Union Army</ORGANIZATION> decreased in numbers due to casualties, more soldiers were needed to replace the ranks. <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s 1863 military drafts were considered "odious" among many in the north, particularly immigrants. The <LOCATION>New York</LOCATION> Draft Riots of July, 1863 were the most notable manifestation of this discontent.

Writing to <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> in September 1863, the Governor of <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Andrew Curtin</PERSON>, warned that political sentiments were turning against <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> and the war effort:



At the dedication of the Soldiers' National Cemetery in <LOCATION>Gettysburg</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION>, on November 19, beginning with the now-iconic phrase "Four score and seven years ago...", <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> referred to the events of the Civil War and described the ceremony at <LOCATION>Gettysburg</LOCATION> as an opportunity not only to dedicate the grounds of a cemetery, but also to consecrate the living in the struggle to ensure that "government of the people, by the people, for the people, shall not perish from the earth".

After Union victories at <LOCATION>Gettysburg</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Vicksburg</LOCATION> and <LOCATION>Chattanooga</LOCATION> in 1863, overall victory seemed at hand, and <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> promoted <PERSON>Ulysses S. Grant</PERSON> General-in-Chief ( March 12, 1864). When the spring campaigns turned into bloody stalemates, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> supported <PERSON>Grant</PERSON>'s strategy of wearing down <PERSON>Lee</PERSON>'s Confederate army at the cost of heavy Union casualties. With an election looming, he easily defeated efforts to deny his renomination. At the Convention, the <ORGANIZATION>Republican Party</ORGANIZATION> selected <PERSON>Andrew Johnson</PERSON>, a War Democrat from the Southern state of <LOCATION>Tennessee</LOCATION>, as his running mate in order to form a broader coalition. They ran on the new <ORGANIZATION>Union Party</ORGANIZATION> ticket uniting Republicans and War Democrats.



Nevertheless, Republicans across the country feared that <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> would be defeated. Acknowledging this fear, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> wrote and signed a pledge that, if he should lose the election, he would still defeat the Confederacy before turning over the White House:



<PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> did not show the pledge to his cabinet, but asked them to sign the sealed envelope.

While the Democratic platform followed the Peace wing of the party and called the war a "failure," their candidate, General <PERSON>George B. McClellan</PERSON>, supported the war and repudiated the platform.

<PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> provided <PERSON>Grant</PERSON> with new replacements and mobilized his party to support <PERSON>Grant</PERSON> and win local support for the war effort. <PERSON>Sherman</PERSON>'s capture of <LOCATION>Atlanta</LOCATION> in September ended defeatist jitters; the <ORGANIZATION>Democratic Party</ORGANIZATION> was deeply split, with some leaders and most soldiers openly for <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>; the Union party was united and energized, and <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> was easily reelected in a landslide. He won all but two states, capturing 212 of 233 electoral votes.

On March 4, 1865, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> delivered his second inaugural address, his favorite of all his speeches. At this time, a victory over the rebels was at hand, slavery was dead, and <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> was looking to the future.



The war was a source of constant frustration for the president, and occupied nearly all of his time. He had a contentious relationship with General <PERSON>McClellan</PERSON>, who became general-in-chief of all the Union armies in the wake of the embarrassing Union defeat at the <ORGANIZATION>First Battle of Bull Run</ORGANIZATION> and after the retirement of <PERSON>Winfield Scott</PERSON> in late 1861. Despite his inexperience in military affairs, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> wanted to take an active part in determining war strategy. His priorities were twofold: to ensure that <LOCATION>Washington</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>D.C.</LOCATION> was well defended; and to conduct an aggressive war effort in the hope of ending the war quickly and appeasing the Northern public and press. <PERSON>McClellan</PERSON>, a youthful West Point graduate and railroad executive called back to active military service, took a more cautious approach. He took several months to plan and execute his Peninsula Campaign, with the objective of capturing <LOCATION>Richmond</LOCATION> by moving the Army of the <LOCATION>Potomac</LOCATION> by boat to the peninsula between the <PERSON>James</PERSON> and <ORGANIZATION>York Rivers</ORGANIZATION>. <PERSON>McClellan</PERSON>'s delay irritated <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>, as did his insistence that no troops were needed to defend <LOCATION>Washington</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>D.C.</LOCATION> <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> insisted on holding some of <PERSON>McClellan</PERSON>'s troops to defend the capital, a decision <PERSON>McClellan</PERSON> blamed for the ultimate failure of the Peninsula Campaign.

<PERSON>McClellan</PERSON>, a lifelong Democrat who was temperamentally conservative, was relieved as general-in-chief after releasing his <PERSON>Harrison</PERSON>'s Landing Letter, where he offered unsolicited political advice to <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> urging caution in the war effort. <PERSON>McClellan</PERSON>'s letter incensed Radical Republicans, who successfully pressured <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> to appoint <PERSON>John Pope</PERSON>, a Republican, as head of the new Army of <LOCATION>Virginia</LOCATION>. Pope complied with <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s strategic desire to move toward <LOCATION>Richmond</LOCATION> from the north, thus protecting the capital from attack. But <PERSON>Pope</PERSON> was soundly defeated at the Second Battle of Bull Run in the summer of 1862, forcing the Army of the <LOCATION>Potomac</LOCATION> to defend <LOCATION>Washington</LOCATION> for a second time. In response to his failure, <PERSON>Pope</PERSON> was sent to <LOCATION>Minnesota</LOCATION> to fight the <LOCATION>Sioux</LOCATION>.



Panicked by <PERSON>Lee</PERSON>'s invasion of <LOCATION>Maryland</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> restored <PERSON>McClellan</PERSON> to command of all forces around <LOCATION>Washington</LOCATION> in time for the Battle of <ORGANIZATION>Antietam</ORGANIZATION> (September 1862). The ensuing Union victory enabled <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> to release his Emancipation Proclamation, but he relieved <PERSON>McClellan</PERSON> of his command shortly after the 1862 midterm elections and appointed Republican <PERSON>Ambrose Burnside</PERSON> to head the Army of the <LOCATION>Potomac</LOCATION>. <PERSON>Burnside</PERSON> had promised to follow through on <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s strategic vision for a strong offensive against <PERSON>Lee</PERSON> and <LOCATION>Richmond</LOCATION>. After <PERSON>Burnside</PERSON> was stunningly defeated at <LOCATION>Fredericksburg</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Joseph Hooker</PERSON> was given the command, despite his idle talk about the necessity for a military dictator to win the war and a past history of criticizing his commanders. <PERSON>Hooker</PERSON> was routed by <PERSON>Lee</PERSON> at the Battle of <LOCATION>Chancellorsville</LOCATION> (May 1863), and relieved of command early in the subsequent <LOCATION>Gettysburg</LOCATION> Campaign replaced by <PERSON>George Meade</PERSON>.

After the Union victory at <LOCATION>Gettysburg</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Meade</PERSON>'s failure to pursue <PERSON>Lee</PERSON> and months of inactivity for the Army of the <LOCATION>Potomac</LOCATION> persuaded <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> to bring in a western general, <PERSON>Ulysses S. Grant</PERSON>. <PERSON>Grant</PERSON> already had a solid string of victories in the Western Theater, including the battles of <LOCATION>Vicksburg</LOCATION> and <LOCATION>Chattanooga</LOCATION>. Responding to criticism of <PERSON>Grant</PERSON>, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> was quoted as saying, "I cannot spare this man. He fights." <PERSON>Grant</PERSON> waged his bloody Overland Campaign in 1864 with a strategy of a war of attrition, characterized by high Union losses at battles such as the Wilderness and Cold Harbor, but by proportionately higher Confederate losses. His invasion campaign eventually bottled <PERSON>Lee</PERSON> up in the Siege of <LOCATION>Petersburg</LOCATION>, so that <PERSON>Grant</PERSON> could take <LOCATION>Richmond</LOCATION>, and bring the war to a close in the spring of 1865.

<PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> authorized <PERSON>Grant</PERSON> to target civilians and infrastructure, hoping to destroy the South's morale and weaken its economic ability to continue fighting. This allowed <PERSON>Generals Sherman</PERSON> and <PERSON>Sheridan</PERSON> to destroy farms and towns in the <LOCATION>Shenandoah Valley</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Georgia</LOCATION>, and <LOCATION>South Carolina</LOCATION>. The damage caused by <PERSON>Sherman</PERSON>'s March to the Sea through <LOCATION>Georgia</LOCATION> totaled in excess of $100 million by <PERSON>Sherman</PERSON>'s own estimate.

<PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> had a star-crossed record as a military leader, possessing a keen understanding of strategic points (such as the <LOCATION>Mississippi River</LOCATION> and the fortress city of <LOCATION>Vicksburg</LOCATION>) and the importance of defeating the enemy's army, rather than simply capturing cities. He had, however, limited success in motivating his commanders to adopt his strategies until late 1863, when he found a man who shared his vision of the war in <PERSON>Ulysses S. Grant</PERSON>. Only then could he insist on using African American troops and relentlessly pursue a series of coordinated offensives in multiple theaters.

Throughout the war, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> showed a keen curiosity with the military campaigns. He spent hours at the War Department telegraph office, reading dispatches from his generals. He visited battle sites frequently, and seemed fascinated by watching scenes of war. During <PERSON>Jubal Anderson</PERSON> Early's raid on <LOCATION>Washington</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>D.C.</LOCATION> in 1864, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> had to be told to duck to avoid being shot while observing the battle.

Reconstruction began during the war as <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> and his associates pondered questions of how to reintegrate the Southern states and what to do with Confederate leaders and the freed slaves. <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> led the "moderates" regarding Reconstructionist policy, and was usually opposed by the Radical Republicans, under <PERSON>Thaddeus Stevens</PERSON> in the <ORGANIZATION>House</ORGANIZATION> and <PERSON>Charles Sumner</PERSON> and <PERSON>Benjamin Wade</PERSON> in the <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION> (though he cooperated with these men on most other issues). Determined to find a course that would reunite the nation and not alienate the <LOCATION>South</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> urged that speedy elections under generous terms be held throughout the war in areas behind Union lines. His Amnesty Proclamation of December 8, 1863, offered pardons to those who had not held a Confederate civil office, had not mistreated Union prisoners, and would sign an oath of allegiance. Critical decisions had to be made as state after state was reconquered. Of special importance were <LOCATION>Tennessee</LOCATION>, where <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> appointed <PERSON>Andrew Johnson</PERSON> as governor, and <LOCATION>Louisiana</LOCATION>, where <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> attempted a plan that would restore statehood when 10% of the voters agreed to it. The Radicals thought this policy too lenient, and passed their own plan, the Wade-Davis <PERSON>Bill</PERSON>, in 1864. When <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> pocket-vetoed the bill, the Radicals retaliated by refusing to seat representatives elected from <LOCATION>Louisiana</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Arkansas</LOCATION>, and <LOCATION>Tennessee</LOCATION>.

Near the end of the war, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> made an extended visit to <PERSON>Grant</PERSON>'s headquarters at <LOCATION>City Point</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Virginia</LOCATION>. This allowed the president to visit <LOCATION>Richmond</LOCATION> after it was taken by the Union forces and to make a public gesture of sitting at <PERSON>Jefferson Davis</PERSON>'s own desk, symbolically saying to the nation that the President of the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> held authority over the entire land. He was greeted at the city as a conquering hero by freed slaves, whose sentiments were epitomized by one admirer's quote, "I know I am free for I have seen the face of Father <PERSON>Abraham</PERSON> and have felt him." When a general asked <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> how the defeated Confederates should be treated, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> replied, "Let 'em up easy." <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> arrived back in <LOCATION>Washington</LOCATION> on the evening of April 9, 1865, the day <PERSON>Lee</PERSON> surrendered at <ORGANIZATION>Appomattox Court House</ORGANIZATION> in <LOCATION>Virginia</LOCATION>. The war was effectively over. The other rebel armies surrendered soon after, and there was no subsequent guerrilla warfare.

<PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s rhetoric defined the issues of the war for the nation, the world, and posterity. The <ORGANIZATION>Gettysburg Address</ORGANIZATION> defied <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s own prediction that "the world will little note, nor long remember what we say here." His second inaugural address is also greatly admired and often quoted.

In recent years, historians have stressed <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s use of and redefinition of republican values. As early as the 1850s, a time when most political rhetoric focused on the sanctity of the Constitution, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> shifted emphasis to the Declaration of Independence as the foundation of American political values what he called the "sheet anchor" of republicanism. The Declaration's emphasis on freedom and equality for all, rather than the Constitution's tolerance of slavers, shifted the debate. As <PERSON>Diggins</PERSON> concludes regarding the highly influential <ORGANIZATION>Cooper Union</ORGANIZATION> speech, "<PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> presented Americans a theory of history that offers a profound contribution to the theory and destiny of republicanism itself." His position gained strength because he highlighted the moral basis of republicanism, rather than its legalisms. Nevertheless, in 1861 <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> justified the war in terms of legalisms (the Constitution was a contract, and for one party to get out of a contract all the other parties had to agree), and then in terms of the national duty to guarantee a "republican form of government" in every state. That duty was also the principle underlying federal intervention in Reconstruction.

In his <ORGANIZATION>Gettysburg Address Lincoln</ORGANIZATION> redefined the American nation, arguing that it was born not in 1789 but in 1776, "conceived in Liberty, and dedicated to the proposition that all men are created equal." He declared that the sacrifices of battle had rededicated the nation to the propositions of democracy and equality, "that this nation shall have a new birth of freedom and that government of the people, by the people, for the people, shall not perish from the earth." By emphasizing the centrality of the nation, he rebuffed the claims of state sovereignty. While some critics say <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> moved too far and too fast, they agree that he dedicated the nation to values that marked "a new founding of the nation."

During the Civil War, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> appropriated powers no previous President had wielded: he used his war powers to proclaim a blockade, suspended the writ of habeas corpus, spent money without congressional authorization, and imprisoned 18,000 suspected Confederate sympathizers without trial.

<PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> believed in the Whig theory of the presidency, which left <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> to write the laws while he signed them, vetoing only those bills that threatened his war powers. Thus, he signed the Homestead Act in 1862, making millions of acres of government-held land in the West available for purchase at very low cost. The Morrill Land-Grant Colleges Act, also signed in 1862, provided government grants for agricultural universities in each state. The Pacific Railway Acts of 1862 and 1864 granted federal support for the construction of the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION>' <ORGANIZATION>First Transcontinental Railroad</ORGANIZATION>, which was completed in 1869. Other important legislation involved economic matters, including the first income tax and higher tariffs. Also included was the creation of the system of national banks by the National Banking Acts of 1863, 1864, and 1865, which allowed the creation of a strong national financial system. <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> created and <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> approved the <ORGANIZATION>Department of Agriculture</ORGANIZATION> in 1862, although that institution would not become a Cabinet-level department until 1889.

The Legal Tender Act of 1862 established the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> Note, the first paper currency in <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> history. This was done to increase the money supply to pay for fighting the war.

During the war, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s <ORGANIZATION>Treasury Department</ORGANIZATION> effectively controlled all cotton trade in the occupied South the most dramatic incursion of federal controls on the economy.

In 1862, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> sent a senior general, <PERSON>John Pope</PERSON>, to put down the "Sioux Uprising" in <LOCATION>Minnesota</LOCATION>. Presented with 303 death warrants for convicted <LOCATION>Santee Dakota</LOCATION> who had massacred innocent farmers, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> affirmed 39 of these for execution (one was later reprieved).

Illnesses included: frostbitten feet, malaria, physical trauma and smallpox. Claims that <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> had syphilis about 1835 have been controversial, but a recent analysis finds them credible.

As a child, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> was tall for his age. He reached his adult height of  no later than age 21. Friends noticed his arms, legs, hands, and feet were long. Although well muscled as a young adult, he was always thin. Fragmentary evidence says he weighed around 170 pounds before the Presidency, but lost weight while in the White House. Based on <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s unusual physical appearance, Dr. <PERSON>Abraham Gordon</PERSON> proposed in 1962 that <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> had Marfan syndrome. <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s unremarkable cardiovascular history and his normal visual acuity have been the chief objections to the theory, and today the diagnosis is considered unlikely. Testing <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s DNA for Marfan syndrome was contemplated in the 1990s, but such a test was not performed.

In 2007, Dr. <PERSON>John Sotos</PERSON> proposed that <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> had multiple endocrine neoplasia, type 2B (MEN2B). This theory suggests <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> had all the major features of the disease: a marfan-like body shape, large, bumpy lips, constipation, hypotonia, a history compatible with cancer and a family history of the disorder - his sons <PERSON>Eddie</PERSON>, <PERSON>Willie</PERSON>, and Tad, and probably his mother. The "mole" on <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s right cheek, the asymmetry of his face, his large jaw, his drooping eyelid, and "pseudo-depression" are also suggested as manifestations of MEN2B. <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s longevity is the principal challenge to the MEN2B theory, which could be proven by DNA testing.



Originally, <PERSON>John Wilkes Booth</PERSON>, a well-known actor and a Confederate spy from <LOCATION>Maryland</LOCATION>, had formulated a plan to kidnap <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> in exchange for the release of Confederate prisoners. After attending an April 11 speech in which <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> promoted voting rights for blacks, an incensed <PERSON>Booth</PERSON> changed his plans and determined to assassinate the president. Learning that the President and First Lady would be attending <ORGANIZATION>Ford</ORGANIZATION>'s Theatre, he laid his plans, assigning his co-conspirators to assassinate Vice President <PERSON>Andrew Johnson</PERSON> and Secretary of State <PERSON>William H. Seward</PERSON>.

Without his main bodyguard <PERSON>Ward Hill Lamon</PERSON>, to whom he related his famous dream regarding his own assassination, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> left to attend the play Our American Cousin on April 14, 1865. As a lone bodyguard wandered, and <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> sat in his state box (Box 7) in the balcony, <PERSON>Booth</PERSON> crept up behind the President and waited for what he thought would be the funniest line of the play ("You sock-dologizing old man-trap"), hoping the laughter would muffle the noise of the gunshot. When the laughter began, <PERSON>Booth</PERSON> jumped into the box and aimed a single-shot, round-slug 0.44 caliber <PERSON>Henry Deringer</PERSON> at his head, firing at point-blank range. Major <PERSON>Henry Rathbone</PERSON> momentarily grappled with <PERSON>Booth</PERSON> but was cut by <PERSON>Booth</PERSON>'s knife. <PERSON>Booth</PERSON> then leapt to the stage and shouted "Sic semper tyrannis!" () and escaped, despite a broken leg suffered in the leap. A twelve-day manhunt ensued, in which <PERSON>Booth</PERSON> was chased by Federal agents (under the direction of Secretary of War <PERSON>Edwin M. Stanton</PERSON>). He was eventually cornered in a <LOCATION>Virginia</LOCATION> barn house and shot, dying of his wounds soon after.

An army surgeon, Doctor <PERSON>Charles Leale</PERSON>, initially assessed <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s wound as mortal. The President was taken across the street from the theater to the <LOCATION>Petersen House</LOCATION>, where he lay in a coma for nine hours before dying. Several physicians attended <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>, including <ORGANIZATION>U.S. Army</ORGANIZATION> Surgeon General <PERSON>Joseph K. Barnes</PERSON> of the <ORGANIZATION>Army Medical Museum</ORGANIZATION>. Using a probe, <PERSON>Barnes</PERSON> located some fragments of <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s skull and the ball lodged 6 inches (15 cm) inside his brain. <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> never regained consciousness and was pronounced dead at 7:22:10 a.m. April 15, 1865. There is some disagreement among historians as to <PERSON>Stanton</PERSON>'s words after <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> died. All agree that he began "Now he belongs to the..." with some stating he said "ages" while others believe he said "angels." After <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s body was returned to the <ORGANIZATION>White House</ORGANIZATION>, a brain-only autopsy was performed, and his body was prepared for his lying in repose in the <LOCATION>East Room</LOCATION>. He was the first president to be assassinated or to lie in state.

The <ORGANIZATION>Army Medical Museum</ORGANIZATION>, now named the <ORGANIZATION>National Museum of Health and Medicine</ORGANIZATION>, has retained in its collection several artifacts relating to the assassination. Currently on display are the bullet that was fired from the <ORGANIZATION>Philadelphia Deringer</ORGANIZATION> pistol, the probe used by <PERSON>Barnes</PERSON>, pieces of <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s skull and hair, and the surgeon's cuff stained with <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s blood. The chair in which <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> was shot is on display at the <ORGANIZATION>Henry Ford Museum</ORGANIZATION> in <LOCATION>Detroit</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Michigan</LOCATION>.

<PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s body was carried by train in a grand funeral procession through several states on its way back to <LOCATION>Illinois</LOCATION>. While much of the nation mourned him as the savior of the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION>, Copperheads celebrated the death of a man they considered a tyrant. The Lincoln Tomb in <LOCATION>Oak Ridge Cemetery</LOCATION> in <LOCATION>Springfield</LOCATION>, is 177 feet (54 m) tall and, by 1874, was surmounted with several bronze statues of <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>. To prevent repeated attempts to steal <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s body and hold it for ransom, <PERSON>Robert Todd Lincoln</PERSON> had <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> exhumed and reinterred in concrete several feet thick in 1901.

<PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> was known for appointing political rivals to high positions in his cabinet to keep in line all factions of his party and to let them battle each other and not combine against him. Historians agree that, except for <PERSON>Simon Cameron</PERSON>, it was a highly effective group.

In March 1860 in a speech in <LOCATION>New Haven</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Connecticut</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> said, with respect to slavery, Whenever this question shall be settled, it must be settled on some philosophical basis. No policy that does not rest upon some philosophical public opinion can be permanently maintained." The philosophical basis for Lincolns beliefs regarding slavery and other issues of the day require that <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> be examined "seriously as a man of ideas." <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> was a strong supporter of the American Whig version of liberal capitalism who, more than most politicians of the time, was able to express his ideas within the context of Nineteenth Century religious beliefs.

There were few people who strongly or directly influenced Lincolns moral and intellectual development and perspectives. There was no teacher, mentor, church leader, community leader, or peer that <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> would credit in later years as a strong influence on his intellectual development. Lacking a formal education, Lincolns personal philosophy was shaped by "an amazingly retentive memory and a passion for reading and learning." It was Lincolns reading, rather than his relationships, that were most influential in shaping his personal beliefs.

<PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> did, even as a boy, largely reject organized religion, but the Calvinistic "doctrine of necessity" would remain a factor throughout his life. In 1846 <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> described the effect of this doctrine as "that the human mind is impelled to action, or held in rest by some power, over which the mind itself has no control." In April 1864, in justifying his actions in regard to Emancipation, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> wrote, "I claim not to have controlled events, but confess plainly that events have controlled me. Now, at the end of three years struggle the nation's condition is not what either party, or any man devised, or expected. God alone can claim it."

As <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> matured, and especially during his term as president, the idea of a divine will somehow interacting with human affairs more and more influenced his public expressions. On a personal level, the death of his son <PERSON>Willie</PERSON> in February 1862 may have caused <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> to look towards religion for answers and solace. After Willies death, in the summer or early fall of 1862, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> attempted to put on paper his private musings on why, from a divine standpoint, the severity of the war was necessary:



Lincolns religious skepticism was fueled by his exposure to the ideas of the Lockean Enlightenment and classical liberalism, especially economic liberalism. Consistent with the common practice of the Whig party, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> would often use the Declaration of Independence as the philosophical and moral expression of these two philosophies. In a February 22, 1861 speech at Independence Hall in <LOCATION>Philadelphia Lincoln</LOCATION> said,



He found in the Declaration justification for Whig economic policy and opposition to territorial expansion and the nativist platform of the Know Nothings. In claiming that all men were created free, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> and the Whigs argued that this freedom required economic advancement, expanded education, territory to grow, and the ability of the nation to absorb the growing immigrant population.

It was the Declaration of Independence, rather than the Bible, that <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> most relied on in order to oppose any further territorial expansion of slavery. He saw the Declaration as more than a political document. To him, as well as to many abolitionists and other antislavery leaders, it was, foremost, a moral document that had forever determined valuable criteria in shaping the future of the nation.

<PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s death made the President a martyr to many. Repeated polls of historians have ranked <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> as among the greatest presidents in <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> history, often appearing in the first position. Among contemporary admirers, <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> is usually seen as personifying classical values of honesty and integrity, as well as respect for individual and minority rights, and human freedom in general.

Many American organizations of all purposes and agendas continue to cite his name and image, with interests ranging from the gay rights-supporting Log Cabin Republicans to the insurance corporation <ORGANIZATION>Lincoln National Corporation</ORGANIZATION>. The Lincoln automobile is also named after him. The ballistic missile submarine ''<PERSON>Abraham Lincoln</PERSON>'' (SSBN-602) and the aircraft carrier ''<PERSON>Abraham Lincoln</PERSON>'' (CVN-72) were named in his honor. Also, the Liberty ship SS <PERSON>Nancy Hanks</PERSON> was named for his mother. During the Spanish Civil War, the American faction of the International Brigades named themselves the <PERSON>Abraham Lincoln Brigade</PERSON>.

<PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> has been memorialized in many city names, notably the capital of <LOCATION>Nebraska</LOCATION>. <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>, <LOCATION>Illinois</LOCATION>, is the only city to be named for <PERSON>Abraham Lincoln</PERSON> before he became President. <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s name and image appear in numerous places. Counties in 18 <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> states (<LOCATION>Arkansas</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Colorado</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Idaho</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Kansas</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Maine</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Minnesota</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Mississippi</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Montana</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Nebraska</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Nevada</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>New Mexico</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Oklahoma</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Oregon</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>South Dakota</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>West Virginia</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Washington</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Wisconsin</LOCATION>, and <LOCATION>Wyoming</LOCATION>) are named after <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>.

<PERSON>Abraham Lincoln</PERSON>'s birthday, February 12, was formerly a national holiday, now commemorated as Presidents' Day. <PERSON>Ernest Miller Hemingway</PERSON> (July 21, 1899 July 2, 1961) was an American novelist, short-story writer, and journalist. He was part of the 1920s expatriate community in <LOCATION>Paris</LOCATION>, and one of the veterans of World War I later known as "the Lost Generation." He received the Pulitzer Prize in 1953 for The Old Man and the Sea, and the Nobel Prize in Literature in 1954.

<PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>'s distinctive writing style is characterized by economy and understatement, and had a significant influence on the development of twentieth-century fiction writing. His protagonists are typically stoical men who exhibit an ideal described as "grace under pressure." Many of his works are now considered classics of American literature.

<PERSON>Ernest Miller Hemingway</PERSON> was born on July 21, 1899 in <LOCATION>Oak Park</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Illinois</LOCATION>, a suburb of <LOCATION>Chicago</LOCATION>. <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> was the first son and the second child born to <PERSON>Clarence Edmonds</PERSON> "Doc Ed" <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> - a country doctor, and <PERSON>Grace Hall Hemingway</PERSON>. <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>'s father attended the birth of <PERSON>Ernest</PERSON> and blew a horn on his front porch to announce to the neighbors that his wife had given birth to a boy. The <ORGANIZATION>Hemingways</ORGANIZATION> lived in a six-bedroom Victorian house built by <PERSON>Ernest</PERSON>'s widowed maternal grandfather, <PERSON>Ernest Miller Hall</PERSON>, an English immigrant and Civil War veteran who lived with the family. <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> was his namesake. <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>'s mother once aspired to an opera career and earned money giving voice and music lessons. She was domineering and narrowly religious, mirroring the strict Protestant ethic of <LOCATION>Oak Park</LOCATION>, which <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> later said had "wide lawns and narrow minds". While his mother hoped that her son would develop an interest in music, <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> adopted his father's outdoorsman hobbies of hunting, fishing and camping in the woods and lakes of <LOCATION>Northern Michigan</LOCATION>. The family owned a summer home called <LOCATION>Windemere</LOCATION> on <LOCATION>Walloon Lake</LOCATION>, near <LOCATION>Petoskey</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Michigan</LOCATION> and often spent summers vacationing there. These early experiences in close contact with nature instilled in <LOCATION>Hemingway</LOCATION> a lifelong passion for outdoor adventure and for living in remote or isolated areas.

<PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> attended <LOCATION>Oak Park</LOCATION> and <ORGANIZATION>River Forest High School</ORGANIZATION> from September 1913 until graduation in June 1917. He excelled both academically and athletically; he boxed, played American football, and displayed particular talent in English classes. His first writing experience was writing for "Trapeze" and "Tabula" (the school's newspaper and yearbook, respectively) in his junior year, then serving as editor in his senior year. He sometimes wrote under the pen name <PERSON>Ring Lardner</PERSON>, <PERSON>Jr.</PERSON>, a nod to his literary hero <PERSON>Ring Lardner</PERSON>.

After high school, <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> did not want to go to college. Instead, at age eighteen, he began his writing career as a cub reporter for The <ORGANIZATION>Kansas City Star</ORGANIZATION>. Although he worked at the newspaper for only six months (October 17, 1917-April 30, 1918), throughout his lifetime he used the guidance of the Star's style guide as a foundation for his writing style: "Use short sentences. Use short first paragraphs. Use vigorous English. Be positive, not negative." In honor of the centennial year of <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>'s birth (1899), The Star named <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> its top reporter of the last hundred years.

<PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> left his reporting job after only a few months and, against his father's wishes, tried to join the <ORGANIZATION>United States Army</ORGANIZATION> to see action in World War I. He failed the medical examination due to poor vision, and instead joined the <ORGANIZATION>Red Cross Ambulance Corps</ORGANIZATION>. On his route to the Italian front, he stopped in <LOCATION>Paris</LOCATION>, which was under constant bombardment from German artillery. Instead of staying in the relative safety of the <LOCATION>Hotel Florida</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> tried to get as close to combat as possible.Soon after arriving on the Italian Front <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> witnessed the brutalities of war. On his first day on duty an ammunition factory near <LOCATION>Milan</LOCATION> blew up. <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> had to pick up the humanprimarily femaleremains. <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> wrote about this experience in his short story "A Natural History of the Dead". This first encounter with death left him shaken.

The soldiers he met later did not lighten the horror. One of them, <PERSON>Eric Dorman-Smith</PERSON>, entertained <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> with a line from Part Two of <PERSON>Shakespeare</PERSON>'s ''<PERSON>Henry</PERSON> IV'', <PERSON>Act III</PERSON>, Scene II: "By my troth, I care not; a man can die but once; we owe God a death...and let it go which way it will, he that dies this year is quit for the next." (<PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>, for his part, would quote this line in "The Short Happy Life of <PERSON>Francis Macomber</PERSON>", one of his famous short stories set in <LOCATION>Africa</LOCATION>.) To another soldier, <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> once said, "You are troppo vecchio (It. too old) for this war, pop." The 50-year old soldier replied, "I can die as well as any man."

On July 8, 1918, <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> was wounded delivering supplies to soldiers, which ended his career as an ambulance driver. He was hit by an Austrian trench mortar shell that left fragments in his legs, and was also hit by a burst of machine-gun fire. He was later awarded the Silver Medal of Military Valor (medaglia d'argento) from the Italian government for dragging a wounded Italian soldier to safety in spite of his own injuries. He was credited as the first American wounded in <LOCATION>Italy</LOCATION> during WWI by newspapers at the time but there is debate surrounding the veracity of this claim.

<PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> received treatment in a <LOCATION>Milan</LOCATION> hospital run by the <ORGANIZATION>American Red Cross</ORGANIZATION>. With very little in the way of entertainment, he often drank heavily and read newspapers to pass the time. ("'That's what you are. That's what you all are,' Miss <PERSON>Stein</PERSON> said. 'All of you young people who served in the war.
