A Farewell to Arms was published at a time when many other World War I books were prominent, including <PERSON>Frederic Manning</PERSON>'s Her Privates We, <PERSON>Erich Maria Remarque</PERSON>'s All Quiet on the <ORGANIZATION>Western Front</ORGANIZATION>, <PERSON>Richard Aldington</PERSON>'s Death of a Hero, and <PERSON>Robert Graves</PERSON>' Goodbye to All That. The success of A Farewell to Arms made <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> financially independent.

Following the advice of <PERSON>John Dos Passos</PERSON>, <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> returned to <LOCATION>Key West</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Florida</LOCATION> in 1931, where he established his first <LOCATION>American</LOCATION> home, which has since been converted to a museum. From this 1851 solid limestone house -- a wedding present from <PERSON>Pauline</PERSON>'s uncle -- <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> fished in the waters around the Dry Tortugas with his longtime friend <PERSON>Waldo Pierce</PERSON>, went to the famous bar Sloppy <PERSON>Joe</PERSON>'s, and occasionally traveled to <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION>, gathering material for Death in the Afternoon and Winner Take Nothing. Over the next 9 years, until the end of this marriage in 1940, and then in a second period throughout the 1950s, <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> would do an estimated 70% of his lifetime's writing in the writer's den in the upper floor of the converted garage, in back of this house.

Death in the Afternoon, a book about bullfighting, was published in 1932. <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> had become an aficionado after seeing the <LOCATION>Pamplona</LOCATION> fiesta of 1925, fictionalized in The Sun Also Rises. In Death in the Afternoon, <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> extensively discussed the metaphysics of bullfighting: the ritualized, almost religious practice. <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> considered becoming a bullfighter himself and showed middling aptitude in several novieros before deciding that writing was his true and only suitable professional metier. In his writings on <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION>, he was influenced by the Spanish master <PERSON>Po Baroja</PERSON>. When <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> won the Nobel Prize, he traveled to see <PERSON>Baroja</PERSON>, then on his death bed, specifically to tell him he thought <PERSON>Baroja</PERSON> deserved the prize more than he. <PERSON>Baroja</PERSON> agreed and something of the usual <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> tiff with another writer ensued despite his original good intentions.

A safari in the fall of 1933 led him to <LOCATION>Mombasa</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Nairobi</LOCATION>, and <LOCATION>Machakos</LOCATION> in <LOCATION>Kenya</LOCATION>, moving on to <LOCATION>Tanzania</LOCATION>, where he hunted in the <LOCATION>Serengeti</LOCATION>, around <LOCATION>Lake Manyara</LOCATION> and west and southeast of the present-day <LOCATION>Tarangire National Park</LOCATION>. 1935 saw the publication of <LOCATION>Green Hills of Africa</LOCATION>, an account of his safari. The Snows of Kilimano and The Short Happy Life of <PERSON>Francis Macomber</PERSON> were the fictionalized results of his African experiences. <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> fell ill on this trip, suffering a prolapsed intestine.

In 1937, <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> traveled to <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION> in order to report on the Spanish Civil War for the <ORGANIZATION>North American Newspaper Alliance</ORGANIZATION>. While there, <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> broke his friendship with <PERSON>John Dos Passos</PERSON> because, despite warnings, <PERSON>Dos Passos</PERSON> continued to report on the atrocities of not only the fascist Nationalists whom <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> disliked, but also those of the elected and radicalized left-leaning Republicans whom he favored; characteristically, <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> spread a story that <PERSON>Dos Passos</PERSON> had fled <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION> out of cowardice. In this context <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>'s colleague and associate <PERSON>Herbert Matthews</PERSON>, who would become more well known for his favorable reports on <PERSON>Fidel Castro</PERSON>, showed a similar predilection for the Republican side as <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>. <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>, who was a convert to Catholicism during his marriage to his wife <PERSON>Pauline</PERSON>, began to question his religion at this time, eventually leaving the church (though friends indicate that he had "funny ties" to Catholicism for the rest of his life). The war also strained <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>'s marriage. <PERSON>Pauline Pfieffer</PERSON> was a devout <ORGANIZATION>Catholic</ORGANIZATION> and, as such, sided with the fascist, pro-Catholic regime of Franco, whereas <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> mostly supported the Republican government, for all his criticisms of it. During this time, <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> wrote a little known essay, The Denunciation, which would not be published until 1969 within a collection of stories, the Fifth Column and Four Stories of the Spanish Civil War. The story seems autobiographical, suggesting that <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> might have been an informant for the Republic as well as a weapons instructor during the war.

Some health problems characterized this period of <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>'s life: an anthrax infection, a cut eyeball, a gash in his forehead, grippe, toothache, hemorrhoids, kidney trouble from fishing, torn groin muscle, finger gashed to the bone in an accident with a punching ball, lacerations (to arms, legs, and face) from a ride on a runaway horse through a deep <LOCATION>Wyoming</LOCATION> forest, and a broken arm from a car accident.

In 1938--along with his only full-length play, titled The Fifth Column--49 stories were published in the collection The Fifth Column and the First Forty-Nine Stories. <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>'s intention was, as he openly stated in his foreword, to write more. Many of the stories that make up this collection can be found in other abridged collections, including In Our Time, Men Without Women, Winner Take Nothing, and The Snows of <LOCATION>Kilimanjaro</LOCATION>.

Some of the collection's important stories include Old Man at the Bridge, On The Quai at <LOCATION>Smyrna</LOCATION>, Hills Like White Elephants, One Reader Writes, The Killers and (perhaps most famously) A Clean, Well-Lighted Place. While these stories are rather short, the book also includes much longer stories, among them The Snows of Kilimanjaro and The Short Happy Life of <PERSON>Francis Macomber</PERSON>.

In the spring of 1939, <PERSON>Francisco Franco</PERSON> and the Nationalists defeated the Republicans, ending the Spanish Civil War. <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> lost an adopted homeland to <PERSON>Franco</PERSON>'s fascists, and would later lose his beloved Key West, <LOCATION>Florida</LOCATION>, home due to his 1940 divorce. 

A few weeks after the divorce, he married his companion of four years in <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Martha Gellhorn</PERSON>, his third wife. 

His novel For Whom the <ORGANIZATION>Bell Tolls</ORGANIZATION> was published in 1940. It was written in 1939 in <LOCATION>Cuba</LOCATION> and Key West, and was finished in July 1940. The long work, which is set during the Spanish Civil War, was based on real events and tells of an American named <PERSON>Robert Jordan</PERSON> fighting with <LOCATION>Spanish</LOCATION> soldiers on the Republican side. It was largely based on <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>'s experience of living in <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION> and reporting on the war. It is one of his most notable literary accomplishments. The title is taken from the penultimate paragraph of <PERSON>John Donne</PERSON>'s Meditation XVII.

The <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> entered World War II on December 8, 1941, and for the first time in his life, <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> sought to take part in naval warfare. Aboard the <PERSON>Pilar</PERSON>, now a Q-Ship, <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>'s crew was charged with sinking German submarines threatening shipping off the coasts of <LOCATION>Cuba</LOCATION> and the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION>. After the <ORGANIZATION>FBI</ORGANIZATION> took over <LOCATION>Caribbean</LOCATION> counter-espionage, he went to <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION> as a war correspondent for <PERSON>Collier</PERSON>'s magazine. There <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> observed the D-Day landings from an LCVP (landing craft), although he was not allowed to go ashore. He later became angry that his wife, <PERSON>Martha Gellhorn</PERSON> -- by then, more a rival war correspondent than a wife -- had managed to get ashore in the early hours of June 7 dressed as a nurse, after she had crossed the <LOCATION>Atlantic</LOCATION> to <LOCATION>England</LOCATION> in a ship loaded with explosives. <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> acted as an unofficial liaison officer at Chteau de Rambouillet, and afterwards formed his own partisan group which, as he later wrote, took part in the liberation of <LOCATION>Paris</LOCATION>.  Although this claim has been challenged by many historians, he was nevertheless unquestionably on the scene. It has also been purported that while traveling to the front he threw a hand grenade into a basement room full of German field grade officers despite his official non-combatant role. 

After the war, <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> started work on The Garden of Eden, which was never finished and would be published posthumously in a much-abridged form in 1986. At one stage, he planned a major trilogy which was to comprise "The Sea When Young", "The Sea When Absent" and "The Sea in Being" (the latter eventually published in 1952 as The Old Man and the Sea). He spent time in a small Italian town called <LOCATION>Acciaroli</LOCATION> (located approximately 136 km south of <LOCATION>Naples</LOCATION>). There was also a "Sea-Chase" story; three of these pieces were edited and stuck together as the posthumously-published novel Islands in the Stream (1970).

Newly divorced from <PERSON>Gellhorn</PERSON> after four contentious years, <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> married war correspondent <PERSON>Mary Welsh Hemingway</PERSON>, whom he had met overseas in 1944. He returned to <LOCATION>Cuba</LOCATION>, and in 1945 at the <ORGANIZATION>Soviet Embassy</ORGANIZATION> became public witness to the <PERSON>Rolando Masferrer</PERSON> schism within the <LOCATION>Cuban</LOCATION> communist party (<PERSON>Garca Montes</PERSON>, and <PERSON>Alonso</PERSON> vila, 1970 p. 362).

<PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>'s first novel after For Whom the <ORGANIZATION>Bell Tolls</ORGANIZATION> was Across the River and into the Trees (1950), set in post-World War II <LOCATION>Venice</LOCATION>. He derived the title from the last words of American Civil War Confederate General <PERSON>Stonewall Jackson</PERSON>. Enamored of a young Italian girl (<PERSON>Adriana Ivancich</PERSON>) at the time, <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> wrote Across the River and into the Trees as a romance between a war-weary Colonel <PERSON>Cantwell</PERSON> (based on his friend, then Colonel <PERSON>Charles Lanham</PERSON>) and the young <PERSON>Renata</PERSON> (clearly based on <PERSON>Adriana</PERSON>; "Renata" has an assonance with "rinata", meaning "reborn" in Italian). The novel received largely bad reviews, many of which accused <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> of tastelessness, stylistic ineptitude, and sentimentality; however this criticism was not shared by all critics.

One section of the sea trilogy was published as The Old Man and the Sea in 1952. That novella's great success, both commercial and critical, satisfied and fulfilled <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>. It earned him the Pulitzer Prize in 1953. The next year he was awarded with the Nobel Prize in Literature. Upon receiving the latter he noted that he would have been "happy; happier...if the prize had been given to that beautiful writer <PERSON>Isak Dinesen</PERSON>". These awards helped to restore his international reputation.

Then, his legendary bad luck struck once again; on a safari, he was seriously injured in two successive plane crashes; he sprained his right shoulder, arm, and left leg, had a grave concussion, temporarily lost vision in his left eye and the hearing in his left ear, suffered paralysis of the spine, a crushed vertebra, ruptured liver, spleen and kidney, and first degree burns on his face, arms, and leg. Some American newspapers mistakenly published his obituary, thinking he had been killed.

<PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> was then badly injured one month later in a bushfire accident, which left him with second degree burns on his legs, front torso, lips, left hand and right forearm. The pain left him in prolonged anguish, and he was unable to travel to <LOCATION>Stockholm</LOCATION> to accept his Nobel Prize.

A glimmer of hope came with the discovery of some of his old manuscripts from 1928 in the Ritz cellars, which were transformed into A Moveable Feast. Although some of his energy seemed to be restored, severe drinking problems kept him down. His blood pressure and cholesterol were perilously high, he suffered from aortal inflammation, and his depression was aggravated by his dipsomania. However, in October 1956, <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> found the strength to travel to <LOCATION>Madrid</LOCATION> and act as a pallbearer at <PERSON>Po Baroja</PERSON>'s burial. <PERSON>Baroja</PERSON> was one of <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>'s literary influences.

Following the revolution in <LOCATION>Cuba</LOCATION> and the ousting of General <PERSON>Fulgencio Batista</PERSON> in 1959, expropriations of foreign owned property led many Americans to return to the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION>. <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> chose to stay a little longer. It is commonly said that he maintained good relations with <PERSON>Fidel Castro</PERSON> and declared his support for the revolution, and he is quoted as wishing <PERSON>Castro</PERSON> "all luck" with running the country. However, the <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> account "The Shot" is used by <PERSON>Cabrera Infante</PERSON> and others as evidence of conflict between <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> and <PERSON>Fidel Castro</PERSON> dating back to 1948 and the killing of "Manolo" <PERSON>Castro</PERSON>, a friend of <LOCATION>Hemingway</LOCATION>. <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> came under surveillance by the <ORGANIZATION>FBI</ORGANIZATION> both during World War II and afterwards (most probably because of his long association with marxist Spanish Civil War veterans who were again active in <LOCATION>Cuba</LOCATION>) for his residence and activities in <LOCATION>Cuba</LOCATION>. In 1960, he left the island and <PERSON>Finca Viga</PERSON>, his estate outside <LOCATION>Havana</LOCATION>, that he owned for over twenty years. The official <LOCATION>Cuban</LOCATION> government account is that it was left to the <LOCATION>Cuban</LOCATION> government, which has made it into a museum devoted to the author. In 2001, <LOCATION>Cuba</LOCATION>'s state-owned tourism conglomerate, <PERSON>El Gran-Caribe</PERSON> SA, began licensing the <ORGANIZATION>La Bodeguita del Medio</ORGANIZATION> international restaurant chain relying largely on the original <LOCATION>Havana</LOCATION> restaurant's association with <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>, a frequent visitor.

In February 1960, <PERSON>Ernest Hemingway</PERSON> was unable to get his bullfighting narrative The Dangerous Summer to the publishers. He therefore had his wife <PERSON>Mary</PERSON> summon his friend, <ORGANIZATION>Life Magazine</ORGANIZATION> bureau head <PERSON>Will Lang Jr.</PERSON>, to leave <LOCATION>Paris</LOCATION> and come to <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION>. <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> persuaded <PERSON>Lang</PERSON> to let him print the manuscript, along with a picture layout, before it came out in hardcover. Although not a word of it was on paper, the proposal was agreed upon. The first part of the story appeared in <ORGANIZATION>Life Magazine</ORGANIZATION> on September 5, 1960, with the remaining installments being printed in successive issues.

<PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> was upset by the photographs in his The Dangerous Summer article. He was receiving treatment in <LOCATION>Ketchum</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Idaho</LOCATION> for high blood pressure and liver problems -- and also electroconvulsive therapy (ECT) for depression and continued paranoia, although this may in fact have helped to precipitate his suicide, since he reportedly suffered significant memory loss as a result of the shock treatments. He also lost weight, his 6-foot (183 cm) frame appearing gaunt at 170 pounds (77 kg, 12st 2lb).

<PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> attempted suicide in the spring of 1961, and received <ORGANIZATION>ECT</ORGANIZATION> treatment again. On the morning of July 2, 1961, some three weeks short of his 62nd birthday, he died at his home in <LOCATION>Ketchum</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Idaho</LOCATION>, the result of a self-inflicted shotgun wound to the head. Judged not mentally responsible for his final act, he was buried in a Roman Catholic service. <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> himself blamed the <ORGANIZATION>ECT</ORGANIZATION> treatments for "putting him out of business" by destroying his memory; some medical and scholarly opinion has been receptive to this view, although others, including one of the physicians who prescribed the electroshock regimen, dispute that opinion.

<PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> is believed to have purchased the <ORGANIZATION>Boss & Co.</ORGANIZATION> shotgun he used to commit suicide through <ORGANIZATION>Abercrombie & Fitch</ORGANIZATION>, which was then an elite excursion goods retailer and firearm supplier. In a particularly gruesome suicide, he rested the gun butt of the double-barreled shotgun on the floor of a hallway in his home, leaned over it to put the twin muzzles to his forehead just above the eyes, and pulled both triggers. The coroner, at request of the family, did not do an autopsy.

Other members of <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>'s immediate family also committed suicide, including his father, <PERSON>Clarence Hemingway</PERSON>, his siblings <PERSON>Ursula</PERSON> and <LOCATION>Leicester</LOCATION>, and possibly his granddaughter <PERSON>Margaux Hemingway</PERSON>. Some believe that certain members of <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>'s paternal line had a hereditary disease known as haemochromatosis (bronze diabetes), in which an excess of iron concentration in the blood causes damage to the pancreas and also causes depression or instability in the cerebrum. <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>'s father is known to have developed haemochromatosis in the years prior to his suicide at age fifty-nine. Throughout his life, <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> had been a heavy drinker, succumbing to alcoholism in his later years.

<PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> possibly suffered from manic depression, and was subsequently treated with electroshock therapy at <ORGANIZATION>Menninger Clinic</ORGANIZATION>. He later blamed his memory loss, which he cited as a reason for not wanting to live, upon the ECT sessions.

<PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> is interred in the town cemetery in <LOCATION>Ketchum</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Idaho</LOCATION>, at the north end of town. A memorial was erected in 1966 at another location, overlooking <LOCATION>Trail Creek</LOCATION>, north of <LOCATION>Ketchum</LOCATION>. It is inscribed with a eulogy he wrote for a friend, Gene Van Guilder:



Best of all he loved the fallThe leaves yellow on the cottonwoodsLeaves floating on the trout streamsAnd above the hillsThe high blue windless skiesNow he will be a part of them forever

<PERSON>Ernest Hemingway</PERSON> - <LOCATION>Idaho</LOCATION> - 1939

Celebrating <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>'s love for <LOCATION>Idaho</LOCATION> and the frontier, The Ernest Hemingway Festival takes place annually in <LOCATION>Ketchum</LOCATION> and <LOCATION>Sun Valley</LOCATION> in late September with scholars, a reading by the PEN/Hemingway Award winner and many more events, including historical tours, open mic nights and a sponsored dinner at <LOCATION>Hemingway</LOCATION>'s home in Warm Springs now maintained by the Nature Conservancy in <LOCATION>Ketchum</LOCATION>.

<PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> was a prolific letter writer and, in 1981, many of these were published by Scribner in <PERSON>Ernest Hemingway</PERSON> Selected Letters. It was met with some controversy as <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> himself stated he never wished to publish his letters. Further letters were published in a book of his correspondence with his editor <PERSON>Max Perkins</PERSON>, The Only Thing that Counts [1996].

A long-term project is now underway to publish the thousands of letters <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> wrote during his lifetime. The project is being undertaken as a joint venture by <ORGANIZATION>Penn State University</ORGANIZATION> and the <ORGANIZATION>Ernest Hemingway Foundation</ORGANIZATION>. <PERSON>Sandra Spanier</PERSON>, Professor of English and wife of <ORGANIZATION>Penn State</ORGANIZATION> president <PERSON>Graham Spanier</PERSON>, is serving as general editor of the collection.

<PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> was still writing up to his death; most of the unfinished works which were <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>'s sole creation have been published posthumously; they are A Moveable Feast, Islands in the Stream, The Nick Adams Stories (portions of which were previously unpublished), The Dangerous Summer, and The Garden of Eden. In a note forwarding "Islands in the Stream", <PERSON>Mary Hemingway</PERSON> indicated that she worked with <PERSON>Charles Scribner, Jr.</PERSON> on "preparing this book for publication from <PERSON>Ernest</PERSON>'s original manuscript". She also stated that "beyond the routine chores of correcting spelling and punctuation, we made some cuts in the manuscript, I feel that <PERSON>Ernest</PERSON> would surely have made them himself. The book is all Ernest's. We have added nothing to it." Some controversy has surrounded the publication of these works, insofar as it has been suggested that it is not necessarily within the jurisdiction of <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>'s relatives or publishers to determine whether these works should be made available to the public. For example, scholars often disapprovingly note that the version of The Garden of Eden published by <PERSON>Charles Scribner</PERSON>'s Sons in 1986, though in no way a revision of <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>'s original words, nonetheless omits two-thirds of the original manuscript.

The <PERSON>Nick Adams</PERSON> Stories appeared posthumously in 1972. What is now considered the definitive compilation of all of <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>'s short stories was published as The Complete Short Stories Of <PERSON>Ernest Hemingway</PERSON>, first compiled and published in 1987. As well, in 1969 The Fifth Column and Four Stories Of The Spanish Civil War was published. It contains <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>'s only full length play, The Fifth Column, which was previously published along with the <ORGANIZATION>First Forty-Nine Stories</ORGANIZATION> in 1938, along with four unpublished works written about <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>'s experiences during the Spanish Civil War.

In 1999, another novel entitled True at First Light appeared under the name of <PERSON>Ernest Hemingway</PERSON>, though it was heavily edited by his son <PERSON>Patrick Hemingway</PERSON>. Six years later, Under Kilimanjaro, a re-edited and considerably longer version of True at First Light appeared. In either edition, the novel is a fictional account of <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>'s final African safari in 19531954. He spent several months in <LOCATION>Kenya</LOCATION> with his fourth wife, <PERSON>Mary</PERSON>, before his near-fatal plane crashes. Anticipation of the novel, whose manuscript was completed in 1956, adumbrates perhaps an unprecedentedly large critical battle over whether it is proper to publish the work (many sources mention that a new, light side of <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> will be seen as opposed to his canonical, macho image), even as editors <PERSON>Robert W. Lewis</PERSON> of <ORGANIZATION>University of North Dakota</ORGANIZATION> and <PERSON>Robert E. Fleming</PERSON> of <ORGANIZATION>University of New Mexico</ORGANIZATION> have pushed it through to publication; the novel was published on September 15, 2005.

Also published posthumously were several collections of his work as a journalist. These contain his columns and articles for <ORGANIZATION>Esquire Magazine</ORGANIZATION>, <ORGANIZATION>The North American Newspaper Alliance</ORGANIZATION>, and the <ORGANIZATION>Toronto Star</ORGANIZATION>; they include Byline: <PERSON>Ernest Hemingway</PERSON> edited by <PERSON>William White</PERSON>, and Hemingway: The Wild Years edited by <PERSON>Gene Z. Hanrahan</PERSON>. Finally, a collection of introductions, forwards, public letters and other miscellanea was published as <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> and the Mechanism of Fame in 2005.

The influence of <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>'s writings on American literature was considerable and continues today. <PERSON>James Joyce</PERSON> called "A Clean, Well Lighted Place" "one of the best stories ever written". (The same story also influenced several of <PERSON>Edward Hopper</PERSON>'s best known paintings, most notably "Nighthawks." ) Pulp fiction and "hard boiled" crime fiction (which flourished from the 1920s to the 1950s) often owed a strong debt to <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>. 

During World War II, <PERSON>J. D. Salinger</PERSON> met and corresponded with <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>, whom he acknowledged as an influence. In one letter to <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>, <PERSON>Salinger</PERSON> wrote that their talks "had given him his only hopeful minutes of the entire war," and jokingly "named himself national chairman of the <ORGANIZATION>Hemingway Fan Clubs</ORGANIZATION>." 

<PERSON>Hunter S. Thompson</PERSON> often compared himself to <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>, and terse Hemingway-esque sentences can be found in his early novel, The Rum Diary. <PERSON>Thompson</PERSON>'s later suicide by gunshot to the head mirrored <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>'s. 

<PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>'s terse prose style--"<PERSON>Nick</PERSON> stood up. He was all right."-- is known to have inspired <PERSON>Charles Bukowski</PERSON>, <PERSON>Chuck Palahniuk</PERSON>, <PERSON>Douglas Coupland</PERSON> and many Generation X writers. <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON>'s style also influenced <PERSON>Jack Kerouac</PERSON> and other Beat Generation writers. <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> also provided a role model to fellow author and hunter <PERSON>Robert Ruark</PERSON>, who is frequently referred to as "the poor man's <PERSON>Ernest Hemingway</PERSON>". 

Popular novelist <PERSON>Elmore Leonard</PERSON>, who has authored scores of western- and crime-genre novels, cites <PERSON>Hemingway</PERSON> as his preeminent influence, and this is evident in his tightly written prose. Though <PERSON>Leonard</PERSON> has never claimed to write serious literature, he has said: "I learned by imitating Hemingway.... until I realized that I didn't share his attitude about life. I didn't take myself or anything as seriously as he did."

<TITLE><PERSON>Colin Powell</PERSON></TITLE>

<PERSON>Colin Luther Powell</PERSON>, <ORGANIZATION>KCB</ORGANIZATION> (Honorary), <ORGANIZATION>MSC</ORGANIZATION>, (born April 5, 1937) is a retired General in the <ORGANIZATION>United States Army</ORGANIZATION>. He was the 65th <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> Secretary of State (2001-2005), serving under President <PERSON>George W. Bush</PERSON>. He was the first African American appointed to that position. As a General in the <ORGANIZATION>United States Army</ORGANIZATION>, <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> also served as National Security Advisor (19871989) and as Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff (19891993), holding the latter position during the Gulf War. He was the first and, so far, the only African American to serve on the Joint Chiefs of Staff.

<PERSON>Powell</PERSON> was born in <LOCATION>Harlem</LOCATION>, a neighborhood in the <LOCATION>New York City</LOCATION> borough of <LOCATION>Manhattan</LOCATION> in 1937 to Jamaican immigrant parents and was raised in the <LOCATION>South Bronx</LOCATION>. He also has Scottish, and other ancestries. <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> attended <ORGANIZATION>Morris High School</ORGANIZATION>, a former public school in <LOCATION>The Bronx</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>New York City</LOCATION>, from which he graduated in 1954. While in school, he worked a local shop where he picked up Yiddish from the shopkeepers and some of the customers. He remembers some of his Yiddish to this day. He gained a bachelor's degree in geology from <ORGANIZATION>City College</ORGANIZATION> of <LOCATION>New York</LOCATION> attaining a "C" average, according to his 2006 graduation address at <ORGANIZATION>Marymount University</ORGANIZATION>. He later obtained an MBA from The <ORGANIZATION>George Washington University</ORGANIZATION> after his second tour in <LOCATION>Vietnam</LOCATION> in 1971.

In 1962, he married <PERSON>Alma</PERSON>, who is now the co-chair of <LOCATION>America</LOCATION>'s Promise. He is the father of <PERSON>Michael Powell</PERSON>, the former chair of the <ORGANIZATION>Federal Communications Commission</ORGANIZATION> (<PERSON>Michael Powell</PERSON> was known mostly for a public battle with radio host <PERSON>Howard Stern</PERSON>).

<PERSON>Powell</PERSON>'s first name is fairly common in the <LOCATION>United Kingdom</LOCATION> and <LOCATION>Ireland</LOCATION> but rare in the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION>. He pronounces his name "cole-in"; most other men with this name pronounce it "coll-in" in the <LOCATION>United Kingdom</LOCATION>. In general, public officials and radio and television reporters have used <PERSON>Powell</PERSON>'s preferred pronunciation.

While at <ORGANIZATION>City College Powell</ORGANIZATION> joined the <ORGANIZATION>Reserve Officers' Training Corps</ORGANIZATION> and later described it as one of the happiest experiences of his life: finding something he loved and could do well, he had "found himself." Cadet <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> joined the <LOCATION>Pershing Rifles</LOCATION>, the <ORGANIZATION>ROTC</ORGANIZATION> fraternal organization and drill team started by GEN <PERSON>John Pershing</PERSON>. Even after <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> became a General, he still kept on his desk a pen set he had won for a drill team competition. After graduating from <ORGANIZATION>City College</ORGANIZATION> in June 1958, he was granted a commission as an Army Second Lieutenant. <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> was a professional soldier for 35 years, during which time he held a variety of command and staff positions and rose to the rank of General. 

While serving with the Third Armored Division in <LOCATION>Germany</LOCATION> as a Lieutenant, he met <PERSON>Elvis Presley</PERSON>, then serving in that unit. During the Vietnam War, <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> was a captain, serving as a <ORGANIZATION>South Vietnamese Army</ORGANIZATION> advisor from 1962 to 1963. While on patrol in a Viet Cong-held area, he was wounded after stepping on a punji stake trap. He returned to <LOCATION>Vietnam</LOCATION> as a major from 1968 to 1970, where he first served as the executive officer for the Americal Division (23rd Infantry Division), then as the assistant chief of staff of operations for the Americal Division. In that post, he was charged with investigating a detailed letter by <PERSON>Tom Glen</PERSON> (a soldier from the 11th Light Infantry Brigade), which backed up rumored allegations of the My Lai Massacre. <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> wrote: "In direct refutation of this portrayal is the fact that relations between <LOCATION>Americal</LOCATION> soldiers and the Vietnamese people are excellent." Later, <PERSON>Powell</PERSON>'s assessment would be described as whitewashing the news of the massacre, and questions would continue to remain undisclosed to the public. In May 2004 <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> said to <PERSON>Larry King</PERSON>, "I mean, I was in a unit that was responsible for My <LOCATION>Lai</LOCATION>. I got there after My Lai happened. So, in war, these sorts of horrible things happen every now and again, but they are still to be deplored."

<PERSON>Powell</PERSON> obtained an MBA from <ORGANIZATION>George Washington University</ORGANIZATION> in 1971 and then served a White House fellowship under President <PERSON>Richard Nixon</PERSON>. 

In his autobiography My American Journey, <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> mentioned several officers he served under that inspired and mentored him. As a Lieutenant Colonel serving in <LOCATION>South Korea</LOCATION>, for example, <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> was very close to General <PERSON>Henry</PERSON> "Gunfighter" <PERSON>Emerson</PERSON>. <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> said he regarded this man as one of the most caring officers he ever served under. <PERSON>Emerson</PERSON> reputedly had a somewhat eccentric personality. For example, he insisted his troops train only at night and made them repeatedly watch the television film <PERSON>Brian</PERSON>'s Song to promote racial harmony. <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> always professed, however, that what set <PERSON>Emerson</PERSON> apart was his great love of his soldiers and concern for their welfare.

 the early 1980s, <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> served at <LOCATION>Fort Carson</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Colorado</LOCATION>. It was there that he had a major clash with General <PERSON>John Hudachek</PERSON>, his commander. <PERSON>Hudachek</PERSON> said in an efficiency evaluation that <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> was a poor leader who should not be promoted. <PERSON>Powell</PERSON>'s rising military career was unhindered by <PERSON>Hudachek</PERSON>'s evaluation report. After he left <PERSON>Fort Carson</PERSON>, <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> became senior military assistant to Secretary of Defense <PERSON>Caspar Weinberger</PERSON>, whom he assisted during the 1983 invasion of <LOCATION>Grenada</LOCATION> and the 1986 airstrike on <LOCATION>Libya</LOCATION>. 

In 1986, he took over the command of V <ORGANIZATION>Corps</ORGANIZATION> in <LOCATION>Frankfurt Germany</LOCATION> from <PERSON>Robert Lewis</PERSON> "Sam" <PERSON>Wetzel</PERSON>. In 1989, prior to being named Chairman of the <ORGANIZATION>Joint Chiefs of Staff</ORGANIZATION>, <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> served as the Commander in Chief, Forces Command headquartered at <LOCATION>Fort McPherson</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Georgia</LOCATION>.

At the age of 49, <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> became <PERSON>Ronald Reagan</PERSON>'s National Security Advisor, serving from 1987 to 1989. He retained his Army commission (he was a Lieutenant General at the time of his nomination) while serving as National Security Advisor. After his tenure with the <ORGANIZATION>NSC</ORGANIZATION>, <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> was promoted to 4-star General under President <PERSON>George H.W. Bush</PERSON> and served as Commander-in-Chief (<ORGANIZATION>CINC</ORGANIZATION>) of the <ORGANIZATION>United States Army</ORGANIZATION>'s Forces Command (FORSCOM), overseeing all <ORGANIZATION>Army</ORGANIZATION>, <ORGANIZATION>Army Reserve</ORGANIZATION>, and <ORGANIZATION>National Guard</ORGANIZATION> units in the Continental <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Alaska</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Hawaii</LOCATION>, and <LOCATION>Puerto Rico</LOCATION>.



His last military assignment, from October 1, 1989 to September 30, 1993, was as the 12th Chairman of the <ORGANIZATION>Joint Chiefs of Staff</ORGANIZATION>, the highest military position in the <ORGANIZATION>Department of Defense</ORGANIZATION>. At age 52, he became the youngest officer, and first Afro-Caribbean American, to serve in this position. In 1989, he joined <PERSON>Dwight D. Eisenhower</PERSON> and <PERSON>Alexander Haig</PERSON> as the third general since World War II to reach four-star rank without ever being a divisional commander. <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> was also the first JCS Chair who was not a collegiate graduate of a <ORGANIZATION>United States Service</ORGANIZATION> academy, but rather, through ROTC. During this time, he oversaw 28 crises, including the invasion of <LOCATION>Panama</LOCATION> in 1989 to remove General <PERSON>Manuel Noriega</PERSON> from power in the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> invasion of <LOCATION>Panama</LOCATION> and Operation Desert Storm in the 1991 Persian Gulf War. During these events, <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> earned his nickname, "the reluctant warrior". He rarely advocated military intervention as the first solution to an international dispute, and instead usually prescribed diplomacy and containment.



<PERSON>Powell</PERSON> mentioned in his autobiography that he is haunted by the nightmare of the Vietnam War. He felt the leadership was very ineffective. <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> served a tour in <LOCATION>Vietnam</LOCATION> as a military advisor, and was mildly injured when he stepped on a bamboo "punji stick." The large infection made it difficult for him to walk, and caused his foot to swell for a short time, shortening his first tour. It was also during his <LOCATION>Vietnam</LOCATION> service, his second tour, that <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> was decorated for bravery. He single-handedly rescued several men from a burning helicopter, one of them being Maj. Gen. <PERSON>Charles Gettys</PERSON>, the commander of the Americal Division.

<PERSON>Powell</PERSON> opposed the majority of <PERSON>George H.W. Bush</PERSON> Administration officials who advocated the deployment of troops to the <LOCATION>Middle East</LOCATION> to force <LOCATION>Iraqi</LOCATION> leader <PERSON>Saddam Hussein</PERSON> to withdraw his armies from neighboring <LOCATION>Kuwait</LOCATION>, believing the dictator could instead be contained through sanctions and a buildup of forces around <LOCATION>Kuwait</LOCATION>.

As an officer, <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> also valued loyalty very highly. As a military strategist, he has advocated an approach to military conflicts that maximizes the potential for success and minimizes casualties. A component of this approach is the use of overwhelming force, which he applied to Operation Desert Storm in 1991. His approach has been dubbed the "Powell Doctrine".

<PERSON>Colin Powell</PERSON>'s experience in military matters made him a very popular figure with both American political parties. Many Democrats admired his moderate stance on military matters, while many Republicans saw him as a great asset associated with the successes of past <ORGANIZATION>Republican</ORGANIZATION> administrations. <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> eventually declared himself a Republican, and began to campaign for Republican candidates. He was touted as a possible opponent of <PERSON>Bill Clinton</PERSON> in the 1996 <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> Presidential Election, but <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> declined.

In 1997 <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> founded <LOCATION>America</LOCATION>'s Promise with the objective of helping children from all socioeconomic sectors. <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> often wears the logo of the organization in the form of a red wagon pin on his lapel.

In the 2000 <ORGANIZATION>U.S. Presidential Election Powell</ORGANIZATION> campaigned for <LOCATION>Texas</LOCATION> Governor <PERSON>George W. Bush</PERSON>, serving as a key foreign policy advisor to the campaign. At the same time, it was often hinted that <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> might have been appointed to a position within a Democratic administration, should <PERSON>Al Gore</PERSON> have won. <PERSON>Bush</PERSON> eventually won, and <PERSON>Colin Powell</PERSON> was appointed Secretary of State.



As Secretary of State in the <PERSON>Bush</PERSON> administration, <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> was perceived as moderate. <PERSON>Powell</PERSON>'s great asset was his tremendous popularity among the American people. Over the course of his tenure he traveled less than any other <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> Secretary of State in 30 years. <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> was unanimously voted in by the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION>.

On September 11, 2001, <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> was in <LOCATION>Lima</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Peru</LOCATION>, meeting with President <PERSON>Alejandro Toledo</PERSON> and <LOCATION>US</LOCATION> Ambassador to <LOCATION>Peru</LOCATION> <PERSON>John Hamilton</PERSON>, and attending the special session of the <ORGANIZATION>OAS General Assembly</ORGANIZATION> that subsequently adopted the Inter-American Democratic Charter.

After September 11, <PERSON>Powell</PERSON>'s job became of critical importance in managing <LOCATION>America</LOCATION>'s relationships with foreign countries in order to secure a stable coalition in the War on Terrorism.

In April 2002, he visited the site of the alleged <LOCATION>Jenin Massacre</LOCATION> in the <LOCATION>West Bank</LOCATION> and later said while testifying to <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION>, "I've seen no evidence that would suggest a massacre took place." Details of the events were unclear at the time, <PERSON>Shimon Peres</PERSON> was quoted by <ORGANIZATION>Ha'aretz</ORGANIZATION> speaking of a  and <ORGANIZATION>IDF</ORGANIZATION> estimates of the dead were in the 100s. Later investigations by human rights organizations and the <ORGANIZATION>United Nations</ORGANIZATION> placed the number of deaths amongst Palestinians at 52.

<PERSON>Powell</PERSON> came under fire for his role in building the case for the 2003 Invasion of <LOCATION>Iraq</LOCATION>. In a press statement on February 24, 2001 he had said that sanctions against <LOCATION>Iraq</LOCATION> had prevented the development of any weapons of mass destruction by <PERSON>Saddam Hussein</PERSON>. As was the case in the days leading up to the Persian Gulf War, <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> was initially opposed to a forcible overthrow of <PERSON>Hussein</PERSON>, preferring to continue a policy of containment. However, <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> eventually agreed to go along with the <PERSON>Bush</PERSON> administration's determination to remove <PERSON>Hussein</PERSON>. He had often clashed with others in the administration, who were reportedly planning an <LOCATION>Iraq</LOCATION> invasion even before the September 11 attacksan insight supported by testimony by former terrorism czar <PERSON>Richard Clarke</PERSON> in front of the 9/11 Commission. The main concession <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> wanted before he would offer his full support for the <LOCATION>Iraq</LOCATION> War was the involvement of the international community in the invasion, as opposed to the unilateral approach some advocated. He was also successful in persuading <PERSON>Bush</PERSON> to take the case of <LOCATION>Iraq</LOCATION> to the <ORGANIZATION>United Nations</ORGANIZATION>, and in moderating other initiatives. <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> was placed at the forefront of this diplomatic campaign.



<PERSON>Powell</PERSON>'s chief role was to garner international support for a multi-national coalition to mount the invasion. To this end, <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> addressed a plenary session of the <ORGANIZATION>United Nations Security Council</ORGANIZATION> on February 5, 2003 to argue in favor of military action. Citing "numerous" anonymous Iraqi defectors, <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> asserted that "there can be no doubt that <PERSON>Saddam Hussein</PERSON> has biological weapons and the capability to rapidly produce more, many more." <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> also stated that there was "no doubt in my mind" that <PERSON>Saddam</PERSON> was working to obtain key components to produce nuclear weapons.

Most observers praised <PERSON>Powell</PERSON>'s oratorical skills. However, <LOCATION>Britain</LOCATION>'s Channel 4 News reported soon afterwards that a <LOCATION>UK</LOCATION> intelligence dossier that <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> had referred to as a "fine paper" during his presentation had been based on old material and plagiarized an essay by an American graduate student. A 2004 report by the <ORGANIZATION>Iraq Survey Group</ORGANIZATION> concluded that the evidence that <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> offered to support the allegation that the <LOCATION>Iraqi</LOCATION> government possessed weapons of mass destruction (WMDs) was inaccurate. 

A <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION> report on intelligence failures would later detail the intense debate that went on behind the scenes on what to include in <PERSON>Powell</PERSON>'s speech. <ORGANIZATION>State Department</ORGANIZATION> analysts had found dozens of factual problems in drafts of the speech. Some of the claims were taken out, but others were left in, such as claims based on the yellowcake forgery. The administration came under fire for having acted on faulty intelligence. Reports have indicated that <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> himself was skeptical of the evidence presented to him. <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> later recounted how Vice President <PERSON>Cheney</PERSON> had joked with him before he gave the speech, telling him, "You've got high poll ratings; you can afford to lose a few points." <PERSON>Larry Wilkerson</PERSON> later characterized <PERSON>Cheney</PERSON>'s view of <PERSON>Powell</PERSON>'s mission as to "go up there and sell it, and we'll have moved forward a peg or two. Fall on your damn sword and kill yourself, and I'll be happy, too."

In September 2005, <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> was asked about the speech during an interview with <PERSON>Barbara Walters</PERSON> and responded that it was a "blot" on his record. He went on to say, "It will always be a part of my record. It was painful. It's painful now."

Mr. <PERSON>Powell</PERSON>'s longtime aide-de-camp Colonel <PERSON>Lawrence B. Wilkerson</PERSON> said that he participated in a hoax on the American people in preparing Mr. <PERSON>Powell</PERSON>'s erroneous testimony before the <ORGANIZATION>United Nations Security Council</ORGANIZATION>.

Because <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> was seen as more moderate than most figures in the administration, he was spared many of the attacks that have been leveled at more controversial advocates of the invasion, such as <PERSON>Donald Rumsfeld</PERSON> and <PERSON>Paul Wolfowitz</PERSON>. At times, infighting between the Powell-led <ORGANIZATION>State Department</ORGANIZATION>, the Rumsfeld-led <ORGANIZATION>Defense Department</ORGANIZATION>, and Vice President <PERSON>Dick Cheney</PERSON>'s office had the effect of paralyzing the administration on crucial issues, such as what actions to take regarding <LOCATION>Iran</LOCATION> and <LOCATION>North Korea</LOCATION>.



After <PERSON>Saddam Hussein</PERSON> had been deposed, <PERSON>Powell</PERSON>'s new role was to once again establish a working international coalition, this time to assist in the rebuilding of post-war <LOCATION>Iraq</LOCATION>. On September 13, 2004, <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> testified before the <ORGANIZATION>Senate Governmental Affairs Committee</ORGANIZATION>, acknowledging that the sources who provided much of the information in his February 2003 <ORGANIZATION>UN</ORGANIZATION> presentation were "wrong" and that it was "unlikely" that any stockpiles of WMDs would be found. Claiming that he was unaware that some intelligence officials questioned the information prior to his presentation, <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> pushed for reform in the intelligence community, including the creation of a national intelligence director who would assure that "what one person knew, everyone else knew".

<PERSON>Colin Powell</PERSON> announced his resignation as Secretary of State on Monday, November 15, 2004. News of <PERSON>Powell</PERSON>'s leaving the Administration spurred mixed reactions from politicians around the worldsome upset at the loss of a statesman seen as a moderating factor within the <PERSON>Bush</PERSON> administration, but others hoping for <PERSON>Powell</PERSON>'s successor to wield more influence within the cabinet, and thus be a more credible negotiator.

In mid-November, <PERSON>Colin Powell</PERSON> stated that he had seen new evidence suggesting that <LOCATION>Iran</LOCATION> was adapting missiles for a nuclear delivery system. The accusation came at the same time as the settlement of an agreement between the <ORGANIZATION>IAEA</ORGANIZATION>, the <ORGANIZATION>European Union</ORGANIZATION> and <LOCATION>Iran</LOCATION>.

A moderate Republican, <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> is well known for his willingness to support liberal or centrist causes. He is pro-choice regarding abortion, and in favor of "reasonable" gun control. He stated in his autobiography that he supports affirmative action that levels the playing field, without giving a leg up to undeserving person because of racial issues. <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> was also instrumental in the implementation of the military's Don't Ask, Don't Tell policy.

The Vietnam War had a profound effect on <PERSON>Powell</PERSON>'s views of the proper use of military force. These views are described in detail in the autobiography My American Journey. The <ORGANIZATION>Powell Doctrine</ORGANIZATION>, as the views became known, was a central component of <LOCATION>US</LOCATION> policy in the Gulf War (the first <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> war in <LOCATION>Iraq</LOCATION>) and <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> invasion of <LOCATION>Afghanistan</LOCATION> (the overthrow of the <ORGANIZATION>Taliban</ORGANIZATION> regime in <LOCATION>Afghanistan</LOCATION> following the events of "9/11"). The hallmark of both operations was strong international cooperation, and the use of overwhelming military force.

<PERSON>Powell</PERSON> was the subject of controversy in 2004 when, in a conversation with British Foreign Secretary, <PERSON>Jack Straw</PERSON>, he reportedly referred to neoconservatives within the <PERSON>Bush</PERSON> administration as "fucking crazies". In addition to being reported in the press (though generally, the expletive was censored in the <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> press), the quote was used by <PERSON>James Naughtie</PERSON> in his book, The Accidental American: <PERSON>Tony Blair</PERSON> and the Presidency, and by <PERSON>Chris Patten</PERSON> in his book, Cousins and Strangers: <LOCATION>America</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Britain</LOCATION>, and <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION> in a New Century.

In a letter to Sen. <PERSON>John McCain</PERSON>, General <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> expressed opposition to President <PERSON>Bush</PERSON>'s push for military tribunals of those formerly and currently classified as enemy combatants. In the end, <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> did not give a speech at the Democratic National Convention and was not nominated to be the Vice Presidential candidate under <PERSON>John McCain</PERSON>.

In July 2007 <PERSON>Powell</PERSON> revealed that he spent two and a half hours trying to persuade <PERSON>George W. Bush</PERSON> not to invade <LOCATION>Iraq</LOCATION> but that he did not prevail.
