<PERSON>Powell</PERSON> went on to say that he believed <LOCATION>Iraq</LOCATION> was in a state of civil war. "The civil war will ultimately be resolved by a test of arms. It's not going to be pretty to watch, but I don't know any way to avoid it. It is happening now." He further noted, "It is not a civil war that can be put down or solved by the armed forces of the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION>," and suggested that all the <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> military could do was put "a heavier lid on this pot of boiling sectarian stew".

<PERSON>Powell</PERSON>'s civilian awards includes two Presidential Medals of Freedom, the President's Citizens Medal, the Congressional Gold Medal, the Secretary of State Distinguished Service Medal, the Secretary of Energy Distinguished Service Medal, and the <PERSON>Ronald Reagan</PERSON> Freedom Award. Several schools and other institutions have been named in his honor and he holds honorary degrees from universities and colleges across the country.







 







<TITLE><PERSON>Dwight Eisenhower</PERSON></TITLE>

<PERSON>Dwight David</PERSON> "Ike" <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> (October 14, 1890 March 28, 1969) was President of the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> from 1953 until 1961 and a five-star general in the <ORGANIZATION>United States Army</ORGANIZATION>. During the Second World War, he served as Supreme Commander of the Allied forces in <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION>, with responsibility for planning and supervising the successful invasion of <LOCATION>France</LOCATION> and <LOCATION>Germany</LOCATION> in 194445. In 1951, he became the first supreme commander of <ORGANIZATION>NATO</ORGANIZATION>.

As President, he oversaw the cease-fire of the Korean War, kept up the pressure on the <LOCATION>Soviet Union</LOCATION> during the Cold War, made nuclear weapons a higher defense priority, launched the Space Race, enlarged the Social Security program, and began the Interstate Highway System.

<PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> was born <PERSON>David Dwight Eisenhower</PERSON> in <LOCATION>Denison</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Texas</LOCATION>, the first president born in that state. He was the third of seven sons born to <PERSON>David Jacob Eisenhower</PERSON> and <PERSON>Ida Elizabeth Stover</PERSON>. The house in which he was born has been preserved as <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> Birthplace State Historic Site and is operated by the <ORGANIZATION>Texas Historical Commission</ORGANIZATION>.

He was named <PERSON>David Dwight</PERSON> and was called <PERSON>Dwight</PERSON>; he reversed the order of his given names when he entered <LOCATION>West Point</LOCATION>,, which is also where "Ike" received his nickname.

<PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON>'s paternal ancestors can be traced back to <PERSON>Hans Nicolas Eisenhauer</PERSON>, whose surname was German for "iron worker." <PERSON>Hans Eisenhauer</PERSON> and his family emigrated from <LOCATION>Karlsbrunn</LOCATION> (<LOCATION>Saarland</LOCATION>), <LOCATION>Germany</LOCATION> to <LOCATION>Lancaster</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION> in 1741. Descendants made their way west. <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON>'s family settled in <LOCATION>Abilene</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Kansas</LOCATION> in 1892. His father <PERSON>David Eisenhower</PERSON> was a college-educated engineer. <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> graduated from <ORGANIZATION>Abilene High School</ORGANIZATION> in 1909.

<PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> married <PERSON>Mamie Geneva Doud</PERSON> (1896 1979) of <LOCATION>Denver</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Colorado</LOCATION> on July 1, 1916. The couple had two sons. <PERSON>Doud Dwight Eisenhower</PERSON> was born September 24, 1917, and died of scarlet fever on January 2, 1921, at the age of three. Their second son, <PERSON>John Sheldon David Doud Eisenhower</PERSON>, was born the following year on August 3, 1922; <PERSON>John</PERSON> served in the <ORGANIZATION>United States Army</ORGANIZATION> (retiring as a brigadier general from the <ORGANIZATION>Army</ORGANIZATION> reserve), became an author, and served as <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> Ambassador to <LOCATION>Belgium</LOCATION> from 1969 to 1971. <PERSON>John</PERSON>, coincidentally, graduated from <LOCATION>West Point</LOCATION> on D-Day, June 6, 1944, and was married to <PERSON>Barbara Jean Thompson</PERSON> in a June wedding in 1947. <PERSON>John</PERSON> and <PERSON>Barbara</PERSON> had four children: <PERSON>Dwight David II</PERSON>, <PERSON>Barbara Ann</PERSON>, <PERSON>Susan Elaine</PERSON> and <PERSON>Mary Jean</PERSON>. <PERSON>John</PERSON>'s son, <PERSON>David</PERSON>, after whom Camp <PERSON>David</PERSON> is named, married <PERSON>Richard Nixon</PERSON>'s daughter <PERSON>Julie</PERSON> in 1968.

<PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON>'s paternal ancestor, <PERSON>Hans Nicholas Eisenhauer</PERSON>, was probably of Lutheran or Reformed Protestant practice. <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON>'s mother, <PERSON>Ida E. Eisenhower</PERSON>, previously a member of the <LOCATION>River Brethren</LOCATION> sect of the Mennonites, joined the Watch Tower Bible and <ORGANIZATION>Tract Society</ORGANIZATION> (now more commonly known as <PERSON>Jehovah</PERSON>'s Witnesses) between 1895 and 1900, when <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> was a child. The <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> home served as the local meeting hall from 1896 to 1915.

When <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> joined the <ORGANIZATION>U.S. Military Academy</ORGANIZATION> at <LOCATION>West Point</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>New York</LOCATION> in 1911, his ties to Jehovahs Witnesses were weakened because of the group's anti-militarist stance. By 1915, his parents' home no longer served as the meeting hall. All the men in the household abandoned the Witnesses as adults. Some hid their previous affiliation. At his death in 1942, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON>'s father was given funeral rites as though he remained a <ORGANIZATION>Jehovah</ORGANIZATION>'s Witness. <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON>'s mother continued as an active <PERSON>Jehovah</PERSON>'s Witness until her death. Despite their differences in religious beliefs, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> enjoyed a close relationship with his mother. 

<PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> was baptized, confirmed, and became a communicant in the Presbyterian Church in a single ceremony on February 1, 1953, just 12 days after his first inauguration. He is the only president known to have undertaken these rites while in office. <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> was instrumental in the addition of the words "under God" to the Pledge of Allegiance in 1954, and the 1956 adoption of "In God We Trust" as the motto of the <LOCATION>US</LOCATION>, and its 1957 introduction on paper currency. In his retirement years, he was a member of the <ORGANIZATION>Gettysburg Presbyterian Church</ORGANIZATION>. The chapel at his presidential library is intentionally inter-denominational.

He questioned <PERSON>Billy Graham</PERSON> about how people can be certain they are going to Heaven after death.

<PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> was sworn into office with his personal West Point Bible, open to Psalm 33:12, at both his 1953 and 1957 inaugural ceremonies. Additionally for 1953, he included the Bible that <PERSON>George Washington</PERSON> had used in 1789 (belonging to <ORGANIZATION>St. John's Masonic Lodge</ORGANIZATION> No. 1), opened to II Chronicles 7:14.

<PERSON>Dwight D. Eisenhower</PERSON> attended <ORGANIZATION>Abilene High School</ORGANIZATION> in <LOCATION>Abilene</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Kansas</LOCATION> and graduated with the class of 1909. He then took a job as a night foreman at the Belle Springs Creamery.

After <PERSON>Dwight</PERSON> worked for two years to support his brother <PERSON>Edgar</PERSON>'s college education, a friend urged him to apply to the <ORGANIZATION>Naval Academy</ORGANIZATION>. Though <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> passed the entrance exam, he was beyond the age of eligibility for admission to the <ORGANIZATION>Naval Academy</ORGANIZATION>.

<LOCATION>Kansas</LOCATION> Senator <PERSON>Joseph L. Bristow</PERSON> recommended <PERSON>Dwight</PERSON> for an appointment to the <ORGANIZATION>Military Academy</ORGANIZATION> in 1911, which he received. <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> graduated in the upper half of the class of 1915.

<PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> long had aspirations of playing professional baseball. He is quoted as saying:

At <LOCATION>West Point</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> tried out for the baseball team but did not make it. He would later be quoted as saying that "Not making the baseball team at <LOCATION>West Point</LOCATION> was one of the greatest disappointments of my life, maybe my greatest." But <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> did make the football team. He started as a varsity running back and linebacker in 1912. In a bit of a fabled match-up, he even tackled the legendary <PERSON>Jim Thorpe</PERSON> in a 1912 game. The next week however, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> would hurt his knee after being tackled around the ankles, which he would soon worsen and permanently damage on horseback and in the boxing ring. He would later serve as junior varsity football coach and yell leader.

Controversy persists over whether <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> played minor league (semi-professional) baseball for <LOCATION>Junction City</LOCATION> in the <ORGANIZATION>Central Kansas League</ORGANIZATION> the year before he attended <LOCATION>West Point</LOCATION> and played amateur football there.

In 1916, while stationed at <LOCATION>Fort Sam Houston</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> was football coach for <ORGANIZATION>St. Louis College</ORGANIZATION>, now <ORGANIZATION>St. Mary's University</ORGANIZATION>.

<PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> enrolled at the <ORGANIZATION>United States Military Academy</ORGANIZATION> at <LOCATION>West Point</LOCATION> in June 1911. His parents were against militarism, but did not object to his entering West Point because they supported his education. <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> was a strong athlete and enjoyed notable successes in his competitive endeavors. In 1912, a spectacular <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> touchdown won praise from the sports reporter of the <ORGANIZATION>New York Herald</ORGANIZATION>, and he even managed, with the help of a linebacker teammate, to tackle the legendary <PERSON>Jim Thorpe</PERSON>. In the very next week, however, his promising sports career ended when he incurred a severe knee injury.



<PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> graduated in 1915. He served with the infantry until 1918 at various camps in <LOCATION>Texas</LOCATION> and <LOCATION>Georgia</LOCATION>. During World War I, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> became the #3 leader of the new tank corps and rose to temporary (Bvt.) Lieutenant Colonel in the <ORGANIZATION>National Army</ORGANIZATION>. He spent the war training tank crews in <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION> and never saw combat. After the war, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> reverted to his regular rank of captain (and was promoted to major a few days later) before assuming duties at <LOCATION>Camp Meade</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Maryland</LOCATION>, where he remained until 1922. His interest in tank warfare was strengthened by many conversations with <PERSON>George S. Patton</PERSON> and other senior tank leaders; however their ideas on tank warfare were strongly discouraged by superiors.

<PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> became executive officer to General <PERSON>Fox Conner</PERSON> in the <LOCATION>Panama Canal Zone</LOCATION>, where he served until 1924. Under <PERSON>Conner</PERSON>'s tutelage, he studied military history and theory (including <PERSON>Karl von Clausewitz</PERSON>'s On War), and later cited <PERSON>Conner</PERSON>'s enormous influence on his military thinking. In 192526, he attended the Command and <ORGANIZATION>General Staff College</ORGANIZATION> at <LOCATION>Fort Leavenworth</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Kansas</LOCATION>, and then served as a battalion commander at <LOCATION>Fort Benning</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Georgia</LOCATION> until 1927.



During the late 1920s and early 1930s <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON>'s career in the peacetime Army stagnated; many of his friends resigned for high paying business jobs. He was assigned to the <ORGANIZATION>American Battle Monuments Commission</ORGANIZATION>, directed by General <PERSON>John J. Pershing</PERSON>, then to the <ORGANIZATION>Army War College</ORGANIZATION>, and then served as executive officer to General <PERSON>George V. Mosely</PERSON>, Assistant Secretary of War, from 1929 to 1933. He then served as chief military aide to <ORGANIZATION>General Douglas MacArthur</ORGANIZATION>, <ORGANIZATION>Army Chief of Staff</ORGANIZATION>, until 1935, when he accompanied <ORGANIZATION>MacArthur</ORGANIZATION> to the <LOCATION>Philippines</LOCATION>, where he served as assistant military adviser to the <LOCATION>Philippine</LOCATION> government. It is sometimes said that this assignment provided valuable preparation for handling the challenging personalities of <PERSON>Winston Churchill</PERSON>, <PERSON>George S. Patton</PERSON> and <PERSON>Bernard Law Montgomery</PERSON> during World War II. <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> was promoted to lieutenant colonel (in a non-brevet status) in 1936 after sixteen years as a major. He also learned to fly, although he was never rated as a military pilot. He made a solo flight over the <LOCATION>Philippines</LOCATION> in 1937.

<PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> returned to the <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> in 1939 and held a series of staff positions in <LOCATION>Washington</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>D.C.</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>California</LOCATION> and <LOCATION>Texas</LOCATION>. In June 1941, he was appointed Chief of Staff to General <PERSON>Walter Krueger</PERSON>, Commander of the <ORGANIZATION>3rd Army</ORGANIZATION>, at <LOCATION>Fort Sam Houston</LOCATION> in <LOCATION>San Antonio</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Texas</LOCATION>. He was promoted to brigadier general on October 3, 1941. Although his administrative abilities had been noticed, on the eve of the <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> entry into World War II he had never held an active command and was far from being considered as a potential commander of major operations.



After the Japanese attack on <LOCATION>Pearl Harbor</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> was assigned to the General Staff in <LOCATION>Washington</LOCATION>, where he served until June 1942 with responsibility for creating the major war plans to defeat <LOCATION>Japan</LOCATION> and <LOCATION>Germany</LOCATION>. He was appointed Deputy Chief in charge of <LOCATION>Pacific</LOCATION> Defenses under the Chief of War Plans Division, General <PERSON>Leonard T. Gerow</PERSON>, and then succeeded <PERSON>Gerow</PERSON> as Chief of the War Plans Division. Then he was appointed Assistant Chief of Staff in charge of Operations Division under Chief of Staff General <PERSON>George C. Marshall</PERSON>. It was his close association with <PERSON>Marshall</PERSON> that finally brought <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> to senior command positions. <PERSON>Marshall</PERSON> recognized his great organizational and administrative abilities.

In 1942, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> was appointed Commanding General, European Theater of Operations (ETOUSA) and was based in <LOCATION>London</LOCATION>. In November, he was also appointed Supreme Commander Allied (Expeditionary) Force of the North African Theater of Operations (NATOUSA) through the new operational Headquarters A(E)FHQ. The word "expeditionary" was dropped soon after his appointment for security reasons. In February 1943, his authority was extended as commander of AFHQ across the Mediterranean basin to include the British <ORGANIZATION>8th Army</ORGANIZATION>, commanded by General <PERSON>Bernard Law Montgomery</PERSON>. The 8th Army had advanced across the <LOCATION>Western Desert</LOCATION> from the east and was ready for the start of the <LOCATION>Tunisia</LOCATION> Campaign. <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> gained his fourth star and gave up command of <ORGANIZATION>ETOUSA</ORGANIZATION> to be commander of <ORGANIZATION>NATOUSA</ORGANIZATION>. After the capitulation of Axis forces in <LOCATION>North Africa</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> remained in command of the renamed Mediterranean Theater of Operations (MTO), keeping the operational title and continued in command of <ORGANIZATION>NATOUSA</ORGANIZATION> redesignated MTOUSA. In this position he oversaw the invasion of <LOCATION>Sicily</LOCATION> and the invasion of the Italian mainland.



In December 1943, it was announced that <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> would be Supreme Allied Commander in <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION>. In January 1944, he resumed command of <ORGANIZATION>ETOUSA</ORGANIZATION> and the following month was officially designated as the Supreme Allied Commander of the Allied Expeditionary Force (SHAEF), serving in a dual role until the end of hostilities in <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION> in May 1945. In these positions he was charged with planning and carrying out the Allied assault on the coast of <LOCATION>Normandy</LOCATION> in June 1944 under the code name Operation Overlord, the liberation of western <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION> and the invasion of <LOCATION>Germany</LOCATION>. A month after the Normandy D-Day landings on June 6, 1944, the invasion of southern <LOCATION>France</LOCATION> took place, and control of the forces which took part in the southern invasion passed from the AFHQ to the SHAEF. From then until the end of the War in <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION> on May 8, 1945, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> through SHAEF had supreme command of all operational Allied forces2, and through his command of <LOCATION>ETOUSA</LOCATION>, administrative command of all <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> forces, on the <ORGANIZATION>Western Front</ORGANIZATION> north of the <LOCATION>Alps</LOCATION>.

As recognition of his senior position in the Allied command, on December 20, 1944, he was promoted to General of the <ORGANIZATION>Army</ORGANIZATION> equivalent to the rank of Field Marshal in most European armies. In this and the previous high commands he held, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> showed his great talents for leadership and diplomacy. Although he had never seen action himself, he won the respect of front-line commanders. He dealt skillfully with difficult subordinates such as <PERSON>Omar Bradley</PERSON> and <PERSON>Patton</PERSON>, and allies such as <PERSON>Winston Churchill</PERSON>, Field Marshal <PERSON>Bernard Montgomery</PERSON> and General <PERSON>Charles de Gaulle</PERSON>. He had fundamental disagreements with <PERSON>Churchill</PERSON> and <PERSON>Montgomery</PERSON> over questions of strategy, but these rarely upset his relationships with them. He negotiated with Soviet Marshal <PERSON>Zhukov</PERSON>, and such was the confidence that President <PERSON>Franklin D. Roosevelt</PERSON> had in him, he sometimes worked directly with <PERSON>Stalin</PERSON>, much to the chagrin of the British High Command who disliked being bypassed. During the advance towards <LOCATION>Berlin</LOCATION>, he was notified by General <PERSON>Bradley</PERSON> that Allied forces would suffer an estimated 100,000 casualties before taking the city. The Soviet Army sustained 80,000 casualties during the fighting in and around <LOCATION>Berlin</LOCATION>, the last large number of casualties suffered in the war against Nazism.

It was never certain that Operation Overlord would succeed. The seriousness surrounding the entire decision, including the timing and the location of the <LOCATION>Normandy</LOCATION> invasion, might be summarized by a second shorter speech that <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> wrote in advance, in case he needed it. Long after the successful landings on D-Day and the <ORGANIZATION>BBC</ORGANIZATION> broadcast of <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON>'s brief speech concerning them, the never-used second speech was found in a shirt pocket by an aide. It read:

<PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> served as Chief of Staff of the <ORGANIZATION>U.S. Army</ORGANIZATION> from 194548.  Following the German unconditional surrender on May 8, 1945, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> was appointed Military Governor of the <ORGANIZATION>U.S. Occupation Zone</ORGANIZATION>, based in <LOCATION>Frankfurt</LOCATION> am Main. <LOCATION>Germany</LOCATION> was divided into four Occupation Zones, one each for the <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Britain</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>France</LOCATION>, and the <LOCATION>Soviet Union</LOCATION>. Upon full discovery of the death camps that were part of the Final Solution (Holocaust), he ordered camera crews to comprehensively document evidence of the atrocity for use in the war crimes tribunals. He made the decision to reclassify German prisoners of war (POWs) in <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> custody as Disarmed Enemy Forces (DEFs), thus depriving them of the protection of the <LOCATION>Geneva</LOCATION> convention. As DEFs, their food rations could be lowered and they could be compelled to serve as unfree labor (see <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> and German POWs). <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> was an early supporter of the Morgenthau Plan to permanently remove <LOCATION>Germany</LOCATION>'s industrial capacity to wage future wars. In November 1945 he approved the distribution of 1000 free copies of <PERSON>Morgenthau</PERSON>'s book <LOCATION>Germany</LOCATION> is Our Problem, which promoted and described the plan in detail, to American military officials in occupied <LOCATION>Germany</LOCATION>. Historian <PERSON>Stephen Ambrose</PERSON> draws the conclusion that, despite <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON>'s later claims the act was not an endorsement of the <PERSON>Morgenthau</PERSON> plan, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> both approved of the plan and had previously given <PERSON>Morgenthau</PERSON> at least some of his ideas about how <LOCATION>Germany</LOCATION> should be treated. He also incorporated officials from <PERSON>Morgenthau</PERSON>'s <ORGANIZATION>Treasury</ORGANIZATION> into the army of occupation. These were commonly called "Morgenthau boys" for their zeal in interpreting the occupation directive JCS 1067, which had been heavily influenced by <PERSON>Morgenthau</PERSON> and his plan, as strictly as possible.

In 1948, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> became President of <ORGANIZATION>Columbia University</ORGANIZATION>. In December 1950, he took leave from the university when he became the Supreme Commander of the <ORGANIZATION>North Atlantic Treaty Organization</ORGANIZATION> (<ORGANIZATION>NATO</ORGANIZATION>), and given operational command of <ORGANIZATION>NATO</ORGANIZATION> forces in <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION>. <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> retired from active service on May 31, 1952, and resumed the university presidency, which he held until January 1953.

1948 also was the year that <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON>'s memoir, Crusade in <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION>, was published. It is widely regarded as one of the finest <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> military memoirs.

After his many wartime successes, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> was a great hero in the <LOCATION>U.S</LOCATION>. He was unusual for a military hero as he never saw the front line in his life. The nearest he came to being under enemy fire was in 1944 when a German fighter strafed the ground while he was inspecting troops in <LOCATION>Normandy</LOCATION>. <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> dove for cover like everyone else and after the plane flew off, a British brigadier helped him up and seemed very relieved he was not hurt. When <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> thanked him for his solicitude, the brigadier deflated him by explaining "my concern was that you should not be injured in my sector." 

Not long after his return in 1952, a "Draft Eisenhower" movement in the Republican party persuaded him to declare his candidacy in the 1952 presidential election to counter the candidacy of non-inteventionists Senator <PERSON>Robert Taft</PERSON>. (<PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> had been courted by both parties in 1948 and had declined to run then.) <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> defeated <PERSON>Taft</PERSON> for the nomination but came to an agreement that <PERSON>Taft</PERSON> would stay out of foreign affairs while <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> followed a conservative domestic policy. <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON>'s campaign was a crusade against the <PERSON>Truman</PERSON> administration's policies regarding "<LOCATION>Korea</LOCATION>, Communism and Corruption" and was also noted for the simple but effective phrase "I Like Ike." <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> promised to go to <LOCATION>Korea</LOCATION> himself and end the war and maintain both a strong <ORGANIZATION>NATO</ORGANIZATION> abroad against Communism and a corruption-free frugal administration at home. He and his running mate <PERSON>Richard Nixon</PERSON>, whose daughter later married <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON>'s grandson <PERSON>David</PERSON>, defeated Democrats <PERSON>Adlai Stevenson</PERSON> and <PERSON>John Sparkman</PERSON> in a landslide, marking the first Republican return to the White House in 20 years. <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> was the only general to serve as President in the 20th century, and the most recent President to have never held elected office prior to the Presidency. (The other Presidents not to have sought prior elected office were <PERSON>Zachary Taylor</PERSON>, <PERSON>Ulysses S. Grant</PERSON>, <PERSON>William Taft</PERSON>, and <PERSON>Herbert Hoover</PERSON>.)



Throughout his presidency, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> preached a doctrine of dynamic conservatism. He continued all the major New Deal programs still in operation, especially Social Security. He expanded its programs and rolled them into a new cabinet-level agency, the <ORGANIZATION>Department of Health, Education and Welfare</ORGANIZATION>, while extending benefits to an additional ten million workers. His cabinet, consisting of several corporate executives and one labor leader, was dubbed by one journalist, "Eight millionaires and a plumber."

<PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> won his second term in 1956 with 457 of 531 votes in the <ORGANIZATION>Electoral College</ORGANIZATION>, and 57.6% of the popular vote.



One of <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON>'s enduring achievements was championing and signing the bill that authorized the Interstate Highway System in 1956. He justified the project through the Federal Aid Highway Act of 1956 as essential to American security during the Cold War. It was believed that large cities would be targets in a possible future war, and the highways were designed to evacuate them and allow the military to move in.

<PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON>'s goal to create improved highways was influenced by his involvement in the <ORGANIZATION>U.S. Army</ORGANIZATION>'s 1919 Transcontinental Motor Convoy. He was assigned as an observer for the mission, which involved sending a convoy of <ORGANIZATION>U.S. Army</ORGANIZATION> vehicles coast to coast. His subsequent experience with German autobahns during World War II convinced him of the benefits of an Interstate Highway System. Noticing the improved ability to move logistics throughout the country, he thought an Interstate Highway System in the <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> would not only be beneficial for military operations, but be the building block for continued economic growth.

After the Suez Crisis, the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> became the protector of most Western interests in the <LOCATION>Middle East</LOCATION>. As a result, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> proclaimed the "Eisenhower Doctrine" in January 1957. In relation to the <LOCATION>Middle East</LOCATION>, the <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> would be "prepared to use armed force...[to counter] aggression from any country controlled by international communism." On July 15, 1958, he sent just under 15,000 soldiers to <LOCATION>Lebanon</LOCATION> (a combined force of <ORGANIZATION>Army</ORGANIZATION> and <ORGANIZATION>Marine Corps</ORGANIZATION>) as part of Operation Blue Bat, a non-combat peace keeping mission to stabilize the pro-Western government. They left the following October.

In addition, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> explored the option of supporting the French colonial forces in <LOCATION>Vietnam</LOCATION> who were fighting an independence insurrection there. However, Chief of Staff <PERSON>Matthew Ridgway</PERSON> dissuaded the President from intervening by presenting a comprehensive estimate of the massive military deployment that would be necessary.

<PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> supported the 1954 <PERSON>Brown</PERSON> v. <ORGANIZATION>Board of Education of Topeka U.S. Supreme Court</ORGANIZATION> decision, in which segregated ("separate but equal") schools were ruled to be unconstitutional. The very next day he told <ORGANIZATION>District of Columbia</ORGANIZATION> officials to make <LOCATION>Washington</LOCATION> a model for the rest of the country in integrating black and white public school children. He proposed to <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> the Civil Rights Acts of 1957 and 1960 and signed those acts into law. Although both Acts were weaker than subsequent civil rights legislation, they constituted the first significant civil rights acts since the 1870s.The "Little Rock Nine" incident of 1957 involved the refusal by <LOCATION>Arkansas</LOCATION> to honor a Federal court order to integrate the schools. Under , <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> placed the <ORGANIZATION>Arkansas National Guard</ORGANIZATION> under Federal control and sent <ORGANIZATION>Army</ORGANIZATION> troops to escort nine black students into an all-white public school. The integration did not occur without violence. <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> and <LOCATION>Arkansas</LOCATION> governor <PERSON>Orval Faubus</PERSON> engaged in tense arguments.

In 1961, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> became the first <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> president to be "constitutionally forced" from office, having served the maximum two terms allowed by the 22nd Amendment to the <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> Constitution. The amendment was ratified in 1951, during <PERSON>Harry S. Truman</PERSON>'s term, but it stipulated that <PERSON>Truman</PERSON> would not be affected by the amendment.

<PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> was also the first outgoing President to come under the protection of the Former Presidents Act (two then living former Presidents, <PERSON>Herbert Hoover</PERSON> and <PERSON>Harry S. Truman</PERSON>, left office before Act was passed). Under the act, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> was entitled to receive a lifetime pension, state-provided staff and a Secret Service detail. 

In the 1960 election to choose his successor, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> endorsed his own Vice President, Republican <PERSON>Richard Nixon</PERSON> against Democrat <PERSON>John F. Kennedy</PERSON>. However, he only campaigned for <PERSON>Nixon</PERSON> in the campaign's final days and even did <PERSON>Nixon</PERSON> some harm. When asked by reporters at the end of a televised press conference to list one of <PERSON>Nixon</PERSON>'s policy ideas he had adopted, he replied, "If you give me a week, I might think of one." <PERSON>Kennedy</PERSON>'s campaign used the quote in one of its campaign commercials. <PERSON>Nixon</PERSON> lost narrowly to <PERSON>Kennedy</PERSON>.

On January 17, 1961, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> gave his final televised Address to the Nation from the Oval Office. In his farewell speech to the nation, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> raised the issue of the Cold War and role of the <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> armed forces. He described the Cold War saying: "We face a hostile ideology global in scope, atheistic in character, ruthless in purpose and insidious in method..." and warned about what he saw as unjustified government spending proposals and continued with a warning that "we must guard against the acquisition of unwarranted influence, whether sought or unsought, by the military-industrial complex... Only an alert and knowledgeable citizenry can compel the proper meshing of the huge industrial and military machinery of defense with our peaceful methods and goals, so that security and liberty may prosper together."

Because of legal issues related to holding a military rank while in a civilian office, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> resigned his permanent commission as General of the Army before entering the office of President of the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION>. Upon completion of his Presidential term, his commission on the retired list was reactivated and <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> again was commissioned a five-star general in the <ORGANIZATION>United States Army</ORGANIZATION>.

<PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> retired to the place where he and <PERSON>Mamie</PERSON> had spent much of their post-war time, a working farm adjacent to the battlefield at <LOCATION>Gettysburg</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION>. In 1967, the <ORGANIZATION>Eisenhowers</ORGANIZATION> donated the farm to the <ORGANIZATION>National Park Service</ORGANIZATION> and since 1980 it has been open to the public as the Eisenhower National Historic Site. In retirement, he did not completely retreat from political life; he spoke at the 1964 Republican National Convention and appeared with <PERSON>Barry Goldwater</PERSON> in a <ORGANIZATION>Republican</ORGANIZATION> campaign commercial from <LOCATION>Gettysburg</LOCATION>.

<PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> died of congestive heart failure on March 28, 1969 at <ORGANIZATION>Walter Reed Army Hospital</ORGANIZATION> in <LOCATION>Washington D.C</LOCATION>. The following day his body was moved to the <ORGANIZATION>Washington National Cathedral</ORGANIZATION>'s <LOCATION>Bethlehem Chapel</LOCATION> where he lay in repose for twenty-eight hours. On March 30, his body was brought by caisson to the <LOCATION>United States Capitol</LOCATION> where he lay in state in the Capitol Rotunda. On March 31, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON>'s body was returned to the <ORGANIZATION>National Cathedral</ORGANIZATION> where he was given an <ORGANIZATION>Episcopal Church</ORGANIZATION> funeral service. That evening, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON>'s body was placed onto a train en route to <LOCATION>Abilene</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Kansas</LOCATION>. His body arrived on April 2, and was interred later that day in a small chapel on the grounds of the Eisenhower Presidential Library. <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> is buried alongside his son <PERSON>Doud</PERSON> who died at age 3 in 1921, and his wife, <PERSON>Mamie</PERSON>, who died in 1979.

After <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> left office, his reputation declined and he was seen as having been a "do-nothing" President. This was partly because of the contrast between <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> and his young activist successor, <PERSON>John F. Kennedy</PERSON>, but also because of his reluctance to support the civil rights movement to the degree that activists would have preferred, his handling of the 1960 U-2 incident, the <LOCATION>Soviet Union</LOCATION>'s perceived leadership in the Arms race and the Space race, and his failure publicly to oppose McCarthyism, in particular his failure to defend <PERSON>George Marshall</PERSON> from attacks by <PERSON>Joseph McCarthy</PERSON>, though he privately deplored <PERSON>McCarthy</PERSON>'s tactics and claims. Such omissions were held against him during the liberal climate of the 1960s and 1970s. Since that time, however, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON>'s reputation has risen. In recent surveys of historians, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> often is ranked in the top 10 among all <LOCATION>US</LOCATION> Presidents.

<PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON>'s picture was on the dollar coin from 1971 to 1978. Nearly 700 million of the copper-nickel clad coins were minted for general circulation, and far smaller numbers of uncirculated and proof issues (in both copper-nickel and 40% silver varieties) were produced for collectors. He reappeared on a commemorative silver dollar issued in 1990, celebrating the 100th anniversary of his birth, which with a double image of him showed his two roles, as both a soldier and a statesman. As part of the Presidential $1 Coin Program, <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON> will be featured on a gold-colored dollar coin in 2015.

He is remembered for ending the Korean War. USS ''<PERSON>Dwight D. Eisenhower</PERSON>'', the second ''Nimitz''-class supercarrier, was named in his honor.

The Eisenhower Expressway (Interstate 290), a  long expressway in the <LOCATION>Chicago</LOCATION> area, was renamed after him.

The British A4 class steam locomotive No. 4496 (renumbered 60008) Golden Shuttle was renamed <PERSON>Dwight D. Eisenhower</PERSON> in 1946. It is preserved at the <ORGANIZATION>National Railroad Museum</ORGANIZATION> in <LOCATION>Green Bay</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Wisconsin</LOCATION>.

<ORGANIZATION>Eisenhower College</ORGANIZATION> was a small, liberal arts college chartered in <LOCATION>Seneca Falls</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>New York</LOCATION> in 1965, with classes beginning in 1968. Financial problems forced the school to fall under the management of the <ORGANIZATION>Rochester Institute of Technology</ORGANIZATION> in 1979. Its last class graduated in 1982.

The <ORGANIZATION>Dwight D. Eisenhower Army Medical Center</ORGANIZATION>, located at <LOCATION>Fort Gordon</LOCATION> near <LOCATION>Augusta</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Georgia</LOCATION>, was named in his honor.

In February 1971, <PERSON>Dwight D. Eisenhower</PERSON> School of <LOCATION>Freehold Township</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>New Jersey</LOCATION> was officially opened.

The <PERSON>Eisenhower Tunnel</PERSON> was completed in 1979; it conveys westbound traffic on I-70 through the Continental Divide,  west of <LOCATION>Denver</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Colorado</LOCATION>.

In 1983, The <ORGANIZATION>Eisenhower Institute</ORGANIZATION> was founded in <LOCATION>Washington</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>D.C.</LOCATION>, as a policy institute to advance <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON>'s intellectual and leadership legacies.

In 1999, the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> created the <ORGANIZATION>Dwight D. Eisenhower Memorial Commission</ORGANIZATION>, which is in the planning stages of creating an enduring national memorial in <LOCATION>Washington</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>D.C.</LOCATION>, across the street from the <ORGANIZATION>National Air</ORGANIZATION> and Space Museum on the National Mall.

This building is part of the White House Complex, west of the <LOCATION>West Wing</LOCATION>. It currently houses a number of executive offices, including ones for the Vice President and his or her spouse.

Many public high schools and middle schools in the <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> are named after <PERSON>Eisenhower</PERSON>.

<TITLE><PERSON>Christopher Columbus</PERSON></TITLE>

<PERSON>His</PERSON> initial 1492 voyage came at a critical time of growing national imperialism and economic competition between developing nation states seeking wealth from the establishment of trade routes and colonies. In this sociopolitical climate, <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION>'s far-fetched scheme won the attention of Queen <PERSON>Isabella</PERSON> of <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION>. Severely underestimating the circumference of the <LOCATION>Earth</LOCATION>, he hypothesized that a westward route from <ORGANIZATION>Iberia</ORGANIZATION> to the Indies would be shorter and more direct than the overland trade route through Arabia. If true, this would allow <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION> entry into the lucrative spice tradeheretofore commanded by the Arabs and Italians. Following his plotted course, he instead landed within the <LOCATION>Bahamas Archipelago</LOCATION> at a locale he named <LOCATION>San Salvador</LOCATION>. Mistaking the North-American island for the East-Asian mainland, he referred to its inhabitants as "Indians".

Academic consensus is that <PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> was born in <LOCATION>Genoa</LOCATION>, though there are other theories. The name <PERSON>Christopher Columbus</PERSON> is the Anglicisation of the Latin Christophorus Columbus. The name is rendered in modern Italian as <PERSON>Cristoforo Colombo</PERSON>, in <LOCATION>Portuguese</LOCATION> as Cristvo <LOCATION>Colombo</LOCATION> (formerly Christovam Colom), and in Spanish as <PERSON>Cristbal Coln</PERSON>.

The anniversary of <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION>' 1492 landing in the Americas (Columbus Day) is observed throughout the <LOCATION>Americas</LOCATION> and in <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION> on October 12.

At age 14, he attended Prince <PERSON>Henry</PERSON>'s school of navigation in <LOCATION>Sagres</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Portugal</LOCATION>. He spoke a Genoese dialect. In one of his writings, <ORGANIZATION>Columbus</ORGANIZATION> claims to have gone to the sea at the age of 10. In 1470 the <ORGANIZATION>Columbus Family</ORGANIZATION> moved to <LOCATION>Savona</LOCATION>, where <PERSON>Domenico</PERSON> took over a tavern. In the same year, <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION> was on a Genoese ship hired in the service of Ren I of <LOCATION>Anjou</LOCATION> to support his attempt to conquer the Kingdom of <LOCATION>Naples</LOCATION>. 

In 1473 <PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> began his apprenticeship as business agent for the important Centurione, <PERSON>Di Negro</PERSON> and <PERSON>Spinola</PERSON> families of <LOCATION>Genoa</LOCATION>. Later he allegedly made a trip to Chios, a Genoese colony in the <LOCATION>Aegean Sea</LOCATION>. In May 1476, he took part in an armed convoy sent by <ORGANIZATION>Genoa</ORGANIZATION> to carry a valuable cargo to northern <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION>. He docked in <LOCATION>Bristol</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Galway</LOCATION>, in <LOCATION>Ireland</LOCATION> and was possibly in <LOCATION>Iceland</LOCATION> in 1477. In 1479 Columbus reached his brother <PERSON>Bartolomeo</PERSON> in <LOCATION>Lisbon</LOCATION>, keeping on trading for the Centurione family. He married <PERSON>Filipa Moniz Perestrello</PERSON>, daughter of the <ORGANIZATION>Porto Santo</ORGANIZATION> governor, the <LOCATION>Portuguese</LOCATION> nobleman of Genoese origin <PERSON>Bartolomeu Perestrello</PERSON>. In 1479 or 1480, his son, <PERSON>Diego</PERSON>, was born.



<LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION> had long enjoyed a safe land passage to <LOCATION>China</LOCATION> and <LOCATION>India</LOCATION> sources of valued goods such as silk, spices, and opiates under the hegemony of the Mongol Empire (the <LOCATION>Pax Mongolica</LOCATION>, or Mongol peace). With the Fall of Constantinople to the Ottoman Turks in 1453, the land route to <LOCATION>Asia</LOCATION> became more difficult. The Columbus brothers had a different idea. By the 1480s, they had developed a plan to travel to the Indies, then construed roughly as all of south and east <LOCATION>Asia</LOCATION>, by sailing directly west across the "<LOCATION>Ocean Sea</LOCATION>," i.e., the <LOCATION>Atlantic</LOCATION>.

<PERSON>Washington Irving</PERSON>'s 1828 biography of <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION> popularized the idea that <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION> had difficulty obtaining support for his plan because Europeans thought the Earth was flat. In fact, the primitive maritime navigation of the time relied on the stars and the curvature of the spherical Earth. The knowledge that the Earth was spherical was widespread and the means of calculating its diameter using an astrolabe was known to both scholars and navigators. The spherical view of the earth had been the general opinion of Ancient Greek science, and continued as the standard view in the Middle Ages (for example of Bede in The Reckoning of Time). In fact the Earth had generally been believed to be spherical since the 4th century BCE by most scholars and almost all navigators, and <PERSON>Eratosthenes</PERSON> had measured the diameter of the Earth with good precision in the second century BC. Columbus put forth (incorrect) arguments based on a significantly smaller diameter for the <LOCATION>Earth</LOCATION>, claiming that <LOCATION>Asia</LOCATION> could be easily reached by sailing west across the <LOCATION>Atlantic</LOCATION>. Most scholars accepted <PERSON>Ptolemy</PERSON>'s correct assessment that the terrestrial landmass (for Europeans of the time, comprising <ORGANIZATION>Eurasia</ORGANIZATION> and <LOCATION>Africa</LOCATION>) occupied 180 degrees of the terrestrial sphere, and correctly dismissed <ORGANIZATION>Columbus</ORGANIZATION>'s claim that the <LOCATION>Earth</LOCATION> was much smaller, and that <LOCATION>Asia</LOCATION> was only a few thousand nautical miles to the west of <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION>. Columbus' error was put down to his lack of experience in navigation at sea.

Columbus believed the (incorrect) calculations of <ORGANIZATION>Marinus of Tyre</ORGANIZATION>, putting the landmass at 225 degrees, leaving only 135 degrees of water. Moreover, <PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> believed that one degree represented a shorter distance on the earth's surface than was actually the case. Finally, he read maps as if the distances were calculated in Italian miles (1,238 meters). Accepting the length of a degree to be 56 miles, from the writings of <LOCATION>Alfraganus</LOCATION>, he therefore calculated the circumference of the <LOCATION>Earth</LOCATION> as 25,255 kilometers at most, and the distance from the <LOCATION>Canary Islands</LOCATION> to <LOCATION>Japan</LOCATION> as 3,000 Italian miles (3,700 km, or 2,300 statute miles) <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION> did not realize <PERSON>Al-Farghani</PERSON> used the much longer Arabic mile (about 1,830 m).

The main problem was that experts did not accept his estimate. The true circumference of the <LOCATION>Earth</LOCATION> is about 40,000 km (25,000 sm), a figure established by Eratosthenes in the second century BC, and the distance from the <LOCATION>Canary Islands</LOCATION> to <LOCATION>Japan</LOCATION> 19,600 km (12,200 sm). No ship that was readily available in the 15th century could carry enough food and fresh water for such a journey. Most European sailors and navigators concluded, likely correctly, that sailors undertaking a westward voyage from <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION> to <LOCATION>Asia</LOCATION> non-stop would die of thirst or starvation long before reaching their destination. <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION>, however, having completed an expensive war, was desperate for a competitive edge over other European countries in trade with the <LOCATION>East Indies</LOCATION>. <PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> promised such an advantage.

While Columbus' calculations underestimated the circumference of the <LOCATION>Earth</LOCATION> and the distance from the <LOCATION>Canary Islands</LOCATION> to <LOCATION>Japan</LOCATION> by the standards of his peers as well as in fact, almost all Europeans held the mistaken opinion that the aquatic expanse between <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION> and <LOCATION>Asia</LOCATION> was uninterrupted. As the 16th century developed it was the route to <LOCATION>America</LOCATION>, rather than to <LOCATION>Japan</LOCATION>, that gave <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION> a competitive edge in developing an overseas empire.

There was a further element of key importance in the plans of <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION>, a closely-held fact discovered by or otherwise learned by Columbus: the trade winds. A brisk wind from the east, commonly called an "easterly", propelled <LOCATION>Santa Maria</LOCATION>, <ORGANIZATION>La Nina, & La Pinta</ORGANIZATION> for 5 weeks from the Canaries. To return to <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION> eastward against this prevailing wind would have required several months of an arduous sailing technique, called beating, during which food & drinkable water would have been utterly exhausted. Columbus returned home by following prevailing winds northeastward from the southern zone of the <LOCATION>North Atlantic</LOCATION> to the middle latitudes of the <LOCATION>North Atlantic</LOCATION>, where prevailing winds are eastward (westerly) to the coastlines of <LOCATION>Western Europe</LOCATION>, where the winds curve southward towards the <LOCATION>Iberian Peninsula</LOCATION>. In fact, <PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> was wrong about degrees of longitude to be traversed and wrong about distance per degree, but he was right about a more vital fact: how to use the <LOCATION>North Atlantic</LOCATION>'s great circular wind pattern, clockwise in direction, to get home. 

Some mean that his confidence in that land was within reach, was based on his travel in 1477 (1476-1477), which may have taken him to <LOCATION>Baffin Island</LOCATION> at 73 degrees north (in German). On this tour he could also get information from norse sailors and inuit kayakers trading on the long coast extending from north to south at about this longitude, almost to <LOCATION>Africa</LOCATION>. A History of <LOCATION>Norway</LOCATION>..., 12th century p. 3, lines 2-5: This country, which was discovered, settled and confirmed in the universal faith by Icelanders, is the western boundary of <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION>, almost touching the African islands where the waters of ocean flood in.

In 1485, <ORGANIZATION>Columbus</ORGANIZATION> presented his plans to <PERSON>John II</PERSON>, King of <LOCATION>Portugal</LOCATION>. He proposed the king equip three sturdy ships and grant Columbus one year's time to sail out into the <LOCATION>Atlantic</LOCATION>, search for a western route to Orient, and then return home. Columbus also requested he be made "Great Admiral of the Ocean", created governor of any and all lands he discovered, and given one-tenth of all revenue from those lands discovered. The king submitted the proposal to his experts, who rejected it. It was their considered opinion that <PERSON>Columbus</PERSON>' proposed route of  was, in fact, far too short.



In 1488 <PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> appealed to the court of <LOCATION>Portugal</LOCATION> once again, and once again <PERSON>John</PERSON> invited him to an audience. It too was to come to nothing, for not long afterwards came the arrival of <LOCATION>Portugal</LOCATION>'s native son <PERSON>Bartholomeu Dias</PERSON> from a successful rounding of the southern tip of <LOCATION>Africa</LOCATION>. <LOCATION>Portugal</LOCATION> was no longer interested in trailblazing a western route to the East. 

Columbus travelled from <LOCATION>Portugal</LOCATION> once more to both <ORGANIZATION>Genoa</ORGANIZATION> and <LOCATION>Venice</LOCATION>, but he received encouragement from neither. Previously he had his brother sound out <PERSON>Henry VII</PERSON> of <LOCATION>England</LOCATION>, to see if the English monarch might not be more amenable to Columbus' proposal. After much carefully considered hesitation <PERSON>Henry</PERSON>'s invitation came, too late. <PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> had already committed himself to <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION>.



He had sought an audience from the monarchs <PERSON>Ferdinand II</PERSON> of <PERSON>Aragon</PERSON> and <PERSON>Isabella I</PERSON> of <LOCATION>Castile</LOCATION>, who had united the largest kingdoms of <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION> by marrying, and were ruling together. On May 1, 1486, permission having been granted, <PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> laid his plans before <PERSON>Queen Isabella</PERSON>, who, in turn, referred it to a committee. After the passing of much time, these savants of <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION>, like their counterparts in <LOCATION>Portugal</LOCATION>, reported back that <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION> had judged the distance to <LOCATION>Asia</LOCATION> much too short. They pronounced the idea impractical, and advised their Royal Highnesses to pass on the proposed venture.

However, to keep <PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> from taking his ideas elsewhere, and perhaps to keep their options open, the King and Queen of <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION> gave him an annual annuity of 12,000 maravedis ($840) and in 1489 furnished him with a letter ordering all Spanish cities and towns to provide him food and lodging at no cost.

After continually lobbying at the Spanish court and two years of negotiations, he finally had success in 1492. <PERSON>Ferdinand</PERSON> and <PERSON>Isabella</PERSON> had just conquered <PERSON>Granada</PERSON>, the last Muslim stronghold on the <LOCATION>Iberian</LOCATION> peninsula, and they received <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION> in <LOCATION>Crdoba</LOCATION>, in the Alczar castle. <PERSON>Isabella</PERSON> turned <ORGANIZATION>Columbus</ORGANIZATION> down on the advice of her confessor, and he was leaving town in despair, when <PERSON>Ferdinand</PERSON> intervened. <PERSON>Isabella</PERSON> then sent a royal guard to fetch him and <PERSON>Ferdinand</PERSON> later rightfully claimed credit for being "the principal cause why those islands were discovered". King <PERSON>Ferdinand</PERSON> is referred to as "losing his patience" in this issue, but this cannot be proven.

About half of the financing was to come from private Italian investors, whom <ORGANIZATION>Columbus</ORGANIZATION> had already lined up. Financially broke after the <PERSON>Granada</PERSON> campaign, the monarchs left it to the royal treasurer to shift funds among various royal accounts on behalf of the enterprise. Columbus was to be made "Admiral of the Seas" and would receive a portion of all profits. The terms were unusually generous, but as his own son later wrote, the monarchs did not really expect him to return.

According to the contract that <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION> made with King <PERSON>Ferdinand</PERSON> and <PERSON>Queen Isabella</PERSON>, if <ORGANIZATION>Columbus</ORGANIZATION> discovered any new islands or mainland, he would receive many high rewards. In terms of power, he would be given the rank of Admiral of the <LOCATION>Ocean Sea</LOCATION> (<LOCATION>Atlantic Ocean</LOCATION>) and appointed <PERSON>Viceroy</PERSON> and Governor of all the new lands. He had the right to nominate three persons, from whom the sovereigns would choose one, for any office in the new lands. He would be entitled to 10 percent of all the revenues from the new lands in perpetuity; this part was denied to him in the contract, although it was one of his demands.Finally, he would also have the option of buying one-eighth interest in any commercial venture with the new lands and receive one-eighth of the profits.

<PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> was later arrested in 1500 and supplanted from these posts. After his death, <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION>'s sons, <PERSON>Diego</PERSON> and <PERSON>Fernando</PERSON>, took legal action to enforce their father's contract. Many of the smears against <ORGANIZATION>Columbus</ORGANIZATION> were initiated by the Spanish crown during these lengthy court cases, known as the pleitos colombinos. The family had some success in their first litigation, as a judgment of 1511 confirmed <PERSON>Diego</PERSON>'s position as <PERSON>Viceroy</PERSON>, but reduced his powers. <PERSON>Diego</PERSON> resumed litigation in 1512, which lasted until 1536, and further disputes continued until 1790.

On the evening of August 3, 1492, <ORGANIZATION>Columbus</ORGANIZATION> departed from <LOCATION>Palos de la Frontera</LOCATION> with three ships; one larger carrack, <LOCATION>Santa Mara</LOCATION>, nicknamed Gallega (the Galician), and two smaller caravels, Pinta (the Painted) and <LOCATION>Santa Clara</LOCATION>, nicknamed <PERSON>Nia</PERSON> after her owner <PERSON>Juan Nio</PERSON> of <PERSON>Moguer</PERSON>. They were property of <PERSON>Juan de la Cosa</PERSON> and the <LOCATION>Pinzn</LOCATION> brothers (<ORGANIZATION>Martin Alonzo</ORGANIZATION> and <PERSON>Vicente Yez</PERSON>), but the monarchs forced the Palos inhabitants to contribute to the expedition. Columbus first sailed to the <LOCATION>Canary Islands</LOCATION>, which were owned by <LOCATION>Castile</LOCATION>, where he restocked the provisions and made repairs, and on September 6, he started what turned out to be a five-week voyage across the ocean. 

Land was sighted at 2 a.m. on October 12, 1492, by a sailor named <PERSON>Rodrigo de Triana</PERSON> (also known as <PERSON>Juan Rodrguez Bermejo</PERSON>) aboard <LOCATION>Pinta</LOCATION>. Columbus called the island (in what is now The <LOCATION>Bahamas</LOCATION>) <LOCATION>San Salvador</LOCATION>; the natives called it Guanahani. Exactly which island in the <LOCATION>Bahamas</LOCATION> this corresponds to is an unresolved topic; prime candidates are <PERSON>Samana Cay</PERSON>, Plana Cays, or <LOCATION>San Salvador Island</LOCATION> (named <LOCATION>San Salvador</LOCATION> in 1925 in the belief that it was Columbus's <LOCATION>San Salvador</LOCATION>). The indigenous people he encountered, the <LOCATION>Lucayan</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Tano</PERSON> or Arawak, were peaceful and friendly. From the October 12, 1492 entry in his journal he wrote of them, "Many of the men I have seen have scars on their bodies, and when I made signs to them to find out how this happened, they indicated that people from other nearby islands come to <LOCATION>San Salvador</LOCATION> to capture them; they defend themselves the best they can. I believe that people from the mainland come here to take them as slaves. They ought to make good and skilled servants, for they repeat very quickly whatever we say to them. I think they can very easily be made Christians, for they seem to have no religion. If it pleases our Lord, I will take six of them to Your Highnesses when I depart, in order that they may learn our language." Lacking modern weaponry and even metal-forged swords or pikes, he remarked upon their tactical vulnerability, writing, "I could conquer the whole of them with 50 men, and govern them as I pleased." 

Columbus also explored the northeast coast of <LOCATION>Cuba</LOCATION> (landed on October 28) and the northern coast of <LOCATION>Hispaniola</LOCATION>, by December 5. Here, the <LOCATION>Santa Maria</LOCATION> ran aground on Christmas morning 1492 and had to be abandoned. He was received by the native cacique <PERSON>Guacanagari</PERSON>, who gave him permission to leave some of his men behind. <PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> left 39 men and founded the settlement of <LOCATION>La Navidad</LOCATION> in what is now present-day <LOCATION>Haiti</LOCATION>. Before returning to <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION> also kidnapped some ten to twenty-five natives and took them back with him. Only seven or eight of the native Indians arrived in <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION> alive, but they made quite an impression on <LOCATION>Seville</LOCATION>.

<LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION> headed for <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION>, but another storm forced him into <LOCATION>Lisbon</LOCATION>. He anchored next to the King's harbor patrol ship on March 4, 1493 in <LOCATION>Portugal</LOCATION>. After spending more than one week in <LOCATION>Portugal</LOCATION>, he set sail for <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION>. He reached <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION> on March 15, 1493. Word of his finding new lands rapidly spread throughout <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION>.

There is increasing modern scientific evidence that this voyage also brought syphilis back from the <LOCATION>New World</LOCATION>. Many of the crew members who served on this voyage later joined the army of King <PERSON>Charles VIII</PERSON> in his invasion of <LOCATION>Italy</LOCATION> in 1495 resulting in the spreading of the disease across <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION> and as many as 5 million deaths.



<PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> left <PERSON>Cdiz</PERSON>, <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION>, on September 24, 1493 to find new territories, with 17 ships carrying supplies, and about 1,200 men to colonize the region. On October 13, the ships left the <LOCATION>Canary Islands</LOCATION> as they had on the first voyage, following a more southerly course.

On November 3, 1493, <ORGANIZATION>Columbus</ORGANIZATION> sighted a rugged island that he named <LOCATION>Dominica</LOCATION> (Latin for Sunday); later that day, he landed at Marie-Galante, which he named <PERSON>Santa Maria la Galante</PERSON>. After sailing past <PERSON>Les Saintes</PERSON> (<ORGANIZATION>Los Santos</ORGANIZATION>, The Saints), he arrived at <LOCATION>Guadeloupe</LOCATION> (<LOCATION>Santa Mara de Guadalupe de Extremadura</LOCATION>, after the image of the <PERSON>Virgin Mary</PERSON> venerated at the Spanish monastery of <LOCATION>Villuercas</LOCATION>, in <LOCATION>Guadalupe</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION>), which he explored between November 4 and November 10, 1493.

The exact course of his voyage through the Lesser Antilles is debated, but it seems likely that he turned north, sighting and naming several islands, including <LOCATION>Montserrat</LOCATION> (for <LOCATION>Santa Maria de Montserrate</LOCATION>, after the Blessed Virgin of the Monastery of <LOCATION>Montserrat</LOCATION>, which is located on the Mountain of <LOCATION>Montserrat</LOCATION>, in <LOCATION>Catalonia</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION>), <LOCATION>Antigua</LOCATION> (after a church in <LOCATION>Seville</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION>, called <LOCATION>Santa Maria la Antigua</LOCATION>, meaning "Old St. <PERSON>Mary</PERSON>'s"), Redonda (for <LOCATION>Santa Maria la Redonda</LOCATION>, Spanish for "round", owing to the island's shape), <LOCATION>Nevis</LOCATION> (derived from the Spanish, <PERSON>Nuestra Seora de las Nieves</PERSON>, meaning "Our Lady of the Snows", because <ORGANIZATION>Columbus</ORGANIZATION> thought the clouds over <LOCATION>Nevis Peak</LOCATION> made the island resemble a snow-capped mountain), Saint <PERSON>Kitts</PERSON> (for <PERSON>St. Christopher</PERSON>, patron of sailors and travelers), <LOCATION>Sint Eustatius</LOCATION> (for the early Roman martyr, St. Eustachius), <LOCATION>Saba</LOCATION> (also for St. <PERSON>Christopher</PERSON>?), Saint <PERSON>Martin</PERSON> (<ORGANIZATION>San Martin</ORGANIZATION>), and Saint <PERSON>Croix</PERSON> (from the <LOCATION>Spanish Santa Cruz</LOCATION>, meaning "Holy Cross"). He also sighted the island chain of the <LOCATION>Virgin Islands</LOCATION> (and named them <ORGANIZATION>Islas de Santa Ursula y las Once Mil Virgenes</ORGANIZATION>, Saint <PERSON>Ursula</PERSON> and the 11,000 Virgins, a cumbersome name that was usually shortened, both on maps of the time and in common parlance, to <PERSON>Islas Virgenes</PERSON>), and he also named the islands of <PERSON>Virgin Gorda</PERSON> (the fat virgin), <LOCATION>Tortola</LOCATION>, and <PERSON>Peter Island</PERSON> (<LOCATION>San Pedro</LOCATION>).

He continued to the Greater Antilles, and landed at <LOCATION>Puerto Rico</LOCATION> (originally <LOCATION>San Juan Bautista</LOCATION>, in honor of Saint <PERSON>John</PERSON> the Baptist, a name that was later supplanted by <LOCATION>Puerto Rico</LOCATION> (English: <LOCATION>Rich Port</LOCATION>) while the capital retained the name, <LOCATION>San Juan</LOCATION>) on November 19, 1493. One of the first skirmishes between native Americans and Europeans since the time of the <ORGANIZATION>Vikings</ORGANIZATION> took place when <ORGANIZATION>Columbus</ORGANIZATION>'s men rescued two boys who had just been castrated by their captors.

On November 22, <PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> returned to <LOCATION>Hispaniola</LOCATION>, where he intended to visit Fuerte de la Navidad (Christmas Fort), built during his first voyage, and located on the northern coast of <LOCATION>Haiti</LOCATION>; <ORGANIZATION>Fuerte de la Navidad</ORGANIZATION> was found in ruins, destroyed by the native Taino people, whereupon, <PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> moved more than 100 kilometers eastwards, establishing a new settlement, which he called <LOCATION>La Isabela</LOCATION>, likewise on the northern coast of <LOCATION>Hispaniola</LOCATION>, in the present-day <LOCATION>Dominican Republic</LOCATION>. However, <PERSON>La Isabela</PERSON> proved to be a poorly-chosen location, and the settlement was short-lived.

He left <LOCATION>Hispaniola</LOCATION> on April 24, 1494, arrived at <LOCATION>Cuba</LOCATION> (naming it Juana) on April 30. He explored the southern coast of <LOCATION>Cuba</LOCATION>, which he believed to be a peninsula rather than an island, and several nearby islands, including the Isle of <LOCATION>Pines</LOCATION> (<ORGANIZATION>Isla de las Pinas</ORGANIZATION>, later known as <ORGANIZATION>La Evangelista</ORGANIZATION>, The Evangelist). He reached <LOCATION>Jamaica</LOCATION> on May 5. He retraced his route to <LOCATION>Hispaniola</LOCATION>, arriving on August 20, before he finally returned to <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION>.

During his second voyage, <PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> and his men instituted a policy in Hispaniola which has been referred to by numerous historians as genocide. The native Taino people of the island were systematically enslaved and murdered. Hundreds were rounded up and shipped to <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION> to be sold; many died en route. For the rest of the population, <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION> demanded that all Taino under his control should bring the Spaniards gold. Those that didn't were to have their hands cut off. Since there was, in fact, little gold to be had, the Taino fled, and the Spaniards hunted them down and killed them. The Taino tried to mount a resistance, but the <LOCATION>Spanish</LOCATION> weaponry was superior, and European diseases ravaged their population. In despair, the Taino engaged in mass suicide, even killing their own children to save them from the Spaniards. Within two years, half of what may have been 250,000 Taino were dead. The remainder were taken as slaves and set to work on plantations, where the mortality rate was very high. By 1550, 60 years after <PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> landed, only a few hundred Taino were left on their island. In another hundred years, perhaps only a handful remained.



On May 30, 1498, <PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> left with six ships from <LOCATION>Sanlcar</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION>, for his third trip to the New World. He was accompanied by the young <PERSON>Bartolom de Las Casas</PERSON>, who would later provide partial transcripts of Columbus' logs. 

Columbus led the fleet to the <LOCATION>Portuguese</LOCATION> island of <LOCATION>Porto Santo</LOCATION>, his wife's native land. He then sailed to <LOCATION>Madeira</LOCATION> and spent some time there with the Portuguese captain <PERSON>Joo Gonalves</PERSON> da Camara before sailing to the <LOCATION>Canary Islands</LOCATION> and <LOCATION>Cape Verde</LOCATION>. Columbus landed on the south coast of the island of <LOCATION>Trinidad</LOCATION> on July 31. From August 4 through August 12, he explored the Gulf of <LOCATION>Paria</LOCATION> which separates <LOCATION>Trinidad</LOCATION> from <LOCATION>Venezuela</LOCATION>. He explored the mainland of <LOCATION>South America</LOCATION>, including the <LOCATION>Orinoco River</LOCATION>. He also sailed to the islands of Chacachacare and <LOCATION>Margarita Island</LOCATION> and sighted and named <LOCATION>Tobago</LOCATION> (<LOCATION>Bella Forma</LOCATION>) and <LOCATION>Grenada</LOCATION> (Concepcion). 

Columbus returned to <LOCATION>Hispaniola</LOCATION> on August 19 to find that many of the Spanish settlers of the new colony were discontented, having been misled by <ORGANIZATION>Columbus</ORGANIZATION> about the supposedly bountiful riches of the new world. An entry in his journal from September 1498 reads, "From here one might send, in the name of the Holy Trinity, as many slaves as could be sold..." Indeed, as a fierce supporter of slavery, <ORGANIZATION>Columbus</ORGANIZATION> ultimately refused to baptize the native people of <LOCATION>Hispanolia</LOCATION>, since <ORGANIZATION>Catholic</ORGANIZATION> law forbade the enslavement of Christians. 

Columbus repeatedly had to deal with rebellious settlers and natives.  He had some of his crew hanged for disobeying him. A number of returning settlers and sailors lobbied against <ORGANIZATION>Columbus</ORGANIZATION> at the <LOCATION>Spanish</LOCATION> court, accusing him and his brothers of gross mismanagement. On his return he was arrested for a period (see Governorship and arrest section below).

<PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> made a fourth voyage nominally in search of the Strait of <LOCATION>Malacca</LOCATION> to the <LOCATION>Indian Ocean</LOCATION>. Accompanied by his brother <PERSON>Bartolomeo</PERSON> and his 13-year-old son <PERSON>Fernando</PERSON>, he left <PERSON>Cdiz</PERSON>, <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION>, on May 11, 1502, with the ships Capitana, Gallega, <PERSON>Vizcana</PERSON> and <PERSON>Santiago de Palos</PERSON>. He sailed to Arzila on the Moroccan coast to rescue <LOCATION>Portuguese</LOCATION> soldiers whom he had heard were under siege by the Moors. On June 15, they landed at <LOCATION>Carbet</LOCATION> on the island of <LOCATION>Martinique</LOCATION> (<PERSON>Martinica</PERSON>). A hurricane was brewing, so he continued on, hoping to find shelter on Hispaniola. He arrived at <LOCATION>Santo Domingo</LOCATION> on June 29, but was denied port, and the new governor refused to listen to his storm prediction. Instead, while <ORGANIZATION>Columbus</ORGANIZATION>' ships sheltered at the mouth of the <LOCATION>Rio Jaina</LOCATION>, the first Spanish treasure fleet sailed into the hurricane. Columbus' ships survived with only minor damage, while twenty-nine of the thirty ships in the governor's fleet were lost to the July 1 storm. In addition to the ships, 500 lives (including that of the governor, <PERSON>Francisco de Bobadilla</PERSON>) and an immense cargo of gold were surrendered to the sea. 

After a brief stop at <LOCATION>Jamaica</LOCATION>, <ORGANIZATION>Columbus</ORGANIZATION> sailed to <LOCATION>Central America</LOCATION>, arriving at <LOCATION>Guanaja</LOCATION> (<ORGANIZATION>Isla de Pinos</ORGANIZATION>) in the <LOCATION>Bay Islands</LOCATION> off the coast of <LOCATION>Honduras</LOCATION> on July 30. Here <PERSON>Bartolomeo</PERSON> found native merchants and a large canoe, which was described as "long as a galley" and was filled with cargo. On August 14, he landed on the American mainland at <LOCATION>Puerto Castilla</LOCATION>, near <LOCATION>Trujillo</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Honduras</LOCATION>. He spent two months exploring the coasts of <LOCATION>Honduras</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Nicaragua</LOCATION>, and <LOCATION>Costa Rica</LOCATION>, before arriving in <LOCATION>Almirante Bay</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Panama</LOCATION> on October 16.

On December 5, 1502, <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION> and his crew found themselves in a storm unlike any they had ever experienced. In his journal <ORGANIZATION>Columbus</ORGANIZATION> writes,For nine days I was as one lost, without hope of life. Eyes never beheld the sea so angry, so high, so covered with foam. The wind not only prevented our progress, but offered no opportunity to run behind

any headland for shelter; hence we were forced to keep out in this bloodyocean, seething like a pot on a hot fire. Never did the sky look more terrible; for one whole day and night it blazed like a furnace, and the lightning broke with such violence that each time I wondered if it had carried off my spars and sails; the flashes came with such fury and frightfulness that we all thought that the ship would be blasted. Allthis time the water never ceased to fall from the sky; I do not say it rained, for it was like another deluge. The men were so worn out that they longed for death to end their dreadful suffering.

In <LOCATION>Panama</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> learned from the natives of gold and a strait to another ocean. After much exploration, in January 1503 he established a garrison at the mouth of the <LOCATION>Rio Belen</LOCATION>. On April 6 one of the ships became stranded in the river. At the same time, the garrison was attacked, and the other ships were damaged. Columbus left for <LOCATION>Hispaniola</LOCATION> on April 16, heading north. On May 10 he sighted the <LOCATION>Cayman Islands</LOCATION>, naming them "Las Tortugas" after the numerous sea turtles there. His ships next sustained more damage in a storm off the coast of <LOCATION>Cuba</LOCATION>. Unable to travel farther, on June 25, 1503, they were beached in <LOCATION>St. Ann's Bay</LOCATION>, Jamaica.For a year <PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> and his men remained stranded on <LOCATION>Jamaica</LOCATION>. A Spaniard, <PERSON>Diego Mendez</PERSON>, and some natives paddled a canoe to get help from <LOCATION>Hispaniola</LOCATION>. That island's governor, <PERSON>Nicols de Ovando</PERSON> y Cceres, detested <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION> and obstructed all efforts to rescue him and his men. In the meantime <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION>, in a desperate effort to induce the natives to continue provisioning him and his hungry men, successfully intimidated the natives by correctly predicting a lunar eclipse for February 29, 1504, using the Ephemeris of the German astronomer Regiomontanus. Help finally arrived, no thanks to the governor, on June 29, 1504, and <PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> and his men arrived in <LOCATION>Sanlcar</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION>, on November 7.

During Columbus' stint as governor and viceroy, he had been accused of governing tyrannically. . <PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> was physically and mentally exhausted; his body was wracked by arthritis and his eyes by ophthalmia. In October 1499, he sent two ships to <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION>, asking the Court of <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION> to appoint a royal commissioner to help him govern.

The Court appointed <PERSON>Francisco de Bobadilla</PERSON>, a member of the Order of Calatrava; however, his authority stretched far beyond what <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION> had requested. <PERSON>Bobadilla</PERSON> was given total control as governor from 1500 until his death in 1502. Arriving in <LOCATION>Santo Domingo</LOCATION> while <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION> was away, <PERSON>Bobadilla</PERSON> was immediately peppered with complaints about all three <PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> brothers: <PERSON>Christopher</PERSON>, <PERSON>Bartolom</PERSON>, and Diego. <PERSON>Consuelo Varela</PERSON>, a <LOCATION>Spanish</LOCATION> historian, states: "Even those who loved him [<ORGANIZATION>Columbus</ORGANIZATION>] had to admit the atrocities that had taken place." 

As a result of these testimonies and without being allowed a word in his own defense, <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION> upon his return, had manacles placed on his arms and chains on his feet and was cast into prison to await return to <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION>. He was 53 years old. 

On October 1, 1500, <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION> and his two brothers, likewise in chains, were sent back to <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION>. Once in <LOCATION>Cdiz</LOCATION>, a grieving <PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> wrote to a friend at court:

It is now seventeen years since I came to serve these princes with the Enterprise of the Indies. They made me pass eight of them in discussion, and at the end rejected it as a thing of jest. Nevertheless I persisted therein... Over there I have placed under their sovereignty more land than there is in <LOCATION>Africa</LOCATION> and <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION>, and more than 1,700 islands... In seven years I, by the divine will, made that conquest. At a time when I was entitled to expect rewards and retirement, I was incontinently arrested and sent home loaded with chains... The accusation was brought out of malice on the basis of charges made by civilians who had revolted and wished to take possession on the land....

I beg your graces, with the zeal of faithful Christians in whom their Highnesses have confidence, to read all my papers, and to consider how I, who came from so far to serve these princes... now at the end of my days have been despoiled of my honor and my property without cause, wherein is neither justice nor mercy.

According to testimony of 23 witnesses during his trial, <PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> regularly used barbaric acts of torture to govern Hispaniola.

<PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> and his brothers lingered in jail for six weeks before the busy King <PERSON>Ferdinand</PERSON> ordered their release. Not long thereafter, the king and queen summoned the Columbus brothers to their presence at the <LOCATION>Alhambra</LOCATION> palace in <LOCATION>Granada</LOCATION>. There the royal couple heard the brothers' pleas; restored their freedom and their wealth; and, after much persuasion, agreed to fund Columbus' fourth voyage. But the door was firmly shut on <PERSON>Christopher Columbus</PERSON>'s role as governor. From that point forward, <PERSON>Nicols de Ovando</PERSON> y Cceres was to be the new governor of the <LOCATION>West Indies</LOCATION>.



While <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION> had always given the conversion of non-believers as one reason for his explorations, he grew increasingly religious in his later years. He claimed to hear divine voices, lobbied for a new crusade to capture <LOCATION>Jerusalem</LOCATION>, often wore Franciscan habit, and described his explorations to the "paradise" as part of God's plan which would soon result in the Last Judgment and the end of the world.

In his later years, <PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> demanded that the Spanish Crown give him 10 per cent of all profits made in the new lands, pursuant to earlier agreements. Because he had been relieved of his duties as governor, the crown did not feel bound by these contracts, and his demands were rejected. After his death his family later sued for part of the profits from trade with <LOCATION>America</LOCATION> in the pleitos colombinos.

On May 20, 1506, at about the age of 55, <PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> died in <LOCATION>Valladolid</LOCATION>, fairly wealthy from the gold his men had accumulated in <LOCATION>Hispaniola</LOCATION>. When he died he was still convinced that his journeys had been along the east coast of <LOCATION>Asia</LOCATION>. According to a study, published in February 2007, by <PERSON>Antonio Rodriguez Cuartero</PERSON>, <ORGANIZATION>Department of Internal Medicine of the University of Granada</ORGANIZATION>, he died of a heart attack caused by <PERSON>Reiter</PERSON>'s Syndrome (also called reactive arthritis). According to his personal diaries and notes by contemporaries, the symptoms of this illness (burning pain during urination, pain and swelling of the knees, and conjunctivitis of the eyes) were clearly visible in his last three years.

His remains were first buried in <LOCATION>Valladolid</LOCATION> and then at the monastery of <LOCATION>La Cartuja</LOCATION> in <LOCATION>Seville</LOCATION> (southern <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION>), by the will of his son <PERSON>Diego</PERSON>, who had been governor of <LOCATION>Hispaniola</LOCATION>. Then in 1542, his remains were transferred to <LOCATION>Santo Domingo</LOCATION>, in eastern Hispaniola. In 1795, the French took over <PERSON>Hispaniola</PERSON>, and his remains were moved to <LOCATION>Havana</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Cuba</LOCATION>. After <LOCATION>Cuba</LOCATION> became independent following the Spanish-American War in 1898, his remains were moved back to the Cathedral of <LOCATION>Seville</LOCATION> in <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION>, where they were placed on an elaborate catafalque. However, a lead box bearing an inscription identifying "<PERSON>Don Christopher Columbus</PERSON>" and containing fragments of bone and a bullet was discovered at <LOCATION>Santo Domingo</LOCATION> in 1877. 

To lay to rest claims that the wrong relics were moved to <LOCATION>Havana</LOCATION> and that the remains of <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION> were left buried in the cathedral of <LOCATION>Santo Domingo</LOCATION>, DNA samples were taken in June 2003 (History Today August 2003). The results are not conclusive. Initial observations suggested that the bones did not appear to belong to somebody with the physique or age at death associated with <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION>.
