DNA extraction proved difficult; only a few limited fragments of mitochondrial DNA could be isolated. However, such as they are, these do appear to match corresponding DNA from <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION>'s brother, giving support to the idea that the two had the same mother and that the body therefore may be that of <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION>. The authorities in <LOCATION>Santo Domingo</LOCATION> have not allowed the remains there to be exhumed, so it is unknown if any of those remains could be from <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION>'s body. The location of the <LOCATION>Dominican</LOCATION> remains is in the "Columbus Lighthouse" or Faro A Colon which is in <LOCATION>Santo Domingo</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Dominican Republic</LOCATION>.



Sometimes thought of erroneously as the discoverer of the New World, <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION> was preceded by the various cultures and civilizations of the indigenous peoples of the <LOCATION>Americas</LOCATION>, as well as the Western world's <ORGANIZATION>Vikings</ORGANIZATION>. He is regarded more accurately as the person who brought the <LOCATION>Americas</LOCATION> into the forefront of Western attention. "Columbus' claim to fame isn't that he got there first," explains historian <PERSON>Martin Dugard</PERSON>, "it's that he stayed." Equally false is the notion that he was first to envision a rounded earth. This was known in ancient times, though largely forgotten in the Middle Ages. By <PERSON>Columbus</PERSON>'s day, educated men were in agreement as to its spherical shape, even if many or most people believed otherwise. More contentious was the size of the earth, and whether it was possible in practical terms to cross such a vast body of water: the longest any ship (European or otherwise) had gone without making landfall did not much exceed 30 days when <PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> embarked on his first audacious voyage.

<PERSON>Amerigo Vespucci</PERSON>'s travel journals, published 1502-4, convinced <PERSON>Martin Waldseemller</PERSON> that the discovered place was not <LOCATION>India</LOCATION>, as <PERSON>Columbus</PERSON> always believed, but a new continent, and in 1507, a year after <PERSON>Columbus</PERSON>' death, <PERSON>Waldseemller</PERSON> published a world map calling the new continent <LOCATION>America</LOCATION> from <PERSON>Vespucci</PERSON>'s Latinized name "Americus". 

Historically, the British had downplayed <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION> and emphasized the role of <PERSON>John Cabot</PERSON> as a pioneering explorer. But for the emerging <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Cabot</PERSON> made a poor national hero. Veneration of <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION> in <LOCATION>America</LOCATION> dates back to colonial times. America itself was sometimes referred to as <LOCATION>Columbia</LOCATION>. The use of <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION> as a founding figure of New World nations and the use of the word <ORGANIZATION>Columbia</ORGANIZATION> spread rapidly after the American Revolution. During the last two decades of the 18th century the name "<ORGANIZATION>Columbia</ORGANIZATION>" was given to the federal capital <LOCATION>District of Columbia</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>South Carolina</LOCATION>'s new capital city, <LOCATION>Columbia</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>South Carolina</LOCATION>, the <LOCATION>Columbia River</LOCATION>, and numerous other places. Attempts to rename the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> "<ORGANIZATION>Columbia</ORGANIZATION>" failed, but <ORGANIZATION>Columbia</ORGANIZATION> became a female national personification of <LOCATION>America</LOCATION>, similar to the male Uncle <PERSON>Sam</PERSON>. Outside the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> the name was used in 1819 for the Republic of <LOCATION>Colombia</LOCATION>, a precursor of the modern nation of <LOCATION>Colombia</LOCATION>.

A candidate for sainthood in the <ORGANIZATION>Catholic Church</ORGANIZATION> in 1866, Celebration of <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION>' legacy perhaps reached a zenith in 1892 when the 400th anniversary of his first arrival in the <LOCATION>Americas</LOCATION> occurred. Monuments to Columbus like the Columbian Exposition in <LOCATION>Chicago</LOCATION> were erected throughout the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> and <LOCATION>Latin America</LOCATION> extolling him. Numerous cities, towns, counties, and streets have been named after him, including the capital cities of two <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> states (<LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Ohio</LOCATION> and <LOCATION>Columbia</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>South Carolina</LOCATION>). 

In 1909, descendants of <ORGANIZATION>Columbus</ORGANIZATION> undertook to dismantle the Columbus family chapel in <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION> and move it to a site near <LOCATION>State College</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION>, where it may now be visited by the public. At the museum associated with the chapel, there are a number of Columbus relics worthy of note, including the armchair which the "Admiral of the Ocean Sea" used at his chart table.

Culpability is sometimes placed on contemporary governments and their citizens for the hardship suffered by Native Americans during the time of <PERSON>Christopher Columbus</PERSON>. Columbus myths and celebrations are generally a positive affair, making less room for this concept in history books. <PERSON>Christopher Columbus</PERSON> was strongly criticised in a song by Jamaican artiste Burning Spear titled 'A Damn Blasted Liar.' The controversial song opened a strong opinionated debate across much of the <LOCATION>Caribbean</LOCATION> region on the effects that <PERSON>Christopher Columbus</PERSON> and his leadership had on the region's native peoples.

The Spanish colonization of the <LOCATION>Americas</LOCATION>, and the subsequent effects on the native peoples, were dramatized in the 1992 feature film  to commemorate the 500th anniversary of his landing in the <LOCATION>Americas</LOCATION>. *In 2003, Venezuelan President <PERSON>Hugo Chvez</PERSON> urged Native American Latin Americans to not celebrate the Columbus Day holiday. <PERSON>Chavez</PERSON> blamed <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION> for leading the way in the mass genocide of the Native Americans by the Spanish.

Although an abundance of artwork involving <PERSON>Christopher Columbus</PERSON> exists, no authentic contemporary portrait has been found. There is a portrait painted by <PERSON>Alejo Fernndez</PERSON>, between 1505 and 1536, titled <ORGANIZATION>Virgen de los Navegantes</ORGANIZATION> in the Royal <PERSON>Alcazar</PERSON> in <LOCATION>Seville</LOCATION>. At the 1893 World's Columbian Exposition, 71 alleged portraits of <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION> were displayed, most did not match contemporary descriptions. These writings describe him as having reddish hair, which turned to white early in his life, light colored eyes, as well as being a lighter skinned person with too much sun exposure turning his face red. 

In keeping with descriptions of <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION> having had auburn hair or (later) white hair, textbooks use the Sebastiano del Piombo painting (which in its normal-sized resolution shows <ORGANIZATION>Columbus</ORGANIZATION>'s hair as auburn) so often that it has become the iconic image of <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION> accepted by popular culture.

<PERSON>Colombus</PERSON> is a significant historical figure and has been depicted in fiction and in popular films and television. 

In 1991, author <PERSON>Salman Rushdie</PERSON> published a fictional representation of <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION> in The New Yorker, "<PERSON>Christopher Columbus</PERSON> and <PERSON>Queen Isabella</PERSON> of <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION> Consummate Their Relationship, <LOCATION>Santa Fe</LOCATION>, January, 1492," (The New Yorker, June 17, 1991, p. 32). In  (1996) science fiction novelist <PERSON>Orson Scott Card</PERSON> focuses on Columbus' life and activities, but the novel's action also deals with a group of scientists from the future who travel back to the 15th century with the goal of changing the pattern of European contact with the <LOCATION>Americas</LOCATION>. British author <PERSON>Stephen Baxter</PERSON> includes <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION>' quest for royal sponsorship as a crucial historical event in his 2007 science fiction novel Navigator (ISBN 978-0-441-01559-7), the third entry in the author's Time's Tapestry Series. American author <PERSON>Mark Twain</PERSON> based the time traveller's trick in A <ORGANIZATION>Connecticut Yankee</ORGANIZATION> in King Arthur's Court on <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION>'s sucessful prediction of a lunar eclipse on his fourth voyage to the new world.

Columbus has also been portrayed in cinema and television, including mini-series, films and cartoons. Most notably he was portrayed by <PERSON>Grard Depardieu</PERSON> in 1992 film by <PERSON>Ridley Scott</PERSON> . <PERSON>Scott</PERSON> presented <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION> as an idealist as opposed to the view that he was ruthless and responsible for the misfortune of Native Americans. As in many of <PERSON>Scott</PERSON>'s movies, the character is presented as having some ideas that weren't current at his time. 

Other more recent productions include TV mini-series <ORGANIZATION>Christopher Columbus</ORGANIZATION> (1985) with <PERSON>Gabriel Byrne</PERSON> as <PERSON>Colombus</PERSON>, , a 1992 biopic film by <PERSON>Alexander Salkind</PERSON>, <PERSON>Christopher Columbus</PERSON>, a 1949 film starring <PERSON>Fredric March</PERSON> as <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION>, and comedy Carry On <LOCATION>Columbus</LOCATION> (1992).













<TITLE><PERSON>Billy Crystal</PERSON></TITLE>

<PERSON>William Edward</PERSON> "Billy" Crystal (born March 14, 1948) is an <ORGANIZATION>American Golden Globe</ORGANIZATION> Award-nominated and Emmy Award-winning actor, writer, producer, comedian, and film director. He gained prominence in the 1970s for playing <PERSON>Jodie Dallas</PERSON> on the <ORGANIZATION>ABC</ORGANIZATION> sitcom Soap and became a Hollywood film star during the late 1980s and 1990s, appearing in the box office successes When <PERSON>Harry Met Sally</PERSON>... and <ORGANIZATION>City Slickers</ORGANIZATION>. 

Crystal was born in the Doctor's <LOCATION>Hospital</LOCATION> in <LOCATION>Manhattan</LOCATION> and grew up in <LOCATION>Long Beach</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>New York</LOCATION>, the son of <PERSON>Helen</PERSON> (ne <PERSON>Gabler</PERSON>), a housewife, and <PERSON>Jack Crystal</PERSON>, a record company executive and producer of jazz records, who owned and operated the Commodore Record store. His uncle was musician and songwriter <PERSON>Milt Gabler</PERSON>, and his brother, <PERSON>Richard Crystal</PERSON>, is a television producer. Crystal grew up in a Jewish family that he has described as "large" and "loving". After graduation from <ORGANIZATION>Long Beach High School</ORGANIZATION>, Crystal attended <ORGANIZATION>Marshall University</ORGANIZATION> in <LOCATION>Huntington</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>West Virginia</LOCATION>, on a baseball scholarship, having learned the game from his father, who pitched for <ORGANIZATION>St. John's University</ORGANIZATION>. Crystal never played a game at <ORGANIZATION>Marshall</ORGANIZATION> because the program was suspended during his freshman year and he didn't return as a sophomore, staying back in <LOCATION>New York</LOCATION> with his future wife. Later, he attended <ORGANIZATION>New York University</ORGANIZATION> and <ORGANIZATION>Nassau Community College</ORGANIZATION>. 

Crystal has been married to <PERSON>Janice Goldfinger</PERSON>, whom he met when he was 18 and she 17, since 1970. They have two daughters, actresses <PERSON>Jennifer</PERSON> and <PERSON>Lindsay</PERSON>, and are now grandparents. They reside in <LOCATION>Pacific Palisades</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>California</LOCATION>.

Crystal returned to <LOCATION>New York</LOCATION> and performed regularly at The Improv and Catch a Rising Star. He studied film and television direction under <PERSON>Martin Scorsese</PERSON> at <ORGANIZATION>New York University</ORGANIZATION>. Crystal's earliest prominent role was as <PERSON>Jodie Dallas</PERSON> on Soap, one of the first gay characters portrayed on American television. In 1976, Crystal appeared on an episode of All in the Family. He was scheduled to appear on the first episode of Saturday Night Live (October 11, 1975), but his sketch was cut. He did do a stand-up bit later on that first season as "Bill Crystal", on the April 17, 1976, episode. After hosting a show years later, in 1984, he joined the cast. His most famous recurring sketch was his parody of <PERSON>Fernando Lamas Fernando</PERSON>, a smarmy talk show host whose catch phrase, "You look... mahvelous!," became a media sensation.

Crystal's first film role was in <PERSON>Joan Rivers</PERSON>'s 1978 film Rabbit Test. Crystal also made game show appearances such as The Hollywood Squares and The $20,000 Pyramid. He holds the record for getting his contestant partner to the top of the pyramid in the bonus round in the fastest time, 26 seconds. 

Crystal hosted the Academy Awards broadcast in 1990-1993, 1997, 1998, 2000, and 2004; and he reportedly turned down the opportunity to host the 2006 ceremony to concentrate on his one-man show, 700 Sundays. His eight times as the emcee is second only to legendary Oscar host <PERSON>Bob Hope</PERSON> in most ceremonies hosted.

Crystal appeared briefly in <PERSON>Rob Reiner</PERSON>'s 1984 "rockumentary" This Is Spinal Tap as Morty The Mime, a waiter dressed as a mime at one of Spinal Tap's parties. He shared the scene with a then-unknown, non-speaking <PERSON>Dana Carvey</PERSON>. Crystal's memorable line in the film was "Mime is money." Eventually, <PERSON>Reiner</PERSON> directed Crystal again in The Princess Bride, and then in the romantic comedy When Harry Met Sally..., for which Crystal was nominated for a Golden Globe. Crystal also appeared in the box-office hit City Slickers (1991).

<PERSON>Crystal</PERSON> wrote, directed, and starred in Mr. Saturday Night (1992) and Forget <LOCATION>Paris</LOCATION> (1995). In the former, <ORGANIZATION>Crystal</ORGANIZATION> played a serious role in aging makeup, as an egotistical comedian who reflects back on his career. He directed the made-for-television movie 61* (2001) based on <PERSON>Roger Maris</PERSON>'s and <PERSON>Mickey Mantle</PERSON>'s race to break Babe <PERSON>Ruth</PERSON>'s single-season home run record in 1961. This earned <ORGANIZATION>Crystal an Emmy</ORGANIZATION> nomination for Outstanding Directing for a Miniseries, Movie or a Special.

Crystal has continued working in film, including the popular Analyze This (1999) and Analyze That (2002) with <PERSON>Robert De Niro</PERSON>. Crystal lent his voice to the character <PERSON>Mike Wazowski</PERSON> in <ORGANIZATION>Pixar</ORGANIZATION>'s animated feature film Monsters, <ORGANIZATION>Inc.</ORGANIZATION>, and in the English version of Howl's Moving Castle as the voice of Calcifer. <ORGANIZATION>Pixar</ORGANIZATION> originally approached him to provide the voice of Buzz Lightyear in Toy Story. He turned down that offer, but regretted it after the film became one of the most popular releases of the year.

Crystal won the 2005 Tony Award for Best Special Theatrical Event for 700 Sundays, a two-act, one-man play, which he conceived and wrote about his parents and his childhood growing up on <LOCATION>Long Island</LOCATION>. He toured the <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> with the show in 2006 and <LOCATION>Australia</LOCATION> in 2007.

Following the initial success of the play, <ORGANIZATION>Crystal</ORGANIZATION> wrote the book 700 Sundays for <ORGANIZATION>Warner Books</ORGANIZATION>, which was published on October 31, 2005. In conjunction with the book and the play that also paid tribute to his uncle, <PERSON>Milt Gabler</PERSON>, Crystal produced two CD compilations: Billy Crystal Presents: The <PERSON>Milt Gabler Story</PERSON>, which featured his uncle's most influential recordings from Billie Holiday's "Strange Fruit" to "Rock Around the Clock" by <PERSON>Bill Haley</PERSON> & His Comets; and <PERSON>Billy Remembers Billie</PERSON> featuring <ORGANIZATION>Crystal</ORGANIZATION>'s favorite Holiday recordings.

In 1986, Crystal started hosting Comic Relief on <ORGANIZATION>HBO</ORGANIZATION> with <PERSON>Robin Williams</PERSON> and <PERSON>Whoopi Goldberg</PERSON>. Founded by <PERSON>Bob Zmuda</PERSON>, Comic Relief raises money for homeless people in the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION>.

On September 6, 2005, on The Tonight Show, Crystal and <PERSON>Jay Leno</PERSON> were the first celebrities to sign a <ORGANIZATION>Harley-Davidson</ORGANIZATION> motorcycle to be auctioned off for <LOCATION>Gulf Coast</LOCATION> relief.

Crystal has also participated in the <ORGANIZATION>Simon Wiesenthal Center Museum of Tolerance</ORGANIZATION> in <LOCATION>Los Angeles</LOCATION>. Portraying himself in a video, <ORGANIZATION>Crystal</ORGANIZATION> introduces museum guests to the genealogy wing of the museum.

On March 12, , <ORGANIZATION>Crystal</ORGANIZATION> signed a minor league contract, for a single day, to play with the <ORGANIZATION>New York Yankees</ORGANIZATION>, and was invited to the team's major league spring training. <PERSON>Billy</PERSON> wore uniform number 60, in honor of his upcoming 60th birthday. On March 13, in a spring training game against the <ORGANIZATION>Pittsburgh Pirates</ORGANIZATION>, <ORGANIZATION>Crystal</ORGANIZATION> led off as the designated hitter. He managed to make contact, fouling a fastball up the first base line, but was eventually struck out by <ORGANIZATION>Pirates</ORGANIZATION> pitcher <PERSON>Paul Maholm</PERSON> on 6 pitches and was later replaced in the batting order by <PERSON>Johnny Damon</PERSON>. He was released on March 14, his 60th birthday.

In addition to his Golden Globe Award-nominations, Emmy Awards, and Tony Award, Crystal is the 2007 recipient of the Mark Twain Prize for American Humor.













<TITLE><PERSON>Elvis Presley</PERSON></TITLE>

In 1954, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> began his career as one of the first performers of rockabilly, an uptempo fusion of country and rhythm and blues with a strong back beat. His novel versions of existing songs, mixing "black" and "white" sounds, made him popular--and controversial--as did his uninhibited stage and television performances. He recorded songs in the rock and roll genre, with tracks like "Hound Dog" and "Jailhouse Rock" later embodying the style. Presley had a versatile voice and had unusually wide success encompassing other genres, including gospel, blues, ballads and pop. To date, he has been inducted into four music halls of fame.

In the 1960s, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> made the majority of his thirty-one movies--mainly poorly reviewed, but financially successful, musicals. In 1968, he returned with acclaim to live music in a television special, and thereafter performed across the <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION>, notably in <LOCATION>Las Vegas</LOCATION>. Throughout his career, he set records for concert attendance, television ratings and recordings sales. He is one of the best-selling and most influential artists in the history of popular music. Health problems, drug dependency and other factors led to his premature death at age 42.

<PERSON>Elvis Presley</PERSON> was of mixed ancestry. His father, <PERSON>Vernon</PERSON> (April 10, 1916-June 26, 1979), had several low-paying jobs, including sharecropper and truck driver. His mother, <PERSON>Gladys Love Smith</PERSON> (April 25, 1912 - August 14, 1958) worked as a sewing machinist. They met in <LOCATION>Tupelo</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Mississippi</LOCATION>, and eloped to <LOCATION>Pontotoc County</LOCATION> where they married on June 17, 1933.

Presley's ancestry is discussed at the following sites:

Presley was born in a two-room shotgun house, built by his father, in <LOCATION>East Tupelo</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Mississippi</LOCATION>. He was an identical twin--his brother was stillborn and given the name <PERSON>Jesse Garon</PERSON>. Growing up as an only child he "was, everyone agreed, unusually close to his mother." The family lived just above the poverty line and attended an Assembly of God church. <PERSON>Vernon</PERSON> has been described as "a malingerer, always averse to work and responsibility." His wife was "voluble, lively, full of spunk" and had a fondness for drink. In 1938, <PERSON>Vernon</PERSON> was jailed for an eight dollar check forgery. His incarceration caused <PERSON>Gladys</PERSON> and her son to lose the family home, and they had to move in with relatives.

In September 1942, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> entered first grade at <ORGANIZATION>Lawhorn School</ORGANIZATION> in <LOCATION>Tupelo</LOCATION>. <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> was sometimes bullied; classmates threw "things at him--rotten fruit and stuff--because he was different... he stuttered and he was a mama's boy." Despite this, he was considered a "well-mannered and quiet child".

On October 3, 1945, at age ten, he made his first public performance in a singing contest at the <LOCATION>Mississippi-Alabama</LOCATION> Fair and Dairy Show at the suggestion of his teacher Mrs. <PERSON>J.C. Grimes</PERSON>. Dressed as a cowboy, the young <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> had to stand on a chair to reach the microphone and sang <PERSON>Red Foley</PERSON>'s "Old Shep." He came second, winning $5 and a free ticket to all the Fair rides.

In 1946, for his eleventh birthday, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> received his first guitar. He had wanted a bicycle or rifle for his birthday, but his parents could only afford a guitar. Over the following year, <PERSON>Vernon</PERSON>'s brother, <PERSON>Vester</PERSON>, gave <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> basic guitar lessons. In September 1948, the family moved to <LOCATION>Memphis</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Tennessee</LOCATION>, allegedly because Vernon--in addition to needing work--had to escape the law for transporting bootleg liquor. In 1949, they lived at <ORGANIZATION>Lauderdale Courts</ORGANIZATION>, a public housing development in one of <LOCATION>Memphis</LOCATION>' poorer sections. Presley practiced playing guitar in the laundry room and also played in a five-piece band with other tenants. Another resident, <PERSON>Johnny Burnette</PERSON>, recalled, "Wherever <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> went he'd have his guitar slung across his back... [H]e'd go in to one of the cafes or bars... Then some folks would say: 'Let's hear you sing, boy.'" Presley enrolled at <ORGANIZATION>L. C. Humes High School</ORGANIZATION> where some fellow students viewed his performing unfavorably; one recalled that he was "a sad, shy, not especially attractive boy" whose guitar playing was not likely to win any prizes. Presley was made fun of as a 'trashy' kind of boy, playing 'trashy' hillbilly music." Other children however, "would beg him" to sing, but he was apparently too shy to perform.

In September 1950, Presley occasionally worked evenings as an usher at <LOCATION>Loew</LOCATION>'s State Theater--his first job--to boost the family income, but his mother made him quit as she feared it was affecting his school work. He worked again at <LOCATION>Loew</LOCATION>'s in June the following year, but was fired after a fistfight over a female employee. He began to grow his sideburns and, when he could afford to, dress in the wild, flashy clothes of <ORGANIZATION>Lansky Brothers</ORGANIZATION> on <LOCATION>Beale Street</LOCATION>. He stood out, especially in the conservative Deep South of the 1950s, and was mocked and bullied for it. Childhood friend <PERSON>Red West</PERSON> said: "In the sea of 1600 pink-scalped kids at school, <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> stood out like a camel in the arctic. ... [but] ... his appearance expressed a defiance which his demeanor did not match..." Despite any unpopularity or shyness, he was a contestant in his school's 1952 "Annual Minstrel Show" and won by receiving the most applause. His prize was to sing encores, including "Cold Cold Icy Fingers" and "Till I Waltz Again With You".

After graduation, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> was still rather shy, a "kid who had spent scarcely a night away from home". His third job was driving a truck for the <ORGANIZATION>Crown Electric Company</ORGANIZATION>. He began wearing his hair longer with a "ducktail"--the style of truck drivers at that time.

Initial influences came through his family's attendance at the Assembly of God, a Pentecostal Holiness church. <ORGANIZATION>Rolling Stone</ORGANIZATION> wrote: "Gospel pervaded <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON>' character and was a defining and enduring influence all of his days." Throughout his life--in the recording studio, in private, or after concerts--<PERSON>Presley</PERSON> joined with others singing and playing gospel music at informal sessions.

The young Presley frequently listened to local radio; his first musical hero was family friend <LOCATION>Mississippi</LOCATION> Slim, a hillbilly singer with a radio show on Tupelos WELO. Presley performed occasionally on Slims Saturday morning show, <PERSON>Singin</PERSON> and Pickin Hillbilly. "He was crazy about music... Thats all he talked about," recalls his sixth grade friend, <PERSON>James Ausborn</PERSON>, Slims younger brother. Before he was a teenager, music was already Presleys "consuming passion". <PERSON>J. R. Snow</PERSON>, son of 1940s country superstar <PERSON>Hank Snow</PERSON>, recalls that even as a young man <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> knew all of <PERSON>Hank Snows</PERSON> songs, "even the most obscure". Presley himself said: "I loved records by Sister <PERSON>Rosetta Thorpe</PERSON>, ... <PERSON>Roy Acuff</PERSON>, <PERSON>Ernest Tubbs</PERSON>, <PERSON>Ted Daffan</PERSON>, <PERSON>Jimmie Rodgers</PERSON>, <PERSON>Jimmy Davis</PERSON> and <PERSON>Bob Wills</PERSON>."

In <LOCATION>Memphis</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> went to record stores that had jukeboxes and listening booths, playing old records and new releases for hours. He was an audience member at the all-night white--and black--"gospel sings" downtown. Memphis Symphony Orchestra concerts at <LOCATION>Overton Park</LOCATION> were another Presley favorite, along with the Metropolitan Opera. His small record collection included <PERSON>Mario Lanza</PERSON> and <PERSON>Dean Martin</PERSON>. Presley later said, "I just loved music. Music period."

<LOCATION>Memphis</LOCATION> had a strong tradition of blues music and Presley went to blues as well as hillbilly venues. Many of his future recordings were inspired by local African American composers and recording artists, including <PERSON>Arthur Crudup</PERSON>, <PERSON>Rufus Thomas</PERSON> and <PERSON>B.B. King</PERSON>. King says that he "knew <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> before he was popular. He used to come around and be around us a lot ... on Beale Street."

Presley "was an untrained musician who played entirely by ear. 'I don't read music,' he confessed, 'but I know what I like.' ... Because he was not a songwriter, Presley [would] rarely [have] material prepared for recording sessions..." When later, as a young singer, he "ventured into the recording studio he was heavily influenced by the songs he had heard on the jukebox and radio."

On July 18, 1953, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> went to <ORGANIZATION>Sun Records' Memphis Recording Service</ORGANIZATION> to record "My Happiness" with "That's When Your Heartaches Begin", supposedly a present for his mother. During his initial introduction at <ORGANIZATION>Sun Records</ORGANIZATION>, assistant <PERSON>Marion Keisker</PERSON> asked him who he sounded like. Presley replied: "I don't sound like nobody." On January 4, 1954, he cut a second acetate. At the time, Sun Records boss <PERSON>Sam Phillips</PERSON> was on the lookout for someone who could deliver a blend of black blues and boogie-woogie music; he thought it would be very popular among white people. When <PERSON>Phillips</PERSON> acquired a demo recording of "Without Love (There Is Nothing)" and was unable to identify the vocalist, <PERSON>Keisker</PERSON> reminded him about the young truck driver. She called him on June 26, 1954. Presley was not able to do justice to the song (though he would record it years later). <PERSON>Phillips</PERSON> would later recall that "<PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> was probably as nervous as anybody, black or white, that I had seen in front of a microphone." Despite this, <PERSON>Phillips</PERSON> invited local musicians <PERSON>Winfield</PERSON> "Scotty" <PERSON>Moore</PERSON> and <PERSON>Bill Black</PERSON> to audition Presley. Though they were not overly impressed, a studio session was planned.

During a recording break, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> began "acting the fool" first with <PERSON>Arthur Crudup</PERSON>'s "That's All Right (Mama)". <PERSON>Phillips</PERSON> quickly got them all to restart, and began taping. This was the sound he had been looking for. The group recorded other songs, including <PERSON>Bill Monroe</PERSON>'s "Blue Moon of <LOCATION>Kentucky</LOCATION>". After the session, according to <PERSON>Scotty Moore</PERSON>, <PERSON>Bill Black</PERSON> remarked: "Damn. Get that on the radio and they'll run us out of town".

"That's All Right" was aired on July 8, 1954, by <PERSON>DJ Dewey Phillips</PERSON>. Listeners to the show began phoning in, eager to find out who the singer was. (The DJ mispronounced Presley's apparently unusual name as "<PERSON>Elton Preston</PERSON>.") The interest was such that <PERSON>Phillips</PERSON> played the demo fourteen times. During an interview on the show, <PERSON>Phillips</PERSON> asked Presley what high school he attended--to clarify <PERSON>Presley</PERSON>'s color for listeners who assumed he must be black. The first release of Presley's music featured "That's All Right" and "Blue Moon of <LOCATION>Kentucky</LOCATION>". With Presley's version of <PERSON>Monroe</PERSON>'s song consistently rated higher,both sides began to chart across the <LOCATION>South</LOCATION>.

<PERSON>Moore</PERSON> and <PERSON>Black</PERSON> began playing regularly with Presley. They gave performances on the July 17 and July 24, 1954 to promote the Sun single at the <ORGANIZATION>Bon Air</ORGANIZATION>, a rowdy music club in <LOCATION>Memphis</LOCATION>, where the band was not well-received. On July 30 the trio, billed as <ORGANIZATION>The Blue Moon Boys</ORGANIZATION>, made their first paid appearance at the <ORGANIZATION>Overton Park Shell</ORGANIZATION>, with <PERSON>Slim Whitman</PERSON> headlining. A nervous Presley's legs were said to have shaken uncontrollably during this show: his wide-legged pants emphasized his leg movements, apparently causing females in the audience to go "crazy". <PERSON>Scotty Moore</PERSON> claims it was just the natural way he moved and had nothing to do with "nerves." Presley consciously incorporated similar movements into future shows.

DJ and promoter <PERSON>Bob Neal</PERSON> became the trio's manager (replacing <PERSON>Scotty Moore</PERSON>). <PERSON>Moore</PERSON> and <PERSON>Black</PERSON> left their band, the Starlight Wranglers and, from August through October 1954, appeared with Presley at <ORGANIZATION>The Eagle</ORGANIZATION>'s Nest. Presley debuted at the Grand Ole Opry in <LOCATION>Nashville</LOCATION> on October 2; <PERSON>Hank Snow</PERSON> introduced Presley on stage. He performed "Blue Moon of <ORGANIZATION>Kentucky</ORGANIZATION>" but received only a polite response. Afterwards, the singer was allegedly told by the Opry's <PERSON>Jim Denny</PERSON>: "Boy, youd better keep driving that truck.", though others deny it was <PERSON>Denny</PERSON> who made that statement.Country music promoter and manager <PERSON>Tillman Franks</PERSON> booked Presley for the <LOCATION>Louisiana</LOCATION> Hayride on October 16. Before <PERSON>Franks</PERSON> saw Presley, he referred to him as "that new black singer with the funny name". During Presley's first set, the reaction was muted; <PERSON>Franks</PERSON> then advised <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> to "Let it all go!" for the second set. House drummer <PERSON>D.J. Fontana</PERSON> (who had worked in strip clubs) complemented Presley's movements with accented beats. <PERSON>Bill Black</PERSON> also took an active part in encouraging the audience, and the crowd became more responsive.

According to one source, regarding Presley's engagements from that time, "Audiences had never before heard [such] music... [or] seen anyone who performed like Presley either. The shy, polite, mumbling boy gained self-confidence with every appearance... People watching the show were astounded and shocked, both by the ferocity of his performance, and the crowds reaction to it... <PERSON>Roy Orbison</PERSON> saw Presley for the first time in <LOCATION>Odessa</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Texas</LOCATION>: 'His energy was incredible, his instinct was just amazing... I just didnt know what to make of it. There was just no reference point in the culture to compare it.'" <PERSON>Sam Phillips</PERSON> said <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> "put every ounce of emotion ... into every song, almost as if he was incapable of holding back."

By August 1955, <ORGANIZATION>Sun Studios</ORGANIZATION> had released ten sides credited to "Elvis Presley, <PERSON>Scotty</PERSON> and Bill", all typical of the developing Presley style. That style proved hard to categorize; he was billed or labeled in the media as "The King of Western Bop", "The Hillbilly Cat" and "The Memphis Flash".

On August 15, 1955, "Colonel" <PERSON>Tom Parker</PERSON> became Presley's manager, signing him to a one year contract, plus renewals. Several record labels had shown interest in signing Presley and, by the end of October 1955, three major labels had made offers up to $25,000. On November 21, 1955, <PERSON>Parker</PERSON> and <PERSON>Phillips</PERSON> negotiated a deal with <ORGANIZATION>RCA Victor Records</ORGANIZATION> to acquire Presley's Sun contract for an unprecedented $40,000, $5,000 of which was a bonus for the singer for back royalties owed to him by <ORGANIZATION>Sun Records</ORGANIZATION> (<LOCATION>Presley</LOCATION>, at 20, was officially still a minor, so his father had to sign the contract). By December 1955, RCA had begun to heavily promote its newest star, and by the month's end had re-released all of his Sun recordings.

On January 10, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> made his first recordings for RCA in <LOCATION>Nashville</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Tennessee</LOCATION>. The session produced "Heartbreak Hotel/I Was The One" which was released on January 27. The public reaction to "Heartbreak Hotel" prompted RCA to release it as a single in its own right (February 11). By April it had hit number one in the <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> charts, selling in excess of one million copies. 

To increase the singer's exposure, <PERSON>Parker</PERSON> finally brought Presley to national television (In March 1955, Presley had failed an audition for <PERSON>Arthur Godfrey</PERSON>'s Talent Scouts). He booked six <ORGANIZATION>Dorsey Brothers</ORGANIZATION>' Stage Show appearances in <LOCATION>New York</LOCATION> for <ORGANIZATION>CBS</ORGANIZATION>, beginning January 28, 1956. Presley was introduced on the first by <LOCATION>Cleveland</LOCATION> DJ <PERSON>Bill Randle</PERSON>. He stayed in town and on January 30, he and the band headed for the <ORGANIZATION>RCA</ORGANIZATION>'s <LOCATION>New York Studio</LOCATION>. The sessions yielded eight songs, including "My Baby Left Me" and "Blue Suede Shoes". The latter was the only hit single from the collection, but the recordings marked the point at which Presley started moving away from the raw, pure Sun sound to the more commercial and mainstream sound RCA had envisioned for him.

On March 23, RCA <PERSON>Victor</PERSON> released <PERSON>Elvis Presley</PERSON>, his first album. Like the Sun recordings, the majority of the tracks were country songs. The album went on to top the pop album chart for 10 weeks.

On April 1, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> launched his acting career with a screen-test for <ORGANIZATION>Paramount Pictures</ORGANIZATION>. His first motion-picture, Love Me Tender, was released on November 21 (See 'Acting career').

Colonel <PERSON>Parker</PERSON> had also obtained a deal for two lucrative shows with <PERSON>Milton Berle</PERSON> at <ORGANIZATION>NBC</ORGANIZATION>. Presley first appeared on The <PERSON>Milton Berle</PERSON> Show from the deck of the USS Hancock in <LOCATION>San Diego</LOCATION> on April 3. His performance was cheered by a live audience of appreciative sailors and their dates. A few days after, a flight taking Presley's band to <LOCATION>Nashville</LOCATION> for a recording session left all three badly shaken (the plane lost an engine and almost went down over <LOCATION>Texas</LOCATION>). After more hectic touring, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> returned to The <PERSON>Milton Berle</PERSON> Show on June 5 and performed "Hound Dog" (without his guitar). Singing the song uptempo, he then began a slower version. His exaggerated, straight-legged shuffle around the microphone stand stirred the audience--as did his vigorous leg shaking and hip thrusts in time to the beat. Presley's "gyrations" created a storm of controversy--even eclipsing the 'communist threat' headlines prevalent at the time. The press described his performance as "vulgar" and "obscene". The furore was such that <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> was pressured to explain himself on the local <LOCATION>New York City</LOCATION> TV show <PERSON>Hy Gardner</PERSON> Calling: "Rock and roll music, if you like it, and you feel it, you can't help but move to it. That's what happens to me. I have to move around. I can't stand still. I've tried it, and I can't do it." After this performance he was dubbed "Elvis the Pelvis". Presley disliked the name, calling it "one of the most childish expressions I ever heard."

From April 23, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> was scheduled to perform four weeks at the New Frontier Hotel and Casino on the <LOCATION>Las Vegas Strip</LOCATION>--billed this time as "the Atomic Powered Singer" (Since <LOCATION>Nevada</LOCATION> was the home of the <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION>'s atomic weapons testing, <PERSON>Parker</PERSON> thought the name would be catchy). His shows were so badly received by critics and the conservative, middle-aged guests, that Colonel <PERSON>Parker</PERSON> cut short the engagement from four weeks to two. . <PERSON>D.J. Fontana</PERSON> said, "I don't think the people there were ready for Elvis..... We tried everything we knew. Usually <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> could get them on his side. It didn't work that time". While in <LOCATION>Vegas</LOCATION>, Presley saw <ORGANIZATION>Freddie Bell</ORGANIZATION> and the Bellboys live, and liked their version of <PERSON>Leiber</PERSON> and <PERSON>Stoller</PERSON>'s "Hound Dog". By May 16, he had added the song to his own act.

The <PERSON>Berle</PERSON> shows drew such huge ratings that <PERSON>Steve Allen</PERSON> (NBC), not a fan of rock and roll, booked him for one appearance in <LOCATION>New York</LOCATION> on July 1. <PERSON>Allen</PERSON> wanted "to do a show the whole family can watch" and introduced a "new Elvis" in white bow tie and black tails. Presley sang "Hound Dog" for less than a minute to a Basset Hound in a top hat. According to one author, "<PERSON>Allen</PERSON> thought Presley was talentless and absurd... [he] set things up so that <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> would show his contrition..." In his book "Hi-Ho Steverino!" <PERSON>Allen</PERSON> wrote the following: "When I booked <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON>, I naturally had no interest in just presenting him vaudeville-style and letting him do his spot as he might in concert. Instead we worked him into the comedy fabric of our program. We certainly didn't inhibit <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON>' then-notorious pelvic gyrations, but I think the fact that he had on formal evening attire made him, purely on his own, slightly alter his presentation. The day after (July 2), the single "Hound Dog" was recorded and <PERSON>Scotty Moore</PERSON> said they were "all angry about their treatment the previous night". (Presley often referred to the <PERSON>Allen</PERSON> show as the most ridiculous performance of his career.) A few days later, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> made a "triumphant" outdoor appearance in <LOCATION>Memphis</LOCATION> at which he announced: "You know, those people in <LOCATION>New York</LOCATION> are not gonna change me none. I'm gonna show you what the real <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> is like tonight."

Country vocalists The <ORGANIZATION>Jordanaires</ORGANIZATION> accompanied Presley on The <PERSON>Steve Allen</PERSON> Show and their first recording session together produced "Any Way You Want Me", "Don't Be Cruel" and "Hound Dog". The Jordanaires would work with the singer through the 1960s.

Though Presley had been unhappy, <PERSON>Allen</PERSON>'s show had, for the first time, beaten The Ed Sullivan Show in the ratings, causing a critical <PERSON>Sullivan</PERSON> (CBS) to book Presley for three appearances for an unprecedented $50,000.

Presley's first <PERSON>Ed Sullivan</PERSON> appearance (September 9, 1956) was seen by some 55-60 million viewers. Biographer <PERSON>Greil Marcus</PERSON> has written: "Compared to moments on the <PERSON>Dorsey</PERSON> shows and on the <PERSON>Berle</PERSON> show, it was ice cream." On the third <PERSON>Sullivan</PERSON> show, in spite of Presley's established reputation as a "gyrating" performer, he sang only slow paced ballads and a gospel song. <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> was nevertheless only shown to the television audience 'from the waist up', as if to censor the singer. <PERSON>Marcus</PERSON> claims he "stepped out in the outlandish costume of a pasha, if not a harem girl", and was shot in close up during this last broadcast, as if <PERSON>Sullivan</PERSON> had tried to 'bury' the singer, It was also claimed that Colonel <PERSON>Parker</PERSON> had himself orchestrated the 'censorship' merely to generate publicity. In spite of any misgivings about the controversial nature of his performing style (see 'Sex symbol'), <PERSON>Sullivan</PERSON> declared at the end of the third appearance that <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> was "a real decent, fine boy" and that they had never had "a pleasanter experience" on the show.

On December 4, Presley dropped into <ORGANIZATION>Sun Records</ORGANIZATION> where <PERSON>Carl Perkins</PERSON> and <PERSON>Jerry Lee Lewis</PERSON> were recording. <PERSON>Sam Phillips</PERSON> made sure the session of the three performing was recorded; the results would later appear on a bootlegged recording titled ''The Million Dollar Quartet'' in 1977 (<PERSON>Johnny Cash</PERSON> is often thought to have performed with the trio, but he was only present briefly at <PERSON>Phillips</PERSON>' instigation for a photo opportunity). RCA would eventually iron out legal difficulties and release an authorized version a few years later.

On December 29, Billboard revealed that <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> had placed more songs in the Top 100 than any other artist since chart records began. This news was followed by a front page report in the Wall Street Journal on December 31, that suggested Presley merchandise had grossed more than $22 million in sales.

When "That's All Right" was played, many listeners were sure Presley must be black, prompting white disc-jockeys to ignore his Sun singles. However, black disc-jockeys did not want anything to do with any record they knew was made by a white man. To many black adults, Presley had undoubtedly "stolen" or at least "derived his style from the Negro rhythm-and-blues performers of the late 1940s", , though such criticism ignored Presley's use of "white" musical styles. Some black entertainers, notably <PERSON>Jackie Wilson</PERSON>, argued: "A lot of people have accused <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> of stealing the black mans music, when in fact, almost every black solo entertainer copied his stage mannerisms from <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON>."

By the spring of 1956, Presley was becoming popular nationwide and teenagers flocked to his concerts. <PERSON>Scotty Moore</PERSON> recalled: "Hed start out, 'You aint nothin but a Hound Dog,' and theyd just go to pieces. Theyd always react the same way. Thered be a riot every time." <PERSON>Bob Neal</PERSON> wrote: "It was almost frightening, the reaction... from [white] teenage boys. So many of them, through some sort of jealousy, would practically hate him." In <LOCATION>Lubbock</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Texas</LOCATION>, a teenage gang fire-bombed Presley's car. Some performers became resentful (or resigned to the fact) that Presley's unmatched hustle onstage before them would "kill" their own act; he thus rose quickly to top billing. At the two concerts he performed at the 1956 <LOCATION>Mississippi-Alabama</LOCATION> Fair and Dairy Show, one hundred National Guardsmen were on hand to prevent crowd trouble.

To many white adults, the singer was "the first rock symbol of teenage rebellion. ... they did not like him, and condemned him as depraved. Anti-Negro prejudice doubtless figured in adult antagonism. Regardless of whether parents were aware of the Negro sexual origins of the phrase 'rock 'n' roll', Presley impressed them as the visual and aural embodiment of sex." In 1956, a critic for the <ORGANIZATION>New York Daily News</ORGANIZATION> wrote that popular music "has reached its lowest depths in the 'grunt and groin' antics of one Elvis Presley" and the Jesuits denounced him in its weekly magazine, <LOCATION>America</LOCATION>. Even <PERSON>Frank Sinatra</PERSON> opined: "His kind of music is deplorable, a rancid smelling aphrodisiac. It fosters almost totally negative and destructive reactions in young people." Presley responded to this (and other derogatory comments <PERSON>Sinatra</PERSON> made) by saying: "I admire the man. He has a right to say what he wants to say. He is a great success and a fine actor, but I think he shouldn't have said it... This ... [rock and roll] ... is a trend, just the same as he faced when he started years ago."

Presley was even seen as a "definite danger to the security of the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION>." His actions and motions were called "a strip-tease with clothes on" or "sexual self-gratification on stage." They were compared with "masturbation or riding a microphone." Some saw the singer as a sexual pervert, and psychologists feared that teenaged girls and boys could easily be "aroused to sexual indulgence and perversion by certain types of motions and hysteria--the type that was exhibited at the Presley show." Presley would insist, however, that there was nothing vulgar about his stage act, saying: "Some people tap their feet, some people snap their fingers, and some people sway back and forth. I just sorta do em all together, I guess." In August 1956, a <LOCATION>Florida</LOCATION> judge called Presley a "savage" and threatened to arrest him if he shook his body while performing in <LOCATION>Jacksonville</LOCATION>. The judge declared that Presley's music was undermining the youth of <LOCATION>America</LOCATION>. Throughout the performance (which was filmed by police), he kept still as ordered, except for wiggling a finger in mockery at the ruling. (Presley recalls this incident during the '68 Comeback Special.)

In 1957, despite Presley's demonstrable respect for "black" music and performers, he faced accusations of racism. He was alleged to have said in <LOCATION>Boston</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Massachusetts</LOCATION>: "The only thing Negro people can do for me is to buy my records and shine my shoes." Presley always denied saying, or ever wanting to say, such a racist remark. Jet magazine, run by and for African Americans, was subsequently keen to investigate the story. The singer's acquaintances in <LOCATION>Memphis</LOCATION> were pursued for any other racist anecdotes to back up the perception that <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> was racist, but none was found. Nor could any evidence be found of the date, location and persons involved when the remark was allegedly made (Presley had never visited <LOCATION>Boston</LOCATION>). However, the Jet journalist did find plenty of testimony that Presley judged people "regardless of race, color or creed". Even forty-five years later, the alleged remark was still being quoted as if it were a verified fact, to Presley's detriment.

His parents moved home in <LOCATION>Memphis</LOCATION>, but the singer lived there briefly. With increased concerns over privacy and security, <PERSON>Graceland</PERSON> was bought and renovated in 1957, a mansion with several acres of land. This was Presley's primary residence until his death.

Presley's record sales grew quickly throughout the late 1950s, with hits like "All Shook Up", "(Let me Be Your) Teddy Bear" and "Too Much".

On December 20, 1957, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> received his draft notice. <PERSON>Hal Wallis</PERSON> and <ORGANIZATION>Paramount Pictures</ORGANIZATION> had already spent $350,000 on the film King Creole, and did not want to suspend or cancel the project. The <ORGANIZATION>Memphis Draft Board</ORGANIZATION> granted Presley a deferment to finish it. On March 24, 1958, he was inducted as <ORGANIZATION>US Army</ORGANIZATION> private #53310761 and completed basic training at <LOCATION>Fort Hood</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Texas</LOCATION> on September 17, 1958, before being posted to <LOCATION>Friedberg</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Germany</LOCATION> with the 3rd Armored Division, where his service took place from October 1, 1958 until March 2, 1960.

Fellow soldiers have attested to Presley's down-to-earth nature, and his generosity while in service. To supplement meager under-clothing supplies, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> bought an extra set of fatigues for everyone in his outfit. He also donated his <ORGANIZATION>Army</ORGANIZATION> pay to charity, and purchased all the TV sets for personnel on the base at that time.

Presley had chosen not to join 'Special Services', which would have allowed him to avoid certain duties and maintain his public profile. He continued to receive massive media coverage, with much speculation echoing <PERSON>Presley</PERSON>'s own concerns about his enforced absence damaging his career. However, early in 1958, RCA <PERSON>Victor</PERSON> producer <PERSON>Steve Sholes</PERSON> and <PERSON>Freddy Bienstock</PERSON> of <PERSON>Hill</PERSON> and Range (Presley's main music publishers) had both pushed for recording sessions and strong song material, the aim being to release regular hit recordings during Presley's two-year hiatus. Hit singles--and six albums--duly followed during that period.

As Presley's fame grew, his mother continued to drink excessively and began to gain weight. She had wanted her son to succeed, "but... [the] hysteria of the crowd frightened her." In early August 1958, doctors had diagnosed hepatitis and her condition worsened. Presley was granted emergency leave to visit her, arriving in <LOCATION>Memphis</LOCATION> on August 12. Two days later, <PERSON>Gladys Presley</PERSON> died of heart failure, aged forty-six. <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> was distraught, "grieving almost constantly" for days.

Some months later, in <LOCATION>Germany</LOCATION>, "[a] sergeant had introduced [Presley] to amphetamines when they were on maneuvers at Grafenwhr... it seemed like half the guys in the company were taking them." Friends around Presley, like <PERSON>Joe Esposito</PERSON>, also began taking them, "if only to keep up with <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON>, who was practically evangelical about their benefits." The <ORGANIZATION>Army</ORGANIZATION> also introduced Presley to karate--something which he studied seriously, even including it in his later live performances.

<PERSON>Presley</PERSON> returned to the <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> on March 2, 1960, and was honorably discharged with the rank of sergeant on March 5. Any doubts <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> had about his popularity must have been dispelled as "The train which carried him from <LOCATION>New Jersey</LOCATION> to <LOCATION>Memphis</LOCATION> was mobbed all the way, with <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> being called upon to appear ... at whistle-stops" to placate his fans. 

The first recording session, on March 20, 1960, was attended by all of the significant businessmen involved with Presley; none had heard him sing for two years, and there were inevitable concerns about him being able to recapture his previous success. The session was the first at which Presley was recorded using a three-track machine, allowing better quality, postsession remixing and stereophonic recording. This, and a further session in April, yielded some of Presley's best-selling songs. "It's Now or Never" ended with Presley "soaring up to an incredible top G sharp ... pure magic." His voice on "Are You Lonesome Tonight?" has been described as "natural, unforced, dead in tune, and totally distinctive." Although some tracks were uptempo, none could be described as "rock and roll", and many of them marked a significant change in musical direction. Most tracks found their way on to an album--Elvis is Back!--described by one critic as "a triumph on every level... It was as if <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> had... broken down the barriers of genre and prejudice to express everything he heard in all the kinds of music he loved". The album was also notable because of Homer Boots <PERSON>Randolph</PERSON>'s acclaimed saxophone playing on the blues songs "Like A Baby" and "Reconsider Baby", the latter being described as "a refutation of those who do not recognize what a phenomenal artist Presley was."

In 1956, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> launched his career as a film actor. He screen-tested for <ORGANIZATION>Paramount Pictures</ORGANIZATION> by lip-synching "Blue Suede Shoes" and performing a scene as '<PERSON>Bill Starbuck</PERSON>' in ''The Rainmaker''. Despite being quietly confident that The Rainmaker would be his first film--even going as far as saying so in an interview--the role eventually went to <PERSON>Burt Lancaster</PERSON>.

After signing a seven-year contract with Paramount, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> made his big-screen dbut with the musical western, Love Me Tender. It was panned by the critics but did well at the box office.The original title--The <ORGANIZATION>Reno Brothers</ORGANIZATION>--was changed to capitalize on the advanced sales of the song "Love Me Tender". The majority of Presley's films were musical comedies made to "sell records and produce high revenues." He also appeared in more dramatic films, like Jailhouse Rock and King Creole. The erotic, if not homoerotic, dance sequence to the song "Jailhouse Rock", which Presley choreographed himself, "is considered by many as his greatest performance ever captured on film." To maintain box office success, he would later even shift "into beefcake formula comedy mode for a few years." He also made one non-musical western, Charro!.

<PERSON>Presley</PERSON> stopped live performing after his Army service with the exception, ironically--given <PERSON>Sinatra</PERSON>'s previously scathing criticism--of a guest appearance on  (1960). He also performed three charity concerts--two in <LOCATION>Memphis</LOCATION> and one in <LOCATION>Pearl Harbor</LOCATION> (1961).

In the <ORGANIZATION>Army</ORGANIZATION>, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> had said on many occasions that "more than anything, he wanted to be taken seriously as a dramatic actor." His manager, with an eye on long-term earnings, negotiated a multi-picture seven-year contract with <PERSON>Hal Wallis</PERSON>. The singer would later star alongside several established or up-coming actors, including <PERSON>Walter Matthau</PERSON>, <PERSON>Carolyn Jones</PERSON>, <PERSON>Angela Lansbury</PERSON>, <PERSON>Charles Bronson</PERSON>, <PERSON>Barbara Stanwyck</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mary Tyler Moore</PERSON>--and even a very young <PERSON>Kurt Russell</PERSON> in his screen debut. Although <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> was praised by directors, like <PERSON>Michael Curtiz</PERSON>, as polite and hardworking (and as having an exceptional memory), "he was definitely not the most talented actor around." Others were more charitable; critic <PERSON>Bosley Crowther</PERSON> of the <ORGANIZATION>New York Times</ORGANIZATION> said: "This boy can act," about his portrayal in King Creole. Director <PERSON>Joe Pasternak</PERSON> believed "Elvis should be given more meaty parts. ... He would be a good actor. He should do more important pictures."

The movies he did make, and the <ORGANIZATION>AIP</ORGANIZATION> beach movies (which were mainly made for an early sixties teenage audience), were generally criticized as a "pantheon of bad taste." The scripts of his movies "were all the same, the songs progressively worse." For <PERSON>Blue Hawaii</PERSON>, "fourteen songs were cut in just three days." <PERSON>Julie Parrish</PERSON>, who appeared in Paradise, <LOCATION>Hawaiian Style</LOCATION>, says that Presley hated many of the songs chosen for his films; he "couldn't stop laughing while he was recording" one of them. Others noted that the songs seemed to be "written on order by men who never really understood <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> or rock and roll." However, several reputable songwriters/partnerships contributed soundtrack songs, including <PERSON>Jerry Leiber</PERSON> and <PERSON>Mike Stoller</PERSON>, <PERSON>Don Robertson</PERSON>, <PERSON>Sid Tepper</PERSON> and <PERSON>Roy C. Bennett</PERSON>, and <PERSON>Otis Blackwell</PERSON> and <PERSON>Winfield Scott</PERSON>. Whatever the quality of the material, some observers have argued that Presley generally sang well in the studio, with commitment, and always played with distinguished musicians and backing singers. Sight and Sound wrote that in his movies "<PERSON>Elvis Presley</PERSON>, aggressively bisexual in appeal, knowingly erotic, [was] acting like a crucified houri and singing with a kind of machine-made surrealism." Critics would later claim that "No major star suffered through more bad movies than <PERSON>Elvis Presley</PERSON>."

Presley movies were nevertheless very popular, and he "became a film genre of his own." <PERSON>Hal Wallis</PERSON> would later remark: "An <PERSON>Elvis Presley</PERSON> picture is the only sure thing in <LOCATION>Hollywood</LOCATION>." <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> on celluloid was the only chance for his world-wide fans to see him, in the absence of live appearances (the only time he toured outside of the <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> was in <LOCATION>Canada</LOCATION> in 1957). His Blue <LOCATION>Hawaii</LOCATION> even "boosted the new state's tourism. Some of his most enduring and popular songs came from those [kind of] movies," like "Can't Help Falling in Love," "Return to Sender" and "Viva Las Vegas." His 1960s films and soundtracks grossed some $280 million. On December 1, 1968, the <ORGANIZATION>New York Times</ORGANIZATION> wrote: "Three times a year Elvis Presley ... [makes] multimillion-dollar feature-length films, with holiday titles like "Blue <LOCATION>Hawaii</LOCATION>", "Fun in <LOCATION>Acapulco</LOCATION>", "Viva Las Vegas", "Tickle Me", "Easy Come, Easy Go", "Live a Little, Love a Little" and the latest in the series, "Chataqua" [released as Charro!]. For each film <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> receives a million dollars in wages and 50 per cent of the profits. ... [E]very film yields an LP sound-track record which may sell as many as two-million copies."

In 1964, <PERSON>Richard Burton</PERSON> and <PERSON>Peter O'Toole</PERSON> had starred in <PERSON>Hal Wallis</PERSON>' acclaimed <PERSON>Becket</PERSON>. <PERSON>Wallis</PERSON> admitted to the press that the financing of such quality productions was only possible by making a series of profitable B-movies starring Presley. He branded <PERSON>Wallis</PERSON> "a double-dealing sonofabitch" (and he thought little better of <PERSON>Tom Parker</PERSON>), realizing there had never been any intention to let him develop into a serious actor.

<PERSON>Presley</PERSON> was similarly exploited the following year with the film Tickle Me. Allied Artists had serious financial problems and hoped a Presley film would help them "stay afloat". By agreeing to a lower fee, using previously recorded songs and filming on the studio back-lot, Allied Artists were able to keep costs very low. Considered one of the weakest of all Presley pictures, it became the third highest grossing picture in Allied Artists' history, and saved them from bankruptcy at the time.

Presley was one of the highest paid actors during the 1960s, but times were changing. "[The] <PERSON>Elvis Presley</PERSON> film was becoming pass. Young people were tuning in, dropping out and doing acid. Musical acts like <ORGANIZATION>Jefferson Airplane</ORGANIZATION>, Grateful Dead, The Doors, <PERSON>Janis Joplin</PERSON> and many others were dominating the airwaves. <PERSON>Elvis Presley</PERSON> was not considered cool as he once was." <PERSON>Priscilla Presley</PERSON> recalls: "He blamed his fading popularity on his humdrum movies" and "... loathed their stock plots and short shooting schedules." She also notes: "He could have demanded better, more substantial scripts, but he didn't."

Change of Habit (1969) was the singer's final movie role. His last two films were concert documentaries in the early 1970s, though Presley was keen to consider dramatic movie roles. (See: 'Influence of Colonel <PERSON>Parker</PERSON> and others').

As well as the formulaic movie songs of the 1960s, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> added to the studio recordings of Elvis Is Back, by recording other noteworthy songs like "She's Not You", "Suspicion," "Little Sister", "(You're the) Devil in Disguise" and "It Hurts Me." In 1966 he recorded a cover of <PERSON>Bob Dylan</PERSON>'s "Tomorrow is a Long Time" (which RCA <PERSON>Victor</PERSON> relegated to a bonus track on the soundtrack album for Spinout). He also produced two gospel albums: His Hand in Mine (1960) and How Great Thou Art (1966). In 1967, he recorded some well-received singles, like <PERSON>Guitar Man</PERSON>, by songwriter/guitar player <PERSON>Jerry Reed</PERSON>. However, "during the Beatles era (1963-70), only six <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> singles reached number ten or better. 'Suspicious Minds' was the lone number one."

During filming of Paradise, Hawaiian Style, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> returned to his Bel Air home. The Beatles were at the end of their second <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> tour. Colonel <PERSON>Parker</PERSON> had been negotiating a meeting for some time, through The Beatles' manager <PERSON>Brian Epstein</PERSON>, though <PERSON>Parker</PERSON> simply saw it as a valuable publicity opportunity (He had apparently even tried to get the group and Presley to perform the closing song in the same movie, but The Beatles' film contract precluded it). The group arrived in Bel Air amid a flurry of elaborate security arrangements made by <PERSON>Parker</PERSON> at 10pm, on August 27, 1965. The visit lasted about four hours. Many of Presley's closest and trusted friends-- members of the so-called "Memphis Mafia"--were present, including school friend and bodyguard <LOCATION>Red West</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Marty Lacker</PERSON>, <PERSON>Jerry Schilling</PERSON>, <PERSON>Larry Geller</PERSON> and their girlfriends, etc..

Biographer <PERSON>Peter Guralnick</PERSON> maintains that <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> was at best "lukewarm" about playing host to people he did not really know, and it took a while for everyone to feel comfortable. <PERSON>Paul McCartney</PERSON> later said: "It was one of the great meetings of my life. I think he liked us. I think at that time, he may have felt a little bit threatened, but he didn't say anything. We certainly didn't feel any antagonism. I only met him that once, and then I think the success of our career started to push him out a little, which we were very sad about, because we wanted to coexist with him."

<PERSON>Marty Lacker</PERSON> recalls <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> saying: "'Quite frankly, if you guys are going to stare at me all night, I'm going to bed. I thought we'd talk a while and maybe jam a little.' And when he said that, they [The Beatles] went nuts." The group told stories, joked and listened to records. The five of them had an impromptu jam session. "They all went to the piano," says <PERSON>Lacker</PERSON>, "and <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> handed out a couple of guitars. And they started singing Elvis songs, Beatle songs, <PERSON>Chuck Berry</PERSON> songs. <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> played <PERSON>Paul</PERSON>'s bass part on "I Feel Fine", and <PERSON>Paul</PERSON> said something like, 'You're coming along quite promising on the bass there, <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON>.' I remember thinking later, 'Man, if we'd only had a tape recorder.'"

<PERSON>Ringo Starr</PERSON> played pool with two others that night; <PERSON>George Harrison</PERSON> "looked to most of the guys to be stoned" on arrival and allegedly smoked a joint with <PERSON>Larry Geller</PERSON> and talked about <LOCATION>Hinduism</LOCATION> (see: 'Influence of Colonel <PERSON>Parker</PERSON> and others'). <PERSON>Parker</PERSON> played roulette with <PERSON>Epstein</PERSON>. However, <PERSON>Guralnick</PERSON> claims The Beatles were, overall, disappointed by the visit. They still reciprocated with an invitation for <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> to visit them, but only some of Presley's "Memphis Mafia" accepted. "<PERSON>John Lennon</PERSON> went out of his way to tell <PERSON>Jerry</PERSON> [<PERSON>Schilling</PERSON>] how much the evening had meant to him" and asked <PERSON>Schilling</PERSON> to tell Presley, "'[I]f it hadn't been for him I would have been nothing.'" <PERSON>Schilling</PERSON> says that when he told Presley he did not say anything, but "just kind of smiled."(See: '19701972)').

Presley's sexual appeal and photogenic looks have been acknowledged. Director <PERSON>Steve Binder</PERSON>, not a fan of Presley's music at the time, recalled from the '68 Comeback Special: "I'm straight as an arrow and I got to tell you, you stop, whether you're male or female, to look at him. He was that good looking. And if you never knew he was a superstar, it wouldn't make any difference; if he'd walked in the room, you'd know somebody special was in your presence."

According to <PERSON>Marjorie Garber</PERSON>, a "male rock critic writing in 1970 praised <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> as 'The master of the sexual simile, treating his guitar as both phallus and girl.' ... rumor had it that into his skin-tight jeans was sewn a lead bar to suggest a weapon of heroic proportions." She cites a boyhood friend of <PERSON>Presley</PERSON>'s who claims the singer actually used a cardboard toilet roll tube to make it "look to the girls up front like he had one helluva thing there inside his pants."  <PERSON>Ed Sullivan</PERSON> had apparently heard similar rumors and instructed his director <PERSON>Marlo Lewis</PERSON> to film only Presley's chest and head for his final <PERSON>Sullivan</PERSON> appearance. However, <PERSON>Lewis</PERSON> was skeptical about Presley wearing such a device and says simply: "It wasn't there".

Accounts of Presley's numerous sexual conquests may be exaggerated. <PERSON>Cybill Shepherd</PERSON> reveals that <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> kissed her all over her naked body - but refused to have oral sex with her. Ex-Girlfriends <PERSON>Judy Spreckels</PERSON> and June Juanico had no sexual relationships with <PERSON>Presley</PERSON>. <PERSON>Byron Raphael</PERSON> and <PERSON>Alanna Nash</PERSON> have stated that the star "would never put himself inside one of these girls..." <PERSON>Cassandra Peterson</PERSON> ("Elvira") says she knew Presley for only one night, but all they did was talk. <PERSON>Cher</PERSON> regrets turning him down when he asked her to stay with him in <LOCATION>Las Vegas</LOCATION>, because she was too nervous of spending the night with him.  <PERSON>Peggy Lipton</PERSON> claims that he was "virtually impotent" with her, but she attributed this to his boyishness and drug misuse. <PERSON>Guralnick</PERSON> concurs with others, "he wasn't really interested", preferring to lie in bed, watch television and talk.

<PERSON>Ann-Margret</PERSON> (Presley's co-star in <LOCATION>Viva Las Vegas</LOCATION>) refers to <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> as her "soulmate" but has revealed little else. A publicity campaign about Presley and <PERSON>Margret</PERSON>'s romance was launched during the filming of <LOCATION>Viva Las Vegas</LOCATION>, which helped to increase <PERSON>Margret</PERSON>'s popularity. Presley apparently dated many female co-stars for publicity purposes. <PERSON>Lori Williams</PERSON> dated him for a while in 1964. She says their "courtship was not some bizarre story. It was very sweet and <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> was the perfect gentleman."

Former partner <PERSON>Linda Thompson</PERSON> says they did not consummate their relationship until after a few months of dating. After they broke up in December 1976, many say Presley never had sex again. His last girlfriend, <PERSON>Ginger Alden</PERSON> claims to be engaged to Presley at the time of his death, though her story is contradicted by many of Presley's close friends.



<PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> and <PERSON>Priscilla</PERSON> met in 1959 at a party in <LOCATION>Bad Nauheim</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Germany</LOCATION> during his stay in the army. She was 14 at the time they met, while he was 24. They quickly began a serious relationship and frequently visited each other until he left <LOCATION>Germany</LOCATION> in 1960.

<PERSON>Priscilla</PERSON> and <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> stayed in contact over the phone, though they would not see each other again until the summer of 1962, when <PERSON>Priscilla</PERSON>'s parents agreed to let her visit for two weeks. After another visit at Christmas, <PERSON>Priscilla</PERSON>'s parents finally let her move to <LOCATION>America</LOCATION> for good. Part of the agreement was that she would be privately educated, to complete her senior year, and live with <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON>' father and his wife, <PERSON>Dee</PERSON>, in their home--due to <PERSON>Presley</PERSON>'s difficulty with accepting his stepmother, he arranged for them to live in a separate house on the Graceland estate. However, it wasn't long until <PERSON>Priscilla</PERSON> was moved into Graceland to live with <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON>. 

In her autobiography, <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> and Me, <PERSON>Priscilla</PERSON> says that <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> refused to have sex with her until they were married. However, biographer <PERSON>Suzanne Finstad</PERSON> writes that <PERSON>Priscilla</PERSON> and <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> slept together on their second date. 

Shortly before Christmas 1966, Presley proposed to <PERSON>Priscilla</PERSON>. They married on May 1, 1967 at the Aladdin Hotel in <LOCATION>Las Vegas</LOCATION>. In typical fashion, Colonel <PERSON>Parker</PERSON> had arranged a photo session and press conference to be conducted shortly after the ceremony. According to <PERSON>Finstad</PERSON>, this marriage was part of a mastermind for fame hatched by <PERSON>Priscilla</PERSON> and her mother.

Their only child, <PERSON>Lisa Marie</PERSON>, was born on February 1, 1968.

By 1967, Colonel <PERSON>Tom Parker</PERSON> had negotiated a contract that gave him 50% of Presley's earnings. <PERSON>Parker</PERSON>'s excessive gambling--and his subsequent need to have <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> signed up to commercially lucrative contracts--may well have adversely affected the course of Presley's career. <PERSON>Parker</PERSON>'s concerns about his own <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> citizenship may have also been a factor in <PERSON>Parker</PERSON> and the singer never exploiting Presley's popularity abroad (see: '19731976'). It has been claimed that <PERSON>Presley</PERSON>'s original band was fired in order to isolate the singer: <PERSON>Parker</PERSON> wanted no one close to Presley to suggest that a better management deal might exist.

As well as signing Presley to RCA <PERSON>Victor</PERSON>, <PERSON>Parker</PERSON> also cut a deal with <PERSON>Hill</PERSON> and <ORGANIZATION>Range Publishing Company</ORGANIZATION> to create a separate entity-- "Elvis Presley Music Incorporated"--to handle all of <PERSON>Presley</PERSON>'s songs and accrued royalties. <PERSON>Parker</PERSON> would later use this set-up to make songwriters relinquish some of their royalties; this ultimately resulted in the better writers refusing to provide songs for Presley, causing a marked decline in the quality of his output over the years.Presley apparently disliked several songs--even some of the earliest top sellers he became famous for (which suggests commercial influences were sometimes greater than his own desires). Presley's friend <PERSON>Jerry Schilling</PERSON> relates that one way to really annoy the singer was to play a song, like "All Shook Up", on a jukebox at one of his private parties. "Get that crap off," was his typical reaction.

In 1969, record producer Chips <PERSON>Moman</PERSON> and Presley recorded with <PERSON>Moman</PERSON>'s own musicians at his <ORGANIZATION>American Sound Studios</ORGANIZATION> in <LOCATION>Memphis</LOCATION>. Given the control exerted by RCA and the music publishers, this was a significant departure. <PERSON>Moman</PERSON> still had to deal with <PERSON>Hill</PERSON> and Range staff on site and was not happy with their song choices. <PERSON>Moman</PERSON> could only get the best out of the singer when he threatened to quit the sessions and asked Presley to remove the "aggravating" publishing personnel from the studio. RCA <PERSON>Victor</PERSON> executive <PERSON>Joan Deary</PERSON> was later full of praise for the song choices and superior results of <PERSON>Moman</PERSON>'s work, like "In the Ghetto" and "Suspicious Minds", but despite this, no producer was to override <PERSON>Hill</PERSON> and Range's control again.

According to life-long friend and "Memphis Mafia" member <PERSON>George Klein</PERSON>, over the years <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> was offered lead roles in the film Midnight Cowboy and in West Side Story. <PERSON>Robert Mitchum</PERSON> personally offered him the lead in Thunder Road. In 1974, Barbra Streisand approached Presley to star with her in the remake of A Star is Born. In each case, any ambitions the singer may have had to play such parts were thwarted by his manager's negotiating demands, or his flat refusals.

<PERSON>Marty Lacker</PERSON> regarded <PERSON>Parker</PERSON> as a "hustler and scam artist" who abused Presley's trust, but <PERSON>Lacker</PERSON> acknowledged that <PERSON>Parker</PERSON> was a master promoter. <PERSON>Priscilla Presley</PERSON> noted that "Elvis detested the business side of his career. He would sign a contract without even reading it."

Presley's father in turn distrusted <PERSON>Lacker</PERSON> and the other members of the "Memphis Mafia"; he thought they collectively exercised an unhealthy influence over his son. "[I]t was no wonder" that as the singer "slid into addiction and torpor, no one raised the alarm: to them, <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> was the bank, and it had to remain open." Musician <PERSON>Tony Brown</PERSON> noted the urgent need to reverse Presley's declining health as the singer toured in the mid-1970s. "But we all knew it was hopeless because <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> was surrounded by that little circle of people... all those so-called friends and... bodyguards."

<PERSON>Larry Geller</PERSON> became Presley's hairdresser in 1964. Unlike others in the "Memphis Mafia", <PERSON>Geller</PERSON> was interested in 'spiritual studies', and was subsequently viewed with suspicion and scorn by the singer's manager and friends. From their first conversation, <PERSON>Geller</PERSON> recalls how <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> revealed his secret thoughts and anxieties, how "there's got to be a reason... why I was chosen to be <PERSON>Elvis Presley</PERSON>.'" He then poured out his heart in "an almost painful rush of words and emotions," telling <PERSON>Geller</PERSON> about his mother and the hollowness of his Hollywood life, things he could not share with anyone around him. Thereafter, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> voraciously read books <PERSON>Geller</PERSON> supplied, on religion and mysticism. Perhaps most tellingly, he revealed to <PERSON>Geller</PERSON>: "I swear to God, no one knows how lonely I get and how empty I really feel." Presley would be preoccupied by such matters for much of his life, taking trunkloads of books with him on tour.

Presley showed interest in many beliefs, including Mormonism. A Book of Mormon owned by Presley (containing notes allegedly written by the singer) was given to <PERSON>Alan Osmond</PERSON> after his death, and is now said to be in the church's archives in <LOCATION>Salt Lake City</LOCATION> (A second copy may have been given to Presley by <PERSON>Mormon Ed Parker</PERSON>, a karate instructor). However, <PERSON>Linda Thompson</PERSON>, Presley's girlfriend after his separation, notes: "[H]e really followed the bible and he also was on a spiritual quest to find out how other people of other faiths lived their lives... he used to wear an Egyptian ankh, a star of <PERSON>David</PERSON> and a crucifix around his neck. And when people would say are you confused, he would say, no, not at all. I just don't want to miss heaven on a technicality."

In 1968, even Presley's version of <PERSON>Jerry Reed</PERSON>'s hook-laden "Guitar Man" had failed to enter the <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> Top 40. He continued to issue movie soundtrack albums that sold poorly compared to those of films like Blue <LOCATION>Hawaii</LOCATION> from 1961. It had also been nearly six years since the single "Good Luck Charm" had topped the Billboard Hot 100.

Presley was, by now, "profoundly" unhappy with his career. Colonel <PERSON>Parker</PERSON>'s plans once again included television, and he arranged for Presley to appear in his own special. The singer had not been on television since <PERSON>Frank Sinatra</PERSON>'s <ORGANIZATION>Timex</ORGANIZATION> special in May of 1960.. <PERSON>Parker</PERSON> shrewdly manoeuvred a deal with <ORGANIZATION>NBC</ORGANIZATION>'s <PERSON>Tom Sarnoff</PERSON> which included the network's commitment to financing a future Presley feature film--something that <PERSON>Parker</PERSON> had found increasingly difficult to secure.

The special was made in June, but was first aired on December 3, 1968 as a Christmas telecast called simply <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON>. Later dubbed the '68 Comeback Special by fans and critics, the show featured some lavishly staged studio productions. Other songs however, were performed live with a band in front of small audience--Presley's first live appearance as a performer since 1961. The live segments saw Presley clad in black leather, singing and playing guitar in an uninhibited style--reminiscent of his rock and roll days. <ORGANIZATION>Rolling Stone</ORGANIZATION> called it "a performance of emotional grandeur and historical resonance." <PERSON>Jon Landau</PERSON> in Eye magazine remarked: "There is something magical about watching a man who has lost himself find his way back home. He sang with the kind of power people no longer expect of rock 'n' roll singers. He moved his body with a lack of pretension and effort that must have made <PERSON>Jim Morrison</PERSON> green with envy." Its success was helped by director and co-producer, <PERSON>Steve Binder</PERSON>, who worked hard to reassure the nervous singer and to produce a show that was not just an hour of Christmas songs, as Colonel <PERSON>Parker</PERSON> had originally planned.
