By January, 1969, one of the key songs written specifically for the special, "If I Can Dream", reached number 12.. The soundtrack of the special also broke into the Top 10. On December 4, when the TV ratings were released, <ORGANIZATION>NBC</ORGANIZATION> reported that <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> had captured 42 percent of the total viewing audience. It was the network's number one rated show that season.

<PERSON>Jerry Schilling</PERSON> recalls: "The '68 special inspired <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON>; reminding him what he had not been able to do for years, being able to choose the people; being able to choose what songs and not being told what had to be on the soundtrack. What he had to wear. He was out of prison, man." <PERSON>Steve Binder</PERSON> said of Presley's reaction: "I played Elvis the 60-minute show, and he told me in the screening room, "<PERSON>Steve</PERSON>, it's the greatest thing I've ever done in my life. I give you my word I will never sing a song I don't believe in."

Buoyed by the experience, Presley engaged in the prolific series of recording sessions at <ORGANIZATION>American Sound Studios</ORGANIZATION>, which lead to the acclaimed From Elvis in <LOCATION>Memphis</LOCATION> (Chips Moman was its uncredited producer). It was followed by From Memphis To Vegas/From Vegas To <LOCATION>Memphis</LOCATION>, a double-album. The same sessions lead to the hit singles "In the Ghetto", "Suspicious Minds", "Kentucky Rain" and "Don't Cry Daddy".

In 1969, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> was keen to resume regular live performing. Following the success of <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON>, many new offers came in from around the world. The <LOCATION>London</LOCATION> Palladium wanted an "Elvis Spectacular" and offered <PERSON>Parker</PERSON> $28,000 for a one week engagement. He responded: "That's fine for me, now how much can you get for <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON>?". By May, the brand new International Hotel in <LOCATION>Las Vegas</LOCATION> announced that it had booked Presley; he was scheduled to perform from July 31, after Barbra Streisand opened the new venue.

Presley duly delivered fifty-seven shows over four weeks at the hotel, which had the largest showroom in the city. He had assembled some of the finest musicians--including an orchestra--and some of the best soul/gospel back-up singers available..

Despite such a prestigious backing, Presley was nervous; he had last appeared in <LOCATION>Las Vegas</LOCATION> at the New Frontier Hotel in 1956 and it was considered a disaster. <PERSON>Parker</PERSON> subsequently promoted <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON>' appearances all over <LOCATION>Las Vegas</LOCATION>; he rented every available billboard and took out full-page advertisements in local and trade papers. The lobby of the International prominently displayed Presley souvenirs; records, T-shirts, straw boaters and stuffed animals. <PERSON>Parker</PERSON> wanted to make sure that <PERSON>Presley</PERSON>'s return to the stage would be the show business event of the year, and hotel owner <PERSON>Kirk Kerkorian</PERSON> planned to send his own plane to <LOCATION>New York</LOCATION> to fly in the rock press for the debut performance.

<PERSON>Presley</PERSON> took to the stage with no introduction. The audience--which included <PERSON>Pat Boone</PERSON>, Fats Domino, <PERSON>Wayne Newton</PERSON>, <PERSON>Dick Clark</PERSON>, <PERSON>Ann-Margret</PERSON>, <PERSON>George Hamilton</PERSON>, <PERSON>Angie Dickinson</PERSON>, and <PERSON>Henry Mancini</PERSON>--gave him a standing ovation before he sang one note. After a successful performance, he returned to give an encore, of "Can't Help Falling in Love", and received his third standing ovation. Backstage, many celebrities and well-wishers, including <PERSON>Cary Grant</PERSON>, congratulated Presley on his triumphant return which, in the showroom alone, had generated over $1,500,000 in revenues.

Newsweek commented: "There are several unbelievable things about <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON>, but the most incredible is his staying power in a world where meteoric careers fade like shooting stars." <ORGANIZATION>Rolling Stone</ORGANIZATION> magazine declared Presley to be "supernatural, his own resurrection", while Variety proclaimed him a "superstar". At a press conference after his opening show, when a reporter referred to him as "The King", Presley pointed to Fats Domino, standing at the back of the room. "No," he said, "thats the real king of rock and roll."

The next day, <PERSON>Parker</PERSON>'s negotiations with the hotel resulted in a five-year contract for Presley to play each February and August, at a salary of $1 million per year.



In January 1970, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> returned to the International Hotel for a month-long engagement, performing two shows a night. RCA recorded some shows and the best material appeared on the album . In late February, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> performed six more attendance-breaking shows at the <LOCATION>Houston</LOCATION> Astrodome in Texas.In August at the <ORGANIZATION>International Hotel</ORGANIZATION>, <ORGANIZATION>MGM</ORGANIZATION> filmed rehearsal and concert footage for a documentary: . He wore a jumpsuit--a garment that would become a trademark of Presley's live performances in the 1970s. Although he had new hit singles in many countries, some were critical of his song choices and accused him of being distant from trends within contemporary music.

Around this time Presley was threatened with kidnapping at the International Hotel. Phone calls were received, one demanding $50,000; if unpaid, Presley would be killed by a "crazy man". The <ORGANIZATION>FBI</ORGANIZATION> took the threat seriously and security was stepped up for the next two shows. Presley went on stage with a Derringer in his right boot and a .45 in his waistband, but nothing untoward transpired. (The singer had had many threats of varying degrees since the fifties, many of them made without the singer's knowledge).

After closing his Las Vegas engagement on September 7, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> embarked on his first concert tour since 1958. Feeling exhausted, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> spent a month relaxing and recording before touring again in October and November. He would tour extensively in the <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> up to his death; many of the 1,145 concerts setting attendance records.

On December 21, 1970, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> met with President <PERSON>Richard Nixon</PERSON> at the White House (<LOCATION>Presley</LOCATION> arrived with a gift--a handgun. It was accepted but not presented for security reasons). <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> had engineered the encounter to express his patriotism, his contempt for the hippie drug culture and his wish to be appointed a "Federal Agent at Large". He also wished to obtain a <ORGANIZATION>Bureau of Narcotics and Dangerous Drugs</ORGANIZATION> badge to add to similar items he had begun collecting. He offered to "infiltrate hippie groups" and claimed that <ORGANIZATION>The Beatles</ORGANIZATION> had "made their money, then gone back to <LOCATION>England</LOCATION> where they fomented anti-American feeling." <PERSON>Nixon</PERSON> was uncertain and bemused by their encounter, and twice expressed concern that <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> needed to "retain his credibility". <PERSON>Ringo Starr</PERSON> later said he found it very sad to think Presley held such views. "This is Mr. <PERSON>Hips</PERSON>, the man, and he felt we were a danger. I think that the danger was mainly to him and his career." <PERSON>Paul McCartney</PERSON> said also that he "felt a bit betrayed ... The great joke was that we were taking drugs, and look what happened to [<PERSON>Elvis</PERSON>]. ... It was sad, but I still love him. ..."

On January 16, 1971 Presley was named 'One of the Ten Outstanding Young Men of the Nation' by the <ORGANIZATION>U.S. Junior Chamber of Commerce</ORGANIZATION> (The Jaycees). That summer, the City of <LOCATION>Memphis</LOCATION> named part of Highway 51 South "<ORGANIZATION>Elvis Presley Boulevard</ORGANIZATION>", and he won the Lifetime Achievement Award from the <ORGANIZATION>National Academy of Recording Arts and Sciences</ORGANIZATION> (the organization that presents Grammy awards).

In April 1972, <ORGANIZATION>MGM</ORGANIZATION> again filmed Presley, this time for Elvis on Tour, which won a 1972 Golden Globe for Best Documentary. A fourteen-date tour started with an unprecedented four consecutive sold-out shows at Madison Square Garden, <LOCATION>New York</LOCATION>. RCA taped the shows for a live album. After the tour, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> released the 1972 single "Burning Love"--his last top ten hit in the <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> charts.

Off stage, Presley had continuing problems. His lifelong friend, <PERSON>Joe Esposito</PERSON>, said <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> "could never be true to any one woman." It was widely known that <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> had several affairs during his 13-year union with <PERSON>Priscilla</PERSON>, notably with <PERSON>Ann-Margret</PERSON>. In spite of his own infidelity, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> was furious that <PERSON>Priscilla</PERSON> was having an affair with a mutual acquaintance--<PERSON>Mike Stone</PERSON>, a karate instructor she had met in 1971 backstage at one of Presley's concerts. It was Presley himself who first suggested <PERSON>Priscilla</PERSON> should take lessons from Stone. Once the news of their affair came to his attention, he raged obsessively: "There's too much pain in me... Stone [must] die." A bodyguard, <LOCATION>Red West</LOCATION>, felt compelled to get a price for a contract killing and was relieved when <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> decided: "Aw hell... Maybe it's a bit heavy..." The Presleys separated on February 23, 1972 and divorced on October 9, 1973, agreeing to share custody of their daughter.

Following his separation from <PERSON>Priscilla</PERSON>, he lived with <PERSON>Linda Thompson</PERSON>, a songwriter and one-time <LOCATION>Memphis</LOCATION> beauty queen, from July 1972 until just a few months before his death.

In January 1973, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> performed two charity concerts in <LOCATION>Hawaii</LOCATION> for the <PERSON>Kui Lee</PERSON> cancer foundation. The first (January 12) was primarily a practice run for the main show which was broadcast live on January 14 (The first show also served as a backup if technical problems affected the live broadcast). The "Aloha from <LOCATION>Hawaii</LOCATION>" concert was the world's first live concert satellite broadcast, reaching at least a billion viewers live and a further 500 million on delay. The show's album went to number one and spent a year in the charts. The album also proved to be Presley's last <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> Number One album during his lifetime.

After his divorce in 1973, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> became increasingly isolated and overweight, with prescription drugs affecting his health, mood and his stage act. He overdosed twice on barbiturates, spending three days in a coma in his hotel suite after the first. According to Dr. <PERSON>George C. Nichopoulos</PERSON>, Presley's physician, the singer was "near death" in November of 1973 because of side effects of Demerol addiction. <PERSON>Nichopoulos</PERSON> notes that the hospital admission "was crazy", because of the enormous attention Presley attracted, and the measures necessary to protect his medical details. Lab technicians were even exploiting Presley's ill-health by selling samples of his blood and urine.

In his book, Elvis: The Final Years, <PERSON>Jerry Hopkins</PERSON> writes: "<PERSON>Elvis</PERSON>' health plummeted as his weight ballooned." At a <ORGANIZATION>University of Maryland</ORGANIZATION> concert on September 27 (1974), band members "had trouble recognizing him. ... 'He walked on stage and held onto the mike for the first thirty minutes like it was a post. Everybody was scared.' Guitarist <PERSON>John Wilkinson</PERSON> ... recalled, ... 'He was all gut. He was slurring. ... It was obvious he was drugged, that there was something terribly wrong with his body. It was so bad, the words to the songs were barely intelligible. ... We were in a state of shock.' "

Despite this, his "thundering" live version of "How Great Thou Art" won him a Grammy award in 1974. <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> won three competitive "Grammies" for his gospel recordings: "How Great Thou Art"--the album, as well as the single--and for the album He Touched Me (1972). (He had fourteen nominations during his career, though it has been claimed that "<PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> has never been adequately appreciated by those who give the Grammies.")

In April 1974, rumours began that he would actually be playing overseas after years of offers. A $1,000,000 bid came in from a source in <LOCATION>Australia</LOCATION> for him to tour there, but Colonel <PERSON>Parker</PERSON> was uncharacteristically reluctant to accept such large sums. This prompted those closest to <LOCATION>Presley</LOCATION> to speculate about <PERSON>Parker</PERSON>'s past and circumstances, and the reasons for his apparent unwillingness to apply for a passport to travel abroad. He scotched any notions Presley had of overseas work by citing poor security in other countries, and the lack of suitable venues for a star of his status. Presley apparently accepted such excuses, at the time.

Presley continued to play to sell-out crowds in the <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION>; a 1975 tour ended with a concert in <LOCATION>Pontiac</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Michigan</LOCATION>, attended by over 62,000 fans. However the singer now had "no motivation to lose his extra poundage... he became self-conscious... his self-confidence before the audience declined... Headlines such as 'Elvis Battles Middle Age' and 'Time Makes Listless Machine of <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON>' were not uncommon." According to <PERSON>Marjorie Garber</PERSON>, when <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> made his later appearances in <LOCATION>Las Vegas</LOCATION>, he appeared "heavier, in pancake make-up... with an elaborate jewelled belt and cape, crooning pop songs to a microphone ... [He] had become <PERSON>Liberace</PERSON>. Even his fans were now middle-aged matrons and blue-haired grandmothers, ... Mother's Day became a special holiday for <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON>' fans."

On July 13, 1976, Presley's father fired "<ORGANIZATION>Memphis Mafia</ORGANIZATION>" bodyguards <ORGANIZATION>Red West</ORGANIZATION>, <PERSON>Sonny West</PERSON> and <PERSON>David Hebler</PERSON>. All three were taken by surprise, especially the <ORGANIZATION>Wests</ORGANIZATION>, who had been with Presley since the beginning of his career. <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> was away in <LOCATION>Palm Springs</LOCATION> when it happened, and some suggest the singer was too cowardly to face them himself. <PERSON>Vernon Presley</PERSON> cited the need to "cut back on expenses" when dismissing the three, but <PERSON>David Stanley</PERSON> has claimed that the real reason was that the bodyguards were becoming more outspoken over Presley's drug dependency. A "trusted associate" of Presley, <PERSON>John O'Grady</PERSON>, also stated, in agreement with <PERSON>Parker</PERSON> and <PERSON>Vernon Presley</PERSON>, that the bodyguards "were too rough with the fans... resulting in a lot of unnecessary lawsuits" and lawyers' fees. The Wests and <PERSON>Hebler</PERSON> would later write a devastating indictment of Presley in the book: Elvis: What Happened?, published August 1, 1977. (See: 'Final year and death').

Almost throughout the 1970s, RCA <PERSON>Victor</PERSON> had been increasingly concerned about making money from <LOCATION>Presley</LOCATION> material: they often had to rely on live recordings because of problems getting him to attend studio sessions. RCA <PERSON>Victor</PERSON>'s mobile studio was occasionally sent to Graceland in the hope of capturing an inspired vocal performance. Once in a studio, he could lack interest or be easily distracted; often this was linked to his health and drug problems.

In 2006, a journalist recalled: "<PERSON>Elvis Presley</PERSON> had [in 1977] become a grotesque caricature of his sleek, energetic former self... he was barely able to pull himself through his abbreviated concerts." In <LOCATION>Alexandria</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Louisiana</LOCATION>, the singer was on stage for less than an hour and "was impossible to understand." In <LOCATION>Baton Rouge</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> failed to appear: he was unable to get out of his hotel bed, and the rest of the tour was cancelled.In <LOCATION>Knoxville</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Tennessee</LOCATION> on May 20, "there was no longer any pretense of keeping up appearances. The idea was simply to get <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> out on stage and keep him upright..." Despite his obvious problems, shows in <LOCATION>Omaha</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Nebraska</LOCATION> and <LOCATION>Rapid City</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>South Dakota</LOCATION> were recorded for an album and a CBS-TV special: Elvis In Concert.

In <LOCATION>Rapid City</LOCATION>, "he was so nervous on stage that he could hardly talk... He was undoubtedly painfully aware of how he looked, and he knew that in his condition, he could not perform any significant movement." His performance in <LOCATION>Omaha</LOCATION> "exceeded everyone's worst fears... [giving] the impression of a man crying out for help..." According to <PERSON>Guralnick</PERSON>, fans "were becoming increasingly voluble about their disappointment, but it all seemed to go right past <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON>, whose world was now confined almost entirely to his room and his [spiritualism] books." A cousin, <PERSON>Billy Smith</PERSON>, recalled how Presley would sit in his room and chat, recounting things like his favorite <PERSON>Monty Python</PERSON> sketches and his own past japes, but "mostly there was a grim obsessiveness... a paranoia about people, germs... future events", that reminded <PERSON>Smith</PERSON> of <PERSON>Howard Hughes</PERSON>.



The book Elvis: What Happened? was published just fifteen days before Presley's death. It was the first expos to detail Presley's years of drug misuse, and was apparently the authors' revenge for them being sacked and also a plea to get Presley to recognize the extent of his drug problems. The singer "was devastated by the book. Here were his close friends who had written serious stuff that would affect his life. He felt betrayed."

Presley's final performance was in <LOCATION>Indianapolis</LOCATION> at the Market Square Arena, on June 26, 1977. According to many of his entourage who accompanied him on tour, it was the "best show he had given in a long time" with "some strong singing".

Another tour was scheduled to begin August 17, 1977, but at Graceland the day before, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> was found on his bathroom floor by fiance, <PERSON>Ginger Alden</PERSON>. According to the medical investigator, Presley had "stumbled or crawled several feet before he died"; he had apparently been using the toilet at the time. Death was officially pronounced at 3:30 pm at the <ORGANIZATION>Baptist Memorial Hospital</ORGANIZATION>.

Before his funeral, hundreds of thousands of fans, the press and celebrities lined the streets and many hoped to see the open casket in Graceland. One of Presley's cousins, <PERSON>Bobby Mann</PERSON>, accepted $18,000 to secretly photograph the corpse; the picture duly appeared on the cover of the <ORGANIZATION>National Enquirer</ORGANIZATION>, making it the largest and fastest selling issue of all time. Two days after the singer's death, a car ploughed into a group of 2000 fans outside <LOCATION>Presley</LOCATION>'s home, killing two women and critically injuring a third. Among the mourners at the funeral were <PERSON>Ann-Margret</PERSON> (who had remained close to Presley) and his ex-wife. <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> President <PERSON>Jimmy Carter</PERSON> issued a statement (See 'Legacy').

On Thursday, August 18, following a funeral service at Graceland, <PERSON>Elvis Presley</PERSON> was buried at <LOCATION>Forest Hill Cemetery</LOCATION> in <LOCATION>Memphis</LOCATION>, next to his mother. After an attempt to steal the body on August 28, and with no sign of security concerns at <LOCATION>Forest Hills</LOCATION> abating, his--and his mother's--remains were reburied at Graceland in the Meditation Garden in October.

Presley had developed many health problems during his life, some of them chronic. He started taking drugs (amphetamines) regularly in the army; it has also been claimed that pills of some form were first given to him by <PERSON>Memphis DJ Dewey Phillips</PERSON>. <PERSON>Priscilla Presley</PERSON> writes that by 1962, he was taking placidyls to combat severe insomnia in ever-increasing doses and later took Dexedrine to counter the sleeping pills' after-effects. She later saw "problems in Elvis' life, all magnified by taking prescribed drugs." Presley's physician, Dr. <PERSON>Nichopoulos</PERSON>, has said: "[<PERSON>Elvis</PERSON>] felt that by getting [pills] from a doctor, he wasn't the common everyday junkie getting something off the street. He... thought that as far as medications and drugs went, there was something for everything."

According to <PERSON>Guralnick</PERSON>: "[D]rug use was heavily implicated... no one ruled out the possibility of anaphylactic shock brought on by the codeine pills... to which he was known to have had a mild allergy." In two lab reports filed two months later, each indicated "a strong belief that the primary cause of death was polypharmacy," with one report "indicating the detection of fourteen drugs in Elvis' system, ten in significant quantity." It appears he died of Combined Drug Intoxication.

The medical profession has been seriously questioned. Medical Examiner Dr. <PERSON>Jerry Francisco</PERSON> had publicly offered a cause of death while the autopsy was still being performed, but before toxicology results were known. Dr. <PERSON>Francisco</PERSON> dubiously stated that cardiac arrhythmia was the cause of death, a condition that can only be determined in a living person--not post mortem. Many doctors had been flattered to be associated with Presley (or had been bribed with gifts) and supplied him with pills, which simply fed his addictions. The singer allegedly spent at least $1 million annually during his latter years on drugs and doctors' fees or inducements. Although Dr. <PERSON>Nichopoulos</PERSON> was exonerated with regard to <PERSON>Presley</PERSON>'s death, "In the first eight months of 1977 alone, he had [prescribed] more than 10,000 doses of sedatives, amphetamines, and narcotics: all in <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON>' name. On January 20, 1980, the board found [against] him... but decided that he was not unethical [because he claimed he'd been trying to wean the singer off the drugs]." His license was suspended. In July 1995, it was permanently revoked after it was found he had improperly dispensed drugs to several patients including <PERSON>Jerry Lee Lewis</PERSON>.

In 1994, the autopsy into Presley's death was re-opened. Coroner Dr. <PERSON>Joseph Davis</PERSON> declared: "There is nothing in any of the data that supports a death from drugs [i.e. drug overdose]. In fact, everything points to a sudden, violent heart attack." However, there is little doubt that long-term drug abuse caused his premature death.



Author <PERSON>Samuel Roy</PERSON> has argued: "<PERSON>Elvis</PERSON>' death did occur at a time when it could only help his reputation. Just before his death, <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> had been forgotten by society."

Biographer <PERSON>Ernst Jorgensen</PERSON> has observed that when <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> died, it was as if all perspective on his musical career had been lost. His latter-day song choices had been seen as poor; many who disliked Presley had long been dismissive because he did not write his own songs. Others complained--incorrectly--that he could not play musical instruments. Such criticism of Presley continues. The tabloids had ridiculed his obesity and his kitschy, jump-suited performances. Comedian <PERSON>George Carlin</PERSON> remarked, "<LOCATION>America</LOCATION> got what it deserved in Elvis Presley: a big fat, drug-addicted squealer." <PERSON>Sade Adu</PERSON> said about Presley: "when I see him in his fifties movies, Jailhouse Rock and <PERSON>King Creole</PERSON>, that's an image I desire to look like. But when he's in his jumpsuit I just think of him as a drag queen." His sixties' film career was mocked. (In 1980, <PERSON>John Lennon</PERSON> said: "[Presley] died when he went into the army. That's when they killed him, that's when they castrated him.") Acknowledgment of his vocal style had been reduced to mocking the hiccuping, vocalese tricks that he had used on some early recordings--and to the way he said "Thankyouverymuch" after songs during live shows. This was only countered by the uncritical adulation of die-hard fans, who had even denied that he looked "fat" before he died. Any wish to understand <PERSON>Elvis Presley</PERSON>--his genuine abilities and his real influence--"seemed almost totally obscured."

Presley has featured prominently in a variety of polls and surveys designed to measure popularity and influence. However, sociologist <PERSON>Philip Ennis</PERSON> writes: "Perhaps it is an error of enthusiasm to freight <PERSON>Elvis Presley</PERSON> with too heavy a historical load" because, according to a opinion poll of high school students in 1957, <PERSON>Pat Boone</PERSON> was nearly the "two-to-one favorite over <PERSON>Elvis Presley</PERSON> among boys and preferred almost three-to-one by girls..." However, <PERSON>Presley</PERSON>'s early music and live performances are credited with helping to lay a commercial foundation which allowed established African American acts of the 1950s to receive due recognition. Performers like Fats Domino, <PERSON>Chuck Berry</PERSON> and <PERSON>Little Richard</PERSON>, came to national prominence after Presley's mix of musical styles was accepted among White American teenagers. Rather than Presley being seen as a white man who 'stole black music', <PERSON>Little Richard</PERSON> argued: "He was an integrator, <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> was a blessing. They wouldn't let black music through. He opened the door for black music." <PERSON>Al Green</PERSON> agreed, saying; "He broke the ice for all of us." It has also been claimed that Presley's sound and persona helped to relax the rigid color line and thereby fed the fires of the civil rights movement.

In the late 1960s, composer and conductor <PERSON>Leonard Bernstein</PERSON> remarked: "Elvis is the greatest cultural force in the twentieth century. He introduced the beat to everything, music, language, clothes, it's a whole new social revolution... the 60's comes from it."

Other celebrated pop and rock musicians have acknowledged that the young Presley inspired them. The Beatles were all big <LOCATION>Presley</LOCATION> fans. <PERSON>John Lennon</PERSON> said: "Nothing really affected me until I heard <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON>. If there hadn't been an <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON>, there wouldn't have been a Beatles." Deep Purple's <PERSON>Ian Gillan</PERSON> said: "For a young singer he was an absolute inspiration. I soaked up what he did like blotting paper... you learn by copying the maestro." <PERSON>Rod Stewart</PERSON> declared: "<PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> was the King. No doubt about it. People like myself, <PERSON>Mick Jagger</PERSON> and all the others only followed in his footsteps." <PERSON>Cher</PERSON> recalls from seeing <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> live in 1956 that he made her "realize the tremendous effect a performer could have on an audience." <PERSON>Bob Dylan</PERSON> said: "When I first heard <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON>' voice I just knew that I wasn't going to work for anybody; and nobody was going to be my boss. Hearing him for the first time was like busting out of jail."

By 1958, singers obviously adopting Presley's style, like <PERSON>Marty Wilde</PERSON> and <PERSON>Cliff Richard</PERSON> (the so-called "British Elvis"), were rising to prominence in the <LOCATION>UK</LOCATION>. Elsewhere, <LOCATION>France</LOCATION>'s <PERSON>Johnny Hallyday</PERSON> and the Italians <PERSON>Adriano Celentano</PERSON> and <PERSON>Bobby Solo</PERSON> were also heavily influenced by <PERSON>Presley</PERSON>.

Presley's recorded voice is seen by many as his enduring legacy. Music critic <PERSON>Henry Pleasants</PERSON> writes: "<PERSON>Elvis Presley</PERSON> has been described variously as a baritone and a tenor. An extraordinary compass... and a very wide range of vocal color have something to do with this divergence of opinion. The voice covers two octaves and a third ... Moreover, he has not been confined to one type of vocal production. In ballads and country songs he belts out full-voiced high G's and A's that an opera baritone might envy. He is a naturally assimilative stylist with a multiplicity of voices--in fact, <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON>' is an extraordinary voice, or many voices." It has also been noted that "Presleys comprehensive musical knowledge and talent also surprised and impressed songwriters <PERSON>Jerry Leiber</PERSON> and <PERSON>Mike Stoller</PERSON>... [They] considered Presley to be an 'idiot savant' because he knew so many songs. His knowledge of the blues especially impressed them. <PERSON>Leiber</PERSON> remembers that <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> "could imitate anything he heard. He had a perfect ear,"... Presley could sing and/or play a song on the piano after hearing it only once or twice. His natural ear for music, ability to play by ear, and to improvise were well known to his friends and musical associates."

Gospel tenor <PERSON>Shawn Nielsen</PERSON>, who sang backing vocals for Presley on tour, said: "He could sing anything. I've never seen such versatility... He had such great soul. He had the ability to make everyone in the audience think that he was singing directly to them. He just had a way with communication that was totally unique."

Presley's informal jamming in front of a small audience in the '68 Comeback Special is regarded as a forerunner of the so-called 'Unplugged' concept, later popularized by <ORGANIZATION>MTV</ORGANIZATION>.

The singer has been inducted into four music 'Halls of Fame': the Rock and Roll Hall of Fame (1986), the Rockabilly Hall of Fame (1997), the Country Music Hall of Fame (1998), and the Gospel Music Hall of Fame (2001). In 1984, he received the W. C. Handy Award from the <ORGANIZATION>Blues Foundation</ORGANIZATION> and the Academy of Country Musics first Golden Hat Award. In 1987, he received the American Music Awards first posthumous presentation of the Award of Merit.

Presley has a star on the Hollywood Walk of Fame at 7080 <LOCATION>Hollywood Boulevard</LOCATION>. He was also honored by the <ORGANIZATION>Mississippi Blues Commission</ORGANIZATION> with a <ORGANIZATION>Mississippi Blues Trail</ORGANIZATION> historic marker placed in <LOCATION>Tupelo</LOCATION>, his birth place, in recognition of his contribution to the development of the blues in <LOCATION>Mississippi</LOCATION>.

In 1994, the 40th anniversary of Presley's "That's All Right" was recognized with its re-release, which made the charts worldwide, making top three in the <LOCATION>UK</LOCATION>.

During the 2002 World Cup a Junkie XL remix of his "A Little Less Conversation" (credited as "Elvis Vs JXL") topped the charts in over twenty countries and was included in a compilation of Presley's <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> and <LOCATION>UK</LOCATION> number one hits, .

In the <LOCATION>UK</LOCATION> charts (January 2005), three re-issued singles again went to number one ("Jailhouse Rock", "One Night"/"I Got Stung" and "It's Now or Never"). Throughout the year, twenty singles were re-issued--all making top five.

In the same year, <ORGANIZATION>Forbes</ORGANIZATION> magazine named <PERSON>Presley</PERSON>, for the fifth straight year, the top-earning deceased celebrity, grossing US$45 million for the Presley estate during the preceding year. In mid-2006, top place was taken by <PERSON>Nirvana</PERSON>'s <PERSON>Kurt Cobain</PERSON> after the sale of his song catalogue, but <PERSON>Presley</PERSON> reclaimed the top spot in 2007.

<PERSON>Paul F. Campos</PERSON> has written: "The <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> cult touches on so many crucial nerves of American popular culture: the ascent of a workingclass boy from the most obscure backwater to international fame and fortune; the white man with the soul of black music in his voice; the performer whose music tied together the main strands of American folk music country, rhythm and blues, and gospel; and, perhaps most compellingly for a weight-obsessed nation, the sexiest man in <LOCATION>America</LOCATION>'s gradual transformation into a fat, sweating parody of his former self, straining the bounds of a jewel-encrusted bodysuit on a Las Vegas stage. The images of fat <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> and thin <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> live together in the popular imagination." The singer continues to be imitated--and parodied--outside the main music industry and Presley songs remain very popular on the karaoke circuit. People from a diversity of cultures and backgrounds work as <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> impersonators ("the raw 1950s <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> and the kitschy 1970s <PERSON>Elvis</PERSON> are the favorites.")

<TITLE><PERSON>Edgar Allan Poe</PERSON></TITLE>

<PERSON>Edgar Allan Poe</PERSON> (January 19, 1809  October 7, 1849) was an American poet, short-story writer, editor and literary critic, and is considered part of the American Romantic Movement. Best known for his tales of mystery and the macabre, <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> was one of the earliest American practitioners of the short story and is considered the inventor of the detective-fiction genre. He is further credited with contributing to the emerging genre of science fiction. He was the first well-known American writer to try to earn a living through writing alone, resulting in a financially difficult life and career.

Born as <PERSON>Edgar Poe</PERSON> in <LOCATION>Boston</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Massachusetts</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s parents died when he was young. <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> was taken in by <PERSON>John</PERSON> and <PERSON>Frances Allan</PERSON>, of <LOCATION>Richmond</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Virginia</LOCATION>, but they never formally adopted him. After spending a short period at the <ORGANIZATION>University of Virginia</ORGANIZATION> and briefly attempting a military career, <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> and the Allans parted ways. <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s publishing career began humbly, with an anonymous collection of poems, <LOCATION>Tamerlane</LOCATION> and Other Poems (1827), credited only to "a Bostonian".

<PERSON>Poe</PERSON> switched his focus to prose and spent the next several years working for literary journals and periodicals, becoming known for his own style of literary criticism. His work forced him to move between several cities, including <LOCATION>Baltimore</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION> and <LOCATION>New York City</LOCATION>. In <LOCATION>Baltimore</LOCATION> in 1835, he married <PERSON>Virginia Clemm</PERSON>, his 13-year-old cousin. In January 1845, <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> published his poem "The Raven" to instant success. His wife died of tuberculosis two years later. He began planning to produce his own journal, The <LOCATION>Penn</LOCATION> (later renamed The Stylus), though he died before it could be produced. On October 7, 1849, at age 40, <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> died in <LOCATION>Baltimore</LOCATION>; the cause of his death is unknown and has been attributed to alcohol, brain congestion, cholera, drugs, heart disease, rabies, suicide, tuberculosis, and other agents.

<PERSON>Poe</PERSON> and his works influenced literature in the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> and around the world, as well as in specialized fields, such as cosmology and cryptography. <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> and his work appear throughout popular culture in literature, music, films, and television. A number of his homes are dedicated museums today.



Born <PERSON>Edgar Poe</PERSON> in <LOCATION>Boston</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Massachusetts</LOCATION>, on January 19, 1809, the second child of actress <PERSON>Elizabeth Arnold Hopkins Poe</PERSON> and actor <PERSON>David Poe</PERSON>, Jr. He had an elder brother, <PERSON>William Henry Leonard Poe</PERSON>, and a younger sister, <PERSON>Rosalie Poe</PERSON>. <PERSON>Edgar</PERSON> may have been named after a character in <PERSON>William Shakespeare</PERSON>'s King <PERSON>Lear</PERSON>, a play the couple was performing in 1809. His father abandoned their family in 1810, and his mother died a year later from consumption. <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> was then taken into the home of <PERSON>John Allan</PERSON>, a successful Scottish merchant in <LOCATION>Richmond</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Virginia</LOCATION>, who dealt in a variety of goods including tobacco, cloth, wheat, tombstones, and slaves. The Allans served as a foster family but never formally adopted <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>, though they gave him the name "<PERSON>Edgar Allan Poe</PERSON>".

The <PERSON>Allan</PERSON> family had <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> baptized in the <ORGANIZATION>Episcopal Church</ORGANIZATION> in 1812. <PERSON>John Allan</PERSON> alternately spoiled and aggressively disciplined his foster son. The family, including <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> and <PERSON>Allan</PERSON>'s wife, <PERSON>Frances Valentine Allan</PERSON>, sailed to <LOCATION>England</LOCATION> in 1815. <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> attended the grammar school in <LOCATION>Irvine</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Scotland</LOCATION> (where <PERSON>John Allan</PERSON> was born) for a short period in 1815, before rejoining the family in <LOCATION>London</LOCATION> in 1816. He studied at a boarding school in <LOCATION>Chelsea</LOCATION> until summer 1817. He was subsequently entered at the Reverend <ORGANIZATION>John Bransbys Manor House School</ORGANIZATION> at <LOCATION>Stoke Newington</LOCATION>, then a suburb four miles (6 km) north of <LOCATION>London</LOCATION>.

<PERSON>Poe</PERSON> moved back with the <LOCATION>Allans</LOCATION> to <LOCATION>Richmond</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Virginia</LOCATION> in 1820. In March 1825, <PERSON>John Allan</PERSON>'s uncle and business benefactor <PERSON>William Galt</PERSON>, said to be one of the wealthiest men in <LOCATION>Richmond</LOCATION>, died and left <PERSON>Allan</PERSON> several acres of real estate. The inheritance was estimated at $750,000. By summer 1825, <PERSON>Allan</PERSON> celebrated his expansive wealth by purchasing a two-story brick home named <LOCATION>Moldavia</LOCATION>. <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> may have become engaged to <PERSON>Sarah Elmira Royster</PERSON> before he registered at the one-year old <ORGANIZATION>University of Virginia</ORGANIZATION> in February 1826 to study languages. The university, in its infancy, was established on the ideals of its founder, <PERSON>Thomas Jefferson</PERSON>. It had strict rules against gambling, horses, guns, tobacco and alcohol, but these rules were generally ignored. <PERSON>Jefferson</PERSON> had enacted a system of student self-government, the Honor System, allowing students to choose their own studies, make their own arrangements for boarding, and report all wrongdoing to the faculty. The unique system was still in chaos, and there was a high drop-out rate. During his time there, <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> lost touch with <PERSON>Royster</PERSON> and also became estranged from his foster father over gambling debts. <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> claimed that <PERSON>Allan</PERSON> had not given him sufficient money to register for classes, purchase texts, and procure and furnish a dormitory. <PERSON>Allan</PERSON> did send additional money and clothes, but <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s debts increased. <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> gave up on the university after a year, and, not feeling welcome in <LOCATION>Richmond</LOCATION>, especially when he learned that his sweetheart <PERSON>Royster</PERSON> had married <PERSON>Alexander Shelton</PERSON>, he traveled to <LOCATION>Boston</LOCATION> in April 1827, sustaining himself with odd jobs as a clerk and newspaper writer. At some point he started using the pseudonym <PERSON>Henri Le Rennet</PERSON>.'''



Unable to support himself, on May 27, 1827, <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> enlisted in the <ORGANIZATION>United States Army</ORGANIZATION> as a private. Using the name "<PERSON>Edgar A. Perry</PERSON>", he claimed he was  even though he was 18. He first served at <LOCATION>Fort Independence</LOCATION> in <LOCATION>Boston Harbor</LOCATION> for five dollars a month. That same year, he released his first book, a 40-page collection of poetry, <LOCATION>Tamerlane</LOCATION> and Other Poems, attributed with the byline "by a Bostonian". Only 50 copies were printed, and the book received virtually no attention. <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s regiment was posted to <LOCATION>Fort Moultrie</LOCATION> in <LOCATION>Charleston</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>South Carolina</LOCATION> and traveled by ship on the brig <LOCATION>Waltham</LOCATION> on November 8, 1827. <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> was promoted to "artificer", an enlisted tradesman who prepared shells for artillery, and had his monthly pay doubled. After serving for two years and attaining the rank of Sergeant Major for Artillery (the highest rank a noncommissioned officer can achieve), <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> sought to end his five-year enlistment early. He revealed his real name and his circumstances to his commanding officer, Lieutenant <PERSON>Howard</PERSON>. <PERSON>Howard</PERSON> would only allow <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> to be discharged if he reconciled with <PERSON>John Allan</PERSON> and wrote a letter to <PERSON>Allan</PERSON>, who was unsympathetic. Several months passed and pleas to <PERSON>Allan</PERSON> were ignored; <PERSON>Allan</PERSON> may not have written to <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> even to make him aware of his foster mother's illness. <PERSON>Frances Allan</PERSON> died on February 28, 1829, and <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> visited the day after her burial. Perhaps softened by his wife's death, <PERSON>John Allan</PERSON> agreed to support <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s attempt to be discharged in order to receive an appointment to the <ORGANIZATION>United States Military Academy</ORGANIZATION> at <LOCATION>West Point</LOCATION>.

<PERSON>Poe</PERSON> finally was discharged on April 15, 1829, after securing a replacement to finish his enlisted term for him. Before entering <LOCATION>West Point</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> moved back to <LOCATION>Baltimore</LOCATION> for a time, to stay with his widowed aunt <PERSON>Maria Clemm</PERSON>, her daughter, <PERSON>Virginia Eliza Clemm</PERSON> (<PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s first cousin), his brother <PERSON>Henry</PERSON>, and his invalid grandmother <PERSON>Elizabeth Cairnes Poe</PERSON>. Meanwhile, <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> published his second book, <PERSON>Al Aaraaf</PERSON>, <LOCATION>Tamerlane</LOCATION> and Minor Poems, in <LOCATION>Baltimore</LOCATION> in 1829.

<PERSON>Poe</PERSON> traveled to <LOCATION>West Point</LOCATION> and matriculated as a cadet on July 1, 1830. In October 1830, <PERSON>John Allan</PERSON> married his second wife, <PERSON>Louisa Patterson</PERSON>. The marriage, and bitter quarrels with <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> over the children born to <PERSON>Allan</PERSON> out of affairs, led to the foster father finally disowning <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>. <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> decided to leave <LOCATION>West Point</LOCATION> by purposely getting court-martialed. On February 8, 1831, he was tried for gross neglect of duty and disobedience of orders for refusing to attend formations, classes, or church. <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> tactically pled not guilty to induce dismissal, knowing he would be found guilty.

He left for <LOCATION>New York</LOCATION> in February 1831, and released a third volume of poems, simply titled Poems. The book was financed with help from his fellow cadets at <LOCATION>West Point</LOCATION>, many of whom donated 75 cents to the cause, raising a total of $170. They may have been expecting verses similar to the satirical ones <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> had been writing about commanding officers. Printed by <PERSON>Elam Bliss</PERSON> of <LOCATION>New York</LOCATION>, it was labeled as "Second Edition" and included a page saying, "To the <ORGANIZATION>U.S. Corps of Cadets</ORGANIZATION> this volume is respectfully dedicated." The book once again reprinted the long poems "<LOCATION>Tamerlane</LOCATION>" and "Al Aaraaf" but also six previously unpublished poems including early versions of "To Helen", "Israfel", and "<LOCATION>The City</LOCATION> in the Sea". He returned to <LOCATION>Baltimore</LOCATION>, to his aunt, brother and cousin, in March 1831. His elder brother <PERSON>Henry</PERSON>, who had been in ill health in part due to problems with alcoholism, died on August 1, 1831.

After his brother's death, <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> began more earnest attempts to start his career as a writer. He chose a difficult time in American publishing to do so. He was the first well-known American to try to live by writing alone and was hampered by the lack of an international copyright law. Publishers often pirated copies of British works rather than paying for new work by Americans. The industry was also particularly hurt by the Panic of 1837. Despite a booming growth in American periodicals around this time period, fueled in part by new technology, many did not last beyond a few issues and publishers often refused to pay their writers or paid them much later than they promised. <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>, throughout his attempts at pursuing a successful literary career, would be forced to constantly make humiliating pleas for money and other assistance for the rest of his life.

After his early attempts at poetry, <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> had turned his attention to prose. He placed a few stories with a <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION> publication and began work on his only drama, Politian. The Saturday Visitor, a <LOCATION>Baltimore</LOCATION> paper, awarded <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> a prize in October 1833 for his short story "MS. Found in a Bottle". The story brought him to the attention of <PERSON>John P. Kennedy</PERSON>, a Baltimorian of considerable means. He helped <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> place some of his stories, and introduced him to <PERSON>Thomas W. White</PERSON>, editor of the Southern Literary Messenger in <LOCATION>Richmond</LOCATION>. <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> became assistant editor of the periodical in August 1835; however, within a few weeks, he was discharged after being found drunk repeatedly. Returning to <LOCATION>Baltimore</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> secretly married <LOCATION>Virginia</LOCATION>, his cousin, on September 22, 1835. She was 13 at the time, though she is listed on the marriage certificate as being 21. Reinstated by White after promising good behavior, <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> went back to <LOCATION>Richmond</LOCATION> with <LOCATION>Virginia</LOCATION> and her mother. He remained at the Messenger until January 1837. During this period, its circulation increased from 700 to 3,500. He published several poems, book reviews, criticism, and stories in the paper. On May 16, 1836, he had a second wedding ceremony in <LOCATION>Richmond</LOCATION> with <PERSON>Virginia Clemm</PERSON>, this time in public.

The Narrative of <PERSON>Arthur Gordon Pym</PERSON> was published and widely reviewed in 1838. In the summer of 1839, <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> became assistant editor of <PERSON>Burton</PERSON>'s Gentleman's Magazine. He published numerous articles, stories, and reviews, enhancing his reputation as a trenchant critic that he had established at the Southern Literary Messenger. Also in 1839, the collection Tales of the Grotesque and <PERSON>Arabesque</PERSON> was published in two volumes, though he made little money off of it and it received mixed reviews. <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> left <PERSON>Burton</PERSON>'s after about a year and found a position as assistant at <PERSON>Graham</PERSON>'s Magazine.

In June 1840, <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> published a prospectus announcing his intentions to start his own journal, The Stylus. Originally, <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> intended to call the journal The <LOCATION>Penn</LOCATION>, as it would have been based in <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION>. In the June 6, 1840 issue of <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION>'s Saturday Evening Post, <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> bought advertising space for his prospectus: "Prospectus of the <ORGANIZATION>Penn Magazine</ORGANIZATION>, a Monthly Literary journal to be edited and published in the city of <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION> by <PERSON>Edgar A. Poe</PERSON>." The journal would never be produced before <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s death. Around this time, he attempted to secure a position with the <PERSON>Tyler</PERSON> administration, claiming he was a member of the <ORGANIZATION>Whig Party</ORGANIZATION>. He hoped to be appointed to the <ORGANIZATION>Custom House</ORGANIZATION> in <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION> with help from President <PERSON>Tyler</PERSON>'s son <PERSON>Robert</PERSON>, an acquaintance of <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s friend <PERSON>Frederick Thomas</PERSON>. <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> failed to show up for a meeting with <PERSON>Thomas</PERSON> to discuss the appointment in mid-September 1842, claiming to be sick, though <PERSON>Thomas</PERSON> believed he was drunk. Though he was promised an appointment, all positions were filled by others.

One evening in January 1842, <LOCATION>Virginia</LOCATION> showed the first signs of consumption, now known as tuberculosis, while singing and playing the piano. <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> described it as breaking a blood vessel in her throat. She only partially recovered. <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> began to drink more heavily under the stress of <LOCATION>Virginia</LOCATION>'s illness. He left <PERSON>Graham</PERSON>'s and attempted to find a new position, for a time angling for a government post. He returned to <LOCATION>New York</LOCATION>, where he worked briefly at the Evening Mirror before becoming editor of the Broadway Journal and, later, sole owner. There he alienated himself from other writers by publicly accusing <PERSON>Henry Wadsworth Longfellow</PERSON> of plagiarism, though <PERSON>Longfellow</PERSON> never responded. On January 29, 1845, his poem "The Raven" appeared in the Evening Mirror and became a popular sensation. Though it made <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> a household name almost instantly, he only was paid $9 for its publication.

 <ORGANIZATION>Broadway Journal</ORGANIZATION> failed in 1846. <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> moved to a cottage in the <LOCATION>Fordham</LOCATION> section of <LOCATION>The Bronx</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>New York</LOCATION>. That home, known today as the "Poe Cottage", is on the southeast corner of the Grand Concourse and <LOCATION>Kingsbridge Road</LOCATION>. Virginia died there on January 30, 1847. Biographers and critics often suggest <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s frequent theme of the "death of a beautiful woman" stems from the repeated loss of women throughout his life, including his wife.

Increasingly unstable after his wife's death, <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> attempted to court the poet <PERSON>Sarah Helen Whitman</PERSON>, who lived in <LOCATION>Providence</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Rhode Island</LOCATION>. Their engagement failed, purportedly because of <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s drinking and erratic behavior. However, there is also strong evidence that <PERSON>Whitman</PERSON>'s mother intervened and did much to derail their relationship. <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> then returned to <LOCATION>Richmond</LOCATION> and resumed a relationship with a childhood sweetheart, <PERSON>Sarah Elmira Royster</PERSON>.

On October 3, 1849, <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> was found on the streets of <LOCATION>Baltimore</LOCATION> delirious, "in great distress, and... in need of immediate assistance", according to the man who found him, <PERSON>Joseph W. Walker</PERSON>. He was taken to the <LOCATION>Washington College Hospital</LOCATION>, where he died on Sunday, October 7, 1849, at 5:00 in the morning. <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> was never coherent long enough to explain how he came to be in his dire condition, and, oddly, was wearing clothes that were not his own. <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> is said to have repeatedly called out the name "Reynolds" on the night before his death, though it is unclear to whom he was referring. Some sources say <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s final words were "Lord help my poor soul." All medical records, including his death certificate, have been lost. Newspapers at the time reported <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s death as "congestion of the brain" or "cerebral inflammation", common euphemisms for deaths from disreputable causes such as alcoholism. However, the actual cause of death remains a mystery; from as early as 1872, cooping was commonly believed to have been the cause, and speculation has included delirium tremens, heart disease, epilepsy, syphilis, meningeal inflammation, cholera and rabies.

The day <PERSON>Edgar Allan Poe</PERSON> was buried, a long obituary appeared in the <LOCATION>New York</LOCATION> <ORGANIZATION>Tribune</ORGANIZATION> signed "Ludwig". It was soon published throughout the country. The piece began, "<PERSON>Edgar Allan Poe</PERSON> is dead. He died in <LOCATION>Baltimore</LOCATION> the day before yesterday. This announcement will startle many, but few will be grieved by it." "Ludwig" was soon identified as <PERSON>Rufus Wilmot Griswold</PERSON>, an editor, critic and anthologist who had borne a grudge against <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> since 1842. <PERSON>Griswold</PERSON> somehow became <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s literary executor and attempted to destroy his enemy's reputation after his death.

<PERSON>Rufus Griswold</PERSON> wrote a biographical article of <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> called "Memoir of the Author", which he included in an 1850 volume of the collected works. <PERSON>Griswold</PERSON> depicted <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> as a depraved, drunk, drug-addled madman and included <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s letters as evidence. Many of his claims were either outright lies or distorted half-truths. For example, it is now known that <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> was not a drug addict. <PERSON>Griswold</PERSON>'s book was denounced by those who knew <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> well, but it became a popularly accepted one. This occurred in part because it was the only full biography available and was widely reprinted and in part because readers thrilled at the thought of reading works by an "evil" man. Letters that <PERSON>Griswold</PERSON> presented as proof of this depiction of <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> were later revealed as forgeries.



<PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s best known fiction works are Gothic, a genre he followed to appease the public taste. His most recurring themes deal with questions of death, including its physical signs, the effects of decomposition, concerns of premature burial, the reanimation of the dead, and mourning. Many of his works are generally considered part of the dark romanticism genre, a literary reaction to transcendentalism, which <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> strongly disliked. He referred to followers of the movement as "Frogpondians" after the pond on <ORGANIZATION>Boston Common</ORGANIZATION>. and ridiculed their writings as "metaphor-run", lapsing into "obscurity for obscurity's sake" or "mysticism for mysticism's sake." <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> once wrote in a letter to <PERSON>Thomas Holley Chivers</PERSON> that he did not dislike Transcendentalists, "only the pretenders and sophists among them."

Beyond horror, <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> also wrote satires, humor tales, and hoaxes. For comic effect, he used irony and ludicrous extravagance, often in an attempt to liberate the reader from cultural conformity. In fact, "Metzengerstein", the first story that <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> is known to have published, and his first foray into horror, was originally intended as a burlesque satirizing the popular genre. <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> also reinvented science fiction, responding in his writing to emerging technologies such as hot air balloons in "The Balloon-Hoax".

<PERSON>Poe</PERSON> wrote much of his work using themes specifically catered for mass market tastes. To that end, his fiction often included elements of popular pseudosciences such as phrenology and physiognomy.

<PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s writing reflects his literary theories, which he presented in his criticism and also in essays such as "The Poetic Principle". He disliked didacticism and allegory, though he believed that meaning in literature should be an undercurrent just beneath the surface. Works with obvious meanings, he wrote, cease to be art. He believed that quality work should be brief and focus on a specific single effect. To that end, he believed that the writer should carefully calculate every sentiment and idea. In "The Philosophy of Composition", an essay in which <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> describes his method in writing "The Raven", he claims to have strictly followed this method. It has been questioned, however, if he really followed this system. <PERSON>T. S. Eliot</PERSON> said: "It is difficult for us to read that essay without reflecting that if <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> plotted out his poem with such calculation, he might have taken a little more pains over it: the result hardly does credit to the method." Biographer <PERSON>Joseph Wood Krutch</PERSON> described the essay as "a rather highly ingenious exercise in the art of rationalization".

During his lifetime, <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> was mostly recognized as a literary critic. Fellow critic <PERSON>James Russell Lowell</PERSON> called him "the most discriminating, philosophical, and fearless critic upon imaginative works who has written in <LOCATION>America</LOCATION>", though he questioned if he occasionally used prussic acid instead of ink. <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> was also known as a writer of fiction and became one of the first American authors of the 19th century to become more popular in <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION> than in the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION>. <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> is particularly respected in <LOCATION>France</LOCATION>, in part due to early translations by <PERSON>Charles Baudelaire</PERSON>. <PERSON>Baudelaire</PERSON>'s translations became definitive renditions of <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s work throughout <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION>.

<PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s early detective fiction tales starring the fictitious <PERSON>C. Auguste Dupin</PERSON> laid the groundwork for future detectives in literature. Sir <PERSON>Arthur Conan Doyle</PERSON> said, "Each [of <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s detective stories] is a root from which a whole literature has developed.... Where was the detective story until <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> breathed the breath of life into it?" The Mystery Writers of <LOCATION>America</LOCATION> have named their awards for excellence in the genre the "Edgars". <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s work also influenced science fiction, notably <PERSON>Jules Verne</PERSON>, who wrote a sequel to <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s novel The Narrative of <PERSON>Arthur Gordon Pym</PERSON> of <LOCATION>Nantucket</LOCATION> called The Narrative of <PERSON>Arthur Gordon Pym</PERSON>, Le sphinx des glaces. Science fiction author <PERSON>H. G. Wells</PERSON> noted, "<PERSON>Pym</PERSON> tells what a very intelligent mind could imagine about the south polar region a century ago."

Like many famous artists, <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s works have spawned innumerable imitators. One interesting trend among imitators of <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>, however, has been claims by clairvoyants or psychics to be "channeling" poems from <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s spirit. One of the most notable of these was <PERSON>Lizzie Doten</PERSON>, who in 1863 published Poems from the Inner Life, in which she claimed to have "received" new compositions by <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s spirit. The compositions were re-workings of famous <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> poems such as "The Bells", but which reflected a new, positive outlook.

Even so, <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> has not received only praise. <PERSON>William Butler Yeats</PERSON> was generally critical of <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> and denounced him as "vulgar". Transcendentalist <PERSON>Ralph Waldo Emerson</PERSON> reacted to "The Raven" by saying, "I see nothing in it" and derisively referred to <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> as "the jingle man". <PERSON>Aldous Huxley</PERSON> wrote that <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s writing "falls into vulgarity" by being "too poetical" - the equivalent of wearing a diamond ring on every finger.

, an essay written in 1848, included a cosmological theory that presaged the big bang theory by 80 years, as well as the first plausible solution to Olbers' paradox. <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> eschewed the scientific method in <LOCATION>Eureka</LOCATION> and instead wrote from pure intuition. For this reason, he considered it a work of art, not science, but insisted that it was still true and considered it to be his career masterpiece. Even so, <LOCATION>Eureka</LOCATION> is full of scientific errors. In particular, <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s suggestions opposed Newtonian principles regarding the density and rotation of planets.

<PERSON>Poe</PERSON> had a keen interest in the field of cryptography. He had placed a notice of his abilities in the <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION> paper <PERSON>Alexander</PERSON>'s Weekly (Express) Messenger, inviting submissions of ciphers, which he proceeded to solve. In July 1841, <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> had published an essay called "A Few Words on Secret Writing" in <PERSON>Graham</PERSON>'s Magazine. Realizing the public interest in the topic, he wrote "The Gold-Bug" incorporating ciphers as part of the story. <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s success in cryptography relied not so much on his knowledge of that field (his method was limited to the simple substitution cryptogram), as on his knowledge of the magazine and newspaper culture. His keen analytical abilities, which were so evident in his detective stories, allowed him to see that the general public was largely ignorant of the methods by which a simple substitution cryptogram can be solved, and he used this to his advantage. The sensation <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> created with his cryptography stunt played a major role in popularizing cryptograms in newspapers and magazines.

<PERSON>Poe</PERSON> had an influence on cryptography beyond increasing public interest in his lifetime. <PERSON>William Friedman</PERSON>, <LOCATION>America</LOCATION>'s foremost cryptologist, was heavily influenced by <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>. <PERSON>Friedman</PERSON>'s initial interest in cryptography came from reading "The Gold-Bug" as a child  interest he later put to use in deciphering <LOCATION>Japan</LOCATION>'s PURPLE code during World War II.

The historical <PERSON>Edgar Allan Poe</PERSON> has appeared as a fictionalized character, often representing the "mad genius" or "tormented artist" and exploiting his personal struggles. Many such depictions also blend in with characters from his stories, suggesting <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> and his characters share identities. Often, fictional depictions of <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> use his mystery-solving skills in such novels as The Poe Shadow by <PERSON>Matthew Pearl</PERSON>.



No childhood home of <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> is still standing, including the <PERSON>Allan</PERSON> family's <LOCATION>Moldavia</LOCATION> estate. The oldest standing home in <LOCATION>Richmond</LOCATION>, the <ORGANIZATION>Old Stone House</ORGANIZATION>, is in use as the <PERSON>Edgar Allan Poe Museum</PERSON>, though <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> never lived there. The collection includes many items <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> used during his time with the <PERSON>Allan</PERSON> family and also features several rare first printings of <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> works. The dorm room <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> is believed to have used while studying at the <ORGANIZATION>University of Virginia</ORGANIZATION> in 1826 is preserved and available for visits. Its upkeep is now overseen by a group of students and staff known as the <ORGANIZATION>Raven Society</ORGANIZATION>.

The earliest surviving home in which <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> lived is in <LOCATION>Baltimore</LOCATION>, preserved as the <PERSON>Edgar Allan Poe House</PERSON> and Museum. <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> is believed to have lived in the home at the age of 23 when he first lived with <PERSON>Maria Clemm</PERSON> and <LOCATION>Virginia</LOCATION> (as well as his grandmother and possibly his brother <PERSON>William Henry Leonard Poe</PERSON>). It is open to the public and is also the home of the <PERSON>Edgar Allan Poe Society</PERSON>. Of the several homes that <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>, his wife <PERSON>Virginia</PERSON>, and his mother-in-law <PERSON>Maria</PERSON> rented in <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION>, only the last house has survived. The Spring Garden home, where the author lived in 18431844, is today preserved by the <ORGANIZATION>National Park Service</ORGANIZATION> as the <PERSON>Edgar Allan Poe</PERSON> National Historic Site. <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s final home is preserved as the <PERSON>Edgar Allan Poe Cottage</PERSON> in the <LOCATION>Bronx</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>New York</LOCATION>.

Other <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> landmarks include a building in the <LOCATION>Upper West Side</LOCATION>, where <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> temporarily lived when he first moved to <LOCATION>New York</LOCATION>. A plaque suggests that <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> wrote "The Raven" here. In <LOCATION>Boston</LOCATION>, a plaque hangs near the building where <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> was born once stood. Believed to have been located at 62 Carver Street (now <PERSON>Charles Street</PERSON>), the plaque is possibly in an incorrect location. The bar in which legend says <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> was last seen drinking before his death still stands in Fells Point in <LOCATION>Baltimore</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Maryland</LOCATION>. Now known as The Horse You Came In On, local lore insists that a ghost they call "Edgar" haunts the rooms above.

Adding to the mystery surrounding <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s death, an unknown visitor affectionately referred to as the "Poe Toaster" has paid homage to <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s grave every year since 1949. As the tradition has been carried on for more than 50 years, it is likely that the "Poe Toaster" is actually several individuals; however, the tribute is always the same. Every January 19, in the early hours of the morning, the man makes a toast of cognac to <PERSON>Poe</PERSON>'s original grave marker and leaves three roses. Members of the <PERSON>Edgar Allan Poe Society</PERSON> in <LOCATION>Baltimore</LOCATION> have helped in protecting this tradition for decades. On August 15, 2007, <PERSON>Sam Porpora</PERSON>, a former historian at the <LOCATION>Westminster Church</LOCATION> in <LOCATION>Baltimore</LOCATION> where <PERSON>Poe</PERSON> is buried, claimed that he had started the tradition in the 1960s. The claim that the tradition began in 1949, he said, was a hoax in order to raise money and enhance the profile of the church. His story has not been confirmed, and some details he has given to the press have been pointed out as factually inaccurate.
