<TITLE>December 19</TITLE>







<TITLE><PERSON>Alfonso V</PERSON> of <PERSON>Aragon</PERSON></TITLE>

<PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON> the Magnanimous (also <PERSON>Alphonso</PERSON>; Catalan: <PERSON>Alfons</PERSON>) (1396 - 27 June 1458) was the King of <PERSON>Aragon</PERSON> (as <PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON> V), <LOCATION>Valencia</LOCATION> (as <PERSON>Alfonso III</PERSON>), <LOCATION>Majorca</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Sardinia</LOCATION> and <LOCATION>Corsica</LOCATION> (as <PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON> II), and <LOCATION>Sicily</LOCATION> and Count of <ORGANIZATION>Barcelona</ORGANIZATION> (as Alfonso IV) from 1416 and King of <LOCATION>Naples</LOCATION> (as Alfonso I) from 1442 until his death. He was one of the most prominent figures of the early Renaissance and a knight of the Order of the Dragon. 

He was the son of Ferdinand I of <PERSON>Aragon</PERSON> (known as <PERSON>Ferdinand</PERSON> of <PERSON>Antequera</PERSON>) and <PERSON>Eleanor</PERSON> of <PERSON>Alburquerque</PERSON>. He represented the old line of the counts of <ORGANIZATION>Barcelona</ORGANIZATION> only through women, and was on his father's side descended from the <ORGANIZATION>House of Trastamara</ORGANIZATION>, a noble family of <LOCATION>Castile</LOCATION>. By hereditary right he was king of <LOCATION>Sicily</LOCATION> and disputed the island of <LOCATION>Sardinia</LOCATION> with <ORGANIZATION>Genoa</ORGANIZATION>. <PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON> was also in possession of much of <LOCATION>Corsica</LOCATION> by the 1420s.

In 1421 Queen <PERSON>Joan II</PERSON> of <LOCATION>Naples</LOCATION>, who had no children, adopted and named him as heir to the Kingdom of <LOCATION>Naples</LOCATION>, and <PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON> went to <LOCATION>Naples</LOCATION>. Here he hired the famous condottiero Braccio da Montone with the task of reducing the resistance of the other pretender, <PERSON>Louis III</PERSON> of <LOCATION>Anjou</LOCATION>, and his forces led by <PERSON>Muzio Attendolo Sforza</PERSON>. As Pope <PERSON>Martin</PERSON> V supported <PERSON>Sforza</PERSON>, <PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON> switched religious allegiance to the Aragonese antipope <PERSON>Benedict XIII</PERSON>. When <PERSON>Sforza</PERSON> also abandoned <PERSON>Louis</PERSON>, <PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON> seemed to have all his problems solved; however, his relationship with <PERSON>Joanna</PERSON> suddenly worsened, and in May 1423 he had her lover, and a powerful figure in the Neapolitan court, <PERSON>Gianni Caracciolo</PERSON>, arrested. After an attempt to arrest the queen herself failed, <PERSON>Joanna</PERSON> called <PERSON>Sforza</PERSON> who defeated the Aragonese milices near <LOCATION>Castel Capuano</LOCATION> in <LOCATION>Naples</LOCATION>. <PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON> fled to <PERSON>Castel Nuovo</PERSON>, but the help of a fleet of 22 galleys led by <PERSON>Giovanni da Cardona</PERSON> improved his situation. <PERSON>Sforza</PERSON> and <PERSON>Joanna</PERSON> ransomed <PERSON>Caracciolo</PERSON> and retreated to the fortress of <PERSON>Aversa</PERSON>. Here she repudiated her earlier adoption of <PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON> and, with support from <PERSON>Martin V</PERSON>, named <PERSON>Louis III</PERSON> as her heir instead. The Milanese Filippo <PERSON>Maria Visconti</PERSON> joined the anti-Aragonese coalition. <PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON> requested support from <PERSON>Braccio da Montone</PERSON>, who was besieging <PERSON>Joanna</PERSON>'s troops in <LOCATION>L'Aquila</LOCATION>, but had to set sail for <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION>, where a war had broken out between his brothers and the Kingdom of <LOCATION>Castile</LOCATION>. On his way towards <ORGANIZATION>Barcelona</ORGANIZATION>, he destroyed <ORGANIZATION>Marseille</ORGANIZATION>, a possession of <PERSON>Louis III</PERSON>.



In the late 1423 the Genoese fleet of <PERSON>Visconti</PERSON> moved in the southern <LOCATION>Tyrrhenian Sea</LOCATION> and easily conquered <PERSON>Gaeta</PERSON>, <PERSON>Procida</PERSON>, Castellammare and <PERSON>Sorrento</PERSON>. <LOCATION>Naples</LOCATION>, which was held by <PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON>'s brother, <PERSON>Pedro</PERSON>, was besieged in 1424 by the Genoese ships and <PERSON>Joanna</PERSON>'s troops, now led by <PERSON>Francesco Sforza</PERSON>, son of <PERSON>Muzio</PERSON> (who had died at <LOCATION>L'Aquila</LOCATION>). The city fell in the April of that year. <PERSON>Pedro</PERSON>, after a short resistance in <LOCATION>Castel Nuovo</LOCATION>, fled to <LOCATION>Sicily</LOCATION> in August. <PERSON>Joanna II</PERSON> and <PERSON>Louis III</PERSON> again took possession of the realm, although the true power was in the hands of <PERSON>Gianni Caracciolo</PERSON>.

An opportunity for <PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON> to reconquer <LOCATION>Naples</LOCATION> occurred in 1432, when <PERSON>Caracciolo</PERSON> was killed in a conjure. <PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON> tried to regain the favour of the queen, but failed, and had to wait for the death of both <PERSON>Louis</PERSON> (at <LOCATION>Cosenza</LOCATION> in 1434) and <PERSON>Joanna</PERSON> herself (February 1435). In her will, she bequeathed her realm to <PERSON>Ren</PERSON> of <LOCATION>Anjou</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Louis III</PERSON>'s brother. This solution was opposed by the new pope, <PERSON>Eugene IV</PERSON>, who was nominal feudal lord of the King of <LOCATION>Naples</LOCATION>. As the Neapolitans had called for the French, <PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON> decided to intervene and, with the support of several barons of the kingdom, captured <PERSON>Capua</PERSON> and besieged the important sea fortress of <PERSON>Gaeta</PERSON>. His fleet of 25 galleys was met by the Genoese ships sent by <PERSON>Visconti</PERSON>, led by <PERSON>Biagio Assereto</PERSON>. In the battle that ensued, <PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON> was defeated and taken prisoner.

In <LOCATION>Milan</LOCATION>, however, he impressed his captor with his cultured demeanor and persuaded him to let him go by making it plain that it was not in <LOCATION>Milan</LOCATION>'s interest to prevent the victory of the Aragonese party in <LOCATION>Naples</LOCATION>. Helped by a Sicilian fleet, <PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON> recaptured <PERSON>Capua</PERSON> and set his base in <LOCATION>Gaeta</LOCATION> in the February of 1436. Papal troops had invaded the Neapolitan kingdom, but <PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON> corrupted the cardinal <PERSON>Giovanni Vitelleschi</PERSON>, who commanded them, and his successes waned. 



In the meantime, <PERSON>Ren</PERSON> had managed to reach <LOCATION>Naples</LOCATION> on may 19, 1438. <PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON> tried to besiege the city in the following September, but failed. His brother <PERSON>Pedro</PERSON> was killed during the battle. <PERSON>Castel Nuovo</PERSON>, where an Aragonese garrison resisted, fell to the <PERSON>Angevine</PERSON> mercenaries in the August of 1439. After the death of his condottiero <PERSON>Jacopo Caldora</PERSON>, however, <PERSON>Ren</PERSON>'s fortune started to wane: <PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON> could easily capture <PERSON>Aversa</PERSON>, <PERSON>Salerno</PERSON>, <LOCATION>Benevento</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Manfredonia</PERSON> and Bitonto. <PERSON>Ren</PERSON>, whose possession included now only part of the <LOCATION>Abruzzi</LOCATION> and <LOCATION>Naples</LOCATION>, obtained 10,000 men from the pope, but the cardinal leading them signed a truce with <PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON>. <PERSON>Giovanni Sforza</PERSON> came with a reduced corps, as troops sent by <PERSON>Eugene IV</PERSON> had halted his father <PERSON>Francesco</PERSON> in the Marche. 

<PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON>, who was provided with the most impressive artillery of the time, again besieged <LOCATION>Naples</LOCATION>. The siege began on November 10, 1441, and ended on June 2 of the following year. After the return of <PERSON>Ren</PERSON> to <LOCATION>Provence</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON> easily reduced the remaining resistance and made his triumphal entrance in <LOCATION>Naples</LOCATION> on February 26, 1443, as the monarch of a pacified kingdom. In 1446 he conquered also <LOCATION>Sardinia</LOCATION>, becoming the head of the most important kingdom of western <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION>. 



<PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON> had been betrothed to <PERSON>Mara de Castilla</PERSON> (1401-1458; sister of Juan II of <LOCATION>Castile</LOCATION>) in <LOCATION>Valladolid</LOCATION> in 1408; the marriage was celebrated in <LOCATION>Valencia</LOCATION> during 1415. They failed to produce children. <PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON> had been in love with a woman of noble family named <PERSON>Lucrezia d'Alagno</PERSON>, who served as a de facto queen at the Neapolitan court as well as an inspiring muse.

His Spanish possessions were ruled for him by his brother <PERSON>John</PERSON>. <PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON>, by formally submitting his reign to the Papacy, obtained the consent of Pope <PERSON>Eugene IV</PERSON> that the Kingdom of <LOCATION>Naples</LOCATION> would go to his immature son <PERSON>Ferdinand</PERSON>. He died in <LOCATION>Castel</LOCATION> dell'Ovo in 1458, while he was planning the conquest of <LOCATION>Genoa</LOCATION>. At the time, <PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON> was at odds with <PERSON>Callixtus III</PERSON>, who died shortly thereafter.

<LOCATION>Sicily</LOCATION> and <LOCATION>Sardinia</LOCATION> were inherited by his brother <PERSON>John</PERSON>, who survived him.

He was also a powerful and consistent supporter of Scanderbeg, whom he decided to take under his protection as a vassal in 1451, shortly after the latter had scored his second victory against <PERSON>Murad II</PERSON>. In addition to financial assistance, he supplied the Albanian leader with troops, military equipment, and sanctuary for himself and his family if such a need should arise. This was because in 1448, while <PERSON>Scanderbeg</PERSON> was victoriously fighting off the Turkish invasions, three military columns, commanded by <PERSON>Demetrio Reres</PERSON> along with his sons <PERSON>Giorgio</PERSON> and <PERSON>Basilio</PERSON>, were dispatched to help <PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON> V defeat the barons of <LOCATION>Naples</LOCATION> who had rebelled against him.

Like a true prince of the Renaissance he favoured men of letters whom he trusted to preserve his reputation to posterity. He founded the Academy of <LOCATION>Naples</LOCATION> and, for his entrance in the city in 1443, had a magnificent triumphal arch added to the main gate of <LOCATION>Castel Nuovo</LOCATION>. This edifice, considered the most important civil piece of art of the time, was designed by <PERSON>Francesco Laurana</PERSON>. His devotion to the classics was exceptional even for the time. For example, <PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON> halted his army in pious respect before the birthplace of a Latin writer, carried <PERSON>Livy</PERSON> or <PERSON>Caesar</PERSON> on his campaigns with him, and his panegyrist Panormita even stated that the king was cured of an illness when a few pages of Quintus <PERSON>Curtius Rufus</PERSON>' history of <PERSON>Alexander</PERSON> the Great were read to him. However, the classics had not refined his taste, for he was amused by setting itinerant scholars, who swarmed to his court, to abuse one another in the indescribably filthy Latin scolding matches which were then the fashion. 

After his conquest of <LOCATION>Naples</LOCATION> in 1442, <PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON> ruled by his mercenary soldiers and mercenary men of letters. In his Italian kingdom, he maintained the former political and administrative institutions; a unified General Chancellorship for the whole Aragonese reign was set in <LOCATION>Naples</LOCATION>, although the main functionaries were of Aragonese nationality. Apart from financial, administrative and artistical improvements, his other accomplishments in the Sicilian kingdom include the restoration of the aqueducts, the drainage of marshy areas, and the pavement of streets.

<PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON> was the object of diplomatic contacts from the empire of <LOCATION>Ethiopia</LOCATION>. In 1428, he received a letter from Yeshaq I of <LOCATION>Ethiopia</LOCATION>, borne by two dignitaries, which proposed an alliance against the Muslims and would be sealed by a dual marriage that would require the <PERSON>Infante Don Pedro</PERSON> to bring a group of artisans to <LOCATION>Ethiopia</LOCATION> where he would marry <PERSON>Yeshaq</PERSON>'s daughter. In return, <PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON> sent a party of 13 craftsmen, all of whom perished on the way to <LOCATION>Ethiopia</LOCATION>. He later sent a letter to <PERSON>Yeshaq</PERSON>'s successor <PERSON>Zara Yaqob</PERSON> in 1450, in which he wrote that he would be happy to send artisans to <LOCATION>Ethiopia</LOCATION> if their safe arrival could be guaranteed, but it probably never reached the Emperor.









<TITLE><PERSON>Barry Bonds</PERSON></TITLE>

<PERSON>Barry Lamar Bonds</PERSON> (born July 24, 1964) is a Major League Baseball outfielder who is currently a free agent. He is the son of former major league All-Star <PERSON>Bobby Bonds</PERSON>, the godson of Hall of Famer <PERSON>Willie Mays</PERSON>, the nephew of 1964 Olympian <PERSON>Rosie Bonds</PERSON>, and a distant cousin of Hall of <PERSON>Famer Reggie Jackson</PERSON>. He debuted in the Major Leagues with the <ORGANIZATION>Pittsburgh Pirates</ORGANIZATION> in 1986 and joined the <ORGANIZATION>San Francisco Giants</ORGANIZATION> in 1993, where he stayed through 2007. Bonds filed for free agency following the 2007 World Series.

<PERSON>Bonds</PERSON>' accomplishments place him among the greatest baseball players of all-time. He has a record-setting seven Most Valuable Player awards, including a record-setting four consecutive MVPs. He is a fourteen time All-star, eight time gold glove-winner and he holds numerous Major League Baseball records. He currently holds the all-time Major League Baseball home run record with 762, and is also the all-time career leader in both walks (2,558) and intentional walks (688). He holds numerous other records, including the single-season <ORGANIZATION>Major League</ORGANIZATION> record for home runs (73), set in .

Since 2003, <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> has been a key figure in the Bay Area Laboratory Co-operative (BALCO) scandal. He was under investigation by a federal grand jury regarding his testimony in the BALCO case, and was indicted on perjury and obstruction of justice charges on November 15, 2007. The indictment alleges that <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> lied while under oath about his alleged use of steroids.





Born in , <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> grew up in  and attended <ORGANIZATION>Junpero Serra High School</ORGANIZATION> in  and excelled in baseball, basketball and football. As a freshman, he spent the baseball season on the JV team. The next three years1980 to 1982he starred on the varsity team. He batted for a .467 batting average his senior year, and was honored as a prep All-American. The <ORGANIZATION>Giants</ORGANIZATION> drafted <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> in the second round of the 1982 <ORGANIZATION>MLB</ORGANIZATION> draft as a high school senior, but the <ORGANIZATION>Giants</ORGANIZATION> and <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> were unable to agree on contract terms, so Bonds instead decided to attend college.

<PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> attended <ORGANIZATION>Arizona State University</ORGANIZATION>, hitting .347 with 45 home runs and 175 runs batted in (RBI). In 1984 he batted .360 and had 30 stolen bases. In 1985 he hit 23 home runs with 66 RBIs and a .368 batting average. He was a Sporting News All-American selection that year. He tied the <ORGANIZATION>NCAA</ORGANIZATION> record with seven consecutive hits in the College World Series as sophomore and was named to All-Time College World Series Team in 1996. He graduated from <ORGANIZATION>Arizona State</ORGANIZATION> in 1986 with a degree in criminology. During college, he played part of one summer in the amateur <ORGANIZATION>Alaska Baseball League</ORGANIZATION> with the <ORGANIZATION>Alaska Goldpanners</ORGANIZATION>.

<PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> was drafted by the <ORGANIZATION>Pittsburgh Pirates</ORGANIZATION> in the first round (6th overall) of the  Major League Baseball draft. <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> joined the <ORGANIZATION>Prince William Pirates of the Carolina League</ORGANIZATION> and was named July 1985 Player of the Month for the league. In , he hit .311 in 44 games for the <ORGANIZATION>Hawaii Islanders of the Pacific Coast League</ORGANIZATION>, and he made his major league debut on May 30.

In 1986, <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> led <ORGANIZATION>National League</ORGANIZATION> (<ORGANIZATION>NL</ORGANIZATION>) rookies with 16 home runs, 48 RBI, 36 stolen bases and 65 walks, but he finished 6th in Rookie of the Year voting. He hit 25 home runs in his second season, along with 32 stolen bases and 59 RBIs. Bonds improved in 1988, hitting .283 with 24 home runs. Bonds finished with 19 homers, 58 RBIs, and 14 outfield assists, which was 2nd in <ORGANIZATION>NL</ORGANIZATION>. 

<PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> won his first MVP award in 1990, hitting .301 with 33 home runs and 114 RBIs. His 52 stolen bases were third in the league. He won his first Gold Glove and Silver Slugger Awards. In 1991, <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> also put up great numbers, hitting 25 homers and driving in 116 runs, and obtained another Gold Glove and Silver Slugger. He finished second to the <ORGANIZATION>Atlanta Braves</ORGANIZATION>' <PERSON>Terry Pendleton</PERSON> (the NL batting champion) in the MVP voting. The next season, <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> won his second MVP award. He dominated the <ORGANIZATION>NL</ORGANIZATION>, hitting .311 with 34 homers and 103 RBIs, and propelling the <ORGANIZATION>Pirates</ORGANIZATION> to their third straight National League East division title. However, <LOCATION>Pittsburgh</LOCATION> was defeated by the <ORGANIZATION>Braves</ORGANIZATION> in a seven-game National League Championship Series. <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> was involved in the final play of Game 7 of the NLCS, where he fielded a base hit by <PERSON>Francisco Cabrera</PERSON> and attempted to throw out <PERSON>Sid Bream</PERSON> at home plate. But the throw to <ORGANIZATION>Pirates</ORGANIZATION> catcher <PERSON>Mike LaValliere</PERSON> was late and <PERSON>Bream</PERSON> scored the winning run. For the third consecutive season, the <ORGANIZATION>NL East Champion Pirates</ORGANIZATION> were denied a trip to the World Series.

In 1993, <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> left the <ORGANIZATION>Pirates</ORGANIZATION> to sign a lucrative free agent contract worth a then-record $43.75 million over 6 years with the <ORGANIZATION>Giants</ORGANIZATION>, with whom his father spent the first 7 years of his career, and with whom his godfather <PERSON>Willie Mays</PERSON> played 22 of his 24 <ORGANIZATION>Major League</ORGANIZATION> seasons. The deal was at that time the largest in baseball history, in terms of both total value and average annual salary. To honor his father, <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> switched his jersey number to 25 once he signed with the <ORGANIZATION>Giants</ORGANIZATION>, as it had been <PERSON>Bobby</PERSON>'s number in <LOCATION>San Francisco</LOCATION>. (His number during most of his stay with the <ORGANIZATION>Pirates</ORGANIZATION>, 24, was retired in honor of Mays anyway). Bonds hit .336 in 1993, leading the league with 46 home runs and 123 RBI en route to his second consecutive MVP award, and third overall. As good as the <ORGANIZATION>Giants</ORGANIZATION> were (winning 103 games), the <ORGANIZATION>Atlanta Braves</ORGANIZATION> won 104 in what some call the last great pennant race (due to the Wild Card being instituted shortly after).

In the strike-shortened season of , Bonds hit .312 with 37 home runs and a league-leading 74 walks, and he finished 4th in MVP voting. In , <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> hit 33 homers and drove in 104 runs, hitting .294 but finished only 12th in MVP voting.

In , <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> became the first National League player and second (of the current list of four) major league player(s) to hit 40 home runs and steal 40 bases in the same season. The other members of the 40-40 club are <PERSON>Jos</PERSON> Canseco1988, <PERSON>Alex Rodriguez1998</PERSON>, and <PERSON>Alfonso</PERSON> Soriano2006; his father <PERSON>Bobby Bonds</PERSON> was one home run short in 1973 when he hit 39 home runs and stole 43 bases. Bonds drove in 129 runs with a .308 average and walked a then-National League record 151 times. During the 1996 season <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> became the 4th player in history to steal 300 bases and hit 300 home runs for a career, joining <PERSON>Willie Mays</PERSON>, <PERSON>Andre Dawson</PERSON>, and <PERSON>Bobby Bonds</PERSON> in the 300-300 club, but he only finished fifth in the MVP balloting. In  Bonds hit .291, his lowest average since 1989. He hit 40 home runs for the second straight year and drove in 101 runs, leading the league in walks again with 145. He tied his father in 1997 for having the most 30/30 seasons, and he again placed fifth in the MVP balloting.

In , he hit .303 with 37 home runs and drove in 122 runs, winning his eighth Gold Glove, and became the first player ever to enter the 400-400 club by having career totals of 400 home runs and 400 stolen bases. With two outs in the 9th inning of a game against the <ORGANIZATION>Arizona Diamondbacks</ORGANIZATION> on May 28, 1998, <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> became only the fifth player in baseball history to be given an intentional walk with the bases loaded. Nap Lajoie (1901), <PERSON>Del Bissonette</PERSON> (1928) and <PERSON>Bill Nicholson</PERSON> (1944) were three others in the 20th century who received that rare honor; however <PERSON>Abner Dalrymple</PERSON> was the first to receive one in 1881. Bonds finished 8th in the MVP voting.

<PERSON>Bill James</PERSON> ranked <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> as the best player of the 1990s, adding that the decade's second-best player (<PERSON>Craig Biggio</PERSON>) had been closer in production to the decade's 10th-best player than to Bonds. In 1999, with statistics through 1997 being considered, Bonds ranked Number 34 on <ORGANIZATION>The Sporting News</ORGANIZATION>' list of the 100 Greatest Baseball Players, making him the highest-ranking active player. When the <ORGANIZATION>Sporting News</ORGANIZATION> list was redone in 2005, <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> was ranked 6th behind <PERSON>Babe Ruth</PERSON>, <PERSON>Willie Mays</PERSON>, <PERSON>Ty Cobb</PERSON>, <PERSON>Walter Johnson</PERSON>, and <PERSON>Henry Aaron</PERSON>. <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> was omitted from 1999's <ORGANIZATION>Major League Baseball All-Century Team</ORGANIZATION>, to which <PERSON>Ken Griffey</PERSON>, Jr. was elected. <PERSON>James</PERSON> wrote of <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON>, "Certainly the most unappreciated superstar of my lifetime... <PERSON>Griffey</PERSON> has always been more popular, but <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> has been a far, far greater player." In 1999, <PERSON>James</PERSON> rated <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> as the 16th best player of all time. "When people begin to take in all of his accomplishments", <PERSON>James</PERSON> predicted, "Bonds may well be rated among the five greatest players in the history of the game."



In 2000, the following year, Bonds hit .306 with a slugging percentage of .688 (career best at that time) and hit 49 home runs in just 143 games (also a career high to that point), while drawing a league-leading 117 walks.

The next year, <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON>' offensive production reached even higher levels, breaking not only his own personal records but several major league records. In the <ORGANIZATION>Giants</ORGANIZATION>' first 50 games in 2001, <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> hit 28 home runs, including 17 in <LOCATION>Maya</LOCATION> career high. He also hit 39 home runs by the All-star break (a major league record), drew a major league record 177 walks, and had a .515 on-base average, a feat not seen since <PERSON>Mickey Mantle</PERSON> and <PERSON>Ted Williams</PERSON> over forty years earlier. Bonds' slugging percentage was a major league record .863 (411 total bases in 476 at-bats), and, most impressively, he ended the season with a major league record 73 home runs. 

Bonds re-signed with the <ORGANIZATION>Giants</ORGANIZATION> for a five-year, $90 million contract in January 2002. That year, he hit 46 home runs in 403 at-bats. He won the <ORGANIZATION>NL</ORGANIZATION> batting title with a career-high .370 average and struck out only 47 times. Despite playing in nine fewer games than the previous season, he drew 198 walks, a major-league record, 68 of them intentional. He slugged .799, then the fourth-highest total all time. Bonds broke <PERSON>Ted Williams</PERSON>' major league record for on-base average with .582. <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> also hit his 600th home run, less than a year and a half after hitting his 500th. 

In 2003, <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> played in just 130 games. He hit 45 home runs in just 390 at-bats, along with a .341 batting average. He slugged .749, walked 148 times, and had an on-base average well over .500 (.529) for the third straight year. He also became the only member of the career 500 home run/500 stolen base club.

In 2004, <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> had perhaps his best season. He hit .362 en route to his second National League batting title, and broke his own record by walking 232 times. He slugged .812, which was fourth-highest of all time, and broke his on-base percentage record with a .609 average. <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> passed <PERSON>Mays</PERSON> on the career home run list, hitting his 700th near the end of the season. Bonds hit 45 home runs in 373 at-bats, and struck out just 41 times, putting himself in elite company, as few major leaguers have ever had more home runs than strikeouts in a season. <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> would win his fourth consecutive MVP award and his seventh overall. His seven MVP awards are four more than any other player in history. In addition, no other player from either league has been awarded the MVP four times in a row. (The MVP award was first given in 1931). On July 4, 2004 he tied and passed <PERSON>Rickey Henderson</PERSON>'s career bases on balls record with his 2190th and 2191st career walks..

As <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> neared <PERSON>Aaron</PERSON>'s record, <PERSON>Aaron</PERSON> was called on for his opinion of Bonds. He clarified that he was a fan and admirer of <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> and avoided the controversy regarding whether the record should be denoted with an asterisk due Bonds' to alleged steroid usage. He felt recognition and respect for the award was something to be determined by the fans. As the steroid controversy received greater media attention during the offseason before the 2005 season, <PERSON>Aaron</PERSON> expressed some reservations about the statements <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> made on the issue. <PERSON>Aaron</PERSON> expressed that he felt drug and steroid use to boost athletic performance was inappropriate. <PERSON>Aaron</PERSON> was frustrated that the media could not focus on events that occurred in the field of play and wished drugs or gambling allegations such as those associated with <PERSON>Pete Rose</PERSON> could be emphasized less. In 2007, <PERSON>Aaron</PERSON> felt the whole steroid use issue was very controversial and decided that he would not attend any possible record-breaking games. <PERSON>Aaron</PERSON> congratulated <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> through the media when <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> broke <PERSON>Aaron</PERSON>'s record.

Bonds' salary for the 2005 season was $22 million, the second-highest salary in Major League Baseball (the <ORGANIZATION>Yankees</ORGANIZATION>' <PERSON>Alex Rodriguez</PERSON> earned the highest, $25.2 million).<PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> endured a knee injury, multiple surgeries, and rehabilitation. He was activated on September 12, 2005, and started in left field. In his return against the <ORGANIZATION>San Diego Padres</ORGANIZATION>, he nearly hit a home run in his first at-bat. Bonds finished the night 1-for-4. Upon his return, <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> resumed his high-caliber performance at the plate, hitting home runs in four consecutive games from September 18, 2005 to September 21, 2005 and finishing with five homers in only 14 games.

In 2006, <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> earned $20 million (not including bonuses), the fourth highest salary in baseball. Through the 2006 season he had earned approximately $172 million during his then 21-year career, making him baseball's all-time highest paid player.Bonds hit under .200 for his first 10 games of the season and did not hit a home run until April 22, 2006. This 10-game stretch was his longest home run slump since the 1998 season. On May 7, 2006, <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> drew within one home run of tying Babe <PERSON>Ruth</PERSON> for second place on the all time list, hitting his 713th career home run into the second level of <ORGANIZATION>Citizens Bank Park</ORGANIZATION> in <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION>, off pitcher <PERSON>Jon Lieber</PERSON> in an <ORGANIZATION>ESPN</ORGANIZATION> nationally-televised game in which the <ORGANIZATION>Giants</ORGANIZATION> lost to the <ORGANIZATION>Philadelphia Phillies</ORGANIZATION>. Then, on May 20, 2006, <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> tied <PERSON>Ruth</PERSON>, hitting his 714th career home run to deep right field to lead off the top of the 2nd inning.  On May 28, 2006, <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> passed <PERSON>Ruth</PERSON>, hitting his 715th career home run to center field off <ORGANIZATION>Colorado Rockies</ORGANIZATION> pitcher <PERSON>Byung-Hyun Kim</PERSON>. In 2006, <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> recorded his lowest slugging percentage (a statistic that he has historically ranked among league leaders season after season) since 1991 with the <ORGANIZATION>Pittsburgh Pirates</ORGANIZATION>.

In January 2007, the <ORGANIZATION>New York Daily News</ORGANIZATION> reported that <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> had tested positive for amphetamines. Under baseball's amphetamine policy, which had been in effect for one season, players testing positive were to submit to six additional tests and undergo treatment and counseling. <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> resumed his march to the all-time record early in the 2007 season. <PERSON>Bonds</PERSON> did not homer again until April 13, 2007 when he hit two (736 and 737) in a 3 for 3 night that included 4 RBI against the <ORGANIZATION>Pittsburgh Pirates</ORGANIZATION>. The hype surrounding Bonds' pursuit of the home run record escalated on May 14, 2007. <TITLE><PERSON>Alexander II</PERSON> of <LOCATION>Russia</LOCATION></TITLE>

<PERSON>Alexander</PERSON> (<PERSON>Aleksandr</PERSON>) II Nikolaevich () (<LOCATION>Moscow</LOCATION>, 29 April 1818 13 March 1881 in <LOCATION>St. Petersburg</LOCATION>) was the Emperor of the Russian Empire from 3 March 1855 until his assassination in 1881. He was also the Grand Duke of <LOCATION>Finland</LOCATION> and King of <LOCATION>Poland</LOCATION> until 1867 when it was annexed into the Russian Empire.

Born in 1818, he was the eldest son of <PERSON>Nicholas</PERSON> I and <LOCATION>Charlotte</LOCATION> of <LOCATION>Prussia</LOCATION>, daughter of <PERSON>Frederick William III</PERSON> of <LOCATION>Prussia</LOCATION> and <PERSON>Louise of Mecklenburg-Strelitz</PERSON>. His early life gave little indication of his ultimate potential; until the time of his accession in 1855, few imagined that he would be known to posterity as a leader able to implement the most challenging reforms undertaken in <LOCATION>Russia</LOCATION> since the reign of <PERSON>Peter</PERSON> the Great. 

In the period of over thirty-six years during which he was heir apparent, the atmosphere of <LOCATION>St Petersburg</LOCATION> was unfavourable to the development of any intellectual or political innovation. Government was based on principles under which all freedom of thought and all private initiative were, as far as possible, suppressed vigorously. Personal and official censorship was rife; criticism of the authorities was regarded as a serious offence. This was also regarded as one of the reasons which led to his assassination.

Under supervision of the liberal poet <PERSON>Vasily Zhukovsky</PERSON>, <PERSON>Alexander</PERSON> received the education commonly given to young Russians of good family at that time: a smattering of a great many subjects, and exposure to the chief modern European languages. He took little personal interest in military affairs to the disappointment of his father, who was passionate about the military. <PERSON>Alexander</PERSON> gave evidence of a kind disposition and a warmheartedness which were considered out of place in one destined to become a military autocrat.

<PERSON>Alexander II</PERSON> succeeded to the throne upon the death of his father in 1855. The first year of his reign was devoted to the prosecution of the Crimean War, and after the fall of <LOCATION>Sevastopol</LOCATION> to negotiations for peace, led by his trusted counselor, <PERSON>Prince Gorchakov</PERSON>. Then he began a period of radical reforms, encouraged by public opinion but carried out with autocratic power. All who had any pretensions to enlightenment declared loudly that the country had been exhausted and humiliated by the war, and that the only way of restoring it to its proper position in <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION> was to develop its natural resources and thoroughly to reform all branches of the administration. The government therefore found in the educated classes a new-born public spirit, anxious to assist it in any work of reform that it might think fit to undertake. 

Fortunately for <LOCATION>Russia</LOCATION> the autocratic power was now in the hands of a man who was impressionable enough to be deeply influenced by the spirit of the time, and who had sufficient prudence and practicality to prevent his being carried away by the prevailing excitement into the dangerous region of Utopian dreaming. Unlike some of his predecessors, he had no grand, original schemes of his own to impose by force on unwilling subjects, and no pet projects to lead his judgment astray. He looked instinctively with a suspicious, critical eye upon the panaceas which more imaginative and less cautious people recommended. These character traits, together with the peculiar circumstances in which he was placed, determined the part he would play in bringing to fruition the reform aspirations of the educated classes.

However, the growth of a revolutionary movement to the "left" of the educated classes led to an abrupt end to <PERSON>Alexander</PERSON>'s changes when he was assassinated by a bomb in 1881. It is interesting to note that after <PERSON>Alexander</PERSON> became tsar in 1855, he maintained a generally liberal course at the helm while providing a target for numerous assassination attempts (1866, 1873, 1880).

Though he carefully guarded his autocratic rights and privileges, and obstinately resisted all efforts to push him farther than he felt inclined to go, <PERSON>Alexander</PERSON> for several years acted somewhat like a constitutional sovereign of the continental type. Soon after the conclusion of peace, important changes were made in legislation concerning industry and commerce, and the new freedom thus afforded produced a large number of limited liability companies. Plans were formed for building a great network of railways -- partly for the purpose of developing the natural resources of the country, and partly for the purpose of increasing its power for defense and attack.

Then it was found that further progress was blocked by a formidable obstacle: the existence of serfdom. <PERSON>Alexander</PERSON> showed that, unlike his father, he meant to grapple boldly with this difficult and dangerous problem. Taking advantage of a petition presented by the Polish landed proprietors of the Lithuanian provinces, and hoping that their relations with the serfs might be regulated in a more satisfactory way (meaning in a way more satisfactory for the proprietors), he authorized the formation of committees "for ameliorating the condition of the peasants," and laid down the principles on which the amelioration was to be effected.

This step was followed by one still more significant. Without consulting his ordinary advisers, <PERSON>Alexander</PERSON> ordered the Minister of the Interior to send a circular to the provincial governors of European <LOCATION>Russia</LOCATION>, containing a copy of the instructions forwarded to the governor-general of <LOCATION>Lithuania</LOCATION>, praising the supposed generous, patriotic intentions of the Lithuanian landed proprietors, and suggesting that perhaps the landed proprietors of other provinces might express a similar desire. The hint was taken: in all provinces where serfdom existed, emancipation committees were formed.

The deliberations at once raised a host of important, thorny questions. The emancipation was not merely a humanitarian question capable of being solved instantaneously by imperial ukase. It contained very complicated problems, deeply affecting the economic, social and political future of the nation.

<PERSON>Alexander</PERSON> had little of the special knowledge required for dealing successfully with such problems, and he had to restrict himself to choosing between the different measures recommended to him. The main point at issue was whether the serfs should become agricultural labourers dependent economically and administratively on the landlords, or whether they should be transformed into a class of independent communal proprietors. The emperor gave his support to the latter project, and the Russian peasantry became one of the last groups of peasants in <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION> to shake off serfdom.

The architects of the emancipation manifesto were <PERSON>Alexander</PERSON>'s brother <PERSON>Konstantin</PERSON>, <PERSON>Yakov Rostovtsev</PERSON>, and <PERSON>Nikolay Milyutin</PERSON>. On 3 March 1861, the sixth anniversary of his accession, the emancipation law was signed and published.

Other reforms followed: army and navy re-organization (1874); a new judicial administration based on the French model (1864); a new penal code and a greatly simplified system of civil and criminal procedure; an elaborate scheme of local self-government (Zemstvo) for the rural districts (1864) and the large towns (1870), with elective assemblies possessing a restricted right of taxation, and a new rural and municipal police under the direction of the Minister of the Interior. <PERSON>Alexander II</PERSON> would be the second monarch (after King Louis I of <LOCATION>Portugal</LOCATION>) to abolish capital punishment, a penalty which is still legal (although not practised) in <LOCATION>Russia</LOCATION>.

However, the workers wanted better working conditions; national minorities wanted freedom. When radicals began to resort to the formation of secret societies and to revolutionary agitation, <PERSON>Alexander II</PERSON> felt constrained to adopt severe repressive measures.

<PERSON>Alexander II</PERSON> resolved to try the effect of some moderate liberal reforms in an attempt to quell the revolutionary agitation, and for this purpose he instituted a ukase for creating special commissions, composed of high officials and private personages who should prepare reforms in various branches of the administration.



During his bachelor days, <PERSON>Alexander</PERSON> made a state visit to <LOCATION>England</LOCATION> in 1838. Just a year older than the young <PERSON>Queen Victoria</PERSON>, <PERSON>Alexander</PERSON> took a liking to his distant cousin. The fondness however, was short-lived. While <PERSON>Victoria</PERSON> married her German cousin, Prince <PERSON>Albert</PERSON> in February 1840, <PERSON>Alexander</PERSON> became a husband the next year. On 16 April 1841 he married Princess <PERSON>Marie of Hesse</PERSON> in <LOCATION>St Petersburg</LOCATION>, thereafter known as <PERSON>Maria Alexandrovna</PERSON>. The Tsarevitch claimed to be deeply in love with the young Princess and vowed to marry no one else. <PERSON>Marie</PERSON> was the legal daughter of <PERSON>Ludwig II</PERSON>, Grand Duke of <PERSON>Hesse</PERSON> and by <LOCATION>Rhine</LOCATION> and Princess <PERSON>Wilhelmina</PERSON> of <PERSON>Baden</PERSON>, although there was a question of whether the Grand Duke or her mother's lover, <PERSON>Baron August von Senarclens de Grancy</PERSON>, was her biological father. <PERSON>Alexander</PERSON> was aware of the question of her paternity. The marriage produced six sons and two daughters:

<PERSON>Alexander</PERSON> had many mistresses during his marriage and fathered 7 known illegitimate children. These included <PERSON>Antoinette Bayer</PERSON> (20 June 1856-24 January 1948) with his mistress <PERSON>Wilhelmine Bayer</PERSON>; <PERSON>Michael-Bogdan Oginski</PERSON> (10 October 1848-25 March 1909) with mistress Countess <PERSON>Olga Kalinovskya</PERSON> (1818-1854); and <PERSON>Joseph Raboxicz</PERSON>.

On 6 July 1880, less than a month after <PERSON>Tsarina Maria</PERSON>'s death on 8 June, <PERSON>Alexander</PERSON> formed a morganatic marriage with his mistress Princess <PERSON>Catherine Dolgorukov</PERSON>, with whom he already had four children. 

At the beginning of his reign, <PERSON>Alexander</PERSON> expressed the famous statement "No dreams" addressed for Poles, populating <ORGANIZATION>Congress Poland</ORGANIZATION>, <LOCATION>Western Ukraine</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Lithuania</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Livonia</LOCATION> and <LOCATION>Belarus</LOCATION>. The result was the January Uprising of 1863-1864 that was suppressed after eighteen months of fighting. Thousands of Poles were executed, tens of thousands were deported to <LOCATION>Siberia</LOCATION>. The price for suppression was Russian support for Prussian-united <LOCATION>Germany</LOCATION>. Twenty years later, <LOCATION>Germany</LOCATION> became the major enemy of <LOCATION>Russia</LOCATION> on the continent.

All territories of the former Poland-Lithuania were excluded from liberal policies introduced by <PERSON>Alexander</PERSON>. The martial law in <LOCATION>Lithuania</LOCATION>, introduced in 1863, lasted for the next 50 years. Native languages, Lithuanian, Ukrainian and Belarusian were completely banned from printed texts, see a , e.g., Ems Ukase. The Polish language was banned in both oral and written form from all provinces except <LOCATION>Congress Kingdom</LOCATION>, where it was allowed in private conversations only.



In 1863 <PERSON>Alexander II</PERSON> re-established the Diet of <LOCATION>Finland</LOCATION> and initiated several reforms increasing <LOCATION>Finland</LOCATION>'s autonomy from <LOCATION>Russia</LOCATION> including establishment of own currency, the Markka. Liberation of enterprise led to increased foreign investment and industrial development. Finally, the elevation of Finnish from a language of the common people to a national language equal to Swedish opened opportunities for a larger proportion of the society. <PERSON>Alexander II</PERSON> is still regarded as "The Good Tsar" in <LOCATION>Finland</LOCATION>.

<PERSON>Alexander</PERSON>'s attitude towards <LOCATION>Finland</LOCATION> could be seen as genuine belief that reforms were easier to test in a small, homogeneous country than the whole of <LOCATION>Russia</LOCATION>. The benevolent treatment of <LOCATION>Finland</LOCATION> may also be seen as a reward for the loyalty of its relatively western oriented population during the Crimean war and during the Polish uprising. Encouraging Finnish nationalism and language can also be seen as an attempt to weaken the strong ties with <LOCATION>Sweden</LOCATION>.

In 1866 there was an attempt on his life in <LOCATION>Petersburg</LOCATION> by <PERSON>Dmitry Karakozov</PERSON>. To commemorate his narrow escape from death (which he referred to only as "the event of April 4, 1866"), a number of churches and chapels were built in many Russian cities.

On the morning of 20 April 1879, <PERSON>Alexander II</PERSON> was briskly walking towards the Square of the Guards Staff and faced <PERSON>Alexander Soloviev</PERSON>, a 33 year-old former student. Having seen a menacing revolver in his hands, the Tsar fled. <PERSON>Soloviev</PERSON> fired five times but missed;and he was sentenced to death and hanged on 28 May.

The student acted on his own, but other revolutionaries were keen to murder <PERSON>Alexander</PERSON>. In December 1879, the <ORGANIZATION>Narodnaya Volya</ORGANIZATION> (People's Will), a radical revolutionary group which hoped to ignite a social revolution, organised an explosion on the railway from <LOCATION>Livadia</LOCATION> to <LOCATION>Moscow</LOCATION>, but they missed the Tsar's train. On the evening of 5 February 1880 the same revolutionaries set off a charge under the dining room of the Winter Palace, right in the resting room of the guards a story below. Being late for supper, the Tsar was unharmed, although 67 other people were killed or wounded. The dining room floor was also heavily damaged.

After the last assassination attempt, <PERSON>Count Loris-Melikov</PERSON> was appointed the head of the <ORGANIZATION>Supreme Executive Commission</ORGANIZATION> and given extraordinary powers to fight the revolutionaries. <PERSON>Loris-Melikov</PERSON>'s proposals called for some form of parliamentary body, and the Emperor seemed to agree; these plans were never realized as on 13 March (1 March Old Style Date), 1881 <PERSON>Alexander</PERSON> fell victim to an assassination plot.

As he had done every Sunday for a score of years, the tsar went to the Manege to review the <ORGANIZATION>Life Guards of the Reserve Infantry</ORGANIZATION> and the Life Guards of the Sapper Battalion regiments. He travelled both to and from the Menege in a closed carriage accompanied by six Cossacks with a seventh sitting on the coachman's left. The tsar's carriage was followed by two sleighs carrying, among others, the chief of police and the chief of the tsar's guards. The route, as always, was via the <ORGANIZATION>Catherine Canal</ORGANIZATION> and over the <LOCATION>Pevchesky Bridge</LOCATION>.

The street was flanked by narrow sidewalks on both the right and left side. A short young man wearing a heavy black overcoat edged towards the imperial carriage making its way down the street. He was carrying a small white package wrapped in a handkerchief. The youth was <PERSON>Nikolai Rysakov</PERSON>,"After a moment's hesitation I threw the bomb. I sent it under the horses' hooves in the supposition that it would blow up under the carriage...The explosion knocked me into the fence."

The explosion, while killing one of the Cossacks and seriously wounding the driver and people on the sidewalk, several critically, had only damaged the carriage. It was bulletproof, a gift from <PERSON>Napoleon III</PERSON> of <LOCATION>France</LOCATION>. The tsar emerged shaken but unhurt. <PERSON>Rysakov</PERSON> was captured almost immediately. Police Chief <PERSON>Dvorzhitsky</PERSON> heard <PERSON>Rysakov</PERSON> shout out to someone in the gathering crowd. Realizing there was another (if not more than one) bomber near by he urged the tsar to leave the area at once. <PERSON>Alexander</PERSON> agreed to do so but only after he had been shown the site of the explosion. Completely surrounded by the guards and the Cossacks, the tsar made his way over to the hole in the street. It was then that a young man, <PERSON>Ignacy Hryniewiecki</PERSON>, standing by the canal fence, raised up both arms and threw something at the tsar's feet. <PERSON>Dvorzhitsky</PERSON> was later to write:

"I was deafened by the new explosion, burned, wounded and thrown to the ground. Suddenly, amid the smoke and snowy fog, I heard His Majesty's weak voice cry, 'Help!' Gathering what strength I had, I jumped up and rushed to the tsar. His Majesty was half-lying, half-sitting, leaning on his right arm. Thinking he was merely wounded heavily, I tried to lift him but the tsar's legs were shattered, and the blood poured out of them. Twenty people, with wounds of varying degree, lay on the sidewalk and on the street. Some managed to stand, others to crawl, still others tried to get out from beneath bodies that had fallen on them. Through the snow, debris, and blood you could see fragments of clothing, epaulets, sabers, and bloody chunks of human flesh."

Later it was learned there was a third bomber in the crowd. <PERSON>Ivan Emelyanov</PERSON> stood ready, clutching a briefcase containing a bomb that would be used if the other two bombs, and bombers, failed.

<PERSON>Alexander</PERSON> was carried by sleigh to the Winter Palace, up the marble staircase, a trail of blood in his wake, and in to his study where, twenty years before almost to the date, he had signed the Emancipation Edict freeing the serfs. <PERSON>Alexander</PERSON> with both legs destroyed, was bleeding to death. Members of the <PERSON>Romanov</PERSON> family came rushing to the scene. One of them was the quiet, sensitive thirteen year old boy named <PERSON>Nicky</PERSON>, elder son of the heir-apparent <PERSON>Alexander</PERSON>; the boy would grow up to be tsar in his own right, <PERSON>Nicholas II</PERSON> of <LOCATION>Russia</LOCATION>.

The dying tsar was given Communion and Extreme Unction. There was nothing to do now but wait. When asked how long it would be, the attending physician Dr. <PERSON>S.P. Borkin</PERSON> replied, "Up to fifteen minutes"At 3:30 that day the standard of <PERSON>Alexander II</PERSON> was lowered for the last time.

The assassination also caused a great setback for the reform movement. One of <PERSON>Alexander II</PERSON>'s last ideas was to draft up plans for an elected parliament, or <ORGANIZATION>Duma</ORGANIZATION>, which were completed the day before he died but not yet released to the Russian people. The first action <PERSON>Alexander III</PERSON> took after his coronation was to tear up those plans. A <ORGANIZATION>Duma</ORGANIZATION> would not come into fruition until 1905, by <PERSON>Alexander II</PERSON>'s grandson, <PERSON>Nicholas II</PERSON>, who commissioned the <ORGANIZATION>Duma</ORGANIZATION> following heavy pressure on the monarchy by the Russian Revolution of 1905.

A second consequence of the assassination was anti-Jewish pogroms and legislation. Despite the fact only one Jew was involved in the assassination conspiracy, over 200 Jews who had nothing to do with the murder of <PERSON>Alexander II</PERSON> were beaten to death in these pogroms.

A third consequence of the assassination was that suppression of civil liberties in <LOCATION>Russia</LOCATION> and police brutality burst back with a full force after experiencing some restraint under the reign of <PERSON>Alexander II</PERSON>. <PERSON>Alexander II</PERSON>'s murder and subsequent death was witnessed firsthand by his son, <PERSON>Alexander III</PERSON>, and his grandson, <PERSON>Nicholas II</PERSON>, both future Tsars, who vowed not to have the same fate befall them. Both used the Okhrana to arrest protestors and uproot suspected rebel groups, creating further suppression of personal freedom for the Russian people.

<TITLE><PERSON>Benjamin Franklin</PERSON></TITLE>

<PERSON>Benjamin Franklin</LOCATION> (January 17, 1706 [O.S. January 6, 1705] April 17, 1790) was one of the Founding Fathers of the <LOCATION>United States of America</LOCATION>. A noted polymath, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> was a leading author and printer, satirist, political theorist, politician, scientist, inventor, civic activist, statesman and diplomat. As a scientist he was a major figure in the Enlightenment and the history of physics for his discoveries and theories regarding electricity. He invented the lightning rod, bifocals, the Franklin stove, a carriage odometer, and a musical instrument. He formed both the first public lending library in <LOCATION>America</LOCATION> and first fire department in <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION>. He was an early proponent of colonial unity and as a political writer and activist he, more than anyone, invented the idea of an American nation and as a diplomat during the American Revolution, he secured the French alliance that helped to make independence possible.

<PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> is credited as being foundational to the roots of American values and character, a marriage of the practical and democratic Puritan values of thrift, hard work, education, community spirit, self-governing institutions, and opposition to authoritarianism both political and religious, with the scientific and tolerant values of the Enlightenment. In the words of <PERSON>Henry Steele Commager</PERSON>, "In <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> could be merged the virtues of Puritanism without its defects, the illumination of the Enlightenment without its heat." To <PERSON>Walter Isaacson</PERSON>, this makes <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>, "the most accomplished American of his age and the most influential in inventing the type of society <LOCATION>America</LOCATION> would become."

<PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> became a newspaper editor, printer, and merchant in <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION>, becoming very wealthy, writing and publishing Poor <PERSON>Richard</PERSON>'s Almanack and the <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION> Gazette. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> was interested in science and technology, and gained international renown for his famous experiments. He played a major role in establishing the <ORGANIZATION>University of Pennsylvania and Franklin & Marshall College</ORGANIZATION> and was elected the first president of the <ORGANIZATION>American Philosophical Society</ORGANIZATION>. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> became a national hero in <LOCATION>America</LOCATION> when he spearheaded the effort to have <ORGANIZATION>Parliament</ORGANIZATION> repeal the unpopular Stamp Act. An accomplished diplomat, he was widely admired among the <LOCATION>French</LOCATION> as American minister to <LOCATION>Paris</LOCATION> and was a major figure in the development of positive Franco-American relations. From 1775 to 1776, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> was Postmaster General under the <ORGANIZATION>Continental Congress</ORGANIZATION> and from 1785 to 1788 was President of the <ORGANIZATION>Supreme Executive Council of Pennsylvania</ORGANIZATION>. Toward the end of his life, he became one of the most prominent abolitionists.

<PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>'s colorful life and legacy of scientific and political achievement, and status as one of <LOCATION>America</LOCATION>'s most influential Founding Fathers, has seen <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> honored on coinage and money; warships; the names of many towns, counties, educational institutions, namesakes, and companies; and more than two centuries after his death, countless cultural references.

<PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>'s father, <PERSON>Josiah Franklin</PERSON>, was born at <LOCATION>Ecton</LOCATION>, <ORGANIZATION>Northamptonshire</ORGANIZATION>, <LOCATION>England</LOCATION> on December 23, 1657, the son of <PERSON>Thomas Franklin</PERSON>, a blacksmith and farmer, and <PERSON>Jane White</PERSON>. His mother, <PERSON>Abiah Folger</PERSON>, was born in <LOCATION>Nantucket</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Massachusetts</LOCATION>, on August 15, 1667, to <PERSON>Peter Folger</PERSON>, a miller and schoolteacher and his wife <PERSON>Mary Morrill</PERSON>, a former indentured servant. A descendant of the Folgers, <PERSON>J.A. Folger</PERSON>, founded <ORGANIZATION>Folgers Coffee</ORGANIZATION> in the 19th century.

<PERSON>Josiah Franklin</PERSON> had seventeen children by his two wives. His first wife was <PERSON>Anne Child</PERSON>, whom he married about 1677 in <LOCATION>Ecton</LOCATION> and immigrated to <LOCATION>Boston</LOCATION> with in 1683; they had three children before immigrating, and four after. After her death, <PERSON>Josiah</PERSON> was married to <PERSON>Abiah Folger</PERSON> on July 9, 1689 in the Old South Meeting House by <PERSON>Samuel Willard</PERSON>. <PERSON>Benjamin</PERSON>, their eighth child, was <PERSON>Josiah Franklin</PERSON>'s fifteenth child and tenth and last son.

<PERSON>Josiah Franklin</PERSON> converted to the Puritanism in the 1670s. Puritanism was a <ORGANIZATION>Protestant</ORGANIZATION> movement in <LOCATION>England</LOCATION> to "purify" Anglicanism from elements of the <ORGANIZATION>Roman Catholic</ORGANIZATION> religion, which they considered superstitious. Three things were important to the Puritans: that each congregation would be self-governing, that ministers give sermons instead of performing rituals such as a Mass and individual Bible study so that each believer could develop a personal understanding and relationship with God. Puritanism appealed to smart, middle-class people such as <PERSON>Benjamin Franklin</PERSON>'s father, who enjoyed the governance meetings, discussion, study and personal independence.

The roots of American democracy can be seen in these Puritan values of self-government, the importance of the individual and active indignation against unjust authority, which were passed on to <PERSON>Benjamin Franklin</PERSON> and other founding fathers such as <PERSON>John Adams</PERSON>. One of <PERSON>Josiah</PERSON>'s core Puritan values was that personal worth is earned through hard work, which makes the industrious man the equal of kings, which <PERSON>Ben Franklin</PERSON> etched onto his father's tombstone, from his father <PERSON>Josiah</PERSON>'s favorite Bible quote, from the Hebrew Bible, Proverbs 22:29: "Seest thou a man diligent in his calling, he shall stand before <ORGANIZATION>Kings</ORGANIZATION>." Hard work and equality were two Puritan values <PERSON>Ben Franklin</PERSON> preached throughout his own life (ibid, p 78) and spread widely through Poor <PERSON>Richard</PERSON>'s Almanac and his autobiography.

<PERSON>Ben Franklin</PERSON>'s mother, <PERSON>Abiah Folger</PERSON>, was born into a Puritan family that was among the first Pilgrims to flee to <LOCATION>Massachusetts</LOCATION> for religious freedom, when King Charles I of <LOCATION>England</LOCATION> began persecuting Protestants. They sailed for <LOCATION>Boston</LOCATION> in 1635. Her father was "the sort of rebel destined to transform colonial <LOCATION>America</LOCATION>." As clerk of the court, he was jailed for disobeying the local magistrate in defense of middle-class shopkeepers and artisans in conflict with wealthy landowners. <PERSON>Ben Franklin</PERSON> followed in his grandfather's footsteps in his battles against the wealthy <PERSON>Penn</PERSON> family that owned the <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION> Colony.



<PERSON>Benjamin Franklin</PERSON> was born on Milk Street, in <LOCATION>Boston</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Massachusetts</LOCATION>, on January 17, 1706 and baptized at Old South Meeting House. His father, <PERSON>Josiah Franklin</PERSON>, was a tallow chandler, a maker of candles and soap, whose second wife, <PERSON>Abiah Folger</PERSON>, was <PERSON>Benjamin</PERSON>'s mother. <PERSON>Josiah</PERSON>'s marriages produced 17 children; <PERSON>Benjamin</PERSON> was the fifteenth child and youngest son. <PERSON>Josiah</PERSON> wanted <PERSON>Ben</PERSON> to attend school with the clergy but only had enough money to send him to school for two years. He attended <ORGANIZATION>Boston Latin School</ORGANIZATION> but did not graduate; he continued his education through voracious reading. Although "his parents talked of the church as a career" for <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>, his schooling ended when he was ten. He then worked for his father for a time and at 12 he became an apprentice to his brother <PERSON>James</PERSON>, a printer, who taught <PERSON>Ben</PERSON> the printing trade. When <PERSON>Ben</PERSON> was 15, <PERSON>James</PERSON> created the <LOCATION>New England Courant</LOCATION>, the first truly independent newspaper in the colonies. When denied the option to write to the paper, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> invented the pseudonym of "Mrs. <PERSON>Silence Dogood</PERSON>," who was ostensibly a middle-aged widow. The letters were published in the paper and became a subject of conversation around town. Neither <PERSON>James</PERSON> nor the Courant's readers were aware of the ruse, and <PERSON>James</PERSON> was unhappy with <PERSON>Ben</PERSON> when he discovered the popular correspondent was his younger brother. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> left his apprenticeship without permission and in so doing became a fugitive.

At age 17, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> ran away to <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION>, seeking a new start in a new city. When he first arrived he worked in several printer shops around town. However, he was not satisfied by the immediate prospects. After a few months, while working in a printing house, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> was convinced by <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION> Governor Sir <PERSON>William Keith</PERSON> to go to <LOCATION>London</LOCATION>, ostensibly to acquire the equipment necessary for establishing another newspaper in <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION>. Finding <PERSON>Keith</PERSON>'s promises of backing a newspaper to be empty, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> worked as a compositor in a printer's shop in what is now the <ORGANIZATION>Church of St Bartholomew-the-Great</ORGANIZATION> in the <ORGANIZATION>Smithfield</ORGANIZATION> area of <LOCATION>London</LOCATION>. Following this, he returned to <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION> in 1726 with the help of a merchant named <PERSON>Thomas Denham</PERSON>, who gave <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> a position as clerk, shopkeeper, and bookkeeper in <PERSON>Denham</PERSON>'s merchant business.

In 1727, <PERSON>Benjamin Franklin</PERSON>, 21, created the Junto, a group of "like minded aspiring artisans and tradesmen who hoped to improve themselves while they improved their community." The Junto was a discussion group for issues of the day; it subsequently gave rise to many organizations in <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION>.

Reading was a great pastime of the Junto, but books were rare and expensive. The members created a library, and initially pooled their own books together. This did not work, however, and <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> initiated the idea of a subscription library, where the members pooled their monetary resources to buy books. This idea was the birth of the <ORGANIZATION>Library Company</ORGANIZATION>, with the charter of the <ORGANIZATION>Library Company</ORGANIZATION> of <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION> created in 1731 by <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> hired the first American librarian in 1732, <PERSON>Louis Timothee</PERSON>. 

Originally, the books were kept in the homes of the first librarians, but in 1739 the collection was moved to the second floor of the <ORGANIZATION>State House</ORGANIZATION> of <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION>, now known as Independence Hall. In 1791, a new building was built specifically for the library. The Library Company flourished with no competition and gained many priceless collections from bibliophiles such as <PERSON>James Logan</PERSON> and his physician brother <PERSON>William</PERSON>. The Library Company is now a great scholarly and research library with 500,000 rare books, pamphlets, and broadsides, more than 160,000 manuscripts, and 75,000 graphic items.

Upon <PERSON>Denham</PERSON>'s death, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> returned to his former trade. By 1730, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> had set up a printing house of his own and had contrived to become the publisher of a newspaper called The <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION> Gazette. The Gazette gave <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> a forum for agitation about a variety of local reforms and initiatives through printed essays and observations. Over time, his commentary, together with a great deal of savvy about cultivating a positive image of an industrious and intellectual young man, earned him a great deal of social respect; though even after <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> had achieved fame as a scientist and statesman, he habitually signed his letters with the unpretentious '<PERSON>B. Franklin</PERSON>, Printer.'

In 1731, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> was initiated into the local Freemason lodge, becoming a grand master in 1734, indicating his rapid rise to prominence in <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION>. That same year, he edited and published the first Masonic book in the <LOCATION>Americas</LOCATION>, a reprint of <PERSON>James Anderson</PERSON>'s Constitutions of the Free-Masons. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> remained a Freemason throughout the rest of his life.

In 1724, while a boarder in the Read home, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> had courted <PERSON>Deborah Read</PERSON> before going to <LOCATION>London</LOCATION> at Governor <PERSON>Keith</PERSON>'s request. At that time, Miss Read's mother was wary of allowing her daughter to wed a seventeen-year old who was on his way to <LOCATION>London</LOCATION>. Her own husband having recently died, Mrs. <PERSON>Read</PERSON> declined <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>'s offer of marriage.

While <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> was in <LOCATION>London</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Deborah</PERSON> married a man named <PERSON>John Rodgers</PERSON>. This proved to be a regrettable decision. <PERSON>Rodgers</PERSON> shortly avoided his debts and prosecution by fleeing to <LOCATION>Barbados</LOCATION>, leaving <PERSON>Deborah</PERSON> behind. With <PERSON>Rodgers</PERSON>' fate unknown, and bigamy illegal, <PERSON>Deborah</PERSON> was not free to remarry.

In 1730, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> acknowledged an illegitimate son named <PERSON>William</PERSON>, who would eventually become the last Loyalist governor of <LOCATION>New Jersey</LOCATION>. While the identity of <PERSON>William</PERSON>'s mother remains unknown, perhaps the responsibility of an infant child gave <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> a reason to take up residence with <PERSON>Deborah Read</PERSON>. <PERSON>William</PERSON> was raised in the Franklin household but eventually broke with his father over the treatment of the colonies at the hands of the crown. However, he was not above using his father's fame to enhance his own standing.

<PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> established a common-law marriage with <PERSON>Deborah Read</PERSON> on September 1, 1730. In addition to raising <PERSON>William</PERSON>, <PERSON>Benjamin</PERSON> and <PERSON>Deborah Franklin</PERSON> had two children together. The first, <PERSON>Francis Folger Franklin</PERSON>, born October 1732, died of smallpox in 1736. <PERSON>Sarah Franklin</PERSON>, nicknamed <PERSON>Sally</PERSON>, was born in 1743. She eventually married <PERSON>Richard Bache</PERSON>, had seven children, and cared for her father in his old age.

<PERSON>Deborah</PERSON>'s fear of the sea meant that she never accompanied <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> on any of his extended trips to <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION>, despite his repeated requests. However, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> did not leave <LOCATION>London</LOCATION> to visit <PERSON>Deborah</PERSON> even after she wrote to him in November 1769 saying her illness was due to dissatisfied distress because of his prolonged absence. <PERSON>Deborah Read Franklin</PERSON> died of a stroke in 1774, while <PERSON>Benjamin</PERSON> was on an extended trip to <LOCATION>England</LOCATION>.

In 1733, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> began to publish the famous Poor <PERSON>Richard</PERSON>'s Almanack (with content both original and borrowed) under the pseudonym <PERSON>Richard Saunders</PERSON>, on which much of his popular reputation is based. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> frequently wrote under pseudonyms. Although it was no secret that <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> was the author, his <PERSON>Richard Saunders</PERSON> character repeatedly denied it. "Poor <PERSON>Richard</PERSON>'s Proverbs," adages from this almanac, such as "A penny saved is twopence dear" (often misquoted as "A penny saved is a penny earned"), "Fish and visitors stink in three days" remain common quotations in the modern world. Wisdom in folk society meant the ability to provide an apt adage for any occasion, and <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>'s readers became well prepared. He sold about ten thousand copies per year (a circulation equivalent to nearly three million today).

In 1758, the year in which he ceased writing for the Almanack, he printed <PERSON>Father Abraham</PERSON>'s Sermon, also known as The Way to Wealth. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>'s autobiography, published after his death, has become one of the classics of the genre.



<PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> was a prodigious inventor. Among his many creations were the lightning rod, the glass harmonica, the Franklin stove, bifocal glasses, and the flexible urinary catheter. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> never patented his inventions; in his autobiography he wrote, "... as we enjoy great advantages from the inventions of others, we should be glad of an opportunity to serve others by any invention of ours; and this we should do freely and generously." His inventions also included social innovations, such as paying forward.

As deputy postmaster, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> became interested in the <LOCATION>North Atlantic Ocean</LOCATION> circulation patterns which carried mail ships. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> worked with <PERSON>Timothy Folger</PERSON>, his cousin and experienced <LOCATION>Nantucket</LOCATION> whaler captain, and other experienced ship captains, learning enough to chart the <LOCATION>Gulf Stream</LOCATION>, giving it the name by which it's still known today. It took many years for British sea captains to follow <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>'s advice on navigating the current, but once they did, they were able to gain two weeks in sailing time.

In 1743, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> founded the <ORGANIZATION>American Philosophical Society</ORGANIZATION> to help scientific men discuss their discoveries and theories. He began the electrical research that, along with other scientific inquiries, would occupy him for the rest of his life, in between bouts of politics and moneymaking.



In 1748, he retired from printing and went into other businesses. He created a partnership with his foreman, <PERSON>David Hall</PERSON>, which provided <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> with half of the shop's profits for 18 years. This lucrative business arrangement provided leisure time for study, and in a few years he had made discoveries that gave him a reputation with the educated throughout <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION> and especially in <LOCATION>France</LOCATION>.

His discoveries included his investigations of electricity. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> proposed that "vitreous" and "resinous" electricity were not different types of "electrical fluid" (as electricity was called then), but the same electrical fluid under different pressures. He was the first to label them as positive and negative respectively, and he was the first to discover the principle of conservation of charge. In 1750, he published a proposal for an experiment to prove that lightning is electricity by flying a kite in a storm that appeared capable of becoming a lightning storm. On May 10, 1752, <PERSON>Thomas-Franois Dalibard</PERSON> of <LOCATION>France</LOCATION> conducted <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>'s experiment (using a -tall iron rod instead of a kite) and extracted electrical sparks from a cloud. On June 15, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> may have possibly conducted his famous kite experiment in <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION> and also successfully extracted sparks from a cloud, although there are theories that suggest he never performed the experiment. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>'s experiment was not written up until <PERSON>Joseph Priestley</PERSON>'s 1767 History and Present Status of Electricity; the evidence shows that <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> was insulated (not in a conducting path, since he would have been in danger of electrocution in the event of a lightning strike). (Others, such as Prof. <PERSON>Georg Wilhelm Richmann</PERSON> of <LOCATION>Saint Petersburg</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Russia</LOCATION>, were electrocuted during the months following <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>'s experiment.) In his writings, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> indicates that he was aware of the dangers and offered alternative ways to demonstrate that lightning was electrical, as shown by his use of the concept of electrical ground. If <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> did perform this experiment, he did not do it in the way that is often described, flying the kite and waiting to be struck by lightning, as it would have been fatal. Instead, he used the kite to collect some electric charge from a storm cloud, which implied that lightning was electrical.

On October 19 in a letter to <LOCATION>England</LOCATION> explaining directions for repeating the experiment, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> wrote:

"When rain has wet the kite twine so that it can conduct the electric fire freely, you will find it streams out plentifully from the key at the approach of your knuckle, and with this key a phial, or <LOCATION>Leiden</LOCATION> jar, maybe charged: and from electric fire thus obtained spirits may be kindled, and all other electric experiments [may be] performed which are usually done by the help of a rubber glass globe or tube; and therefore the sameness of the electrical matter with that of lightening completely demonstrated."

<PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>'s electrical experiments led to his invention of the lightning rod. He noted that conductors with a sharp rather than a smooth point were capable of discharging silently, and at a far greater distance. He surmised that this knowledge could be of use in protecting buildings from lightning, by attaching "upright Rods of Iron, made sharp as a Needle and gilt to prevent Rusting, and from the Foot of those Rods a Wire down the outside of the Building into the Ground;...Would not these pointed Rods probably draw the Electrical Fire silently out of a Cloud before it came nigh enough to strike, and thereby secure us from that most sudden and terrible Mischief!" Following a series of experiments on <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>'s own house, lightning rods were installed on the Academy of <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION> (later the <ORGANIZATION>University of Pennsylvania</ORGANIZATION>) and the <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION> State House (later Independence Hall) in 1752.

In recognition of his work with electricity, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> received the <ORGANIZATION>Royal Society</ORGANIZATION>'s Copley Medal in 1753, and in 1756 he became one of the few eighteenth century Americans to be elected as a Fellow of the Society. The cgs unit of electric charge has been named after him: one franklin (Fr) is equal to one statcoulomb.

On October 21, 1743, according to popular myth, a storm moving from the southwest denied <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> the opportunity of witnessing a lunar eclipse. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> was said to have noted that the prevailing winds were actually from the northeast, contrary to what he had expected. In correspondence with his brother, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> learned that the same storm had not reached <LOCATION>Boston</LOCATION> until after the eclipse, despite the fact that <LOCATION>Boston</LOCATION> is to the northeast of <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION>. He deduced that storms do not always travel in the direction of the prevailing wind, a concept which would have great influence in meteorology.

<PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> noted a principle of refrigeration by observing that on a very hot day, he stayed cooler in a wet shirt in a breeze than he did in a dry one. To understand this phenomenon more clearly <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> conducted experiments. On one warm day in <LOCATION>Cambridge</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>England</LOCATION>, in 1758, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> and fellow scientist <PERSON>John Hadley</PERSON> experimented by continually wetting the ball of a mercury thermometer with ether and using bellows to evaporate the ether. With each subsequent evaporation, the thermometer read a lower temperature, eventually reaching 7F (-14C). Another thermometer showed the room temperature to be constant at 65F (18C). In his letter "Cooling by Evaporation," <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> noted that "one may see the possibility of freezing a man to death on a warm summers day."

<PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> is known to have played the violin, the harp, and the guitar. He also composed music, notably a string quartet in early classical style, and invented a much-improved version of the glass harmonica, in which each glass was made to rotate on its own, with the player's fingers held steady, instead of the other way around; this version soon found its way to <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION>.

In 1736, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> created the <ORGANIZATION>Union Fire Company</ORGANIZATION>, one of the first volunteer fire fighting companies in <LOCATION>America</LOCATION>. In the same year, he printed a new currency for <LOCATION>New Jersey</LOCATION> based on innovative anti-counterfeiting techniques which he had devised.

As he matured, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> began to concern himself more with public affairs. In 1743, he set forth a scheme for <ORGANIZATION>The Academy and College</ORGANIZATION> of <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION>. He was appointed president of the academy in November 13, 1749, and it opened on August 13, 1751. At its first commencement, on May 17, 1757, seven men graduated; six with a Bachelor of Arts and one as Master of Arts. It was later merged with the <ORGANIZATION>University of</ORGANIZATION> the State of <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION> to become the <ORGANIZATION>University of Pennsylvania</ORGANIZATION>.

In 1753, both <ORGANIZATION>Harvard</ORGANIZATION> and <ORGANIZATION>Yale</ORGANIZATION> awarded him honorary degrees.

In 1751, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> and Dr. <PERSON>Thomas Bond</PERSON> obtained a charter from the <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION> legislature to establish a hospital. <LOCATION>Pennsylvania Hospital</LOCATION> was the first hospital in what was to become the <LOCATION>United States of America</LOCATION>.



<PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> became involved in <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION> politics and rapidly progressed. In October 1748, he was selected as a councilman, in June 1749 he became a Justice of the Peace for <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION>, and in 1751 he was elected to the <LOCATION>Pennsylvania Assembly</LOCATION>. On August 10, 1753, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> was appointed joint deputy postmaster-general of <LOCATION>North America</LOCATION>. His most notable service in domestic politics was his reform of the postal system, but his fame as a statesman rests chiefly on his subsequent diplomatic services in connection with the relations of the colonies with <LOCATION>Great Britain</LOCATION>, and later with <LOCATION>France</LOCATION>.

In 1754, he headed the <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION> delegation to the <ORGANIZATION>Albany Congress</ORGANIZATION>. This meeting of several colonies had been requested by the <ORGANIZATION>Board of Trade</ORGANIZATION> in <LOCATION>England</LOCATION> to improve relations with the Indians and defense against the French. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> proposed a broad Plan of Union for the colonies. While the plan was not adopted, elements of it found their way into the Articles of Confederation and the Constitution.

In 1756, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> organized the <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION> Militia (see "Associated Regiment of <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION>" under heading of <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION>'s 103rd Artillery and <ORGANIZATION>111th Infantry Regiment</ORGANIZATION> at <ORGANIZATION>Continental Army</ORGANIZATION>).
