He used <PERSON>Tun Tavern</PERSON> as a gathering place to recruit a regiment of soldiers to go into battle against the Native American uprisings that beset the American colonies. {<PERSON>Reportably Franklin</PERSON> was elected "Colonel" of the <ORGANIZATION>Associated Regiment</ORGANIZATION> but declined the honor}.

In 1757, he was sent to <LOCATION>England</LOCATION> by the <ORGANIZATION>Pennsylvania Assembly</ORGANIZATION> as a colonial agent to protest against the political influence of the <LOCATION>Penn</LOCATION> family, the proprietors of the colony. He remained there for five years, striving to end the proprietors' prerogative to overturn legislation from the elected Assembly, and their exemption from paying taxes on their land. His lack of influential allies in <ORGANIZATION>Whitehall</ORGANIZATION> led to the failure of this mission. In 1759, the <ORGANIZATION>University of St Andrews</ORGANIZATION> awarded him an Honorary Doctor of Laws degree. In 1762, <ORGANIZATION>Oxford University</ORGANIZATION> awarded <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> an honorary doctorate for his scientific accomplishments and from then on he went by "Doctor <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>." He also managed to secure a post for his illegitimate son, <PERSON>William Franklin</PERSON>, as Colonial Governor of <LOCATION>New Jersey</LOCATION>.

During his stay in <LOCATION>London</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> became involved in radical politics. He was a member of the Club of Honest Whigs, alongside thinkers such as <PERSON>Richard Price</PERSON>.

In 1756, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> became a member of the Society for the <ORGANIZATION>Encouragement of Arts</ORGANIZATION>, <ORGANIZATION>Manufactures & Commerce</ORGANIZATION> (now <ORGANIZATION>Royal Society of Arts</ORGANIZATION> or RSA, which had been founded in 1754), whose early meetings took place in coffee shops in <LOCATION>London</LOCATION>'s <LOCATION>Covent Garden</LOCATION> district, close to <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>'s main residence in <LOCATION>Craven Street</LOCATION> (the only one of his residences to survive and which opened to the public as the <ORGANIZATION>Benjamin Franklin House</ORGANIZATION> museum on January 17, 2006). After his return to <LOCATION>America</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> became the <ORGANIZATION>Society</ORGANIZATION>'s Corresponding Member and remained closely connected with the Society. The RSA instituted a Benjamin Franklin Medal in 1956 to commemorate the 250th anniversary of <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>'s birth and the 200th anniversary of his membership of the RSA.

During his stays at <LOCATION>Craven Street</LOCATION> in <LOCATION>London</LOCATION> between 1757 and 1775, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> developed a close friendship with his landlady, <PERSON>Margaret Stevenson</PERSON> and her circle of friends and relations, in particular her daughter <PERSON>Mary</PERSON>, who was more often known as <PERSON>Polly</PERSON>.

In 1759, he visited <LOCATION>Edinburgh</LOCATION> with his son, and recalled his conversations there as "the densest happiness of my life."

He also joined the influential <LOCATION>Birmingham</LOCATION> based <ORGANIZATION>Lunar Society</ORGANIZATION> with whom he regularly corresponded and on occasion, visited in <LOCATION>Birmingham</LOCATION> in the <LOCATION>West Midlands</LOCATION>.



In 1763, soon after <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> returned to <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION>, the western frontier was engulfed in a bitter war known as <LOCATION>Pontiac</LOCATION>'s Rebellion. The <PERSON>Paxton Boys</PERSON>, a group of settlers convinced that the <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION> government was not doing enough to protect them from American Indian raids, murdered a group of peaceful Susquehannock Indians and then marched on <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION>. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> helped to organize the local militia in order to defend the capital against the mob, and then met with the <PERSON>Paxton</PERSON> leaders and persuaded them to disperse. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> wrote a scathing attack against the racial prejudice of the Paxton Boys. "If an Indian injures me," he asked, "does it follow that I may revenge that Injury on all Indians?"

At this time, many members of the <ORGANIZATION>Pennsylvania Assembly</ORGANIZATION> were feuding with <PERSON>William Penn</PERSON>'s heirs, who controlled the colony as proprietors. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> led the "anti-proprietary party" in the struggle against the <LOCATION>Penn</LOCATION> family, and was elected Speaker of the <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION> House in May 1764. His call for a change from proprietary to royal government was a rare political miscalculation, however: Pennsylvanians worried that such a move would endanger their political and religious freedoms. Because of these fears, and because of political attacks on his character, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> lost his seat in the October 1764 Assembly elections. The anti-proprietary party dispatched <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> to <LOCATION>England</LOCATION> to continue the struggle against the <LOCATION>Penn</LOCATION> family proprietorship, but during this visit, events would drastically change the nature of his mission.

In <LOCATION>London</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> opposed the 1765 Stamp Act, but when he was unable to prevent its passage, he made another political miscalculation and recommended a friend to the post of stamp distributor for <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION>. Pennsylvanians were outraged, believing that he had supported the measure all along, and threatened to destroy his home in <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION>. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> soon learned of the extent of colonial resistance to the Stamp Act, and his testimony before the <ORGANIZATION>House of Commons</ORGANIZATION> led to its repeal. With this, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> suddenly emerged as the leading spokesman for American interests in <LOCATION>England</LOCATION>. He wrote popular essays on behalf of the colonies, and <LOCATION>Georgia</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>New Jersey</LOCATION>, and <LOCATION>Massachusetts</LOCATION> also appointed him as their agent to the Crown.



In September 1767, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> visited <LOCATION>Paris</LOCATION> with his usual traveling partner, Sir <PERSON>John Pringle</PERSON>. News of his electrical discoveries was widespread in <LOCATION>France</LOCATION>. His reputation meant that he was introduced to many influential scientists and politicians, and also to <LOCATION>King Louis</LOCATION> XV.

While living in <LOCATION>London</LOCATION> in 1768, he developed a phonetic alphabet in A Scheme for a new Alphabet and a Reformed Mode of Spelling. This reformed alphabet discarded six letters <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> regarded as redundant (c, j, q, w, x and y), and substituted six new letters for sounds he felt lacked letters of their own. His new alphabet, however, never caught on and he eventually lost interest.

In 1771, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> traveled extensively around the <LOCATION>British Isles</LOCATION> staying with, among others, <PERSON>Joseph Priestley</PERSON> and <PERSON>David Hume</PERSON>. In <LOCATION>Dublin</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> was invited to sit with the members of the Irish <ORGANIZATION>Parliament</ORGANIZATION> rather than in the gallery. He was the first American to be given this honor. While touring <LOCATION>Ireland</LOCATION>, he was moved by the level of poverty he saw. <LOCATION>Ireland</LOCATION>'s economy was affected by the same trade regulations and laws of <LOCATION>England</LOCATION> which governed <LOCATION>America</LOCATION>. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> feared that <LOCATION>America</LOCATION> could suffer the same effects should Britains colonial exploitation continue.

In 1773, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> published two of his most celebrated pro-American satirical essays: , and An Edict by the King of <LOCATION>Prussia</LOCATION>. He also published an Abridgment of the Book of Common Prayer, anonymously with <PERSON>Francis Dashwood</PERSON>. Among the unusual features of this work is a funeral service reduced to six minutes in length, "to preserve the health and lives of the living."

<PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> obtained private letters of <LOCATION>Massachusetts</LOCATION> governor <PERSON>Thomas Hutchinson</PERSON> and lieutenant governor <PERSON>Andrew Oliver</PERSON> which proved they were encouraging <LOCATION>London</LOCATION> to crack down on the rights of the Bostonians. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> sent them to <LOCATION>America</LOCATION> where they escalated the tensions. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> now appeared to the British as the fomenter of serious trouble. Hopes for a peaceful solution ended as he was systematically ridiculed and humiliated by the <ORGANIZATION>Privy Council</ORGANIZATION>. He left <LOCATION>London</LOCATION> in March 1775.



By the time <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> arrived in <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION> on May 5, the American Revolution had begun with fighting at <LOCATION>Lexington</LOCATION> and <LOCATION>Concord</LOCATION>. The <LOCATION>New England</LOCATION> militia had trapped the main British army in <LOCATION>Boston</LOCATION>. The <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION> Assembly unanimously chose <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> as their delegate to the <ORGANIZATION>Second Continental Congress</ORGANIZATION>. In June 1776, he was appointed a member of the <ORGANIZATION>Committee of Five</ORGANIZATION> that drafted the Declaration of Independence. Although he was temporarily disabled by gout and unable to attend most meetings of the Committee, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> made several small changes to the draft sent to him by <PERSON>Thomas Jefferson</PERSON>.

At the signing, he is quoted as having replied to a comment by <PERSON>Hancock</PERSON> that they must all hang together: "Yes, we must, indeed, all hang together, or most assuredly we shall all hang separately."

In December 1776, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> was dispatched to <LOCATION>France</LOCATION> as commissioner for the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION>. He lived in a home in the <LOCATION>Parisian</LOCATION> suburb of <LOCATION>Passy</LOCATION>, donated by <PERSON>Jacques-Donatien Le Ray de Chaumont</PERSON> who supported the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION>. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> remained in <LOCATION>France</LOCATION> until 1785, and was such a favorite of French society that it became fashionable for wealthy French families to decorate their parlors with a painting of him. He was highly flirtatious in the French manner (but did not have any actual affairs). He conducted the affairs of his country towards the French nation with great success, which included securing a critical military alliance in 1778 and negotiating the Treaty of <LOCATION>Paris</LOCATION> (1783). During his stay in <LOCATION>France</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Benjamin Franklin</PERSON> as a freemason was Grand Master of the <ORGANIZATION>Lodge Les Neuf Surs</ORGANIZATION> from 1779 until 1781. His number was 24 in the Lodge. He was also a Past Grand Master of <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION>. In 1784, when <PERSON>Franz Mesmer</PERSON> began to publicize his theory of "animal magnetism", which was considered offensive by many, <PERSON>Louis XVI</PERSON> appointed a commission to investigate it. These included the chemist <PERSON>Antoine Lavoisier</PERSON>, the physician <PERSON>Joseph-Ignace Guillotin</PERSON>, the astronomer <PERSON>Jean Sylvain Bailly</PERSON> and <PERSON>Benjamin Franklin</PERSON>.

When he finally returned home in 1785, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> occupied a position only second to that of <PERSON>George Washington</PERSON> as the champion of American independence. <PERSON>Le Ray</PERSON> honored him with a commissioned portrait painted by <PERSON>Joseph Duplessis</PERSON> that now hangs in the National Portrait Gallery of the <ORGANIZATION>Smithsonian Institution</ORGANIZATION> in <LOCATION>Washington</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>D.C</LOCATION>. After his return, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> became an abolitionist, freeing both of his slaves. He eventually became president of the <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION> Abolition Society.

In 1787, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> served as a delegate to the <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION> Convention. He held an honorific position and seldom engaged in debate. He is the only Founding Father who is a signatory of all four of the major documents of the founding of the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION>: the Declaration of Independence, the Treaty of <LOCATION>Paris</LOCATION>, the Treaty of Alliance with <LOCATION>France</LOCATION>, and the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> Constitution.

In 1787, a group of prominent ministers in <LOCATION>Lancaster</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION>, proposed the foundation of a new college to be named in <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>'s honor. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> donated 200 towards the development of <ORGANIZATION>Franklin College</ORGANIZATION>, which is now called <ORGANIZATION>Franklin & Marshall College</ORGANIZATION>.

Between 1771 and 1788, he finished his autobiography. While it was at first addressed to his son, it was later completed for the benefit of mankind at the request of a friend.

In his later years, as <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> was forced to deal with the issue of slavery, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> wrote several essays that attempted to convince his readers of the importance of the abolition of slavery and of the integration of Africans into American society. These writings included:

In 1790, Quakers from <LOCATION>New York</LOCATION> and <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION> presented their petition for abolition. Their argument against slavery was backed by the <ORGANIZATION>Pennsylvania Abolitionist Society</ORGANIZATION> and its president, <PERSON>Benjamin Franklin</PERSON>.

Special balloting conducted October 18, 1785 unanimously elected <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> the sixth President of the <ORGANIZATION>Supreme Executive Council of Pennsylvania</ORGANIZATION>, replacing <PERSON>John Dickinson</PERSON>. The office of President of <LOCATION>Pennsylvania</LOCATION> was analogous to the modern position of Governor. It is not clear why <PERSON>Dickinson</PERSON> needed to be replaced with less than two weeks remaining before the regular election. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> held that office for slightly over three years, longer than any other, and served the Constitutional limit of three full terms. Shortly after his initial election he was re-elected to a full term on October 29, 1785, and again in the fall of 1786 and on October 31, 1787. Officially, his term concluded on November 5, 1788, but there is some question regarding the de facto end of his term, suggesting that the aging Franklin may not have been actively involved in the day-to-day operation of the Council toward the end of his time in office.



Like the other advocates of republicanism, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> emphasized that the new republic could survive only if the people were virtuous in the sense of attention to civic duty and rejection of corruption. All his life he had been exploring the role of civic and personal virtue, as expressed in Poor <PERSON>Richard</PERSON>'s aphorisms.

<PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> had been baptized and educated in a Presbyterian Church based on the doctrines of <PERSON>John Calvin</PERSON>. <PERSON>Deborah Franklin</PERSON>, his wife, retained a life long association with <ORGANIZATION>Christ Church</ORGANIZATION>, <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION>. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> later in life rarely attended Sunday services but commented that "...Sunday being my studying day, I never was without some religious principles. I never doubted, for instance, the existence of the Deity; that He made the world, and governed it by His providence; that the most acceptable service of God was the doing good to man; that our souls are immortal; and that all crime will be punished, and virtue rewarded, either here or hereafter."

One of <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>'s endearing beliefs was in the respect and tolerance of all religious groups. Referring to his experience in <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION>, he wrote in his autobiography, "new Places of worship were continually wanted, and generally erected by voluntary Contribution, my Mite for such purpose, whatever might be the Sect, was never refused."

Although <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>'s parents had intended for him to have a career in the church, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> became disillusioned with organized religion after discovering Deism. "I soon became a thorough Deist." He went on to attack Christian principles of free will and morality in a 1725 pamphlet, A Dissertation on Liberty and Necessity, Pleasure and Pain. He consistently attacked religious dogma, arguing that morality was more dependent upon virtue and benevolent actions than on strict obedience to religious orthodoxy: "I think opinions should be judged by their influences and effects; and if a man holds none that tend to make him less virtuous or more vicious, it may be concluded that he holds none that are dangerous, which I hope is the case with me."

In 1790, just about a month before he died, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> wrote the following in a letter to <PERSON>Ezra Stiles</PERSON>, president of <ORGANIZATION>Yale</ORGANIZATION>, who had asked him his views on religion:



Like most Enlightenment intellectuals, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> separated virtue, morality, and faith from organized religion, although he felt that if religion in general grew weaker, morality, virtue, and society in general would also decline. Thus he wrote <PERSON>Thomas Paine</PERSON>, "If men are so wicked with religion, what would they be if without it." According to <PERSON>David Morgan</PERSON>, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> was a proponent of all religions. He prayed to "Powerful Goodness" and referred to God as the "INFINITE." <PERSON>John Adams</PERSON> noted that <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> was a mirror in which people saw their own religion: "The Catholics thought him almost a Catholic. <ORGANIZATION>The Church of England</ORGANIZATION> claimed him as one of them. The Presbyterians thought him half a Presbyterian, and the Friends believed him a wet Quaker." Whatever else <PERSON>Benjamin Franklin</PERSON> was, concludes <PERSON>Morgan</PERSON>, "he was a true champion of generic religion." <PERSON>Ben Franklin</PERSON> was noted to be "the spirit of the Enlightenment."

<PERSON>Walter Isaacson</PERSON> argues that <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> became uncomfortable with an unenhanced version of Deism and came up with his own conception of the Creator. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> outlined his concept of deity in 1728, in his Articles of Belief and Acts of Religion. From this, <PERSON>Isaacson</PERSON> compares <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>'s conception of deity to that of strict Deists and orthodox Christians. He concludes that unlike most pure Deists, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> believed that a faith in God should inform our daily actions, but that, like other Deists, his faith was devoid of sectarian dogma. <PERSON>Isaacson</PERSON> also discusses <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>'s conception that God had created beings who do interfere in wordly matters, a point that has led some commentators, most notably <PERSON>A. Owen Aldridge</PERSON>, to read <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> as embracing some sort of polytheism, with a bevy of lesser gods overseeing various realms and planets.

On July 4, 1776, <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> appointed a committee that included <PERSON>Benjamin Franklin</PERSON>, <PERSON>Thomas Jefferson</PERSON>, and <PERSON>John Adams</PERSON> to design the Great Seal of the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION>. Each member of the committee proposed a unique design: <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>'s proposal featured a design with the motto: "Rebellion to Tyrants is Obedience to God." This design was to portray a scene from the Book of Exodus, complete with <PERSON>Moses</PERSON>, the Israelites, the pillar of fire, and <PERSON>George III</PERSON> depicted as Pharaoh.

Franklin may have financially supported one particular Presbyterian group in <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION>. According to the epitaph <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> wrote for himself at the age of twenty, it is clear that he believed in a physical resurrection of the body some time after death. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>'s actual grave, however, as he specified in his final will, simply reads "<PERSON>Benjamin</PERSON> and <PERSON>Deborah Franklin</PERSON>."

<PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> sought to cultivate his character by a plan of thirteen virtues, which he developed at age 20 (in 1726) and continued to practice in some form for the rest of his life. His autobiography lists his thirteen virtues as:

<PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> didn't try to work on them all at once. Instead, he would work on one and only one each week "leaving all others to their ordinary chance". While <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> didn't live completely by his virtues and by his own admission, he fell short of them many times, he believed the attempt made him a better man contributing greatly to his success and happiness, which is why in his Autobiography, he devoted more pages to this plan than to any other single point; in his autobiography <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> wrote, "I hope, therefore, that some of my descendants may follow the example and reap the benefit."



<PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> died on April 17, 1790, at age 84. His funeral was attended by approximately 20,000 people. He was interred in Christ Church Burial Ground in <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION>. In 1728, aged 22, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> wrote what he hoped would be his own epitaph:

The Body of B. Franklin Printer; Like the Cover of an old Book, Its Contents torn out, And stript of its Lettering and Gilding, Lies here, Food for Worms. But the Work shall not be wholly lost: For it will, as he believ'd, appear once more, In a new & more perfect Edition, Corrected and Amended By the Author.

<PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>'s actual grave, however, as he specified in his final will, simply reads "<PERSON>Benjamin</PERSON> and <PERSON>Deborah Franklin</PERSON>."

In 1773, when <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>'s work had moved from printing to science and politics, he corresponded with a French scientist on the subject of preserving the dead for later revival by more advanced scientific methods, writing:

I should prefer to an ordinary death, being immersed with a few friends in a cask of <LOCATION>Madeira</LOCATION>, until that time, then to be recalled to life by the solar warmth of my dear country! But in all probability, we live in a century too little advanced, and too near the infancy of science, to see such an art brought in our time to its perfection. (Extended excerpt also online.)

His death is described in the book The Life of <PERSON>Benjamin Franklin</PERSON>, quoting from the account of Dr. <PERSON>John Jones</PERSON>:

...when the pain and difficulty of breathing entirely left him, and his family were flattering themselves with the hopes of his recovery, when an imposthume, which had formed itself in his lungs, suddenly burst, and discharged a quantity of matter, which he continued to throw up while he had power; but, as that failed, the organs of respiration became gradually oppressed; a calm, lethargic state succeeded; and on the 17th instant (April, 1790), about eleven o'clock at night, he quietly expired, closing a long and useful life of eighty-four years and three months.



<PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> bequeathed 1,000 (about $4,400 at the time) each to the cities of <LOCATION>Boston</LOCATION> and <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION>, in trust to gather interest for 200 years. The trust began in 1785 when a French mathematician named <PERSON>Charles-Joseph Mathon de la Cour</PERSON> wrote a parody of <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>'s "Poor <PERSON>Richard</PERSON>'s Almanack" called "Fortunate <PERSON>Richard</PERSON>." Mocking the unbearable spirit of American optimism represented by <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>, the Frenchman wrote that Fortunate <PERSON>Richard</PERSON> left a small sum of money in his will to be used only after it had collected interest for 500 years. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>, who was 79 years old at the time, wrote to the Frenchman, thanking him for a great idea and telling him that he had decided to leave a bequest of 1,000 pounds each to his native <LOCATION>Boston</LOCATION> and his adopted <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION>. As of 1990, more than $2,000,000 had accumulated in <LOCATION>Franklin</LOCATION>'s <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION> trust, which had loaned the money to local residents. From 1940 to 1990, the money was used mostly for mortgage loans. When the trust came due, <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION> decided to spend it on scholarships for local high school students. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>'s <LOCATION>Boston</LOCATION> trust fund accumulated almost $5,000,000 during that same time, and was used to establish a trade school that became the <ORGANIZATION>Franklin Institute of Boston</ORGANIZATION>.

A signer of both the Declaration of Independence and the Constitution, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> is considered one of the Founding Fathers of the <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> His pervasive influence in the early history of the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> has led to his being called "the only President of the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> who was never President of the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION>." <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>'s likeness is ubiquitous. Since 1928, it has adorned American $100 bills, which are sometimes referred to in slang as "Benjamins" or "Franklins." From 1948 to 1964, <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>'s portrait was on the half dollar. He has appeared on a $50 bill and on several varieties of the $100 bill from 1914 and 1918. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> appears on the $1,000 Series EE Savings bond. The city of <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION> contains around 5,000 likenesses of <PERSON>Benjamin Franklin</PERSON>, about half of which are located on the <ORGANIZATION>University of Pennsylvania</ORGANIZATION> campus. <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION>'s <PERSON>Benjamin Franklin Parkway</PERSON> (a major thoroughfare) and <PERSON>Benjamin Franklin Bridge</PERSON> (the first major bridge to connect <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION> with <LOCATION>New Jersey</LOCATION>) are named in his honor.



In 1976, as part of a bicentennial celebration, <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> dedicated a 20-foot (6 m) marble statue in <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION>'s <ORGANIZATION>Franklin Institute</ORGANIZATION> as the Benjamin Franklin National Memorial. Many of <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>'s personal possessions are also on display at the <ORGANIZATION>Institute</ORGANIZATION>, one of the few national memorials located on private property.

In <LOCATION>London</LOCATION>, his house at 36 <LOCATION>Craven Street</LOCATION> was first marked with a blue plaque and has since been opened to the public as the <PERSON>Benjamin Franklin House</PERSON>. In 1998, workmen restoring the building dug up the remains of six children and four adults hidden below the home. The Times reported on February 11, 1998:

Initial estimates are that the bones are about 200 years old and were buried at the time <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> was living in the house, which was his home from 1757 to 1762 and from 1764 to 1775. Most of the bones show signs of having been dissected, sawn or cut. One skull has been drilled with several holes. <PERSON>Paul Knapman</PERSON>, the <PERSON>Westminster Coroner</PERSON>, said yesterday: "I cannot totally discount the possibility of a crime. There is still a possibility that I may have to hold an inquest."

The Friends of <ORGANIZATION>Benjamin Franklin House</ORGANIZATION> (the organization responsible for the restoration) note that the bones were likely placed there by <PERSON>William Hewson</PERSON>, who lived in the house for two years and who had built a small anatomy school at the back of the house. They note that while <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> likely knew what <PERSON>Hewson</PERSON> was doing, he probably did not participate in any dissections because he was much more of a physicist than a medical man.

"The Princess and the Patriot: <PERSON>Ekaterina Dashkova</PERSON>, <PERSON>Benjamin Franklin</PERSON> and the Age of Enlightenment" exhibition opened in <LOCATION>Philadelphia</LOCATION> in February 2006 and ran through December 2006. <PERSON>Benjamin Franklin</PERSON> and <PERSON>Dashkova</PERSON> met only once, in <LOCATION>Paris</LOCATION> in 1781. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> was 75 and <PERSON>Dashkova</PERSON> was 37. <PERSON>Franklin</PERSON> invited <PERSON>Dashkova</PERSON> to become the first woman to join the <ORGANIZATION>American Philosophical Society</ORGANIZATION> and the only woman to be so honored for another 80 years. Later, <PERSON>Dashkova</PERSON> reciprocated by making him the first American member of the Russian Academy of Sciences.





<PERSON>Franklin</PERSON>, in his "Poor Richard" persona, helped create popular culture in <LOCATION>America</LOCATION>. In turn he has been included in many different popular culture media, of which this list is a small, recent sample.

For Young Readers











<TITLE><PERSON>Andrew Johnson</PERSON></TITLE>

<PERSON>Andrew Johnson</PERSON> (December 29, 1808 July 31, 1875) was the seventeenth President of the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> (1865-69), succeeding to the Presidency upon the assassination of <PERSON>Abraham Lincoln</PERSON>. He was one of only two <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> Presidents to be impeached.

At the time of the secession of the Southern states, <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> was a <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> Senator from Greeneville in eastern <LOCATION>Tennessee</LOCATION>. As a Unionist, he was the only southern Senator not to quit his post upon secession. He became the most prominent War Democrat from the <LOCATION>South</LOCATION> and supported the military policies of <LOCATION>US</LOCATION> President <PERSON>Abraham Lincoln</PERSON> during the American Civil War of 18611865. In 1862 <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> appointed <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> military governor of occupied <LOCATION>Tennessee</LOCATION>, where he proved to be energetic and effective in fighting the rebellion and beginning transition to Reconstruction.

<PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> was nominated for the Vice President slot in 1864 on the <ORGANIZATION>National Union Party</ORGANIZATION> ticket. He and <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> were elected in November 1864. <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> succeeded to the Presidency upon <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s assassination on April 15, 1865.

As president he took charge of Presidential Reconstruction the first phase of Reconstruction which lasted until the Radical Republicans gained control of <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> in the 1866 elections. His conciliatory policies towards the <LOCATION>South</LOCATION>, his hurry to reincorporate the former Confederates back into the union, and his vetoes of civil rights bills embroiled him in a bitter dispute with some Republicans. The Radicals in the <ORGANIZATION>House of Representatives</ORGANIZATION> impeached him in 1868 while charging him with violating the Tenure of Office Act, a law enacted by <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> in March 1867 over <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON>'s veto, but he was acquitted by a single vote in the <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION>.

<PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> was born on December 29, 1808, in <LOCATION>Raleigh</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>North Carolina</LOCATION>, to <PERSON>Jacob Johnson</PERSON> and <PERSON>Mary McDonough</PERSON>. <PERSON>Andrew Johnson</PERSON>'s father, <PERSON>Jacob</PERSON>, died when <PERSON>Andrew</PERSON> was around three years old, leaving his family in poverty. <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON>'s mother then took in work spinning and weaving to support her family and later remarried. She bound <PERSON>Andrew</PERSON> as an apprentice tailor when he was 14 or 10. In the 1820s, he worked as a tailor in <LOCATION>Laurens</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>South Carolina</LOCATION>. <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> never attended any type of school and taught himself how to read and spell.

At age 16 or 17 <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> broke his apprenticeship and ran away with his brother to <LOCATION>Greeneville</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Tennessee</LOCATION>, where he found work as a tailor. At the age of 18, <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> married <PERSON>Eliza McCardle</PERSON> in 1827. Between 1828 and 1852, the couple had five children: <PERSON>Martha</PERSON> (1828), <PERSON>Charles</PERSON> (1830), <PERSON>Mary</PERSON> (1832), <PERSON>Robert</PERSON> (1834), and <PERSON>Andrew Jr.</PERSON> (1852). <PERSON>Eliza</PERSON> taught <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> arithmetic and also tutored him to improve his literacy and writing skills.

<PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> participated in debates at the local academy at <LOCATION>Greeneville</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Tennessee</LOCATION> and later organized a workingman's party that elected him as alderman in 1829. He served in this position until he was elected mayor in 1833. In 1835 he was elected to the <ORGANIZATION>Tennessee House of Representatives</ORGANIZATION> where, after serving a single term, he was defeated for re-election.

<PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> was attracted to <PERSON>Andrew Jackson</PERSON>'s states' rights <ORGANIZATION>Democratic Party</ORGANIZATION>. He became a spokesman for the more numerous yeomen farmers and mountaineers against the wealthier, but fewer, planter elite families that had held political control both in the state and nationally. In 1839 <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> was elected to the <LOCATION>Tennessee</LOCATION> <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION>, where he served two consecutive two-year terms. In 1843 he became the first Democrat to win election as the <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> Representative from <LOCATION>Tennessee</LOCATION>'s 1st congressional district. Among his activities for the common man's interests as a member of the <ORGANIZATION>House of Representatives</ORGANIZATION> and the <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION>, <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> advocated 'a free farm for the poor' bill where farms would be given to landless farmers. <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> was a <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> Representative for five terms until 1853, when he was elected governor of <LOCATION>Tennessee</LOCATION>.

<PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> was elected governor of <LOCATION>Tennessee</LOCATION>, serving from 1853 to 1857. He was then elected as a Democrat to the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION>, serving from October 8, 1857 to March 4, 1862. He was chairman of the <ORGANIZATION>Committee</ORGANIZATION> to Audit and Control the Contingent Expense (Thirty-sixth <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION>). Before <LOCATION>Tennessee</LOCATION> voted on secession, <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> who lived in Unionist east <LOCATION>Tennessee</LOCATION> toured the state speaking in opposition to the act, which he said was unconstitutional. <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> was an aggressive stump speaker and often responded to hecklers, even those in the <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION>. At the time of secession of the Confederacy, <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> was the only Senator from the seceded states to continue participation in <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION>. His explanation for this decision was "Damn the negroes, I am fighting those traitorous aristocrats, their masters."

<PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> appointed <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> military governor of occupied <LOCATION>Tennessee</LOCATION> in March 1862 with the rank of brigadier general. During his three years in this office, he "moved resolutely to eradicate all pro-Confederate influences in the state." This "unwavering commitment to the Union" was a significant factor in his choice as vice-president by <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>. <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> vigorously suppressed the Confederates and later spoke out for black suffrage, arguing, "The better class of them will go to work and sustain themselves, and that class ought to be allowed to vote, on the ground that a loyal negro is more worthy than a disloyal white man." According to tradition and local lore, on August 8, 1863, <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> freed his personal slaves.

As a leading War Democrat and pro-Union southerner, <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> was an ideal candidate for the Republicans in 1864 as they enlarged their base to include War Democrats. They changed the party name to the <ORGANIZATION>National Union Party</ORGANIZATION> to reflect this expansion. During the election <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> replaced <PERSON>Hannibal Hamlin</PERSON> as <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s running mate. He was elected Vice President of the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> and was inaugurated March 4, 1865. At the ceremony, <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON>, who had been drinking to offset the pain of typhoid fever (as he explained later), gave a rambling speech and appeared intoxicated to many. In early 1865, <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> talked harshly of hanging traitors like <PERSON>Jefferson Davis</PERSON>, which endeared him to the Radicals.

On April 14, 1865, <PERSON>Abraham Lincoln</PERSON> was shot and mortally wounded by <PERSON>John Wilkes Booth</PERSON>, a Confederate sympathizer, while the President was attending a play at <ORGANIZATION>Ford</ORGANIZATION>'s Theater. <PERSON>Booth</PERSON>'s plan was to destroy the administration by ordering conspirators to assassinate <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> and Secretary of State <PERSON>William H. Seward</PERSON> that same night. <PERSON>Seward</PERSON> narrowly survived his wounds, while <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> escaped attack as his would-be assassin, <PERSON>George Atzerodt</PERSON>, failed to go through with the plan.

The morning after <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s assassination, <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> was sworn in as President of the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> on April 15, 1865 by <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON>'s newly appointed Chief Justice <PERSON>Salmon P. Chase</PERSON>. <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> was the first Vice President to succeed to the <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> Presidency upon the assassination of a President and the third vice president to become a president.

<PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> had an ambiguous party status. He attempted to build a party of loyalists under the National Union label, but he did not identify with the two main parties while President--though he did try for the Democratic nomination in 1868. Asked in 1868 why he did not become a Democrat, he said "It is true I am asked why don't I join the <ORGANIZATION>Democratic Party</ORGANIZATION>. Why don't they join me...if I have administered the office of president so well?"

Northern anger over the assassination of <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> and the immense human cost of the war led to demands for harsh policies. Vice President <PERSON>Andrew Johnson</PERSON> had taken a hard line and spoke of hanging rebel Confederates. In late April, 1865 he was noted telling an <LOCATION>Indiana</LOCATION> delegation that, "Treason must be made odious... traitors must be punished and impoverished ... their social power must be destroyed." However, when he succeeded <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> as President, <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> took a much softer line noting, "I say, as to the leaders, punishment. I also say leniency, reconciliation and amnesty to the thousands whom they have misled and deceived." and ended up pardoning many Confederate leaders and ex-Confederates to maintain their control of Southern state governments, Southern lands, and black people.

His class-based resentment of the rich appeared in a May 1865 statement to <PERSON>W.H. Holden</PERSON>, the man he appointed governor of <LOCATION>North Carolina</LOCATION>: "I intend to confiscate the lands of these rich men whom I have excluded from pardon by my proclamation, and divide the proceeds thereof among the families of the wool hat boys, the Confederate soldiers, whom these men forced into battle to protect their property in slaves." In practice, <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> was not at all harsh toward the Confederate leaders. He allowed the Southern states to hold elections in 1865, resulting in prominent ex-Confederates being elected to the <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION>; however, <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> did not seat them. <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> and <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> argued in an increasingly public way about Reconstruction and the manner in which the Southern secessionist states would be readmitted to the Union. <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> favored a very quick restoration, similar to the plan of leniency that <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> advocated before his death.

Johnson-appointed governments all passed Black Codes that gave the Freedmen second class status. In response to the Black Codes and worrisome signs of Southern recalcitrance, the Radical Republicans blocked the re-admission of the ex-rebellious states to the <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> in fall 1865. <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> also renewed the Freedman's <ORGANIZATION>Bureau</ORGANIZATION>, but <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> vetoed it. Senator <PERSON>Lyman Trumbull</PERSON> of <LOCATION>Illinois</LOCATION>, leader of the moderate Republicans, took affront at the Black Codes. <PERSON>Trumbull</PERSON> proposed the first Civil Rights bill.

Although strongly urged by moderates in <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> to sign the Civil Rights bill, <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> broke decisively with them by vetoing it on March 27. His veto message objected to the measure because it conferred citizenship on the Freedmen at a time when eleven out of thirty-six States were unrepresented and attempted to fix by Federal law "a perfect equality of the white and black races in every State of the Union." <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> said it was an invasion by Federal authority of the rights of the <LOCATION>States</LOCATION>; it had no warrant in the Constitution and was contrary to all precedents. It was a "stride toward centralization and the concentration of all legislative power in the national government." <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON>, in a letter to Governor <PERSON>Thomas C. Fletcher</PERSON> of <LOCATION>Missouri</LOCATION>, wrote, "This is a country for white men, and by God, as long as I am President, it shall be a government for white men."

The <ORGANIZATION>Democratic Party</ORGANIZATION>, proclaiming itself the party of white men, north and <LOCATION>South</LOCATION>, aligned with <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON>. However the Republicans in <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> overrode his veto (the <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION> by the vote of 33:15, the <ORGANIZATION>House</ORGANIZATION> by 182:41) and the Civil Rights bill became law.

The last moderate proposal was the Fourteenth Amendment, also authored by moderate <PERSON>Trumbull</PERSON>. It was designed to put the key provisions of the Civil Rights Act into the Constitution, but it went much further. It extended citizenship to everyone born in the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> (except Indians on reservations), penalized states that did not give the vote to Freedmen, and most importantly, created new Federal civil rights that could be protected by Federal courts. It guaranteed the Federal war debt (and promised the Confederate debt would never be paid). <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> used his influence to block the amendment in the states, as three-fourths of the states were required for ratification. (The Amendment was later ratified.)

The moderate effort to compromise with <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> had failed and an all-out political war broke out between the Republicans (both Radical and moderate) on one side, and on the other <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> and his allies in the Democratic party in the North, and the conservative groupings in the <LOCATION>South</LOCATION>. The decisive battle was the election of 1866. <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> campaigned vigorously but was widely ridiculed. The Republicans won by a landslide (the Southern states were not allowed to vote), and took full control of Reconstruction. <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> was almost powerless.

Historian <PERSON>James Ford Rhodes</PERSON> has explained <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON>'s inability to engage in serious negotiations:As Senator <PERSON>Charles Sumner</PERSON> shrewdly said, "the President himself is his own worst counselor, as he is his own worst defender." <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> acted in accordance with his nature. He had intellectual force but it worked in a groove. Obstinate rather than firm it undoubtedly seemed to him that following counsel and making concessions were a display of weakness. At all events from his December message to the veto of the Civil Rights Bill he yielded not a jot to <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION>. The moderate senators and representatives (who constituted a majority of the Union party) asked him for only a slight compromise; their action was really an entreaty that he would unite with them to preserve <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> and the country from the policy of the radicals.The two projects which <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> had most at heart were the speedy admission of the Southern senators and representatives to <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> and the relegation of the question of 'negro suffrage' to the States themselves. Himself shrinking from the imposition on these communities of the franchise for the colored people, his unyielding position in regard to matters involving no vital principle did much to bring it about. His quarrel with <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> prevented the readmission into the Union on generous terms of the members of the late Confederacy; and for the quarrel and its unhappy results <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON>'s lack of imagination and his inordinate sensitiveness to political gadflies were largely responsible: it was not a contest in which fundamentals were involved.He sacrificed two important objects to petty considerations. His pride of opinion, his desire to beat, blinded him to the real welfare of the <LOCATION>South</LOCATION> and of the whole country.

There were two attempts to remove President <PERSON>Andrew Johnson</PERSON> from office. The first occurred in the fall of 1867. On November 21, 1867, the <ORGANIZATION>House Judiciary committee</ORGANIZATION> produced a bill of impeachment that consisted of a vast collection of complaints against him. After a furious debate, a formal vote was held in the <ORGANIZATION>House of Representatives</ORGANIZATION> on December 5, 1867, which failed 108-57.

<PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> notified <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> that he had removed <PERSON>Edwin Stanton</PERSON> as Secretary of War and was replacing him in the interim with Adjutant-General <PERSON>Lorenzo Thomas</PERSON>. <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> had wanted to replace <PERSON>Stanton</PERSON> with former General <PERSON>Ulysses S. Grant</PERSON>, who refused to accept the position. This violated the Tenure of Office Act, a law enacted by <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> in March 1867 over <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON>'s veto, specifically designed to protect <PERSON>Stanton</PERSON>. <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> had vetoed the act, claiming it was unconstitutional. The act said, "...every person holding any civil office, to which he has been appointed by and with the advice and consent of the <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION> ... shall be entitled to hold such office until a successor shall have been in like manner appointed and duly qualified," thus removing the President's previous unlimited power to remove any of his Cabinet members at will. Years later in the case <PERSON>Myers</PERSON> v. <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> in 1926, the <ORGANIZATION>Supreme Court</ORGANIZATION> ruled that such laws were indeed unconstitutional.

The <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION> and <ORGANIZATION>House</ORGANIZATION> entered into debate. <PERSON>Thomas</PERSON> attempted to move into the war office, for which <PERSON>Stanton</PERSON> had <PERSON>Thomas</PERSON> arrested. Three days after <PERSON>Stanton</PERSON>'s removal, the <ORGANIZATION>House</ORGANIZATION> impeached <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> for intentionally violating the Tenure of Office Act.

On March 5, 1868, a court of impeachment was constituted in the <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION> to hear charges against the President. <PERSON>William M. Evarts</PERSON> served as his counsel. Eleven articles were set out in the resolution, and the trial before the <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION> lasted almost three months. <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON>'s defense was based on a clause in the Tenure of Office Act stating that the then-current secretaries would hold their posts throughout the term of the President who appointed them. Since <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> had appointed <PERSON>Stanton</PERSON>, it was claimed, the applicability of the act had already run its course.

There were three votes in the <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION>: one on May 16 for the 11th article of impeachment, which included many of the charges contained in the other articles, and two on May 26 for the second and third articles, after which the trial adjourned. On all three occasions, thirty-five Senators voted "guilty" and nineteen "not guilty." As the Constitution requires a two-thirds majority for conviction in impeachment trials, <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> was acquitted; the 35-19 vote was one less then the majority required. A single changed vote for guilty would have convicted and removed <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> from office. Seven Republican senators were disturbed by how the proceedings had been manipulated in order to give a one-sided presentation of the evidence. Senators <PERSON>William Pitt Fessenden</PERSON>, <PERSON>Joseph S. Fowler</PERSON>, <PERSON>James W. Grimes</PERSON>, <PERSON>John B. Henderson</PERSON>, <PERSON>Lyman Trumbull</PERSON>, <PERSON>Peter G. Van Winkle</PERSON>, and <PERSON>Edmund G. Ross</PERSON> of <LOCATION>Kansas</LOCATION>, who provided the decisive vote, defied their party and public opinion and voted against conviction.

Before 1960 most historians held the impeachment of <PERSON>Andrew Johnson</PERSON> as a violation of American values regarding division of powers and fair play. Had <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> been successfully removed from office, he would have been replaced with Radical Republican <PERSON>Benjamin Wade</PERSON>, making the presidency and <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> somewhat uniform in ideology, although in many ways <PERSON>Wade</PERSON> was more "radical" than the Republicans in <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION>. This would have established a precedent that a President could be removed not for "high crimes and misdemeanors," but for purely political differences.

One of <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON>'s last significant acts was granting unconditional amnesty to all Confederates on Christmas Day, December 25, 1868. This was after the election of <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> <PERSON>Grant</PERSON> to succeed him, but before <PERSON>Grant</PERSON> took office in March 1869. Earlier amnesties requiring signed oaths and excluding certain classes of people were issued both by <PERSON>Lincoln</PERSON> and by <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON>.



<PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> forced the French out of <LOCATION>Mexico</LOCATION> by sending a combat army to the border and issuing an ultimatum. The French withdrew in 1867, and the government they supported quickly collapsed. Secretary of State <PERSON>Seward</PERSON> negotiated the purchase of <LOCATION>Alaska</LOCATION> from <LOCATION>Russia</LOCATION> on April 9, 1867 for $7.2 million. Critics sneered at "Seward's Folly" and "Seward's Icebox" and "Icebergia." <PERSON>Seward</PERSON> also negotiated to purchase the Danish West Indies, but the <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION> refused to approve the purchase in 1867 (it eventually happened in 1917). The <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION> likewise rejected <PERSON>Seward</PERSON>'s arrangement with the <LOCATION>United Kingdom</LOCATION> to arbitrate the ''<LOCATION>Alabama</LOCATION>'' Claims.

The <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> experienced tense relations with the <LOCATION>United Kingdom</LOCATION> and its colonial government in <LOCATION>Canada</LOCATION> in the aftermath of the war. Lingering resentment over the perception of British sympathy towards the Confederacy resulted in <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> initially turning a blind eye towards a series of armed incursions by Irish-American civil war veterans into British territory in <LOCATION>Canada</LOCATION>, named the Fenian Raids. Eventually, <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> ordered the Fenians disarmed and barred from crossing the border, but his hesitant reaction to the crisis helped motivate the movement toward Canadian Confederation.

<PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> was an unsuccessful candidate for election to the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION> from <LOCATION>Tennessee</LOCATION> in 1868 and to the House of Representatives in 1872. However, in 1874 the <LOCATION>Tennessee</LOCATION> legislature did elect him to the <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION>. <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> served from March 4, 1875, until his death from a stroke near <LOCATION>Elizabethton</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Tennessee</LOCATION>, on July 31 that same year. In his first speech since returning to the <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION>, which was also his last, <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> spoke about political turmoil in <LOCATION>Louisiana</LOCATION>. His passion aroused a standing ovation from many of his fellow senators who had once voted to remove him from the presidency. He is the only former President to serve in the <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION>.

Interment was in the <PERSON>Andrew Johnson</PERSON> National Cemetery, <LOCATION>Greeneville</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Tennessee</LOCATION>, where he was buried with a copy of the Constitution. <PERSON>Andrew Johnson</PERSON> National Cemetery is now part of the <PERSON>Andrew Johnson</PERSON> National Historic Site.

The <ORGANIZATION>Dunning School</ORGANIZATION> of the early 20th century saw <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> as a heroic bulwark against the corruption of the Radical Republicans who tried to remove the entire leadership class of the white South. In their view, <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> seemed to be the legitimate heir of the sainted <PERSON>Abraham Lincoln</PERSON>.

By the 1930s a series of favorable biographies enhanced his prestige. <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON>'s <ORGANIZATION>Republican</ORGANIZATION> critics of the 1860s appeared as disreputable to liberal historians as did the <ORGANIZATION>Republican</ORGANIZATION> critics of <PERSON>Franklin D. Roosevelt</PERSON>. Furthermore, a Beardian School (named after <PERSON>Charles Beard</PERSON> and typified by <PERSON>Howard K. Beale</PERSON>) argued that the <ORGANIZATION>Republican Party</ORGANIZATION> in the 1860s was a tool of corrupt business interests, and that <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> stood for the people. Historian <PERSON>Eric Foner</PERSON> says that by 1948, historians regarded Reconstruction, "as a time of corruption and misgovernment caused by granting black men the right to vote." They rated <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> "near great." but have later changed their minds, rating <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> "a flat failure".

The Civil Rights movement of the 1960s brought a new perspective to the practice of history as well as to civil legislation. Historians noted African American efforts to establish public education and welfare institutions, gave muted praise for Republican efforts to extend suffrage and provide other social institutions, and excoriated <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> for siding with the opposition to extending basic rights to former slaves.

<PERSON>Johnson</PERSON>'s purchase of <LOCATION>Alaska</LOCATION> from the Russian Empire in 1867 is believed to be a most important foreign policy action, with the purchase proving itself vital to national security during the Cold War (mid-1940s until the early 1990s). The idea and implementation is credited to <PERSON>Seward</PERSON> as Secretary of <ORGANIZATION>State</ORGANIZATION>, but <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON> approved the plan. Gold was not discovered in <LOCATION>Alaska</LOCATION> until 1880, thirteen years after the purchase and five years after <PERSON>Johnson</PERSON>'s death, and oil was not discovered until 1968.













<TITLE><PERSON>Bill Clinton</PERSON></TITLE>

<PERSON>William Jefferson</PERSON> "Bill" <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> (born <PERSON>William Jefferson Blythe III</PERSON>, August 19, 1946) served as the forty-second President of the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> from 1993 to 2001. He was the fifteenth Democrat elected to that office. He was the third-youngest president, older only than <PERSON>Theodore Roosevelt</PERSON> and <PERSON>John F. Kennedy</PERSON>. He became president at the end of the Cold War, and as he was born in the period after World War II, is known as the first Baby Boomer president. His wife is the <LOCATION>New York</LOCATION> Senator, <PERSON>Hillary Rodham Clinton</PERSON>.

<PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> was described as a New Democrat and was largely known for the Third Way philosophy of governance that came to epitomize his two terms as president. His policies, on issues such as the North American Free Trade Agreement and welfare reform, have been described as "centrist." <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> presided over the longest period of peace-time economic expansion in American history, which included a balanced budget and a reported federal surplus. Based on <ORGANIZATION>Congressional</ORGANIZATION> accounting rules, at the end of his presidency <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> reported a surplus of $559 billion. On the heels of a failed attempt at health care reform with a <ORGANIZATION>Democratic Congress</ORGANIZATION>, Republicans won control of the <ORGANIZATION>House of Representatives</ORGANIZATION> for the first time in forty years. Two years later, he was re-elected and became the first member of the <ORGANIZATION>Democratic Party</ORGANIZATION> since <PERSON>Franklin D. Roosevelt</PERSON> to win a second term as President. Later he was impeached for obstruction of justice, but subsequently was acquitted by the <ORGANIZATION>U.S. Senate</ORGANIZATION>.

<PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> left office with an approval rating at 65%, the highest end of office rating of any President since World War II. Since then, <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> has been involved in public speaking and humanitarian work. To promote and address international causes, such as treatment and prevention of HIV/AIDS and global warming, he created the <PERSON>William J. Clinton</PERSON> Foundation.

In 2004, he released his autobiography, My Life, and more recently has been involved in his wife <PERSON>Hillary</PERSON>'s 2008 presidential campaign and in that of <PERSON>Barack Obama</PERSON>.





<PERSON>Bill Clinton</PERSON> was born "<PERSON>William Jefferson Blythe III</PERSON>" in Hope, <LOCATION>Arkansas</LOCATION>. In 1950, <PERSON>Bill</PERSON>'s mother returned from nursing school and shortly thereafter married <PERSON>Roger Clinton</PERSON>, who together with his brother owned an automobile dealership in <LOCATION>Hot Springs</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Arkansas</LOCATION>. Kennedy</PERSON>, as a Boys Nation senator. The other was listening to <PERSON>Martin Luther King</PERSON>'s 1963 I Have a Dream speech (he memorized Dr. <PERSON>King</PERSON>'s words).

<PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> made economic growth, job creation and educational improvement high priorities. <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON>'s Governorship answered conservative criticism, but personal and business transactions made by the <PERSON>Clintons</PERSON> during this period became the basis of the Whitewater investigation, which dogged his later presidential administration. <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> won the 1992 presidential election (43.0% of the vote) against <ORGANIZATION>Republican</ORGANIZATION> incumbent <PERSON>George H. W. Bush</PERSON> (37.4% of the vote) and billionaire populist <PERSON>H. Finally, conservatives were previously united by anti-communism, but with the end of the Cold War, the party lacked a uniting issue. When <PERSON>Pat Buchanan</PERSON> and <PERSON>Pat Robertson</PERSON> addressed Christian themesat the Republican National Convention, with <PERSON>Bush</PERSON> criticizing Democrats for omitting God from their platform, many moderates were alienated. <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> then pointed to his moderate, "New Democrat" record as governor of <LOCATION>Arkansas</LOCATION>, though some on the more liberal side of the party remained suspicious. His election ended twelve years of <ORGANIZATION>Republican</ORGANIZATION> rule of the <ORGANIZATION>White House</ORGANIZATION>, and twenty of the previous twenty four years. However, during the campaign questions of conflict of interest regarding state business and the politically powerful Rose Law Firm, at which <PERSON>Hillary Rodham Clinton</PERSON> was a partner, arose. <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> maintained questions were moot because all transactions with the state were deducted prior to determining <PERSON>Hillary</PERSON>'s firm pay. <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> was inaugurated as the 42nd President of the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> on January 20, 1993. In his inaugural address he declared:



Shortly after taking office, <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> signed the Family and Medical Leave Act of 1993, which required large employers to allow employees to take unpaid leave for pregnancy or a serious medical condition. While this action was popular, <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON>'s attempt to fulfill another campaign promise of allowing openly homosexual men and women to serve in the armed forces garnered criticism from the left (for being too tentative in promoting gay rights) and from the right (who opposed any effort to allow gays to serve). After much debate, <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> implemented the "Don't Ask, Don't Tell" policy, stating as long as homosexuals keep their sexuality secret, they may serve in the military. Some gay rights advocates criticized <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> for not going far enough and accused him of making his campaign promise to get votes and contributions. These advocates feel <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> should have integrated the military by executive order, noting President <PERSON>Harry Truman</PERSON> used executive order to racially desegregate the armed forces. <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON>'s defenders argue an executive order might have prompted the <ORGANIZATION>Democratic Senate</ORGANIZATION> to write the exclusion of gays into law, potentially making it harder to integrate the military in the future. Later in his presidency, in 1999, <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> said he did not think any serious person could say the way the policy was being implemented was not "out of whack."

The <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> administration launched the first official <ORGANIZATION>White House</ORGANIZATION> website on October 21, 1994. It was followed by three more versions, resulting in the final edition launched in 2000. The White House website was part of a wider movement of the <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> administration toward web-based communication. According to <PERSON>Robert Longley</PERSON>, "<PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> and <PERSON>Gore</PERSON> were responsible for pressing almost all federal agencies, the <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> court system and the <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> military onto the Internet, thus opening up <LOCATION>America</LOCATION>'s government to more of <LOCATION>America</LOCATION>'s citizens than ever before. On July 17, 1996, President <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> issued Executive Order 13011 Federal Information Technology, ordering the heads of all federal agencies to fully utilize information technology to make the information of the agency easily accessible to the public."



Also in 1993, <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> controversially supported ratification of the North American Free Trade Agreement by the <ORGANIZATION>U.S. Senate</ORGANIZATION>. <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON>, along with most of his <ORGANIZATION>Democratic Leadership Committee</ORGANIZATION> allies, strongly supported free trade measures; there remained, however, strong intra-party disagreement. The bill passed the house with 234 votes against 200 opposed (132 Republicans and 102 Democrats voting in favor, 156 Democrats, 43 Republicans, and 1 independent against). The treaty was then ratified by the <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION> and signed into law by the President on January 1, 1994.

<PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> signed the <PERSON>Brady Bill</PERSON>, which imposed a five-day waiting period on handgun purchases. He also expanded the Earned Income Tax Credit, a subsidy for low income workers.

One of the most prominent items on <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON>'s legislative agenda was the result of a taskforce headed by <PERSON>Hillary Clinton</PERSON>, which was a health care reform plan aimed at achieving universal coverage via a national healthcare plan. Though initially well-received in political circles, it was ultimately doomed by well-organized opposition from conservatives, the <ORGANIZATION>American Medical Association</ORGANIZATION>, and the health insurance industry. Despite his party holding a majority in <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION>, the effort to create a national healthcare system ultimately died under heavy public pressure. Two months later, after two years of <ORGANIZATION>Democratic Party</ORGANIZATION> control, the Democrats lost control of <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> in the mid-term elections in 1994, for the first time in forty years.

<PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> signed the Omnibus Budget Reconciliation Act of 1993 in August 1993, which passed <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> without a Republican vote. It cut taxes for fifteen million low-income families, made tax cuts available to 90% of small businesses, and raised taxes on the wealthiest 1.2% of taxpayers. Additionally, through the implementation of spending restraints, it mandated the budget be balanced over a number of years.



When several longtime employees of the <ORGANIZATION>White House Travel Office</ORGANIZATION> were fired, the White House travel office controversy began on May 19, 1993. A whistleblower's letter, written during the previous administration, triggered an <ORGANIZATION>FBI</ORGANIZATION> investigation, which revealed evidence of financial malfeasance. Independent Counsel <PERSON>Kenneth Starr</PERSON> investigated the firings and found no evidence of wrongdoing on the <ORGANIZATION>Clintons</ORGANIZATION>' part.

The White House FBI files controversy of June 1996 arose around improper access to <ORGANIZATION>FBI</ORGANIZATION> security-clearance documents. <PERSON>Craig Livingstone</PERSON>, head of the <ORGANIZATION>White House Office of Personnel Security</ORGANIZATION>, improperly requested, and received from the <ORGANIZATION>FBI</ORGANIZATION>, background report files without asking permission of the subject individuals; many of these were employees of former <ORGANIZATION>Republican</ORGANIZATION> administrations. In March 2000, Independent Counsel <PERSON>Robert Ray</PERSON> determined that there was no credible evidence of any criminal activity. <PERSON>Ray</PERSON>'s report further stated "there was no substantial and credible evidence that any senior White House official was involved" in seeking the files.



The application of the federal death penalty was expanded to include crimes not resulting in death, such as running a large-scale drug enterprise, by <PERSON>Clintons</PERSON> 1994 Omnibus Crime Bill. During <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON>'s re-election campaign he said, "My 1994 crime bill expanded the death penalty for drug kingpins, murderers of federal law enforcement officers, and nearly 60 additional categories of violent felons."

While campaigning for <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> President, then-Governor <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> returned to <LOCATION>Arkansas</LOCATION> to see that <PERSON>Ricky Ray Rector</PERSON> would be executed. After killing a police officer and a civilian, <PERSON>Rector</PERSON> shot himself in the head, leading to what his lawyers said was a state where he could still talk but didn't understand the concept of death. According to <LOCATION>Arkansas</LOCATION> state and Federal law, a seriously mentally impaired inmate cannot be executed. The courts disagreed with the claim of grave mental impairment and allowed the execution. <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON>'s return to <LOCATION>Arkansas</LOCATION> for the execution was framed in a <ORGANIZATION>New York Times</ORGANIZATION> article as a possible political move to counter "soft on crime" accusations.

According to some sources <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> was a death penalty opponent in his early years who switched positions. During <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON>'s term, <LOCATION>Arkansas</LOCATION> performed its first executions since 1964 (the death penalty was re-enacted on March 23, 1973). As Governor, he oversaw four executions: one by electric chair and three by lethal injection. However, <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> was the first President to pardon a death row inmate since the federal death penalty was reintroduced in 1988. Federal executions were resumed under his successor <PERSON>George W. Bush</PERSON>.



In the 1996 presidential election, <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> was re-elected, receiving 49.2% of the popular vote over Republican <PERSON>Bob Dole</PERSON> (40.7% of the popular vote) and Reform candidate <PERSON>Ross Perot</PERSON> (8.4% of the popular vote), becoming the first Democrat to win presidential reelection since <PERSON>Franklin Roosevelt</PERSON>. The Republicans lost a few seats in the <ORGANIZATION>House</ORGANIZATION> and gained a few in the <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION>, but overall retained control of the <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION>. <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> received 379, or over 70% of the <ORGANIZATION>Electoral College</ORGANIZATION> votes, with <PERSON>Dole</PERSON> receiving 159 electoral votes.

The media and prominent Republicans raised controversy over <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON>'s relationship with a 22-year-old White House intern named <PERSON>Monica Lewinsky</PERSON>, resulting in the <PERSON>Lewinsky</PERSON> scandal, on January 21, 1998. In a lame duck session after the 1998 elections, for the belief <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> lied about his relationship with <PERSON>Lewinsky</PERSON> in a sworn deposition in the <PERSON>Paula Jones</PERSON> lawsuit, the Republican-controlled <ORGANIZATION>House</ORGANIZATION> voted to impeach <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> in 1998, making him the second <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> president to be impeached after <PERSON>Andrew Johnson</PERSON>.

The House held no serious impeachment hearings before the mid-term elections. Though the mid-term elections held in November 1998 were at the 6-year point in an 8-year presidency (a time in the electoral cycle where the party holding the White House usually loses Congressional seats) the <ORGANIZATION>Democratic Party</ORGANIZATION> gained several seats. To hold impeachment proceedings, the Republican leadership called a lame duck session in December 1998.



While the <ORGANIZATION>House Judiciary Committee</ORGANIZATION> hearings were perfunctory and ended in a straight party line vote, there was lively debate on the <ORGANIZATION>House</ORGANIZATION> floor. The two charges passed in the <ORGANIZATION>House</ORGANIZATION> (largely on the basis of Republican support but with a handful of Democratic votes as well) were for perjury and obstruction of justice. The perjury charge arose from <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON>'s testimony about his relationship to <PERSON>Monica Lewinsky</PERSON> during a sexual harassment lawsuit (later dismissed, appealed and settled for $850,000) brought by former <LOCATION>Arkansas</LOCATION> state employee <PERSON>Paula Jones</PERSON>. The obstruction charge was based on his actions during the subsequent investigation of that testimony. The <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION> later voted to acquit <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> on both charges. The <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION> refused to convene to hold an impeachment trial before the end of the old term, so the trial was held over until the next <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION>. <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> was represented by <LOCATION>Washington</LOCATION> law firm <ORGANIZATION>Williams & Connolly</ORGANIZATION>.

The <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION> concluded a twenty-one day trial on February 12, 1999, with the vote on both counts falling short of the Constitutional two-thirds majority requirement to convict and remove an office holder. The final vote was generally along party lines, with no Democrats voting guilty. Some Republicans voted not guilty for both charges. On the perjury charge, fifty five senators voted to acquit, including ten Republicans, and forty five voted to convict; on the obstruction charge the <ORGANIZATION>Senate</ORGANIZATION> voted 5050.

<PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> enacted the Digital Millennium Copyright Act on October 21, 1998. It served as the first significant amendment to the Copyright Act since 1976. The DMCA extended the protection of intellectual property to outlaw reverse engineering of digital protection. It provided a framework for sound recording copyright owners and recording artists to seek public performance royalties under statute, which proved to be a landmark achievement for the recording industry.

Three notable military events occurred during <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON>'s second term. In <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON>'s State of the Union Address, <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> warned <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> of <LOCATION>Iraqi</LOCATION> dictator <PERSON>Saddam Hussein</PERSON>'s pursuit of nuclear weapons:



To weaken <PERSON>Saddam Hussein</PERSON>'s grip of power, <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> signed H.R. 4655 into law on October 31, 1998, which instituted a policy of "regime change" against <LOCATION>Iraq</LOCATION>, though it explicitly stated it did not speak to the use of American military forces. The administration then launched a four-day bombing campaign named Operation <PERSON>Desert Fox</PERSON>, lasting from December 16 to December 19, 1998.

The Battle of <LOCATION>Mogadishu</LOCATION> also occurred in <LOCATION>Somalia</LOCATION> in 1993. During the operation, two <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> MH-60 Black Hawk helicopters were shot down by rocket-propelled grenade attacks to their tail rotors, trapping soldiers behind enemy lines. This resulted in an urban battle that killed 18 American soldiers, wounded 73 others, and one was taken prisoner. There were many more Somali casualties. Some of the American bodies were dragged through the streets and broadcasted on television news programs. In response, <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> forces were withdrawn from <LOCATION>Somalia</LOCATION> and later conflicts were approached with less soldiers on the ground.

To stop the ethnic cleansing and genocide of Albanians by nationalist Serbians in the former <LOCATION>Federal Republic of Yugoslavia</LOCATION>'s province of <LOCATION>Kosovo</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> authorized the use of American troops in a <ORGANIZATION>NATO</ORGANIZATION> bombing campaign against <LOCATION>Yugoslavia</LOCATION> in 1999, named Operation Allied Force. General <PERSON>Wesley Clark</PERSON> was Supreme Allied Commander of <ORGANIZATION>NATO</ORGANIZATION> and oversaw the mission. With <ORGANIZATION>United Nations Security Council</ORGANIZATION> Resolution 1244, the bombing campaign ended on June 10, 1999. The resolution placed <LOCATION>Kosovo</LOCATION> under <ORGANIZATION>UN</ORGANIZATION> administration and authorized a peacekeeping force. <ORGANIZATION>NATO</ORGANIZATION> claimed to have suffered zero combat deaths, and two deaths from an Apache helicopter crash. Opinions in the popular press criticized pre-war genocide claims by the <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> administration as greatly exaggerated. A <ORGANIZATION>U.N. Court</ORGANIZATION> ruled genocide did not take place, but recognized, "a systematic campaign of terror, including murders, rapes, arsons and severe maltreatments". The term "ethnic cleansing" was used as an alternative to "genocide" to denote not just ethnically motivated murder but also displacement, though critics charge there is no difference. <PERSON>Slobodan Miloevi</PERSON>, the President of <LOCATION>Yugoslavia</LOCATION> at the time, was eventually charged with the "murders of about 600 individually identified ethnic Albanians" and "crimes against humanity."

The Whitewater controversy was an American political controversy that began with the real estate dealings of <PERSON>Bill</PERSON> and <PERSON>Hillary Clinton</PERSON> and their associates, <PERSON>Jim</PERSON> and <PERSON>Susan McDougal</PERSON> in the <ORGANIZATION>Whitewater Development Corporation</ORGANIZATION>, a failed business venture in the 1970s and 1980s.

<PERSON>Clinton</PERSON>'s job approval rating ranged from 36% in mid-1993 to 64% in late-1993 and early-1994. In his second term, his rating was consistently ranged from the high-50s to the high-60s. After his impeachment proceedings in 1998 and 1999, <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON>'s rating reached its highest point at 73% approval. He finished with an approval rating of 68%, which was higher than that of any other departing president since polling began more than seventy years earlier.

As he was leaving office, a CNN/USA TODAY/Gallup poll revealed only 45% said they'd miss him. While 55% thought he "would have something worthwhile to contribute and should remain active in public life", 68% thought he'd be remembered for his "involvement in personal scandal", and 58% answered "No" to the question "Do you generally think <PERSON>Bill Clinton</PERSON> is honest and trustworthy?". 47% of the respondents identified themselves as being <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> supporters. 47% said he would be remembered as either "outstanding" or "above average" as a president while 22% said he would be remembered as "below average" or "poor".

The <ORGANIZATION>Gallup Organization</ORGANIZATION> published a poll in February 2007 asking respondents to name the greatest president in <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> history; <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> came in fourth place, capturing 13% of the vote. In a 2006 <ORGANIZATION>Quinnipiac University</ORGANIZATION> poll asking respondents to name the best president since World War II, <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> ranked 3% behind <PERSON>Ronald Reagan</PERSON> to place second with 25% of the vote. However, in the same poll, when respondents were asked to name the worst president since World War II, <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> placed 1% behind <PERSON>Nixon</PERSON> and 18% behind <PERSON>George W. Bush</PERSON> to come in third with 16% of the vote.

In May 2006, a <ORGANIZATION>CNN</ORGANIZATION> poll comparing <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON>'s job performance with that of his successor, <PERSON>George W. Bush</PERSON>, found that a strong majority of respondents said <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> outperformed <PERSON>Bush</PERSON> in six different areas questioned.
