As the first <PERSON>Baby Boomer</PERSON> president, <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON> was the first president in a half-century not to have been shaped by World War II. Authors <PERSON>Martin Walker</PERSON> and <PERSON>Bob Woodward</PERSON> state <PERSON>Clinton</PERSON>'s innovative use of soundbite-ready dialogue, personal charisma, and public perception-oriented campaigning was major for his high public approval ratings. <TITLE><PERSON>Elizabeth Garrett Anderson</PERSON></TITLE>

Dr. <PERSON>Elizabeth Garrett Anderson</PERSON>, LSA, MD (June 9 1836 - December 17 1917), was an English physician and feminist, the first woman to gain a medical qualification in <LOCATION>Britain</LOCATION>.

<PERSON>Garrett Anderson</PERSON> was born in <LOCATION>Aldeburgh</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Suffolk</LOCATION> in 1836, and the sister of <PERSON>Millicent Fawcett</PERSON>. She was also the cousin of <PERSON>Elizabeth Dunnell</PERSON> who married <PERSON>Richard Garrett III</PERSON> the famous owner of <ORGANIZATION>Richard Garrett & Sons</ORGANIZATION>. <PERSON>Elizabeth</PERSON> was educated at home and at a private school. In 1860 she resolved to study medicine, an almost unheard-of thing for a woman at that time, and regarded by some as almost indecent. Having obtained some more or less irregular instruction at the <LOCATION>Middlesex Hospital</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>London</LOCATION>, she was refused admission as a full student both there and at many other medical schools to which she applied. Finally she studied anatomy privately at the <LOCATION>London Hospital</LOCATION>, and with some of the professors at the <ORGANIZATION>University of St Andrews</ORGANIZATION>, and at the <LOCATION>Edinburgh</LOCATION> Extra-Mural school. She had no less difficulty in gaining a qualifying diploma to practise medicine. <ORGANIZATION>London University</ORGANIZATION>, the <ORGANIZATION>Royal Colleges of Physicians</ORGANIZATION> and Surgeons, and many other examining bodies refused to admit her to their examinations; but in the end the Society of Apothecaries allowed her to enter for the Licence of Apothecaries' Hall, which she obtained in 1865. This entitled her to have her name entered on the medical register, the second woman after <PERSON>Elizabeth Blackwell</PERSON>, and the first woman qualified in <LOCATION>Britain</LOCATION> to do so.

In 1866 she was appointed general medical attendant to <ORGANIZATION>St Mary</ORGANIZATION>'s Dispensary, a <LOCATION>London</LOCATION> institution started to enable poor women to obtain medical help from qualified practitioners of their own sex. The dispensary soon developed into the <LOCATION>New Hospital</LOCATION> for Women, and there Dr <PERSON>Garrett</PERSON> worked for over twenty years. In 1870 she obtained the <ORGANIZATION>University of Paris</ORGANIZATION> degree of MD, three months after <PERSON>Frances Hoggan</PERSON> obtained that qualification. The same year she was elected to the first <ORGANIZATION>London School Board</ORGANIZATION>, at the head of the poll for <LOCATION>Marylebone</LOCATION>, and was also made one of the visiting physicians of the <LOCATION>East London Hospital</LOCATION> for Children; but the duties of these two positions she found to be incompatible with her principal work, and she soon resigned them. She also built a medical school for woman.

In 1871 she married <PERSON>James George Skelton Anderson</PERSON> (d. 1907) of the <ORGANIZATION>Orient Steamship Company</ORGANIZATION> co-owned by his uncle <PERSON>Arthur Anderson</PERSON>, but she did not give up her practice. She had three children, <PERSON>Louisa</PERSON>, <PERSON>Margaret</PERSON> who died of meningitis, and <PERSON>Alan</PERSON>. <PERSON>Louisa</PERSON> also became a pioneering doctor of medicine and social campaigner. 

In 1873 she gained membership of the <ORGANIZATION>British Medical Association</ORGANIZATION> and remained the only woman member for 19 years, due to the Association's vote against the admission of further women "one of several instances where <PERSON>Garrett</PERSON>, uniquely, was able to enter a hitherto all male medical institution which subsequently moved formally to exclude any women who might seek to follow her."

<PERSON>Elizabeth</PERSON> worked steadily at the development of the <LOCATION>New Hospital</LOCATION> for Women, and (from 1874) at the creation of the <ORGANIZATION>London School of Medicine</ORGANIZATION> for Women. Both institutions have since been handsomely and suitably housed and equipped, the New hospital for Women (in the <LOCATION>Euston Road</LOCATION>) for many years being worked entirely by medical women, and the schools (in Hunter Street, WC1) having over 200 students, most of them preparing for the medical degree of <ORGANIZATION>London University</ORGANIZATION> (the present-day <ORGANIZATION>University College London</ORGANIZATION>), which was opened to women in 1877. In 1897 Dr <PERSON>Garrett Anderson</PERSON> was elected president of the East Anglian branch of the <ORGANIZATION>British Medical Association</ORGANIZATION>. 

On 9 November 1908 she was elected mayor of <LOCATION>Aldeburgh</LOCATION>, the first female mayor in <LOCATION>England</LOCATION>. 

The movement for the admission of women to the medical profession, of which Dr <PERSON>Anderson</PERSON> was the indefatigable pioneer in <LOCATION>England</LOCATION>, extended in her lifetime to all of <LOCATION>North America</LOCATION> and <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION>, except for <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION> and <LOCATION>Turkey</LOCATION>. She died in 1917 and is buried in <LOCATION>Aldeburgh</LOCATION>.

<TITLE><PERSON>Arthur Miller</PERSON></TITLE>

<PERSON>Arthur Asher Miller</PERSON> (October 17, 1915 - February 10, 2005) was an American playwright and essayist. He was a prominent figure in American literature and cinema for over 61 years, writing a wide variety of plays, including celebrated plays such as The Crucible, A View from the Bridge, All My Sons, and Death of a Salesman, which are still studied and performed worldwide.Miller was often in the public eye, most famously for refusing to give evidence against others to the <ORGANIZATION>House Un-American Activities Committee</ORGANIZATION>, being the recipient of the Pulitzer Prize for Drama among countless other awards, and for his marriage to <PERSON>Marilyn Monroe</PERSON>. <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> is considered by audiences and scholars as one of <LOCATION>America</LOCATION>'s greatest playwrights, and his plays are lauded throughout the world.

<PERSON>Arthur Asher Miller</PERSON> was born to moderately affluent Jewish-American parents, <PERSON>Isidore</PERSON> and <ORGANIZATION>Augusta Miller</ORGANIZATION>, in <LOCATION>Manhattan</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>New York City</LOCATION>, in 1915. His father owned a women's clothes/coat-manufacturing business, which failed in the Wall Street Crash of 1929 after which his family moved to humbler quarters in <LOCATION>Brooklyn</LOCATION>.

Because of the effects of the Great Depression on his family, <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> had little money for college after graduating in 1932 from <ORGANIZATION>Abraham Lincoln High School</ORGANIZATION> (<LOCATION>New York</LOCATION>). Before securing a place at the <ORGANIZATION>University of Michigan</ORGANIZATION>, he worked in a number of menial jobs to pay for his tuition. He continued working in <PERSON>Ann Arbor</PERSON> to supplement his income.

At the <ORGANIZATION>University of Michigan</ORGANIZATION>, <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> first majored in journalism, where he became the reporter and night editor on the student paper, the <LOCATION>Michigan</LOCATION> Daily. It was during this time that he wrote his first work, No Villain. <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> switched his major to English, and subsequently won the Avery Hopwood Award for No Villain. He was mentored by Professor <PERSON>Kenneth Rowe</PERSON>, who instructed him in his early forays into playwrighting. <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> retained strong ties to his alma mater throughout the rest of his life, establishing the university's <ORGANIZATION>Arthur Miller Award</ORGANIZATION> in 1985 and <PERSON>Arthur Miller</PERSON> Award for Dramatic Writing in 1999, and lending his name to the <ORGANIZATION>Arthur Miller Theatre</ORGANIZATION> in 2000. In 1937, <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> wrote Honors at Dawn, which also received the Avery Hopwood Award.

In 1938, <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> received his bachelor's degree in English. After graduation, he joined the Federal Theater Project, a New Deal agency established to provide jobs in the theater. He chose the theater project although he had an offer to work as a scriptwriter for 20th Century Fox. However, <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> worried about possible Communist infiltration, closed the project. <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> began working in the <ORGANIZATION>Brooklyn Navy Yard</ORGANIZATION> while continuing to write radio plays, some of which were broadcast on CBS. 

On August 5, 1940, he married his college sweetheart, <PERSON>Mary Slattery</PERSON>, the <ORGANIZATION>Catholic</ORGANIZATION> daughter of an insurance salesman. The couple had two children, <PERSON>Jane</PERSON> and <PERSON>Robert</PERSON>. <PERSON>Robert</PERSON> became a director, writer and producer who was, among other things, producer of the 1996 movie version of The Crucible. 

<PERSON>Miller</PERSON> was exempted from military service during World War II because of a high-school <LOCATION>american</LOCATION> football injury to his left kneecap.

Early career'''In 1944 <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> wrote The Man Who Had All the Luck, which was produced in <LOCATION>New York</LOCATION> and won the Theater Guild's National Award.

In 1948 <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> built a small studio in <LOCATION>Roxbury</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Connecticut</LOCATION>, a town that was to be his long time home. There, in less than a day, he wrote Act I of Death of a Salesman. Within six weeks, he completed the rest of the play, one of the works for which he is best known. Death of a Salesman premiered on Broadway on February 10, 1949 at the Morosco Theatre, directed by <PERSON>Kazan</PERSON>, and starring <PERSON>Lee J. Cobb</PERSON> as <PERSON>Willy Loman</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mildred Dunnock</PERSON> as <PERSON>Linda</PERSON>, <PERSON>Arthur Kennedy</PERSON> as <PERSON>Biff</PERSON>, and <PERSON>Cameron Mitchell</PERSON> as Happy. The play was commercially successful and critically acclaimed, winning a Tony Award for best play, the <LOCATION>New York City</LOCATION> Drama Circle Critics Award, and the Pulitzer Prize for Drama. It was the first play to win all three of these major awards. The searing drama ran for 742 performances. 

In 1952, <PERSON>Elia Kazan</PERSON> appeared before the <ORGANIZATION>House Un-American Activities Committee</ORGANIZATION> (<ORGANIZATION>HUAC</ORGANIZATION>) and, under fear of being blacklisted from <LOCATION>Hollywood</LOCATION>, named eight black men from the Group Theatre who in recent years had been fellow members of the <ORGANIZATION>Communist Party</ORGANIZATION>. After speaking with <PERSON>Kazan</PERSON> about his testimony <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> traveled to <LOCATION>Salem</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Massachusetts</LOCATION> to research the witch trials of 1692. The Crucible, an allegorical play in which <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> likened the situation with the <ORGANIZATION>House Un-American Activities Committee</ORGANIZATION> to the witch hunt in <LOCATION>Salem</LOCATION>, opened at the Beck Theatre on Broadway on January 22, 1953. Though widely considered only somewhat successful at the time of its initial release, today The Crucible is <PERSON>Miller</PERSON>'s most frequently produced work throughout the world. <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> and <PERSON>Kazan</PERSON> had been close friends throughout the late 1940s and early 1950s, but after <PERSON>Kazan</PERSON>'s testimony to <ORGANIZATION>HUAC</ORGANIZATION>, the pair's friendship ended, and they did not speak to each other for the next ten years. <ORGANIZATION>HUAC</ORGANIZATION> took an interest in <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> himself not long after The Crucible opened, denying him a passport to attend the play's <LOCATION>London</LOCATION> opening in 1954. <PERSON>Kazan</PERSON> defended his own actions through his film On the Waterfront, in which a dockworker heroically testifies against a corrupt union boss. <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> was in turn to respond with the play A View from the Bridge, in which another dockworker's decision to inform on two illegal immigrants is based on ignoble, self-serving motives. <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> did have his case overturned and his passport returned. His feelings about <ORGANIZATION>HUAC</ORGANIZATION> never changed.

<PERSON>Miller</PERSON>'s experience with <ORGANIZATION>HUAC</ORGANIZATION> affected him throughout his life. In the late 1970s he became very interested in the highly publicized <PERSON>Barbara Gibbons</PERSON> murder case, in which <PERSON>Gibbons</PERSON>' son <PERSON>Peter Reilly</PERSON> was convicted of his mother's murder based on what many felt was a coerced confession and little other evidence. City Confidential, an A&E program about the murder, postulates that part of the reason <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> took such an active interest (including supporting <PERSON>Reilly</PERSON>'s defense and using his own celebrity to bring attention to <PERSON>Reilly</PERSON>'s plight) was because he had felt similarly persecuted in his run-in with the <ORGANIZATION>HUAC</ORGANIZATION>. He sympathized with <PERSON>Reilly</PERSON>, whom he firmly believed to be innocent and to have been railroaded by the <ORGANIZATION>Connecticut State Police</ORGANIZATION> and the Attorney General who had initially prosecuted the case. 

In 1955 a one-act version of <PERSON>Miller</PERSON>'s verse drama, A View From The Bridge, opened on Broadway in a joint bill with one of <PERSON>Miller</PERSON>'s lesser-known plays, A Memory of Two Mondays. The following year, <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> returned to A View from the Bridge, revising it into a two-act prose version, which <PERSON>Peter Brook</PERSON> produced in <LOCATION>London</LOCATION>.'''

In June 1956 <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> divorced <PERSON>Mary Slattery</PERSON>, and on June 29, he married <PERSON>Marilyn Monroe</PERSON>. <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> and <PERSON>Monroe</PERSON> had first met in 1951, when they had a brief affair, and had remained in contact since then. 

Taking advantage of the publicity of <PERSON>Miller</PERSON>'s marriage, <ORGANIZATION>HUAC</ORGANIZATION> subpoenaed him to appear before the committee shortly before the nuptials. Before appearing, <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> asked the committee not to ask him to name names, to which the chairman agreed.When <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> attended the hearing, to which <PERSON>Monroe</PERSON> accompanied him, risking her own career, he gave the committee a detailed account of his political activities. Reneging on the chairman's promise, the committee asked him to reveal to the names of friends and colleagues who had partaken in similar activities. <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> refused to comply with the request, saying "I could not use the name of another person and bring trouble on him."As a result a judge found <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> guilty of contempt of <ORGANIZATION>Congress</ORGANIZATION> in May 1957. <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> was fined $500, sentenced to thirty days in prison, blacklisted, and disallowed a <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> passport. In 1958 his conviction was overturned by the court of appeals, which ruled that <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> had been misled by the chairman of <ORGANIZATION>HUAC</ORGANIZATION>.

After his conviction was overturned, <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> began work on The Misfits, which starred his wife. <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> said that the filming was one of the lowest points in his life, and shortly before the film's premiere in 1961, the pair divorced. Nineteen months later, <PERSON>Monroe</PERSON> died of an apparent drug overdose.

<PERSON>Miller</PERSON> married photographer <PERSON>Inge Morath</PERSON> on February 17, 1962, and the first of their two children, <PERSON>Rebecca</PERSON>, was born that September. Their son <PERSON>Daniel</PERSON> was born with Down Syndrome in November, 1966, and was consequently institutionalized and excluded from the Millers' personal life at <PERSON>Miller</PERSON>'s insistence. The couple remained together until <PERSON>Inge</PERSON>'s death in 2002. <PERSON>Arthur Miller</PERSON>'s son-in-law, actor <PERSON>Daniel Day-Lewis</PERSON> is said to have visited <PERSON>Daniel</PERSON> frequently, and to have persuaded <PERSON>Arthur Miller</PERSON> to reunite with his adult son .

In 1964 <PERSON>Miller</PERSON>'s next play was produced. After the Fall is a deeply personal view of <PERSON>Miller</PERSON>'s own experiences during his marriage to <PERSON>Monroe</PERSON>. The play reunited <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> with his former friend <PERSON>Kazan</PERSON>: they collaborated on both the script and the direction. After the Fall opened on January 23, 1964 at the ANTA Theatre in <LOCATION>Washington Square Park</LOCATION> amid a flurry of publicity and outrage at putting a Monroe-like character, called <PERSON>Maggie</PERSON>, on stage. Also in the same year, <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> produced Incident at Vichy.In 1965, <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> was elected the first American president of <ORGANIZATION>International PEN</ORGANIZATION>, a position which he held for four years. During this period <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> wrote the penetrating family drama, The Price, produced in 1968. It was <PERSON>Miller</PERSON>'s most successful play since Death of a Salesman.

In 1969, <PERSON>Miller</PERSON>'s works were banned in the <LOCATION>Soviet Union</LOCATION> after he campaigned for the freedom of dissident writers. Throughout the 1970s, <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> spent much of his time experimenting with the theatre, producing one-act plays such as Fame and The Reason Why, and traveling with his wife, producing In The Country and Chinese Encounters with her. The play was a success in <LOCATION>China</LOCATION> and in 1984, Salesman in <LOCATION>Beijing</LOCATION>, a book about <PERSON>Miller</PERSON>'s experience in <LOCATION>Beijing</LOCATION>, was published. Shown on <ORGANIZATION>CBS</ORGANIZATION>, it attracted 25 million viewers. In late 1987, <PERSON>Miller</PERSON>'s autobiography, Timebends was published. In 1996, a film of The Crucible starring <PERSON>Daniel Day Lewis</PERSON> and <ORGANIZATION>Winona Ryder</ORGANIZATION> opened. <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> spent much of 1996 working on the screenplay to the film. Mr. <PERSON>Peters</PERSON>' Connections was staged off-Broadway in 1998, and Death of a Salesman was revived on Broadway in 1999 to celebrate its fiftieth anniversary. Previous winners include <PERSON>Doris Lessing</PERSON>, Gnter Grass and <PERSON>Carlos Fuentes</PERSON>. Later that year, <PERSON>Ingeborg Morath</PERSON> died of Lymphatic cancer at the age of 78. The following year <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> won the <LOCATION>Jerusalem</LOCATION> Prize.In December 2004, the 89-year-old <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> announced that he has been living with a 34-year-old minimalist painter <PERSON>Agnes Barley</PERSON> at his Connecticut farm since 2002, and that they intended to marry. <PERSON>Miller</PERSON>'s career as a writer spanned over seven decades, and at the time of his death in 2005, <PERSON>Miller</PERSON> was considered to be one of the greatest dramatists of the twentieth century, among the likes of <PERSON>Harold Pinter</PERSON>, <PERSON>Eugene O'Neill</PERSON>, <PERSON>Luigi Pirandello</PERSON>, <PERSON>Samuel Beckett</PERSON>, <PERSON>Jean-Paul Sartre</PERSON>, <PERSON>Bertolt Brecht</PERSON>, and <PERSON>Tennessee Williams</PERSON>. <TITLE><PERSON>Bill Gates</PERSON></TITLE>

<PERSON>William Henry</PERSON> "Bill" <PERSON>Gates III</PERSON>, KBE (born October 28, 1955), is an American business magnate, philanthropist, the world's third richest person (as of February 8, 2008), the second richest American (as of October 10, 2008), and chairman of <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION>, the software company he founded with <PERSON>Paul Allen</PERSON>. During his career at <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION>, <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> held the positions of CEO and chief software architect, and remains the largest individual shareholder with more than 8 percent of the common stock. He has also authored or co-authored several books.

Gates is one of the best-known entrepreneurs of the personal computer revolution. Although he is admired by many, a large number of industry insiders criticize his business tactics, which they consider anti-competitive, an opinion which has in some cases been upheld by the courts. In the later stages of his career, <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> has pursued a number of philanthropic endeavors, donating large amounts of money to various charitable organizations and scientific research programs through the <ORGANIZATION>Bill & Melinda Gates Foundation</ORGANIZATION>, established in 2000.

<PERSON>Bill Gates</PERSON> stepped down as chief executive officer of <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION> in January, 2000. He remained as chairman and created the position of chief software architect. In June, 2006, <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> announced that he would be transitioning from full-time work at <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION> to part-time work at <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION> and full-time work at the <ORGANIZATION>Bill & Melinda Gates Foundation</ORGANIZATION>. He gradually transferred his duties to <PERSON>Ray Ozzie</PERSON>, chief software architect and <PERSON>Craig Mundie</PERSON>, chief research and strategy officer. <PERSON>Gates</PERSON>'s last full-time day at <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION> was June 27, 2008. He remains at <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION> as a part-time, non-executive chairman.

<PERSON>Gates</PERSON> was born in <LOCATION>Seattle</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Washington</LOCATION>, to <PERSON>William H. Gates</PERSON>, Sr. and <PERSON>Mary Maxwell Gates</PERSON>. His family was upper middle class; his father was a prominent lawyer, his mother served on the board of directors for <ORGANIZATION>First Interstate BancSystem</ORGANIZATION> and the <LOCATION>United Way</LOCATION>, and her father, <PERSON>J. W. Maxwell</PERSON>, was a national bank president. <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> has one older sister, <PERSON>Kristi</PERSON> (Kristianne), and one younger sister, <PERSON>Libby</PERSON>. He was the fourth of his name in his family, but was known as <PERSON>William Gates III</PERSON> or "Trey" because his father had dropped his own "III" suffix. Early on in his life, <PERSON>Gates</PERSON>'s parents had a law career in mind for him.

At thirteen he enrolled in the <ORGANIZATION>Lakeside School</ORGANIZATION>, an exclusive preparatory school. When he was in the eighth grade, the <ORGANIZATION>Mothers Club</ORGANIZATION> at the school used proceeds from <ORGANIZATION>Lakeside School</ORGANIZATION>'s rummage sale to buy an ASR-33 teletype terminal and a block of computer time on a <ORGANIZATION>General Electric</ORGANIZATION> (GE) computer for the school's students. <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> took an interest in programming the GE system in BASIC and was excused from math classes to pursue his interest. He wrote his first computer program on this machine: an implementation of tic-tac-toe that allowed users to play games against the computer. <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> was fascinated by the machine and how it would always execute software code perfectly. When he reflected back on that moment, he commented on it and said, "There was just something neat about the machine." After the <ORGANIZATION>Mothers Club</ORGANIZATION> donation was exhausted, he and other students sought time on systems including DEC PDP minicomputers. One of these systems was a PDP-10 belonging to <ORGANIZATION>Computer Center Corporation</ORGANIZATION> (CCC), which banned four Lakeside studentsGates, <PERSON>Paul Allen</PERSON>, <PERSON>Ric Weiland</PERSON>, and <PERSON>Kent Evansfor</PERSON> the summer after it caught them exploiting bugs in the operating system to obtain free computer time.

At the end of the ban, the four students offered to find bugs in CCC's software in exchange for computer time. Rather than use the system via teletype, <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> went to <ORGANIZATION>CCC</ORGANIZATION>'s offices and studied source code for various programs that ran on the system, including programs in <LOCATION>FORTRAN</LOCATION>, LISP, and machine language. The arrangement with <ORGANIZATION>CCC</ORGANIZATION> continued until 1970, when it went out of business. The following year, <ORGANIZATION>Information Sciences Inc.</ORGANIZATION> hired the four Lakeside students to write a payroll program in COBOL, providing them computer time and royalties. After his administrators became aware of his programming abilities, <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> wrote the school's computer program to schedule students in classes. He modified the code so that he was placed in classes with mostly female students. He later stated that "it was hard to tear myself away from a machine at which I could so unambiguously demonstrate success." At age 17, <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> formed a venture with <PERSON>Allen</PERSON>, called Traf-O-Data, to make traffic counters based on the Intel 8008 processor.

<PERSON>Gates</PERSON> graduated from <ORGANIZATION>Lakeside School</ORGANIZATION> in 1973. He scored 1590 out of 1600 on the Scholastic Aptitude Test and subsequently enrolled at <ORGANIZATION>Harvard College</ORGANIZATION> in the fall of 1973. Prior to the mid 1990s, an SAT score of 1590 was equivalent to an IQ of about 170 (roughly the one in a million level), a figure that would frequently be cited by the press. While at <ORGANIZATION>Harvard</ORGANIZATION>, he met his future business partner, <PERSON>Steve Ballmer</PERSON>, whom he later appointed as CEO of <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION>. He also met computer scientist <PERSON>Christos Papadimitriou</PERSON> at <ORGANIZATION>Harvard</ORGANIZATION>, with whom he collaborated on a paper about algorithms. He did not have a definite study plan while a student at <ORGANIZATION>Harvard</ORGANIZATION> and spent a lot of time using the school's computers. He remained in contact with <PERSON>Paul Allen</PERSON>, joining him at <ORGANIZATION>Honeywell</ORGANIZATION> during the summer of 1974. The following year saw the release of the MITS Altair 8800 based on the Intel 8080 CPU, and <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> and <PERSON>Allen</PERSON> saw this as the opportunity to start their own computer software company. He had talked this decision over with his parents, who were supportive of him after seeing how much <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> wanted to start a company.



After reading the January 1975 issue of Popular Electronics that demonstrated the Altair 8800, <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> contacted Micro Instrumentation and Telemetry Systems (MITS), the creators of the new microcomputer, to inform them that he and others were working on a BASIC interpreter for the platform. In reality, <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> and <PERSON>Allen</PERSON> did not have an Altair and had not written code for it; they merely wanted to gauge <ORGANIZATION>MITS</ORGANIZATION>'s interest. <ORGANIZATION>MITS</ORGANIZATION> president <PERSON>Ed Roberts</PERSON> agreed to meet them for a demo, and over the course of a few weeks they developed an Altair emulator that ran on a minicomputer, and then the BASIC interpreter. The demonstration, held at <ORGANIZATION>MITS</ORGANIZATION>'s offices in <LOCATION>Albuquerque</LOCATION>, was a success and resulted in a deal with MITS to distribute the interpreter as Altair BASIC. <PERSON>Paul Allen</PERSON> was hired into MITS, and <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> took a leave of absence from <ORGANIZATION>Harvard</ORGANIZATION> to work with <PERSON>Allen</PERSON> at MITS in <LOCATION>Albuquerque</LOCATION> in November 1975. They named their partnership "Micro-soft" and had their first office located in <LOCATION>Albuquerque</LOCATION>. Within a year, the hyphen was dropped, and on November 26, 1976, the trade name "<ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION>" was registered with the USPTO.

<ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION>'s BASIC was popular with computer hobbyists, but <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> discovered that a pre-market copy had leaked into the community and was being widely copied and distributed. In February 1976, <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> wrote an Open Letter to Hobbyists in the <ORGANIZATION>MITS</ORGANIZATION> newsletter saying that MITS could not continue to produce, distribute, and maintain high-quality software without payment. This letter was unpopular with many computer hobbyists, but <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> persisted in his belief that software developers should be able to demand payment. <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION> became independent of MITS in late 1976, and it continued to develop programming language software for various systems. The company moved from <LOCATION>Albuquerque</LOCATION> to its new home in <LOCATION>Bellevue</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Washington</LOCATION> on January 1, 1979.

During <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION>'s early years, all employees had broad responsibility for the company's business. <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> oversaw the business details, but continued to write code as well. In the first five years, he personally reviewed every line of code the company shipped, and often rewrote parts of it as he saw fit.

In 1980 <ORGANIZATION>IBM</ORGANIZATION> approached <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION> to make the BASIC interpreter for its upcoming personal computer, the <ORGANIZATION>IBM</ORGANIZATION> PC. When <ORGANIZATION>IBM</ORGANIZATION>'s representatives mentioned that they needed an operating system, <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> referred them to <ORGANIZATION>Digital Research</ORGANIZATION> (DRI), makers of the widely used CP/M operating system.IBM's discussions with <ORGANIZATION>Digital Research</ORGANIZATION> went poorly, and they did not reach a licensing agreement. <ORGANIZATION>IBM</ORGANIZATION> representative <PERSON>Jack Sams</PERSON> mentioned the licensing difficulties during a subsequent meeting with <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> and told him to get an acceptable operating system. A few weeks later Gates proposed using 86-DOS (QDOS), an operating system similar to CP/M that <PERSON>Tim Paterson</PERSON> of <ORGANIZATION>Seattle Computer Products</ORGANIZATION> had made for hardware similar to the PC. <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION> made a deal with <ORGANIZATION>SCP</ORGANIZATION> to become the exclusive licensing agent, and later the full owner, of 86-DOS. After adapting the operating system for the PC, <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION> delivered it to <ORGANIZATION>IBM</ORGANIZATION> as PC-DOS in exchange for a one-time fee of $50,000. <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> insisted that <ORGANIZATION>IBM</ORGANIZATION> let <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION> keep the copyright on the operating system, because he believed that other hardware vendors would clone <ORGANIZATION>IBM</ORGANIZATION>'s system. They did, and the sales of MS-DOS made <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION> a major player in the industry.

<PERSON>Gates</PERSON> oversaw <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION>'s company restructuring on June 25, 1981, which re-incorporated the company in <LOCATION>Washington</LOCATION> and made Gates President of <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION> and the Chairman of the Board. <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION> launched its first retail version of <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION> Windows on November 20, 1985, and in August, the company struck a deal with <ORGANIZATION>IBM</ORGANIZATION> to develop a separate operating system called OS/2. Although the two companies successfully developed the first version of the new system, mounting creative differences undermined the partnership. Gates distributed an internal memo on May 16, 1991 announcing that the OS/2 partnership was over and <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION> would shift its efforts to the Windows NT kernel development.

From <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION>'s founding in 1975 until 2006, <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> had primary responsibility for the company's product strategy. He aggressively broadened the company's range of products, and wherever <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION> achieved a dominant position he vigorously defended it.

As an executive, <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> met regularly with <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION>'s senior managers and program managers. Firsthand accounts of these meetings describe him as verbally combative, berating managers for perceived holes in their business strategies or proposals that placed the company's long-term interests at risk.He often interrupted presentations with such comments as, "That's the stupidest thing I've ever heard!" and, "Why don't you just give up your options and join the <ORGANIZATION>Peace Corps</ORGANIZATION>?" The target of his outburst then had to defend the proposal in detail until, hopefully, <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> was fully convinced. When subordinates appeared to be procrastinating, he was known to remark sarcastically, "I'll do it over the weekend."

<PERSON>Gates</PERSON>'s role at <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION> for most of its history was primarily a management and executive role. However, he was an active software developer in the early years, particularly on the company's programming language products. He has not officially been on a development team since working on the TRS-80 Model 100 line, but wrote code as late as 1989 that shipped in the company's products. On June 15, 2006, <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> announced that he would transition out of his day-to-day role over the next two years to dedicate more time to philanthropy. He divided his responsibilities between two successors, placing <PERSON>Ray Ozzie</PERSON> in charge of day-to-day management and <PERSON>Craig Mundie</PERSON> in charge of long-term product strategy.

Many decisions that led to antitrust litigation over <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION>'s business practices have had Gates's approval. In the 1998 <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> v. <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION> case, <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> gave deposition testimony that several journalists characterized as evasive. He argued with examiner <PERSON>David Boies</PERSON> over the contextual meaning of words like "compete," "concerned," and "we." <ORGANIZATION>BusinessWeek</ORGANIZATION> reported:



<PERSON>Gates</PERSON> later said that he had simply resisted attempts by <PERSON>Boies</PERSON> to mischaracterize his words and actions. As to his demeanor during the deposition, he said, "Did I fence with <PERSON>Boies</PERSON>? ... I plead guilty. Whatever that penalty is should be levied against me: rudeness to <PERSON>Boies</PERSON> in the first degree." Despite <PERSON>Gates</PERSON>'s denials, the judge ruled that <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION> had committed monopolization and tying, blocking competition, in violation of the Sherman Antitrust Act.

The <ORGANIZATION>European Union Microsoft</ORGANIZATION> competition case is also a case brought by the <ORGANIZATION>European Commission of the European Union</ORGANIZATION> (<LOCATION>EU</LOCATION>) against <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION> for abuse of its dominant position in the market (according to competition law). It started as a complaint from <ORGANIZATION>Novell</ORGANIZATION> over <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION>'s licensing practices in 1993, and eventually resulted in the <LOCATION>EU</LOCATION> ordering <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION> to divulge certain information about its server products and release a version of <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION> Windows without Windows Media Player.

<PERSON>Bill Gates</PERSON> decided in 2008 to appear in at least 1 commercial in a series of ads to promote <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION>. This commercial, co-starring <PERSON>Jerry Seinfeld</PERSON>, is a 1 and a half minute talk between strangers as <PERSON>Seinfeld</PERSON> walks up on a discount shoe store (Shoe Circus) in a mall and notices <PERSON>Bill Gates</PERSON> buying shoes inside. The salesman is trying to sell Mr. <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> shoes that are a size too big. Mr. <PERSON>Seinfeld</PERSON> begins to inform him about a pair of shoes called Conquistadors that run "a little tight" and sells him on them in a size 10 (whereas the store clerk was attempting an 11). As Mr. <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> is buying the shoes he holds up his discount card, this card uses a slightly altered version of his own mugshot of his arrest in <LOCATION>New Mexico</LOCATION> in 1977 for a traffic violation . As they are walking out of the mall, <PERSON>Jerry Seinfeld</PERSON> asks <PERSON>Bill Gates</PERSON> if he has melded his mind to other developers, after getting a yes, he then asks if they are working on a way to make computers edible, again getting a yes. Most critics are still in debate over the exact meaning of this commercial saying that it is too vague to make sense to them. Though some say that it is an homage to Mr. <PERSON>Seinfeld</PERSON>'s own show about "nothing"(Seinfeld). 

<PERSON>Gates</PERSON> married <PERSON>Melinda Gates</PERSON> (ne French) from <LOCATION>Dallas</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Texas</LOCATION> on January 1, 1994. They have three children: <PERSON>Jennifer Katharine</PERSON>(1996), <PERSON>Rory John</PERSON>(1999) and <PERSON>Phoebe Adele</PERSON>(2002).<PERSON>Bill Gates</PERSON>' house is a 21st century earth-sheltered home in the side of a hill overlooking <LOCATION>Lake Washington</LOCATION> in <LOCATION>Medina</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Washington</LOCATION>. According to King County public records, as of 2006, the total assessed value of the property (land and house) is $125 million, and the annual property tax is $991,000. Also among <PERSON>Gates</PERSON>'s private acquisitions is the <ORGANIZATION>Codex Leicester</ORGANIZATION>, a collection of writings by <PERSON>Leonardo da Vinci</PERSON>, which <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> bought for $30.8 million at an auction in 1994. Gates is also known as an avid reader, and the ceiling of his large home library is engraved with a quotation from The Great Gatsby. He also enjoys playing bridge, tennis, and golf.

<PERSON>Gates</PERSON> was number one on the "Forbes 400" list from 1993 through to 2007 and number one on Forbes list of "The World's Richest People" from 1995 to 2007. In 1999, <PERSON>Gates</PERSON>'s wealth briefly surpassed $101 billion, causing the media to call him a "centibillionaire". Since 2000, the nominal value of his <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION> holdings has declined due to a fall in <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION>'s stock price after the dot-com bubble burst and the multi-billion dollar donations he has made to his charitable foundations. In a May 2006 interview, <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> commented that he wished that he were not the richest man in the world because he disliked the attention it brought. <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> has several investments outside <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION>, which in 2006 paid him a salary of $616,667, and $350,000 bonus totalling $966,667. He founded <ORGANIZATION>Corbis</ORGANIZATION>, a digital imaging company, in 1989. In 2004 he became a director of <ORGANIZATION>Berkshire Hathaway</ORGANIZATION>, the investment company headed by long-time friend <PERSON>Warren Buffett</PERSON>. 

Gates began to realize the expectations others had of him when public opinion mounted that he could give more of his wealth to charity. <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> studied the work of <PERSON>Andrew Carnegie</PERSON> and <PERSON>John D. Rockefeller</PERSON> and in 1994 sold some of his <ORGANIZATION>Microsoft</ORGANIZATION> stock to create the <PERSON>William H. Gates Foundation</PERSON>. In 2000, <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> and his wife combined three family foundations into one to create the charitable <ORGANIZATION>Bill & Melinda Gates Foundation</ORGANIZATION>, which is the largest transparently operated charitable foundation in the world. The foundation is setup to allow benefactors access how its money is being spent, unlike other major charitable organizations such as the <ORGANIZATION>Wellcome Trust</ORGANIZATION>. The generosity and extensive philanthropy of <PERSON>David Rockefeller</PERSON> has been credited as a major influence. <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> and his father have met with <ORGANIZATION>Rockefeller</ORGANIZATION> several times and have modeled their giving in part on the <PERSON>Rockefeller</PERSON> family's philanthropic focus, namely those global problems that are ignored by governments and other organizations. As of 2007 <PERSON>Bill</PERSON> and <PERSON>Melinda Gates</PERSON> were the second most generous philanthropist in <LOCATION>America</LOCATION>, having given over $28 billion to charity.

The foundation has also received criticism because it invests the assets that it has not yet distributed, with the exclusive goal of maximizing the return on investment. As a result, its investments include companies that have been criticized for worsening poverty in the same developing countries where the Foundation is attempting to relieve poverty. These include companies that pollute heavily and pharmaceutical companies that do not sell into the developing world.In response to press criticism, the foundation announced in 2007 a review of its investments to assess social responsibility. It subsequently cancelled the review and stood by its policy of investing for maximum return, while using voting rights to influence company practices.

Time magazine named Gates one of the 100 people who most influenced the 20th century, as well as one of the 100 most influential people of 2004, 2005, and 2006. Time also collectively named <PERSON>Gates</PERSON>, his wife <PERSON>Melinda</PERSON> and alternative rock band U2's lead singer <PERSON>Bono</PERSON> as the 2005 Persons of the Year for their humanitarian efforts. In 2006, he was voted eighth in the list of "Heroes of our time". <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> was listed in the Sunday Times power list in 1999, named CEO of the year by Chief Executive Officers magazine in 1994, ranked number one in the "Top 50 Cyber Elite" by Time in 1998, ranked number two in the Upside Elite 100 in 1999 and was included in The Guardian as one of the "Top 100 influential people in media" in 2001.

Gates has received honorary doctorates from <ORGANIZATION>Nyenrode Business Universiteit</ORGANIZATION>, <LOCATION>Breukelen</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>The Netherlands</LOCATION> in 2000, the <ORGANIZATION>Royal Institute of Technology</ORGANIZATION>, <LOCATION>Stockholm</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Sweden</LOCATION> in 2002, <ORGANIZATION>Waseda University</ORGANIZATION>, <LOCATION>Tokyo</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Japan</LOCATION> in 2005, <ORGANIZATION>Harvard University</ORGANIZATION> in June 2007, and from <LOCATION>Karolinska Institutet</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Stockholm</LOCATION>, in January 2008. <PERSON>Gates</PERSON> was also made an honorary Knight Commander of the Order of the British Empire (KBE) from Queen <PERSON>Elizabeth II</PERSON> in 2005, in addition to having entomologists name the <PERSON>Bill Gates</PERSON> flower fly, <PERSON>Eristalis</PERSON> gatesi, in his honor.

In November 2006, he and his wife were awarded the Order of the Aztec Eagle for their philanthropic work around the world in the areas of health and education, particularly in <LOCATION>Mexico</LOCATION>, and specifically in the program "Un pas de lectores".
