<TITLE><PERSON>Arnold Schwarzenegger</PERSON></TITLE>

<PERSON>Arnold Alois Schwarzenegger</PERSON> (German ; born July 30, 1947) is an Austrian-American bodybuilder, actor, businessman, and politician, currently serving as the 38th Governor of the state of <LOCATION>California</LOCATION>.

<PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> gained worldwide fame as a Hollywood action film icon. He was nicknamed "The Austrian Oak" and "The Styrian Oak" in his bodybuilding days, "Arnold Strong" and "Arnie" during his acting career, and more recently "The Governator" (a portmanteau of Governor and The Terminator, one of his film roles).

As a Republican, he was first elected on October 7, 2003, in a special recall election to replace then-Governor <PERSON>Gray Davis</PERSON>. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> was sworn in on November 17, 2003, to serve the remainder of <PERSON>Davis</PERSON>'s term. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> was then re-elected on November 7, 2006, in <LOCATION>California</LOCATION>'s 2006 gubernatorial election, to serve a full term as governor, defeating Democrat <PERSON>Phil Angelides</PERSON>, who was <LOCATION>California</LOCATION> State Treasurer at the time. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> was sworn in for a second term on January 5, 2007. In May 2004 and 2007, he was named as one of the Time 100 people who help shape the world.

<PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> is married to <PERSON>Maria Shriver</PERSON> and has four children.

<PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> was born in <LOCATION>Thal</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Austria</LOCATION> (German: <PERSON>Thal</PERSON> bei <LOCATION>Graz</LOCATION>), a small village bordering the Styrian capital <LOCATION>Graz</LOCATION>, and was christened <PERSON>Arnold Alois Schwarzenegger</PERSON>. His parents were the local police chief <PERSON>Gustav Schwarzenegger</PERSON> (1907 1972), and his wife, <PERSON>Aurelia Jadrny</PERSON> (1922 1998). They were married on October 20, 1945 <PERSON>Gustav</PERSON> was 38, and <PERSON>Aurelia</PERSON> was a 23-year-old widow with a son named <PERSON>Meinhard</PERSON>. According to <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON>, both of his parents were very strict: "Back then in <LOCATION>Austria</LOCATION> it was a very different world, if we did something bad or we disobeyed our parents, the rod was not spared." He grew up in a Roman Catholic family who attended church service every Sunday.

<PERSON>Gustav</PERSON> had a preference for <PERSON>Meinhard</PERSON>, the elder of the two sons. His favoritism was "strong and blatant," which stemmed from unfounded suspicion "that <PERSON>Arnold</PERSON> wasnt his child." <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> has said his father had "no patience for listening or understanding your problems there was a wall; a real wall." <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> had a good relationship with his mother, and kept in touch with her until her death. In later life, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> commissioned the <PERSON>Simon Wiesenthal Centre</PERSON> to research his father's wartime record, which came up with no evidence of atrocities. At school, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> was apparently in the middle, but stood out for his "cheerful, good-humored and exuberant" character. Money was a problem in the household; <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> has recalled that one of the highlights of his youth was when the family bought a refrigerator.

As a boy, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> played many sports--heavily influenced by his father. He picked up his first barbell in 1960, when his football coach took his team to a local gym. At the age of 14, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> chose bodybuilding over football (soccer) as a career. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> has responded to a question asking if he was age 13 when he started weightlifting: "I actually started weight training when I was fifteen, but I'd been participating in sports, like soccer, for years, so I felt that although I was slim, I was well-developed, at least enough so that I could start going to the gym and start Olympic lifting." However, his official website biography claims: "At 14, he started an intensive training program with <PERSON>Kurt Marnul</PERSON>, studied psychology at 15 (to learn more about the power of mind over body) and at 17, officially started his competitive career." During a speech in 2001, he said, "My own plan formed when I was 14 years old. My father had wanted me to be a police officer like he was. My mother wanted me to go to trade school." <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> took to visiting a gym in <LOCATION>Graz</LOCATION>, where he also frequented the local movie theaters to see bodybuilding idols such as Reg Park, <PERSON>Steve Reeves</PERSON>, and <PERSON>Johnny Weissmuller</PERSON> on the big screen. "I was inspired by individuals like <PERSON>Reg Park</PERSON> and <PERSON>Steve Reeves</PERSON>." When <PERSON>Reeves</PERSON> passed away in 2000, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> fondly remembered him: "As a teenager, I grew up with <PERSON>Steve Reeves</PERSON>. His remarkable accomplishments allowed me a sense of what was possible, when others around me didn't always understand my dreams ... <PERSON>Steve Reeves</PERSON> has been part of everything I've ever been fortunate enough to achieve." In 1961, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> met former Mr. <PERSON>Austria Kurt Marnul</PERSON>, who invited him to train at the gym in <LOCATION>Graz</LOCATION>. He was so dedicated as a youngster that he was known to break into the local gym on weekends, when it was usually closed, so that he could train. "It would make me sick to miss a workout I knew I couldn't look at myself in the mirror the next morning if I didn't do it." When <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> was asked about his first movie experience as a boy, he replied, "I was very young, but I remember my father taking me to the Austrian theaters and seeing some newsreels. The first real movie I saw, that I distinctly remember, was a <PERSON>John Wayne</PERSON> movie."

In 1971, his brother <PERSON>Meinhard</PERSON> died in a car accident. <PERSON>Meinhard</PERSON> had been drinking and was killed instantly, and <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> did not attend his funeral. <PERSON>Meinhard</PERSON> was due to marry <PERSON>Erika Knapp</PERSON>, and the couple shared a three-year-old son <PERSON>Patrick</PERSON>. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> would pay for <PERSON>Patrick</PERSON>'s education and a life in the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION>. <PERSON>Gustav</PERSON> died the following year from a stroke. In Pumping Iron, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> claimed that he did not attend his father's funeral because he was training for a bodybuilding contest. Later, he and the film's producer both stated that this story was taken from another bodybuilder for the purpose of showing the extremes that some would go to for their sport, and to make <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON>'s image more cold and machine-like to fan controversy for the film. <PERSON>Barbara Baker</PERSON>, his first serious girlfriend, has said he informed her of his father's death without emotion and that he never spoke of his brother. Over time, he has given at least three versions of why he did not attend his father's funeral.

In an interview with Fortune magazine in 2004, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> told how he suffered what "would now be called child abuse" at the hands of his father. "My hair was pulled. I was hit with belts. So was the kid next door. It was just the way it was. Many of the children I've seen were broken by their parents, which was the German-Austrian mentality. They didn't want to create an individual. It was all about conforming. I was one who did not conform, and whose will could not be broken. Therefore, I became a rebel. Every time I got hit, and every time someone said, 'you can't do this,' I said, 'this is not going to be for much longer, because I'm going to move out of here. I want to be rich. I want to be somebody.' "

<PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> served in the Austrian army in 1965 to fulfill the one year of service required at the time of all 18-year-old Austrian males. He won the Junior Mr. <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION> contest in 1965. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> went AWOL during basic training so that he could take part in the competition and spent a week in an army jail: "Participating in the competition meant so much to me that I didn't carefully think through the consequences. When I got to <LOCATION>Stuttgart</LOCATION>, I was all confused. I forgot my posing routine, I had to borrow posing trunks, but still I won!" Contrary to popular belief, it was not <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON>'s bodybuilding debut, which had occurred two years earlier at a minor contest in <LOCATION>Graz</LOCATION>, at Steirer Hof Hotel (where he had placed second). He was voted best built man of <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION>, which made him famous.

"The Mr. Universe title was my ticket to <LOCATION>America</LOCATION> the land of opportunity, where I could become a star and get rich." <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> made his first plane trip in 1966, attending the NABBA Mr. Universe competition in <LOCATION>London</LOCATION>. He would come in second in the Mr. Universe competition, not having the muscle definition of American winner <PERSON>Chester Yorton</PERSON>.

<PERSON>Charles</PERSON> "Wag" <PERSON>Bennett</PERSON>, one of the judges at the 1966 competition, was impressed with <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> and offered to coach him. As <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> had little money, <PERSON>Bennett</PERSON> invited him to stay in his crowded family home above one of his two gyms in Forest Gate. <PERSON>Yorton</PERSON>'s leg definition had been judged superior, and <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON>, under a training program devised by <PERSON>Bennett</PERSON>, concentrated on improving the muscle definition and power in his legs. Staying in the East End of <LOCATION>London</LOCATION> helped <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> improve his rudimentary grasp of the English language. The training paid off and in 1967 <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> won the title for the first time in 1967, becoming the youngest ever Mr. Universe at the age of 20. He would go on to win the title a further four times. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> then flew back to <LOCATION>Munich</LOCATION>, training for four to six hours daily, attending business school and working in a health club (<PERSON>Rolf Putzinger</PERSON>'s gym where he worked and trained from 1966-1968), returning in 1968 to <LOCATION>London</LOCATION> to win his next Mr. Universe title. He frequently told <PERSON>Roger C. Field</PERSON>, a friend in <LOCATION>Munich</LOCATION> at that time "I'm going to become the greatest actor!"

<PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> moved to the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> in September 1968 at the age of 21, speaking little English. "Naturally, when I came to this country, my accent was very bad, and my accent was also very strong, which was an obstacle as I began to pursue acting." There he trained at Gold's Gym in <LOCATION>Santa Monica</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>California</LOCATION>, under <PERSON>Joe Weider</PERSON>. From 1970 to 1974, one of <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON>'s weight training partners was <PERSON>Ric Drasin</PERSON>, a professional wrestler who designed the original Gold's Gym logo in 1973. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> also became good friends with professional wrestler "Superstar" <PERSON>Billy Graham</PERSON>. In 1970, at age 23, he captured his first Mr. <PERSON>Olympia</PERSON> title in <LOCATION>New York</LOCATION>, and would go on to win the title a total of seven times.

In 1969, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> met <PERSON>Barbara Outland Baker</PERSON>, an English teacher he lived with until 1974. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> talked about <PERSON>Barbara</PERSON> in his memoir in 1977: "Basically it came down to this: she was a well-balanced woman who wanted an ordinary, solid life, and I was not a well-balanced man, and hated the very idea of ordinary life." <PERSON>Baker</PERSON> has described <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> as "a joyful personality, totally charismatic, adventurous, and athletic" but claims towards the end of the relationship he became "insufferable classically conceited the world revolved around him." <PERSON>Baker</PERSON> published her memoir in 2006, entitled <ORGANIZATION>Arnold</ORGANIZATION> and Me: In the Shadow of the Austrian Oak. Although Baker, at times, paints an unflattering portrait of her former lover <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> actually contributed to the tell-all book with a foreword, and also met with <PERSON>Baker</PERSON> for three hours. <PERSON>Baker</PERSON> claims, for example, that she only learned of his being unfaithful after they split, and talks of a turbulent and passionate love life. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> has made it clear that their respective recollection of events can differ. The couple first met six to eight months after his arrival in the <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> their first date was watching the first Apollo Moon landing on television. They shared an apartment in <LOCATION>Santa Monica</LOCATION> for three and a half years, and having little money, would visit the beach all day, or have barbecues in the back yard. Although <PERSON>Baker</PERSON> claims that when she first met him, he had "little understanding of polite society" and she found him a turn-off, she says, "He's as much a self-made man as it's possible to be he never got encouragement from his parents, his family, his brother. He just had this huge determination to prove himself, and that was very attractive ... I'll go to my grave knowing <PERSON>Arnold</PERSON> loved me."

<PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> met his next love, <PERSON>Sue Moray</PERSON>, a <LOCATION>Beverly Hills</LOCATION> hairdresser's assistant, on <LOCATION>Venice Beach</LOCATION> in July 1977. According to Moray, the couple led an open relationship: "We were faithful when we were both in <LOCATION>LA.</LOCATION>..but when he was out of town, we were free to do whatever we wanted." <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> met <PERSON>Maria Shriver</PERSON> at the <PERSON>Robert F. Kennedy</PERSON> Tennis Tournament in August 1977, and went on to have a relationship with both women until August 1978, when <PERSON>Moray</PERSON> (who knew of his relationship with <PERSON>Shriver</PERSON>) issued an ultimatum.

<PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> has said his big dream from the age of 10 was to move to the <LOCATION>U.S</LOCATION>. He questioned what he was doing "on the farm" in <LOCATION>Austria</LOCATION>, and believed bodybuilding was his "ticket to <LOCATION>America</LOCATION>": "Im sure I can go to <LOCATION>America</LOCATION> if I win Mr. Universe." LA Weekly said in 2002 that <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> is the most famous immigrant in <LOCATION>America</LOCATION>, who "overcame a thick Austrian accent and transcended the unlikely background of bodybuilding to become the biggest movie star in the world in the 1990s."



<PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> is considered among the most important figures in the history of bodybuilding, and his legacy is commemorated in the Arnold Classic annual bodybuilding competition. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> has remained a prominent face in the bodybuilding sport long after his retirement, in part because of his ownership of gyms and fitness magazines. He has presided over numerous contests and awards shows. For many years, he wrote a monthly column for the bodybuilding magazines Muscle & Fitness and Flex. Shortly after being elected Governor, he was appointed executive editor of both magazines, in a largely symbolic capacity. The magazines agreed to donate $250,000 a year to the Governor's various physical fitness initiatives. The magazine <ORGANIZATION>MuscleMag International</ORGANIZATION> has a monthly two-page article on him, and refers to him as "The King."

One of the first competitions he won was the Junior Mr. <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION> contest in 1965. He won Mr. <PERSON>Europe</PERSON> the following year, at age 19. He would go on to compete in and win many bodybuilding contests, as well as some powerlifting contests, including five Mr. Universe (4 <ORGANIZATION>NABBA</ORGANIZATION> [<LOCATION>England</LOCATION>], 1 <ORGANIZATION>IFBB</ORGANIZATION> [<LOCATION>USA</LOCATION>]) wins, and seven Mr. <PERSON>Olympia</PERSON> wins, a record which would stand until <PERSON>Lee Haney</PERSON> won his eighth consecutive Mr. <PERSON>Olympia</PERSON> title in 1991.

In 1967, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> competed in and won the <LOCATION>Munich</LOCATION> stone-lifting contest, in which a stone weighing 508 German pounds (254 kg/560 lbs.) is lifted between the legs while standing on two foot rests. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> has said the following on his size: "During the peak of my career, my calves were 20 inches, thighs 28.5 inches, waist 34 inches, chest 57 inches, and 22-inch arms."

In a full squat (buttocks close to ground) <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> had a personal record of 181 kg/400lbs, for twelve repetitions.

<PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON>'s goal was to become the greatest bodybuilder in the world, which meant becoming Mr. <PERSON>Olympia</PERSON>. His first attempt was in 1969, when he lost to three-time champion <PERSON>Sergio Oliva</PERSON>. However, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> came back in 1970 and won the competition making him the youngest ever Mr. <PERSON>Olympia</PERSON> at the age of 23, a record he holds to this day.

He continued his winning streak in the 1971 1974 competitions. In 1975, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> was once again in top form, and won the title for the sixth consecutive time, beating <PERSON>Franco Columbu</PERSON>. After the 1975 Mr. <PERSON>Olympia</PERSON> contest, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> announced his retirement from professional bodybuilding.

Months before the 1975 Mr. <PERSON>Olympia</PERSON> contest, filmmakers <PERSON>George Butler</PERSON> and <PERSON>Robert Fiore</PERSON> persuaded <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> to compete, in order to film his training in the bodybuilding documentary called Pumping Iron. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> had only three months to prepare for the competition, after losing significant weight to appear in the film Stay Hungry with <PERSON>Jeff Bridges</PERSON>. <PERSON>Lou Ferrigno</PERSON> proved not to be a threat, and a lighter-than-usual <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> convincingly won the 1975 Mr. <PERSON>Olympia</PERSON>. After being declared Mr. <PERSON>Olympia</PERSON> for a sixth consecutive time, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> retired from competition.

<PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> came out of retirement, however, to compete in the 1980 Mr. <PERSON>Olympia</PERSON>. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> was training for his role in <PERSON>Conan</PERSON>, and he got into such good shape because of the running, horseback riding, and sword training, that he decided he wanted to win the Mr. <PERSON>Olympia</PERSON> contest one last time. He kept this plan a secret, in the event that a training accident would prevent his entry and cause him to lose face. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> had been hired to provide color commentary for network television, when he announced at the eleventh hour that while he was there: "Why not compete?" <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> ended up winning the event with only seven weeks of preparation. After being declared Mr. <PERSON>Olympia</PERSON> for a seventh time, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> officially retired from competition.

<PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> has admitted to using performance-enhancing anabolic steroids while they were legal, writing in 1967 that "steroids were helpful to me in maintaining muscle size while on a strict diet in preparation for a contest. I did not use them for muscle growth, but rather for muscle maintenance when cutting up." He has called the drugs "tissue building."

In 1999, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> sued Dr. <PERSON>Willi Heepe</PERSON>, a German doctor who publicly predicted an early death for the bodybuilder, based on a link between steroid use and later heart problems. Because the doctor had never examined him personally, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> collected a DM20,000 ($12,000 USD) libel judgment against him in a German court. In 1999, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> also sued and settled with <ORGANIZATION>The Globe</ORGANIZATION>, a <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> tabloid which had made similar predictions about the bodybuilder's future health. As late as 1996, a year before open heart surgery to replace an aortic valve with a human homograft valve, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> publicly defended his use of anabolic steroids during his bodybuilding career.

<PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> was born with a bicuspid aortic valve, an aortic valve with only two leaflets (a normal aortic valve has three leaflets). Both his father and his brother had the same condition.





<PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> wanted to move from bodybuilding into acting, finally achieving it when he was chosen to play the role of <LOCATION>Hercules</LOCATION> in 1970's Hercules in <LOCATION>New York</LOCATION>. Credited under the name "<PERSON>Arnold Strong</PERSON>," his accent in the film was so thick that his lines were dubbed after production. His second film appearance was as a deaf and mute hit-man for the mob in director <PERSON>Robert Altman</PERSON>'s The Long Goodbye (1973), which was followed by a much more significant part in the film Stay Hungry (1976), for which he was awarded a Golden Globe for New Male Star of the Year. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> has discussed his early struggles in developing his acting career. "It was very difficult for me in the beginning I was told by agents and casting people that my body was 'too weird,' that I had a funny accent, and that my name was too long. You name it, and they told me I had to change it. Basically, everywhere I turned, I was told that I had no chance."

<PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> drew attention and boosted his profile in the bodybuilding film Pumping Iron (1977), elements of which were dramatized. In 1991, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> purchased the rights to the film, its outtakes, and associated still photography. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> auditioned for the title role of The Incredible Hulk, but did not win the role due to his height. Later, <PERSON>Lou Ferrigno</PERSON> got the part of Dr. <PERSON>David Banner</PERSON>'s alter ego. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> appeared with <PERSON>Kirk Douglas</PERSON> and <PERSON>Ann-Margret</PERSON> in the 1979 comedy The Villain.

<PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON>'s breakthrough film was the mythical epic <PERSON>Conan</PERSON> the Barbarian in 1982, which was a box-office hit. This was followed by a sequel, <PERSON>Conan</PERSON> the Destroyer in 1984, although its performance was disappointing. In 1983, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> starred in the promotional video "Carnival in Rio".



In 1984, he made the first of three appearances as the titular character in director <PERSON>James Cameron</PERSON>'s science-fiction film The Terminator. Following The Terminator, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> made <ORGANIZATION>Red Sonja</ORGANIZATION> in 1985, which "sank without a trace."

During the 1980s, audiences had a large appetite for action films, with both <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> and <PERSON>Sylvester Stallone</PERSON> becoming international stars. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON>'s roles reflected his droll, often self-deprecating sense of humor (including sometimes famously bad puns), separating his roles from more serious action hero fare. His alternative-universe comedy/thriller Last Action Hero featured a poster of the movie Terminator 2: Judgment Day which, in the fictional alternate universe, had <PERSON>Sylvester Stallone</PERSON> as its star.

Following his arrival as a Hollywood superstar, he made a number of successful films: Commando (1985), Raw Deal (1986), The Running Man (1987), and Red Heat (1988). In Predator (1987), another successful film, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> led a cast which included future <LOCATION>Minnesota</LOCATION> Governor <PERSON>Jesse Ventura</PERSON> (Ventura also appeared in The Running Man and Batman & <PERSON>Robin</PERSON> with <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON>) and future <ORGANIZATION>Kentucky Gubernatorial</ORGANIZATION> candidate <PERSON>Sonny Landham</PERSON>.

Twins (1988), a comedy with <PERSON>Danny DeVito</PERSON>, was a change of pace, and also proved successful. Total Recall (1990) netted <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> $10 million and 15% of the gross, and was a widely praised, science-fiction script directed by <PERSON>Paul Verhoeven</PERSON>, based on the <PERSON>Philip K. Dick</PERSON> short story, "We Can Remember It for You Wholesale". Kindergarten Cop (1990) reunited him with director <PERSON>Ivan Reitman</PERSON>, who directed him in Twins.

<PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> had a brief foray into directing, first with a 1990 episode of the TV series Tales from the Crypt, entitled "The Switch," and then with the 1992 telemovie Christmas in <LOCATION>Connecticut</LOCATION>. He has not directed since.

<PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON>'s commercial high-water mark was the 1991 sequel to his 1984 hit The Terminator: , which was one of the highest-grossing films of the year. In 1993, the <ORGANIZATION>National Association of Theatre Owners</ORGANIZATION> named him the "International Star of the Decade." His next film project, the 1993 self-aware action comedy spoof Last Action Hero was released opposite Jurassic Park, with the box office suffering accordingly. His next film, the action comedy True Lies (1994) was a highly popular send-up of spy films, and saw <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON>, reunited with The Terminator director <PERSON>James Cameron</PERSON>, appearing opposite <PERSON>Jamie Lee Curtis</PERSON>.

Shortly thereafter came the comedy Junior (1994), directed by <ORGANIZATION>Twins</ORGANIZATION> director <PERSON>Ivan Reitman</PERSON> and again playing opposite co-star <PERSON>Danny DeVito</PERSON>. This film brought <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> his second Golden Globe nomination, this time for Best Actor Musical or Comedy. It was followed by the action thriller Eraser (1996), and the comic book-based <ORGANIZATION>Batman & Robin</ORGANIZATION> (1997), where he played the villain Mr. <PERSON>Freeze</PERSON>. This was his final film before taking time to recuperate from a back injury. Following the failure of <ORGANIZATION>Batman & Robin</ORGANIZATION>, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON>'s film career and box office prominence went into decline.

Several film projects were announced with <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> attached to star, including the remake of Planet of the Apes, a new film version of I Am Legend, and a World War II film scripted by <PERSON>Quentin Tarantino</PERSON> that would have seen <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> play an Austrian.

Instead, he returned after a hiatus with the supernatural thriller ''End of Days'' (1999), later followed by the action films The 6th Day (2000) and Collateral Damage (2002). In 2003, he reprised his role in , which went on to earn over $150 million domestically.



In tribute to <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> in 2002, Forum Stadtpark, a local cultural association, proposed plans to build a 25-meter (82-foot) tall Terminator statue in a park in central <LOCATION>Graz</LOCATION>. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> reportedly said he was flattered, but thought the money would be better spent on social projects and the Special Olympics.

His latest film appearances included a 3-second cameo appearance in The Rundown (AKA, Welcome to the Jungle) with The Rock, and the 2004 remake of Around the World in 80 Days, where he appeared onscreen with action star <PERSON>Jackie Chan</PERSON> for the first time.

<PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> voiced <PERSON>Baron von Steuben</PERSON> in Episode 24 ("Valley Forge") of Liberty's Kids.



<PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> has been a registered Republican for many years. As an actor, his political views were always well-known as they contrasted with those of many other prominent Hollywood stars, who are generally considered to be a liberal and Democratic-leaning community. At the 2004 Republican National Convention, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> gave a speech and explained why he was a Republican:



In 1985, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> appeared in Stop the Madness, an anti-drug music video sponsored by the <PERSON>Reagan</PERSON> administration. He first came to wide public notice as a Republican during the 1988 Presidential election, accompanying then-Vice President <PERSON>George H.W. Bush</PERSON> at a campaign rally.

<PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON>'s first political appointment was as chairman of the President's Council on Physical Fitness and Sports, on which he served from 1990 to 1993. He was nominated by <PERSON>George H. W. Bush</PERSON>, who dubbed him "Conan the Republican". He later served as Chairman for the <LOCATION>California</LOCATION> Governor's <ORGANIZATION>Council on Physical Fitness</ORGANIZATION> and Sports under Governor <PERSON>Pete Wilson</PERSON>. Yet, political analysts have identified <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> as a liberal, as he has become more left-leaning since his election.

Between 1993 and 1994, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> was a <ORGANIZATION>Red Cross</ORGANIZATION> ambassador (a mostly ceremonial role fulfilled by celebrities), recording several television/radio public service announcements to give blood. A small amount of interest was garnered by his wearing of a white t-shirt with the <ORGANIZATION>Red Cross</ORGANIZATION> on it, while posing with a flexed arm; the image made it into several celebrity magazines.

In an interview with Talk magazine in late 1999, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> was asked if he thought of running for office. He replied, "I think about it many times. The possibility is there, because I feel it inside." The Hollywood Reporter claimed shortly after that <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> sought to end speculation that he might run for governor of <LOCATION>California</LOCATION>. Following his initial comments, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> said, "I'm in show business I am in the middle of my career. Why would I go away from that and jump into something else?"

<PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> announced his candidacy in the 2003 <LOCATION>California</LOCATION> recall election for Governor of <LOCATION>California</LOCATION> on the August 6, 2003 episode of The Tonight Show with <PERSON>Jay Leno</PERSON>. As a candidate in the recall election, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> had the most name recognition in a crowded field of candidates, but he had never held public office and his political views were unknown to most Californians. His candidacy immediately became national and international news, with media outlets dubbing him the "Governator" (referring to The Terminator movies, see above) and "The Running Man" (the name of another one of his films), and calling the recall election "Total Recall" (yet another Schwarzenegger starrer). <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> declined to participate in several debates with other recall replacement candidates, and appeared in only one debate on September 24, 2003.

On October 7, 2003, the recall election resulted in Governor <PERSON>Gray Davis</PERSON> being removed from office with 55.4% of the Yes vote in favor of a recall. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> was elected Governor of <LOCATION>California</LOCATION> under the second question on the ballot with 48.6% of the vote to choose a successor to <PERSON>Davis</PERSON>. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> defeated Democrat <PERSON>Cruz Bustamante</PERSON>, fellow Republican <PERSON>Tom McClintock</PERSON>, and others. His nearest rival, <PERSON>Bustamante</PERSON>, received less than 30% of the vote. In total, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> won the election by about 1.3 million votes. Under the regulations of the <LOCATION>California</LOCATION> Constitution, no runoff election was required. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> was the first foreign-born governor of <LOCATION>California</LOCATION> since Ireland-born Governor <PERSON>John G. Downey</PERSON> in 1862.

As soon as <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> was elected governor, <PERSON>Willie Brown</PERSON> said he would start a drive to recall the governor. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> was equally entrenched in what he considered to be his mandate in cleaning up gridlock. Building on a catchphrase from a sketch partly parodying his bodybuilding career, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> called the <ORGANIZATION>Democratic State</ORGANIZATION> politicians "girlie men," (a reference from a Saturday Night Live sketch called "<PERSON>Hans</PERSON> and Franz").



<PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> enjoyed a large degree of success and victories in his early governorship, including repealing an unpopular increase in the vehicle registration fee as well as preventing driver's licenses being given out to illegal immigrants, but later began to feel the backlash when powerful state unions began to oppose his various initiatives. Key among his reckoning with political realities was a special election he called in November 2005, in which four ballot measures he sponsored were defeated. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> accepted personal responsibility for the defeats and vowed to continue to seek consensus for the people of <LOCATION>California</LOCATION>. He would later comment that "no one could win if the opposition raised 160 million dollars to defeat you."

<PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> then went against the advice of fellow <ORGANIZATION>Republican</ORGANIZATION> strategists and appointed a Democrat, <PERSON>Susan Kennedy</PERSON>, as his Chief of Staff. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> gradually moved towards a more politically moderate position, determined to build a winning legacy with only a short time to go until the next gubernatorial election.

He has appeared alongside his fellow actor from Around the World in 80 Days, <PERSON>Jackie Chan</PERSON>, in a government advertisement to combat copyright infringement.

<PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> ran for re-election against Democrat <PERSON>Phil Angelides</PERSON>, the <LOCATION>California</LOCATION> State Treasurer, in the 2006 elections, held on November 7, 2006. Despite a poor year nationally for the Republican party, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> won re-election with 56.0% of the vote compared with 38.9% for <PERSON>Angelides</PERSON>, a margin of well over one million votes. The election further enhanced his political credentials.

It is rumored that <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> might run for the <ORGANIZATION>United States Senate</ORGANIZATION> in 2010 (he will be term-limited then), if incumbent Senator <PERSON>Barbara Boxer</PERSON> retires.

<PERSON>Wendy Leigh</PERSON>, who wrote an unofficial biography on <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON>, claims he plotted his political rise from an early age using the movie business and bodybuilding as building blocks to escape a depressing home. <PERSON>Leigh</PERSON> portrays <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> as obsessed with power and quotes him as saying, "I wanted to be part of the small percentage of people who were leaders, not the large mass of followers. I think it is because I saw leaders use 100% of their potential I was always fascinated by people in control of other people." <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> has said that it was never his intention to enter politics, but he says, "I married into a political family. You get together with them and you hear about policy, about reaching out to help people. I was exposed to the idea of being a public servant and <PERSON>Eunice</PERSON> and <PERSON>Sargent Shriver</PERSON> became my heroes." <PERSON>Eunice Kennedy Shriver</PERSON> was sister of <PERSON>John F. Kennedy</PERSON>, and mother-in-law to <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON>, <PERSON>Sargent Shriver</PERSON> is husband to <PERSON>Eunice</PERSON> and father-in-law to <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON>. According to the 2005 Year-in-Review issue of Time magazine, supporters of <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> are hoping to amend the Constitution so that he can run for President of the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION>. Indeed, in The Simpsons Movie, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> is portrayed as the President and in the <PERSON>Sylvester Stallone</PERSON> movie Demolition Man, it is revealed there was an amendment to the constitution that allowed him to become President.

<PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> is a dual Austria/United States citizen. He holds Austrian citizenship by birth and has held <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> citizenship since becoming naturalized in 1983. Being Austrian and thus European he was able to win the 2007 <ORGANIZATION>European Voice</ORGANIZATION> campaigner of the year award for taking action against climate change with the <LOCATION>California</LOCATION> Global Warming Solutions Act of 2006 and plans to introduce an emissions trading scheme with other <LOCATION>US</LOCATION> states and possibly with the <ORGANIZATION>EU</ORGANIZATION>.

<PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> does not accept his governor's salary of $175,000 per year, and instead donates it to charities.

<PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON>'s endorsement in the Republican primary of the 2008 <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> Presidential election was highly sought; despite being good friends with candidates <PERSON>Rudy Giuliani</PERSON> and Senator <PERSON>John McCain</PERSON>, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> remained neutral throughout 2007 and early 2008. <PERSON>Giuliani</PERSON> dropped out of the Presidential race on January 30, 2008, largely because of a poor showing in <LOCATION>Florida</LOCATION>, and endorsed <PERSON>McCain</PERSON>. Later that night, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> was in the audience at a Republican debate at the <PERSON>Ronald Reagan</PERSON> Presidential Library in <LOCATION>Simi Valley</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>California</LOCATION>. The following day, he endorsed <PERSON>McCain</PERSON>, joking, "It's <PERSON>Rudy</PERSON>'s fault!" (in reference to his friendships with both candidates and that he could not make up his mind). <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON>'s endorsement was thought to be a boost for Senator <PERSON>McCain</PERSON>'s campaign; both spoke about their environmental concerns and concerns for the economy.

Governor <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> played a significant role in opposing Proposition 66, a proposed amendment of the Californian Three Strikes Law, in November 2004. This amendment would have required the third felony to be either violent and/or serious in order to mandate a 25-years-to-life sentence. In the last week before the ballot, governor <PERSON>Arnold Schwarzenegger</PERSON> launched an intensive campaign against Proposition 66. He stated that "it would release 26,000 dangerous criminals and rapists".





On September 27, 2006 <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> signed a bill creating the nations first cap on greenhouse gas emissions. The law set new regulations on the amount of emissions utilities, refineries and manufacturing plants are allowed to release into the atmosphere. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> also signed a second global warming bill that prohibits large utilities and corporations in <LOCATION>California</LOCATION> from making long-term contracts with suppliers who do not meet the states greenhouse gas emission standards. The two bills are part of a plan to reduce Californias emissions by 25 percent to 1990s levels by 2020. In 2005, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> issued an executive order calling to reduce greenhouse gases to 80 percent below 1990 levels by 2050.

<PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> signed another executive order on October 17, 2006 allowing <LOCATION>California</LOCATION> to work with the <ORGANIZATION>Northeasts Regional Greenhouse Gas Initiative</ORGANIZATION>. They plan to reduce carbon dioxide emissions by issuing a limited amount of carbon credits to each power plant in participating states. Any power plants that exceed emissions for the amount of carbon credits they have will have to purchase more credits to cover the difference. The plan is set to be in effect in 2009.In addition to using his political power to fight global warming, the governor has taken steps at his home to reduce his personal carbon footprint. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> has adapted one of his Hummers to run on hydrogen and another to run on biofuels. He has also installed solar panels to heat his home.

In 1977, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON>'s autobiography/weight-training guide Arnold: The Education of a Bodybuilder was published and became a huge success. After taking English classes at <ORGANIZATION>Santa Monica College</ORGANIZATION> in <LOCATION>Santa Monica</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>California</LOCATION>, he earned a B.A., from the <ORGANIZATION>University of Wisconsin-Superior</ORGANIZATION>, where he graduated Business and <ORGANIZATION>International Economics</ORGANIZATION>, in 1979.

On April 26, 1986, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> married television journalist <PERSON>Maria Shriver</PERSON>, niece of the past President of the <LOCATION>United States John F. <PERSON>Arnold Schwarzenegger</PERSON> has also had a highly successful business career. Following his move to the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> became a "prolific goal setter" and would write his objectives at the start of the year on index cards, like starting a mail order business or buying a new car and succeed in doing so. His financial independence came from a series of successful business ventures and investments. In 1968, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> and fellow bodybuilder <PERSON>Franco Columbu</PERSON> started a bricklaying business. He would go on to invest in a number of real estate holding companies. In 1992, <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> and his wife opened a restaurant in <LOCATION>Santa Monica</LOCATION> called Schatzi On Main. <PERSON>Schatzi</PERSON> literally means "little treasure," colloquial for "honey" or "darling" in German. <PERSON>Arnold Schwarzenegger</PERSON> was a founding celebrity investor in the Planet Hollywood chain of international theme restaurants (modeled after the Hard Rock Cafe) along with <PERSON>Bruce Willis</PERSON>, <PERSON>Sylvester Stallone</PERSON>, and <PERSON>Demi Moore</PERSON>. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> severed his financial ties with the business in early 2000. <PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON> said the company had not had the success he had hoped for, claiming he wanted to focus his attention on "new <LOCATION>US</LOCATION> global business ventures" and his movie career.

<PERSON>Schwarzenegger</PERSON>'s net worth has been conservatively estimated at $100 $200 million. Over the years, he invested his bodybuilding and movie earnings in an array of stocks, bonds, privately controlled companies, and real estate holdings in the <LOCATION>U.S.</LOCATION> and worldwide, so his fortune is sometimes estimated as high as $800 million. <TITLE><PERSON>Bill Oddie</PERSON></TITLE>

<PERSON>William Edgar Oddie</PERSON>, OBE (born 7 July 1941) is an English actor, writer, comedian, natural history presenter and musician. He became famous as one of the The Goodies.

A birdwatcher since his childhood in <LOCATION>Birmingham</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Oddie</PERSON> has now established a reputation for himself as an ornithologist, conservationist and television presenter on wildlife issues. Some of his books are illustrated with his own paintings and drawings.

<PERSON>Oddie</PERSON>'s wildlife programmes for the <ORGANIZATION>BBC</ORGANIZATION> include: <LOCATION>Springwatch</LOCATION> / Autumnwatch, How to Watch Wildlife, Wild In Your Garden, Birding with <PERSON>Bill Oddie</PERSON>, <LOCATION>Britain</LOCATION> Goes Wild with <PERSON>Bill Oddie</PERSON> and <PERSON>Bill Oddie</PERSON> Goes Wild.

One of these, a revue called A Clump of Plinths, was so successful during its run at the <ORGANIZATION>Edinburgh Fringe Festival</ORGANIZATION> that it was renamed <LOCATION>Cambridge Circus</LOCATION> and transferred to the <LOCATION>West End</LOCATION> in <LOCATION>London</LOCATION>, then <LOCATION>New Zealand</LOCATION> and Broadway in September 1964. Meanwhile, still at <LOCATION>Cambridge</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Oddie</PERSON> wrote scripts for TV's That Was The Week That Was. 

His first television appearance was in <PERSON>Bernard Braden</PERSON>'s On The Braden Beat in 1964. Subsequently, he was a key member of the performers in the cult <ORGANIZATION>BBC</ORGANIZATION> radio series I'm Sorry, I'll Read That Again (<LOCATION>ISIRTA</LOCATION>; 1965), where many of his musical compositions were featured. Some were released on the album Distinctly <PERSON>Oddie</PERSON> (Polydor, 1967). He was possibly one of the first performers to parody a rock song, arranging the traditional <LOCATION>Yorkshire</LOCATION> folk song "On Ilkla Moor Baht'at" in the style of <PERSON>Joe Cocker</PERSON>'s hit rendition of the Beatles' "With a Little Help from My Friends" (released on <PERSON>John Peel</PERSON>'s <ORGANIZATION>Dandelion Records</ORGANIZATION> in 1970 and featured in Peel's special box of most-treasured singles), and singing "<PERSON>Andy Pandy</PERSON>" in the style of a brassy soul number such as <PERSON>Wilson Pickett</PERSON> or <PERSON>Geno Washington</PERSON> might perform. In one song on I'm Sorry, I'll Read That Again, <PERSON>Oddie</PERSON> performed "What a Wonderful World" with a voice fully reminiscent of <PERSON>Louis Armstrong</PERSON>. During the course of the song, the rest of the cast attributed the gravelly quality of his voice to a sore throat. In the background, during the rest of the song, it is possible to hear the cast dispense cough medicine, then call for a doctor, the arrival of the doctor and his decision that <PERSON>Oddie</PERSON> should go into hospital, the trip to hospital in an ambulance, and the operation extracting his tonsils. After this, the sound of his voice changed to a sound closer to that of <PERSON>Harry Secombe</PERSON>. He thanked the cast for curing him.

<TITLE><PERSON>Albert Camus</PERSON></TITLE>

<PERSON>Albert Camus</PERSON> () (7 November 1913 4 January 1960) was an Algerian-born French author, philosopher, and journalist who won the Nobel prize in 1957. He is often associated with existentialism, but <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> refused this label. On the other hand, as he wrote in his essay The Rebel, his whole life was devoted to opposing the philosophy of nihilism. On the subject of his belief or not in God, he writes in the third volume of his notebooks: "I do not believe in God and I am not an atheist."

In 1949, <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> founded the Group for <ORGANIZATION>International Liaisons</ORGANIZATION> in the <ORGANIZATION>Revolutionary Union Movement</ORGANIZATION>, according to the book <PERSON>Albert Camus</PERSON>, une vie by <PERSON>Olivier Todd</PERSON>, a group opposed to the atheist and communistic tendencies of the surrealistic movement of <LOCATION>Andr Breton</LOCATION>. <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> was the second-youngest recipient of the Nobel Prize for Literature (after <ORGANIZATION>Rudyard Kipling</ORGANIZATION>) when he became the first African-born writer to receive the award, in 1957. He is also the shortest-lived of any literature laureate to date, having died in an automobile accident only three years after receiving the award.

<PERSON>Camus</PERSON> preferred to be known as a man and a thinker, rather than as a member of a school or ideology. He preferred persons over ideas. In an interview in 1945, <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> rejected any ideological associations: "No, I am not an existentialist. <PERSON>Sartre</PERSON> and I are always surprised to see our names linked"

<PERSON>Albert Camus</PERSON> was born on 7 November 1913 in <LOCATION>Mondovi</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Algeria</LOCATION> to a French-Algerian (pied-noir) settler family. His mother was of Spanish extraction and was half-deaf. His father, <PERSON>Lucien</PERSON>, died in the Battle of the Marne in 1914 during the First World War, while serving as a member of the Zouave infantry regiment. <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> lived in poor conditions during his childhood in the <LOCATION>Belcourt</LOCATION> section of <LOCATION>Algiers</LOCATION>. In 1923, he was accepted into the lyce and eventually to the <ORGANIZATION>University of Algiers</ORGANIZATION>. However, he contracted tuberculosis in 1930, which put an end to his football activities (he had been a goalkeeper for the university team) and forced him to make his studies a part-time pursuit. He took odd jobs including private tutor, car parts clerk and work for the <ORGANIZATION>Meteorological Institute</ORGANIZATION>. He completed his licence de philosophie (<ORGANIZATION>BA</ORGANIZATION>) in 1935; in May 1936, he successfully presented his thesis on Plotinus, No-Platonisme et <PERSON>Pense Chrtienne</PERSON>, for his diplme d'tudes suprieures (roughly equivalent to an M.A. by thesis). 

<PERSON>Camus</PERSON> joined the <ORGANIZATION>French Communist Party</ORGANIZATION> in the Spring of 1935 seeing it as a way to "fight inequalities between Europeans and 'natives' in <LOCATION>Algeria</LOCATION>", he did not suggest he was a Marxist or that he had read <PERSON>Das Kapital</PERSON>, but did write that "[w]e might see communism as a springboard and asceticism that prepares the ground for more spiritual activities". In 1936, the independence-minded Algerian <ORGANIZATION>Communist Party</ORGANIZATION> (PCA) was founded. <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> joined the activities of the Algerian <ORGANIZATION>People's Party</ORGANIZATION> (<ORGANIZATION>Le Parti du Peuple Algrien</ORGANIZATION>), which got him into trouble with his Communist party comrades. As a result, he was denounced as a Trotskyite and expelled from the party in 1937. <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> went on to be associated with the French anarchist movement. The anarchist <PERSON>Andre Prudhommeaux</PERSON> first introduced him at a meeting in 1948 of the Cercle des Etudiants Anarchistes (Anarchist Student Circle) as a sympathiser who was familiar with anarchist thought. <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> went on to write for anarchist publications such as <ORGANIZATION>Le Libertaire</ORGANIZATION>, La rvolution Proletarienne and <PERSON>Solidaridad Obrera</PERSON> (the organ of the anarcho-syndicalist CNT). <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> also stood with the anarchists when they expressed support for the uprising of 1953 in <LOCATION>East Germany</LOCATION>. He again stood with the anarchists in 1956, first with the workers uprising in <LOCATION>Poznan</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Poland</LOCATION>, and then later in the year with the Hungarian Revolution. 

In 1934, he married <PERSON>Simone Hie</PERSON>, a morphine addict, but the marriage ended as a consequence of infidelities on both sides. In 1935, he founded Thtre du Travail "Worker's Theatre" (renamed Thtre de l'Equipe ("Team's Theatre") in 1937), which survived until 1939. From 1937 to 1939 he wrote for a socialist paper, <PERSON>Alger-Rpublicain</PERSON>, and his work included an account of the peasants who lived in <LOCATION>Kabylie</LOCATION> in poor conditions, which apparently cost him his job. From 1939 to 1940, he briefly wrote for a similar paper, Soir-Republicain. He was rejected by the French army because of his tuberculosis.

In 1940, <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> married <PERSON>Francine Faure</PERSON>, a pianist and mathematician. Although he loved <PERSON>Francine</PERSON>, he had argued passionately against the institution of marriage, dismissing it as unnatural. Even after <PERSON>Francine</PERSON> gave birth to twins, <PERSON>Catherine</PERSON> and <PERSON>Jean</PERSON>, on 5 September 1945, he continued to joke wearily to friends that he was not cut out for marriage. <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> conducted numerous affairs, particularly an irregular and eventually public affair with the Spanish-born actress <PERSON>Maria Casares</PERSON>. In the same year <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> began to work for <ORGANIZATION>Paris-Soir</ORGANIZATION> magazine. In the first stage of World War II, the so-called Phony War stage, <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> was a pacifist. However, he was in <LOCATION>Paris</LOCATION> to witness how the Wehrmacht took over. On 15 December 1941, <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> witnessed the execution of <PERSON>Gabriel Pri</PERSON>, an event that <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> later said crystallized his revolt against the Germans. Afterwards he moved to <LOCATION>Bordeaux</LOCATION> alongside the rest of the staff of Paris-Soir. In the same year he finished his first books, The Stranger and The Myth of Sisyphus. He returned briefly to <LOCATION>Oran</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Algeria</LOCATION> in 1942.

During the war <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> joined the French Resistance cell Combat, which published an underground newspaper of the same name. This group worked against the <ORGANIZATION>Nazis</ORGANIZATION>, and in it <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> assumed the nom de guerre "Beauchard". <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> became the paper's editor in 1943, and when the Allies liberated <LOCATION>Paris</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> reported on the last of the fighting. He was, however, one of the few French editors to publicly express opposition to the use of the atomic bomb in <LOCATION>Hiroshima</LOCATION> soon after the event on 8 August 1945. He eventually resigned from Combat in 1947, when it became a commercial paper. It was then that <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> became acquainted with <PERSON>Jean-Paul Sartre</PERSON>.

After the war, <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> began frequenting the <ORGANIZATION>Caf de Flore</ORGANIZATION> on the <LOCATION>Boulevard Saint-Germain</LOCATION> in <LOCATION>Paris</LOCATION> with <PERSON>Sartre</PERSON> and others. <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> also toured the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION> to lecture about French thinking. Although he leaned left politically, his strong criticisms of Communist doctrine did not win him any friends in the Communist parties and eventually also alienated <PERSON>Sartre</PERSON>. 

In 1949 his tuberculosis returned and he lived in seclusion for two years. In 1951 he published The Rebel, a philosophical analysis of rebellion and revolution which made clear his rejection of communism. The book upset many of his colleagues and contemporaries in <LOCATION>France</LOCATION> and led to the final split with <PERSON>Sartre</PERSON>. The dour reception depressed him and he began instead to translate plays.

<PERSON>Camus</PERSON>' first significant contribution to philosophy was his idea of the absurd, the result of our desire for clarity and meaning within a world and condition that offers neither, which he explained in The Myth of Sisyphus and incorporated into many of his other works, such as The Stranger and The Plague. Despite the split from his "study partner," <PERSON>Sartre</PERSON>, some still argue that <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> falls into the existentialist camp. However, he rejected that label himself in his essay Enigma and elsewhere (see: The Lyrical and Critical Essays of <PERSON>Albert Camus</PERSON>). The current confusion may still arise as many recent applications of existentialism have much in common with many of <PERSON>Camus</PERSON>' practical ideas (see: Resistance, Rebellion, and Death). However, the personal understanding he had of the world (e.g. "a benign indifference", in The Stranger), and every vision he had for its progress (i.e. vanquishing the "adolescent furies" of history and society, in The Rebel) undoubtedly sets him apart.

In the 1950s <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> devoted his efforts to human rights. In 1952 he resigned from his work for <ORGANIZATION>UNESCO</ORGANIZATION> when the <ORGANIZATION>UN</ORGANIZATION> accepted <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION> as a member under the leadership of General <PERSON>Franco</PERSON>. In 1953 he criticized Soviet methods to crush a workers' strike in <LOCATION>East Berlin</LOCATION>. In 1956 he protested against similar methods in <LOCATION>Poland</LOCATION> (protests in <LOCATION>Pozna</LOCATION>) and the Soviet repression of the Hungarian revolution in October. 



He maintained his pacifism and resistance to capital punishment anywhere in the world. One of his most significant contributions to the movement against capital punishment was an essay collaboration with <PERSON>Arthur Koestler</PERSON>, the writer, intellectual and founder of the League Against Capital Punishment.



When the Algerian War began in 1954 it presented a moral dilemma for <PERSON>Camus</PERSON>. He identified with pied-noirs, and defended the French government on the grounds that the revolt in <LOCATION>Algeria</LOCATION> was really an integral part of the 'new Arab imperialism' led by <LOCATION>Egypt</LOCATION> and an 'anti-Western' offensive orchestrated by <LOCATION>Russia</LOCATION> to 'encircle <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION>' and 'isolate the <LOCATION>United States</LOCATION>'. Although favouring greater Algerian autonomy or even federation, though not full-scale independence, he believed that the pied-noirs and Arabs could co-exist. During the war he advocated civil truce that would spare the civilians, which was rejected by both sides who regarded it as foolish. Behind the scenes, he began to work for imprisoned Algerians who faced the death penalty. 

From 1955 to 1956 <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> wrote for <ORGANIZATION>L'Express</ORGANIZATION>. In 1957 he was awarded the Nobel Prize in literature "for his important literary production, which with clear-sighted earnestness illuminates the problems of the human conscience in our times.", officially not for his novel The Fall, published the previous year, but for his writings against capital punishment in the essay Rflexions sur la Guillotine. When he spoke to students at the <ORGANIZATION>University of Stockholm</ORGANIZATION>, he defended his apparent inactivity in the Algerian question and stated that he was worried about what might happen to his mother, who still lived in <LOCATION>Algeria</LOCATION>. This led to further ostracism by French left-wing intellectuals.

In 1949 <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> founded the Group for <ORGANIZATION>International Liaisons</ORGANIZATION> in the <ORGANIZATION>Revolutionary Union Movement</ORGANIZATION>. With <PERSON>George Orwell</PERSON>, he opposed totalitarian regimes in the East and the West.

As he wrote in L'Homme rvolt (in the chapter about "The Thought on Midday") he was a follower of the ancient Greek 'Solar Tradition' (la pense solaire). So, not only he was the leader of the French resistance movement "Combat" but he also set up in 1947-8 the <ORGANIZATION>Revolutionary Union Movement</ORGANIZATION> (Groupes de liaison internationale - GLI) which was formed in 1949 and can be described as a trade union movement in the context of revolutionary syndicalism (Syndicalisme rvolutionnaire). For more, see the book : <PERSON>Alfred Rosmer</PERSON> et le mouvement rvolutionnaire internationale by Christian Gras).

His colleagues were <PERSON>Nicolas Lazarvitch</PERSON>, <PERSON>Louis Mercier</PERSON>, <PERSON>Roger Lapeyre</PERSON>, <PERSON>Paul Chauvet</PERSON>, <PERSON>Auguste Largentier</PERSON>, <PERSON>Jean de Bo</PERSON> (see the article: "<PERSON>Nicolas Lazarvitch</PERSON>, Itinraire d'un syndicaliste rvolutionnaire" by <PERSON>Sylvain Boulouque</PERSON> in the review Communisme, n 61, 2000). His main aim was to express the positive side of Surrealism and Existentialism, rejecting the negativity and the nihilism of <LOCATION>Andr Breton</LOCATION> and <PERSON>Jean-Paul Sartre</PERSON>.

In 1944 <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> founded the "French Committee for the <ORGANIZATION>European Federation</ORGANIZATION>" (<ORGANIZATION>Comit Franais</ORGANIZATION> pour la Fderation Europene -CFFE) declaring that <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION> "can only evolve along the path of economic progress, democracy and peace if the nation states become a federation".

From 1943, <PERSON>Albert Camus</PERSON> had correspondence with <PERSON>Altiero Spinelli</PERSON> who founded the <ORGANIZATION>European Federalist Movement</ORGANIZATION> in <LOCATION>Milan</LOCATION> (see Ventotene Manifesto and the book "Unire l'Europa, superare gli stati", <PERSON>Altiero Spinelli</PERSON> nel Partito d'Azione del <ORGANIZATION>Nord Italia</ORGANIZATION> e in <LOCATION>Francia</LOCATION> dal 1944 al 1945-annexed a letter by <PERSON>Altiero Spinelli</PERSON> to <PERSON>Albert Camus</PERSON>).

In 22-25 March 1945, the first conference of the <ORGANIZATION>European Federalist Movement</ORGANIZATION> was organised in <LOCATION>Paris</LOCATION> with the participation of <PERSON>Albert Camus</PERSON>, <PERSON>George Orwell</PERSON>, <PERSON>Emmanuel Mounier</PERSON>, <PERSON>Lewis Mumford</PERSON>, <PERSON>Andr Philip</PERSON>, <PERSON>Daniel Mayer</PERSON>, <PERSON>Franois Bondy</PERSON> and <PERSON>Altiero Spinelli</PERSON> (see the book "The Biography of <LOCATION>Europe</LOCATION>" by Pan Drakopoulos). This specific branch of the <ORGANIZATION>European Federalist Movement</ORGANIZATION> disintegrated in 1957 after the domination of <PERSON>Winston Churchill</PERSON>'s ideas about the European integration.

Three essays by Dr. <PERSON>Miho Takashima</PERSON> in the <ORGANIZATION>International Journal of Humanities</ORGANIZATION> ("Revolt and Equilibrium: A Comparative Study of Nineteen Eighty-Four and L'Homme Rvolt, the Views and Struggles of <PERSON>Orwell</PERSON> and <PERSON>Camus</PERSON>", "Art and Representation: A Comparative Study of <PERSON>George Orwell</PERSON> and <PERSON>Albert Camus</PERSON> on their Literary Works", and "<PERSON>George Orwell</PERSON> and <PERSON>Albert Camus</PERSON>: A Comparative Study Their Views and Dilemmas in the Politics of the 1930s and 40s") explore the relation between the work of the French writer <PERSON>Albert Camus</PERSON> and the English writer <PERSON>George Orwell</PERSON>.

<PERSON>Takashima</PERSON> argues that Orwellperhaps intentionally, in order to warn the intellectual elitecompromised with "Big Brother", while <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> confronted with The Plague. This is observed not only in the comparison between Nineteen Eighty-Four and The Rebel but, especially, in <PERSON>Camus</PERSON>' play The State of Siege. This theatrical play was written together with the novel The Plague and the essay The Rebel. It is the work whichaccording to <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> himselfrepresents him best and is a response to <PERSON>George Orwell</PERSON>'s Nineteen Eighty-Four. The hero, <PERSON>Diego</PERSON>, opposes the totalitarian dictator named Plague, and dies in order to set a Spanish town free from the Inquisition.

The State of Siege is a work against totalitarianism, written in the same epoch when <PERSON>Camus</PERSON>' idol, <PERSON>George Orwell</PERSON>, wrote Nineteen Eighty-Four. The play includes an allegorical reference to the end of <PERSON>Orwell</PERSON>'s novel.

The original title of The State of Siege was The Holy Inquisition in <LOCATION>Cadix</LOCATION>. In the French edition of the book, <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> has included an essay under the title "Why <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION>?". In this polemical text, he answers his <ORGANIZATION>Catholic</ORGANIZATION> friend <PERSON>Gabriel Marcel</PERSON> who criticized him for setting the plot in <LOCATION>Spain</LOCATION>. Here <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> expresses his opposition to the totalitarian regimes of the West, and to the behavior of the <LOCATION>Vatican</LOCATION> and the Pope during World War II. The most important phrase of this essay is "Why <LOCATION>Guernica</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Gabriel Marcel</PERSON>?".

<PERSON>Camus</PERSON> died on 4 January 1960 in an automobile accident near <LOCATION>Sens</LOCATION>, in a place named "Le Grand Fossard" in the small town of <LOCATION>Villeblevin</LOCATION>. In his coat pocket lay an unused train ticket. It is possible that he had planned to travel by train, but decided to go by car instead.The driver of the Facel Vega car, <PERSON>Michel Gallimard</PERSON> his publisher and close friend also perished in the accident. <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> was interred in the <LOCATION>Lourmarin Cemetery</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Lourmarin</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Vaucluse</LOCATION>, Provence-Alpes-Cte d'Azur, <LOCATION>France</LOCATION>. 

He was survived by his twin children, <PERSON>Catherine</PERSON> and <PERSON>Jean</PERSON>, who hold the copyrights to his work.

After his death, two of <PERSON>Camus</PERSON>' works were published posthumously. The first, entitled A Happy Death published in 1970, featured a character named <LOCATION>Meursault</LOCATION>, as in The Stranger, but there is some debate as to the relationship between the two stories. The second posthumous publication was an unfinished novel, The First Man, that <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> was writing before he died. The novel was an autobiographical work about his childhood in <LOCATION>Algeria</LOCATION> and was published in 1995.

Many writers have written on the Absurd, each with his or her own interpretation of what the Absurd actually is and their own ideas on the importance of the Absurd. For example, <PERSON>Sartre</PERSON> recognizes the absurdity of individual experience, while <PERSON>Kierkegaard</PERSON> explains that the absurdity of certain religious truths prevent us from reaching God rationally. <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> was not the originator of Absurdism and regretted the continued reference to him as a philosopher of the absurd. He shows less and less interest in the Absurd shortly after publishing ''Le Mythe de Sisyphe'' (''The Myth of Sisyphus''). To distinguish <PERSON>Camus</PERSON>' ideas of the Absurd from those of other philosophers, people sometimes refer to the Paradox of the Absurd, when referring to <PERSON>Camus</PERSON>' Absurd.

His early thoughts on the Absurd appeared in his first collection of essays, L'Envers et l'endroit (The Two Sides Of The Coin) in 1937. Absurd themes appeared with more sophistication in his second collection of essays, Noces (Nuptials), in 1938. In these essays <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> does not offer a philosophical account of the Absurd, or even a definition; rather he reflects on the experience of the Absurd. In 1942 he published the story of a man living an Absurd life as ''L'tranger'' (''The Stranger''), and in the same year released ''Le Mythe de Sisyphe'' (''The Myth of Sisyphus''), a literary essay on the Absurd. He had also written a play about a Roman Emperor, Caligula, pursuing an Absurd logic. However, the play was not performed until 1945. The turning point in <PERSON>Camus</PERSON>' attitude to the Absurd occurs in a collection of four letters to an anonymous German friend, written between July 1943 and July 1944. The first was published in the Revue Libre in 1943, the second in the Cahiers de Libration in 1944, and the third in the newspaper <ORGANIZATION>Liberts</ORGANIZATION>, in 1945. All four letters have been published as Lettres un ami allemand (Letters to a German Friend) in 1945, and have appeared in the collection Resistance, Rebellion, and Death.

In his essays <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> presented the reader with dualisms: happiness and sadness, dark and light, life and death, etc. His aim was to emphasize the fact that happiness is fleeting and that the human condition is one of mortality. He did this not to be morbid, but to reflect a greater appreciation for life and happiness. In <PERSON>Le Mythe</PERSON>, this dualism becomes a paradox: We value our lives and existence so greatly, but at the same time we know we will eventually die, and ultimately our endeavours are meaningless. While we can live with a dualism (I can accept periods of unhappiness, because I know I will also experience happiness to come), we cannot live with the paradox (I think my life is of great importance, but I also think it is meaningless). In <PERSON>Le Mythe</PERSON>, <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> was interested in how we experience the Absurd and how we live with it. Our life must have meaning for us to value it. If we accept that life has no meaning and therefore no value, should we kill ourselves?

<LOCATION>Meursault</LOCATION>, the Absurdist hero of L'tranger, is a murderer who is executed for his crime. Caligula ends up admitting his Absurd logic was wrong and is killed by an assassination he has deliberately brought about. However, while <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> possibly suggests that <PERSON>Caligula</PERSON>'s Absurd reasoning is wrong, the play's anti-hero does get the last word, as the author similarly exalts <LOCATION>Meursault</LOCATION>'s final moments.

<PERSON>Camus</PERSON>' understanding of the Absurd promotes public debate; his various offerings entice us to think about the Absurd and offer our own contribution. Concepts such as cooperation, joint effort and solidarity are of key importance to <PERSON>Camus</PERSON>. 

<PERSON>Camus</PERSON> made a significant contribution to a viewpoint of the Absurd, and always rejected nihilism as a valid response. "If nothing had any meaning, you would be right. But there is something that still has a meaning." Second Letter to a German Friend, December 1943.

What still had meaning for <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> is that despite humans being subjects in an indifferent and "absurd" universe, in which meaning is challenged by the fact that we all die, meaning can be created, however provisionally and unstably, by our own decisions and interpretations.

Throughout his life, <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> spoke out against and actively opposed totalitarianism in its many forms. Early on, <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> was active within the French Resistance to the German occupation of <LOCATION>France</LOCATION> during World War II, even directing the famous Resistance journal, Combat. On the French collaboration with <ORGANIZATION>Nazi</ORGANIZATION> occupiers he wrote:

<PERSON>Camus</PERSON>' well-known falling out with <PERSON>Sartre</PERSON> is linked to this opposition to totalitarianism. <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> detected a reflexive totalitarianism in the mass politics espoused by <PERSON>Sartre</PERSON> in the name of radical Marxism. This was apparent in his work L'Homme Rvolt (The Rebel) which not only was an assault on the Soviet police state, but also questioned the very nature of mass revolutionary politics. <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> continued to speak out against the atrocities of the <LOCATION>Soviet Union</LOCATION>, a sentiment captured in his 1957 speech, The Blood of the Hungarians, commemorating the anniversary of the 1956 Hungarian Revolution, an uprising crushed in a bloody assault by the <ORGANIZATION>Red Army</ORGANIZATION>.



In The Stranger, <PERSON>Albert Camus</PERSON> characterizes his justification of the absurd through the experiences of a protagonist who simply does not conform to the system. His inherent honesty disturbs the status quo; <LOCATION>Meursault</LOCATION>'s inability to lie cannot seamlessly integrate him within society and in turn threatens the simple fabrics of human mannerisms expected of a structurally ordered society. Consequently, the punishment for his crime is not decided on the basis of murder, but rather for the startling indifference towards his mother's recent death. Even after a conflicting spiritual discussion with a pastor inciting <LOCATION>Meursault</LOCATION> to consider a possible path towards redemption, the latter still refuses to take upon salvation and symbolizes his ultimatum by embracing the "gentle indifference of the world"; an act which only furthers his solidarity with a society incapable of realizing his seemingly inhumane and misanthropic behavior.

The plague is an undeniable part of life. As posited in The Plague, it is omnipresent, just like death was always an impeding factor in The Stranger. <PERSON>Albert Camus</PERSON> once again questions the meaning of the moral concepts justifying humanity and human suffering within a religious framework. For <PERSON>Camus</PERSON>, the rationale behind <ORGANIZATION>Christian</ORGANIZATION> doctrine is useless; as mortal beings, we cannot successfully rationalize the impending and inescapable death sentence forced upon every human. The plague, which befalls upon Oran, is a concrete and tangible facilitator of death. Ultimately, the plague enables people to understand that their individual suffering is meaningless. As the epidemic "evolves" within the seasons, so do the citizens of <LOCATION>Oran</LOCATION>, who instead of willfully giving up to a disease they have no control over, decide to fight against their impending death, thus unwillingly creating optimism in the midst of hopelessness. This is where <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> channels his thoughts behind the importance of solidarity: although the plague is still primarily an agent of death, it provides the uncanny opportunity for people to realize that individual suffering is absurd. In the midst of complete suffering, the challenging response adopted by the majority of the citizens of <LOCATION>Oran</LOCATION> demonstrates an inexplicable humanistic connection between distraught and distant characters. Only by taking the choice to fight an irreversible epidemic are people able to create the ever-lacking meaning to a life destined for execution the moment of its creation.

<PERSON>Camus</PERSON> was once asked by his friend <PERSON>Charles Poncet</PERSON> which he preferred, football or the theatre. <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> is said to have replied, "Football, without hesitation."

<PERSON>Camus</PERSON> played as goalkeeper for <ORGANIZATION>Racing Universitaire Algerios</ORGANIZATION> (RUA won both the North African Champions Cup and the North African Cup twice each in the 1930s) junior team from 192830. The sense of team spirit, fraternity, and common purpose appealed to <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> enormously. In match reports <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> would often attract positive comment for playing with passion and courage. Any aspirations in football disappeared at age 17, upon contracting tuberculosisthen incurable, <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> was bedridden for long and painful periods.

When <PERSON>Camus</PERSON> was asked in the 1950s by an alumni sports magazine for a few words regarding his time with the RUA, his response included the following:

<PERSON>Camus</PERSON> was referring to a sort of simplistic morality he wrote about in his early essays, the principle of sticking up for your friends, of valuing bravery and fair-play. <PERSON>Camus</PERSON>' belief was that political and religious authorities try to confuse us with over-complicated moral systems to make things appear more complex than they really are, potentially to serve their own needs.

Several of <PERSON>Camus</PERSON>' works have been adapted into movies. The Stranger has been adapted into an Italian 1967 movie by <PERSON>Luchino Visconti</PERSON>, and also to a 2001 Turkish adaptation titled Yazgi (Fate) by <PERSON>Zeki Demirkubuz</PERSON>.
